xix Author  
G. Keith Still teaches crowd science at several UK universities. He has lec-  
tured at the UK Cabinet Office Emergency Planning College since 1999 and  
developed the EPC’s Crowd Dynamics and Crowd Science materials and  
workshops as well as contributing to several other safety-related courses.  
He is a fellow of the Institute of Mathematics and its Applications, a fellow  
of the Institute of Civil Protection and Emergency Management and a spe-  
cialist fellow of the international institute of risk and safety management.  
Keith’s mathematical, human behaviour modelling and simulation tools  
have led to the development of a systematic blueprint for the crowd safety  
industry. His tools \(Legion, Paramics UAF, Myriad, Shepard, Tawaf,  
DWELL, Progressive Crowd Collapse and Crowd Pressure simulations\)  
have been used for the analysis of crowds in complex and built spaces for  
over a decade. He has also worked as a safety coordinator, safety advisor  
and expert witness on many of the world’s most dangerous and challenging  
events. He has developed educational workshops teaching the theory and  
applications of crowd science, which he has applied directly to major events  
around the world  
Although he has developed and used some of the most sophisticated  
crowd modelling and simulation tools, his work remains true to the prin-  
ciples of his original research\: to develop a model of crowds specifically  
aimed at improving crowd safety. To this aim his teaching and training  
programmes do not use computer simulations to model complex sites.  
xxi Chapter 1  
# Introduction  
Someone who builds a mathematical model can get carried away with  
all the clever things that can be done with it. The model becomes a safe  
little world, free from anxiety, free from office politics, rewarding in its  
own right. This often results in a very clever model that has little to do  
with reality.  
Sam L. Savage, author of INSIGHT.xla\: Business  
Analysis Software for Microsoft Excel  
This is not a book about the clever things you can do with a computer. Nor  
is this book about deep scientific discussion and argument, complex math-  
ematical techniques, simulations or computer technology. In fact, we argue  
that crowd simulations must be used with extreme caution when applied to  
major events—particularly if they relate to crowd safety.  
This is a book about how to apply simple, practical techniques to help  
you understand crowd risks. Specifically, this is a book in which we outline  
how to apply a range of tools and techniques to help you plan for crowds in  
places of public assembly at your event.  
We outline case studies where these tools have improved our understand-  
ing of event risk analysis and made a significant improvement to crowd  
safety around the world. We explore the underlying causes that can lead to  
mass fatalities, and provide the reader with a comprehensive, methodical,  
systematic approach to crowd risk analysis and safety engineering. The aim  
of this book is simple\: to improve your understanding of crowd risks; to  
help you plan for, and manage, your crowds safely.  
WHY DO WE NEED THIS?  
Around the world, event organisers are planning increasingly large, chal-  
lenging events while also trying to minimise expenditure, recoup costs  
and maximise profits. With larger events comes the potential for larger  
incidents, and, sadly, we are seeing an increase in crowd-related incidents  
around the world.  
1 2 Introduction to crowd science  
Crowd injuries—such as overcrowding and crushing—can be avoided  
through the application of foresight, planning and simple risk analysis tech-  
niques. Although crowds are just one element of an event plan, they are an  
element in which safety cannot be compromised.  
The event process, from conception to delivery, consists of three distinct  
phases\: planning, approval\* and operations. During the planning phase, the  
event organiser should define the event in a document that should include a  
safety concept and crowd management plan. This document will describe  
the event, its location and duration, and crowd capacity. It should detail the  
necessary emergency, contingency and safety plans, and it should include  
a comprehensive risk assessment that identifies various potential risks or  
hazards associated with the event.  
The event may require some form of approval \(e.g. a licence or permit\);  
during the approval phase the plan and risk assessment should be checked.  
If the plan and safety concept are deemed feasible, a licence or permit may  
be granted. This phase of the event is a quality assurance process and needs  
to be performed by a competent person. That person, or persons, must  
understand events and the associated crowd risks to ensure the plan is ‘fit for  
purpose’; that the risks have been identified and will be managed safely. The  
event will then run \(the operations phase\) in accordance with the event plan.  
The process varies from country to country, but the principles are the  
same\: planning, approval and operations are three phases of the event pro-  
cess where risk analysis techniques must be both understood and rigorously  
applied in order to be effective. Sadly, this is not always the case. There  
are many events where there are neither approval processes nor competent  
persons checking event safety. Furthermore, many events are unlicensed  
and remain outside of both scrutiny and sanction, with officials there-  
fore unable to enforce safety changes. Some of these may be significant  
crowd events, such as Christmas markets, air shows, fêtes, bonfires, street  
processions and victory parades, charity events, Christmas lights switch-  
on, book-signing, celebrity guest appearances and so on. As a result the  
approval process—a critical part of the safety model—may be neglected.  
Are simulations the answer?  
Over the last 25 years, we have developed a wide range of crowd simulation  
systems and applied them to crowd safety and risk analysis for many of the  
world’s largest and most challenging events. During the earlier years of our  
research, crowd simulations were essential tools. They helped us to develop  
an understanding of crowd risks, and they enabled us to experiment with a  
wide range of behavioural assumptions. You can experiment with crowds  
\*  
Various terms are used around the world; here, we will use the term ‘approval’ to cover all  
terms associated with licensing or permitting an event. Introduction 3  
in a computer-generated environment in a way that is simply not possible in  
real time \(for obvious safety reasons\). This proved to be essential in under-  
standing the interactions between crowds and their environment. With  
crowd simulations, we could understand how risks develop into incidents  
and how incidents can escalate into disasters, but the simulation approach  
was both expensive and time consuming.  
Embedded within the computer simulation, there is a mathematical  
model; this is a numerical recipe describing the crowd dynamics, the inter-  
actions and the way individuals behave in a range of situations. The crowd  
simulation is limited by the assumptions built into the mathematical model;  
the simulation process will not improve on an incorrect or unsuitable set of  
assumptions. As the saying goes, ‘Garbage in, garbage out’.  
In the original PhD research, we stated that a computer simulation would  
assist in both the event planning and the event approval processes. There  
were significant obstacles to achieving that goal. As we stated in the thesis  
\(http\://www.gkstill.com/CV/PhD/Chapter9.html\)\:  
There were three objectives to this research\: Firstly, to understand the  
nature of crowd dynamics with respect to crowd safety; secondly, to  
develop a more appropriate risk analysis methodology for the design  
and management of places where crowds may develop. Finally, to cre-  
ate a tool that allows others to understand the nature, problems and  
necessary components of crowds and crowd dynamics.  
G. Keith Still, PhD thesis\: Crowd Dynamics, 2000  
What we know  
We know more about crowds now than we did 25 years ago, but we may  
never know everything about crowds. There are many things we do not yet  
fully understand about the human condition. For example, how and why  
people react under normal and emergency situations; their decision-making  
process as individuals, as groups, as families and so forth. There are many  
elements we simply cannot measure, such as emotion, anger and aggres-  
sion levels. We do not know exactly how a wide range of internal or exter-  
nal factors—such as mood and weather, aggression and music—­ influence  
crowds. There is a long list of unknowns. We have to make assumptions  
about human behaviour for our mathematical models.  
Crowd simulations can help us to explore these assumptions and build  
better mathematical models. They are useful tools for exploring the theo-  
retical relationships between internal and external factors on the crowd,  
and there are many research applications for these types of simulations.  
Our focus is on crowd risk analysis and safety engineering, our concern  
being whether or not crowd simulation can make a significant improvement  
to the event planning process. 4 Introduction to crowd science  
Uses of a crowd simulation  
Some \(not all\) crowd analysis projects require complex computer simula-  
tions merely in order to develop an understanding of the complexity and  
degree of crowd interactions of people in spaces over time. For example, as  
a special advisor for crowd safety in Saudi Arabia, we worked on the design  
of the Jamarat Bridge \(Mina Valley\), where 3 million pilgrims perform the  
‘stoning of the devil’ ritual every day during Hajj \(the annual pilgrimage  
to Mecca\). This project involved a wide area where millions of pilgrims  
would be moving on five different levels, in multiple directions, completing  
complex rituals. Computer simulation was an important research tool in  
understanding the overall crowd dynamics across such a wide space and on  
so many levels of a complex structure. However, it was not the only tool we  
used to model, and understand the complexity of the site.  
Site mapping  
To understand crowd movements on the proposed new Jamarat Bridge,  
we started by mapping out the site on a drawing board using a series of  
overlays\: transparent sheets of drafting film, placed on top of the site plan.  
We drew the routes the crowds would take under normal and emergency  
situations, and checked dimensions such as travel distance and minimum  
width along those routes. In this way we could separate crowd flow, direc-  
tions, congestion, risk and most/least used spaces. It was an essential step  
in understanding the complexity of the site.  
We applied the same type of analysis to each of the five levels of the new  
bridge design and worked methodically, area-by-area, section-by-section,  
through the whole system. We dissected the site to help us build a clearer  
model of the overall crowd dynamic. Only when we had a complete con-  
struction of the crowd flow paths and areas of potential risk were we able  
to code those parameters into the computer simulation. This process of site  
analysis was key to understanding the large-scale complex environments  
where millions of pilgrims live, move, pray and perform the various rituals  
of Hajj.  
We were working with a range of site engineers and crowd safety opera-  
tions managers during this process, asking them key questions about man-  
agement techniques, infrastructure, systems integration, design constraints,  
information and communications systems. This was a very complex site and  
there were a lot of different groups working together to manage the area.  
We had to develop a systematic approach to collecting all the information  
relevant to normal and emergency processes and procedures. Capturing  
that information was challenging given the language differences, and we  
had to develop techniques of structuring the information for later ­ analysis.  
These processes—mapping the site, identifying crowd movements and Introduction 5  
areas of risk—were tools that helped us collect and collate information for  
later analysis.  
Workshops  
Closer to home, we have worked with the crowd management teams for the  
wedding of Prince William and Kate Middleton \(2011\); London New Year  
events \(2006–2012\); and the London Olympics \(2012\). For these projects a  
combination of computer models, real-time data analysis, meta-models and  
the principles and applications of crowd science were fundamental to the  
event planning, risk analysis and safe management of the events. Typically,  
projects begin with a workshop where the various groups meet to outline  
their concerns, and resolutions are discussed and agreed.  
In addition to working on major projects, we have run teaching and train-  
ing programmes to help delegates understand the key issues around crowd  
risks and safety engineering principles. Out of these, two courses developed\:  
Introduction to Crowd Science \(a foundation degree course\) and Applied  
Crowd Science, BA \(Hons\). We have run 30–40 of these courses every year,  
around the world, where delegates bring their concerns to the workshop and  
we help them through the risk and safety analysis applications.  
Teaching and training  
Since early 2000, we have been teaching practical methods for crowd mod-  
elling, focussing on ‘simple to apply’ and ‘simple to understand’ techniques  
for assessing crowd risks and evaluating crowd safety strategies. We devel-  
oped a workshop approach to problem solving that was similar to what we  
were using in our consulting projects—to identify and catalogue the key  
information for crowd risk analysis. We teach the same methods as we use  
on major projects.  
Workshops had proven to be a successful means of understanding site-related  
issues while teaching delegates how to understand the fundamentals of crowd-  
related risks for major events. Delegates were typically crowd safety operations  
managers—the people who plan and operate events—so there was a wealth  
of practical experience to draw upon for our development projects, and good  
general understanding of the key issues related to crowd risks. Workshops  
have helped us develop better mathematical models of crowd behaviour and  
improve our understanding of crowd risks; specifically, they have helped us  
develop more appropriate crowd-modelling tools for crowd safety.  
As a result of running safety workshops for over 15 years, the teach-  
ing techniques and modelling tools we have developed are not academic  
or theoretical exercises\: they are based on real-world situations. They are  
practical and applicable tools, techniques and methodologies. Those tools  
are the subject of this book. 6 Introduction to crowd science  
CROWD MODELLING  
During these workshops we noted that there were many unreported crowd  
risks at major events. Specifically the delegates’ concerns were about safe-  
guarding against the risks of major incidents. As our focus is crowd safety,  
we developed methods to help the delegates to understand those risks and  
how to apply that understanding to their own events. We show delegates  
how to model crowd movement and how to map areas of potential risk.  
This helps the delegates transfer experience onto paper, and records the  
nature of those risks for further analysis.  
All of our projects start with the same fundamental modelling method of  
route and risk mapping. We have developed and used a wide range of site  
modelling tools to assist in this process. Sometimes it is more appropriate  
to use commercially available tools such as Google Earth and a variety of  
distance/route/area mapping software. Sometimes, a simple map, sketch  
or diagram of the site is sufficient to identify key areas of crowd risk. We  
use all of these tools to help delegates investigate a site, its transportation  
infrastructure, and available spaces for the crowds. This process helps the  
delegates build a model of how the crowd may move under both normal  
and emergency situations.  
Crowd safety projects  
We consult on major projects around the world, and every project begins  
by collecting information and collating it into a form we can analyse. Every  
analysis begins in a similar manner, with crowd flow diagrams and site  
sketches. This is the same fundamental process that we teach in the work-  
shops\: simple line drawings to understand the crowd flow and potential  
risks, area and flow mapping and flow calculations.  
Common to every project, we found that crowd risks could be identi-  
fied through the collection and collation of event information. For complex  
sites, where the use of crowd simulation may have been necessary, we still  
had to collect the same type of information and map out the site before  
coding the computer. We use that information to code the computer simula-  
tion, but the fundamentals of crowd risks are already apparent in the data  
collection and collation process. We asked ourselves the question, ‘What  
did the computer simulation tell us that we could not readily discover using  
the event information, the crowd flow and routing diagrams, the area and  
route mapping, risk analysis and mapping techniques?’  
The answer was very little—and it took a long time to realise this.  
Developing a crowd simulation took years; producing a good event simu-  
lation for a major project took weeks, sometimes months. Testing an event  
simulation took days and validating the crowd and event simulation also  
took time. Inevitably this led us to question our simulation approach. Did Introduction 7  
we really need the computer simulation? The time and cost of development  
of the computer simulation was considerable, the cost to the end user would  
have to reflect those development costs. We decided to revisit the procedure  
in order to see whether the cost of a computer simulation could be justi-  
fied, and, more importantly, whether there was a better, more cost-effective  
method of assessing risks for complex crowded environments.  
Project analysis  
All of our projects began with a series of meetings, during which the cli-  
ent would outline their key concerns. We would capture crowd and event  
information and distil it into three phases\: ingress \(how the crowds arrive  
and entered a site\); circulation \(how the crowds moved around the site\); and  
egress \(how the crowds would leave the site\). We would assess the crowd  
risks for all three phases under both normal and emergency situations.  
We would then review the site/event plans and perform an analysis of the  
system by simplifying the information into a series of models. We would  
map crowd movements as series of overlays on top of site plans; we would  
continue to refine this overlay process, mapping out the crowd movements  
for all three phases \(ingress, circulation and egress\) under normal and  
emergency situations.  
Each phase had two maps\: one for normal crowd movements, and another  
for emergency situations. When placed on top of each other, these layers  
provided us with a deeper insight into the nature of site design and crowd  
risk analysis. We could see how ingress, circulation and egress routing over-  
lapped in critical areas; at high density those areas could develop a risk of  
overcrowding. By tracing back along the routes towards those high-density  
areas, we could see where to implement crowd management procedures.  
If the event site was complex, we would take this overlay information  
and convert it into the parameters for our computer simulation system.  
The computer would enable us to test the sensitivity limits of those critical  
areas—for example, how long it may take for the approaching crowds to  
reach critical density. This process involves varying the initial conditions  
and observing any changes in the developing conditions. By performing  
a range of simulations, we could analyse the range of potential risks. The  
results of these simulations would be documented along with a set of rec-  
ommendations, typically in the form of a report and analysis, which would  
be produced and handed to the client for review. This would be followed by  
further meetings and, if required, more simulations.  
The simulation process was, in general, mostly a graphical display for the cli-  
ent’s benefit, to aid their understanding. The graphs, tables and reams of data  
that were produced by the simulation were included in the report and analy-  
sis of the site, but this was data that only a computer scientist would under-  
stand. This process certainly improved our own understanding of the crowd 8 Introduction to crowd science  
risks for complex environments, but did little to aid ­ clients’ ­ understanding of  
the fundamental principles of crowd risks. Top-level ­ clients—the executives,  
ministers and architects—typically only wanted to see the crowd moving on  
the computer simulation, to assure themselves that the site would function. It  
was someone else’s job to operate and manage the event.  
We were in a unique position of having complete input and control across  
all stages of the process. From a mathematical perspective, we would write  
algorithms turning human characteristics into computer formulae; as pro-  
grammers we could alter the internal algorithms in the simulation; and  
finally, as modellers \(and end users of the tools\) we could run simulations  
to test these hypotheses. If the results were not satisfactory, we could go  
back to the formulae, rewrite the algorithms, recode them into the models,  
retest the simulations and validate these against real-world observations.  
Our objective throughout this entire process was always to understand the  
limits of the crowd dynamic, to understand how risk might develop and  
then to alter the design, information or management processes to reduce  
those risks. The analysis of the crowd dynamic and the event/site risks were  
the inputs to the computer simulation; the data came from underlying mod-  
els of the site, our sketches and diagrams.  
When we discovered a risk, we did not use the computer simulations to  
fix the problem. The crowd simulation was not designed to provide solu-  
tions—only to quantify the location, duration and severity of the problem.  
We were using other tools, models, methods and techniques to address the  
risk mitigation or management process. For example, where a crowd man-  
agement solution was required, the question would be, ‘Where is the best  
place to manage crowds for this specific site?’ The answer to this would  
already have been established during the mapping process—by tracing  
back along the routes towards those high-density areas, we could see where  
to implement crowd management procedures. That was the pivotal point in  
the development of crowd science; we were only using the computer simula-  
tion to gain insight into the nature of specific site- or event-related crowd  
risks. The solutions were often discovered during the mapping process  
prior to running the computer simulation.  
Sometimes we recommended a design change \(such as barrier deploy-  
ment\) and could use the simulation to test the effectiveness of the design  
change. Other times we would recommend information requirements such  
as site maps, public instructions, public announcements, signage and stew-  
ards/ushers/marshals. Those elements we could not test in a computer sim-  
ulation. There were many options that could help us manage those risks,  
but the computer simulation did not give us solutions—it only helped to  
identify some of the problems. We still had to apply a design, information  
or, more typically, a management solution.  
The same is true of all other computer simulations in many other engi-  
neering disciplines\: simulations may help identify the nature of a problem; Introduction 9  
they may help you test one solution against another to see which is the most  
effective. But simulations alone rarely provide a solution to that problem.  
Simulation problems  
The temptation to leap straight to the computer is greatest when the prob-  
lem appears to be complex. We have observed many examples where crowd  
simulations have been applied to problems that appeared complex, but  
which could have been resolved using simpler methods. Computer simula-  
tions are simply tools; they are only as good as the underlying assumptions  
built into them. If these assumptions are unclear, the computer will not  
make them clearer. The results may look good, but they could be garbage!  
The problem is often compounded when a user has invested in a computer  
system and has to justify its expense. The ‘law of the instrument’, a concept  
described by the psychologist Abraham Maslow \(1908–1970\), illustrates  
this over-reliance on one tool \(Chapter 2, Section 5\)\:  
I suppose it is tempting, if the only tool you have is a hammer, to treat  
everything as if it were a nail.  
Abraham Maslow  
Psychology of Science, 1966  
In general, to devise a solution to any problem you need to understand  
a wide range of potential solutions for that specific problem. You need to  
have some method of understanding which solution may work best; you  
need to develop a range of methodologies for problem solving, rather than  
relying on just one method. To accomplish this you can also study the ways  
in which others have solved similar problems, and which solutions they  
have found to work best.  
For crowd-related issues you need to understand how risks may be  
reduced, and how that risk reduction process can be measured or assessed  
in order to make the best improvements. This requires practice and a  
lot of experience, and you cannot practise on real crowds; mistakes can  
cost lives.  
Explore the risk  
Computer simulation is a tool that can help us explore and understand  
the elements that give rise to crowd risks. Crowd simulations may help  
quantify and qualify the scale of the problem, whether the risk is small,  
medium or great. The crowd simulation is not designed to provide solu-  
tions to risk management—only to assist in quantifying the location, dura-  
tion and severity of the risk. Crowd simulations may also mislead the user  
if the underlying mathematical models are inaccurate. Risk reduction, 10 Introduction to crowd science  
management and mitigation processes need to work with real crowds, not  
computer-simulated crowds.  
In the course of our work, and over many years of application in some of  
the world’s most challenging projects, we seriously questioned both the need  
and the value of the crowd simulation process for the following reasons\:  
•  
•  
•  
The simulations did not produce solutions. Our clients engaged us for  
solutions, usually because they had concerns relating to crowd risks. We  
needed tools, methods and frameworks in order to identify and reduce  
those risks; our role was not simply to tell a client he had a problem.  
The continual cost of developing and using computer simulations had  
to be recovered—primarily through increased consulting fees—and  
this was a financial barrier for many clients.  
A crowd simulation is a particularly complex process, and this meant  
developing highly skilled users. Selling a crowd simulation to a client  
meant they had to invest in skilled staff and simulation training; this  
was a significant investment that the client struggled to justify.  
As a result of these costs, organisers of many events, that were in need  
of risk analysis techniques, were not able to afford the process of crowd  
­ simulation—either as a product or as a service. The costs meant we were fail-  
ing to reach a wider audience, and hence failing to improve crowd safety. We  
were failing in our wider objective, which was to create a tool that allowed  
others to understand the nature and problems of crowd dynamics.  
Understanding crowd risks  
Having worked on many challenging international projects, we had exten-  
sive experience of crowd problems and risk analysis. We would not have  
achieved as much so quickly, or produced as many solutions, without the  
use of simulations, but we were in a unique position nonetheless.  
In the workshops, we were teaching the principles of crowd risk analysis  
and working with delegates \(experienced crowd safety operations manag-  
ers\) who had influence over crowd safety across a wide range of events, and  
who understood those risks. A diverse group attended the workshops, from  
stewards to police commanders with responsibility for indoor and outdoor  
events, large and small, the United Kingdom and abroad. Common ques-  
tions began to emerge\:  
How do we identify crowd risks in the planning phase of an event?  
How do we assess whether or not a system of entry/exit points will  
work?  
How do we manage the crowd flow into, around, and leaving a site?  
What are the key parameters of crowd risk and crowd safety? Introduction 11  
There was a wealth of information produced by the workshops, specifi-  
cally regarding near misses; accidents and incidents that did not reach the  
mainstream media and remained unreported and unknown. Also many  
best practices, across a wide range of events that were not being recorded  
or passed on to other parts of the industry. As a result of this ‘isolated  
experience’ there were significant gaps in fundamental understanding of  
crowd risks, risk management and the principles of risk mitigation across  
the industry as a whole.  
Mistakes made in one part of the country \(or indeed anywhere the  
world\), or at one particular type of event, or concerning a specific type of  
crowd behaviour, were neither captured nor published. We noted common  
mistakes—repeated time and time again—made at different events. When  
staff left an organisation their knowledge left with them, and when a new  
management team or event safety contractor arrived they often repeated  
previous mistakes. For over 20 years we have reviewed events, accidents  
and incidents, best practices, situations that came close to failure, near  
misses and so on, and we have integrated all of this information into the  
teaching and training programmes.  
Over the years, there were more than a few occasions when a new del-  
egate would outline an event we had seen before. For some events, the same  
problems, risk analysis or site issues were discussed again, with a new del-  
egate. We introduced new mapping processes and modelling techniques to  
ensure event information and knowledge were captured by the delegates;  
in effect we were learning how the industry should be learning and apply-  
ing these models. To enhance our teaching and training programmes, we  
developed an applied course, with individual training and specific focus on  
the delegates’ events. This led to a Foundation Degree module and a BA  
\(Hons\) module, respectively.  
RESEARCH BACKGROUND  
When we began our research into crowds, we did so from a background  
of mathematical modelling, computer science and operations research.\* We  
were also fortunate enough to be offered unlimited access to the Wembley  
complex \(London\), which meant working with the operations teams at the  
stadium, arena and conference centre. As a result, we could study many of  
the challenges faced in hosting major international events—from both the  
management and the crowds’ perspective.  
Although Wembley has an excellent safety record, the operations direc-  
tor, police and safety managers were always looking for ways to improve,  
\*  
A scientific discipline dealing with the application of advanced analytical methods to  
improve decision making. 12 Introduction to crowd science  
to understand and reduce the risks to crowds. This is one of the key ele-  
ments in crowd safety and risk analysis\: a diligent and proactive crowd  
management team, always looking for better methods, better informa-  
tion and better tools to assist them in their job; always looking to improve  
crowd and event safety. This was typical of the clients we worked with, and  
specifically of the delegates who came on the workshops.  
Risk management  
Understanding risks and managing risks are two different disciplines. The  
workshop delegates wanted to understand crowd risk in order to keep their  
crowds as safe as possible; workshops were designed to facilitate risk analy-  
sis, to understand what may happen when things do not go exactly to plan.  
The delegates wanted to learn about crowd safety, from a scientific perspec-  
tive, and to develop management tools that would help them understand  
the dynamics of risk. Furthermore, risks can change over time; a crowd’s  
reaction to a certain situation may not always be the same. The crowd’s  
perception of a risk can change, but the risk may remain unchanged. For  
example, during one project we evaluated risks of trips, slips and falls on a  
street environment during road works. The risk was constant, it remained  
unchanged, but as people walked past the road works they would pay less  
and less attention to the hazard. Their perceptions of the risk were dimin-  
ishing with each pass-by although the risk remained constant.  
It was at that point the research took a dramatic turn.  
Improving crowd safety  
The aim of our original research was to improve crowd safety. We knew  
of two incidents where mass fatalities had occurred on sites that had used  
crowd simulations and were passed as ‘safe’; we know of many more sites  
where management teams have struggled to maintain crowd safety after  
the site has been built. Many of these designs have also passed as ‘safe’ in a  
crowd simulation system. Crowd simulations seemed to be failing to iden-  
tify many of the crowd risks, and this was a significant cause for concern.  
Clients who wanted crowd simulation were typically site design teams—  
groups who were interested in value engineering and making the most  
economical use of their spaces, and for design-related analysis, crowd simu-  
lations were very useful. We could test one solution against another, which  
meant we could investigate the value of two different solutions and under-  
stand how best to utilise key areas and space, but this was not risk analysis\:  
it was value engineering.  
Risk analysis, and risk management, is an event planning and approval  
process, not a site design function. Our objectives were to understand Introduction 13  
crowd risk and to develop improved methods of addressing those risks; the  
simulations did not help us in that process.  
Safe little world  
We began our work by writing code and algorithms \(mathematical for-  
mulae\), and by designing and programming systems. As computer scien-  
tists, we were using computer simulations with confidence and control in  
programming parameters and environments. We also knew the limitations  
of our models and how easily they could become ‘a safe little world, free  
from anxiety, free from office politics, rewarding in its own right’. We  
could build environments and computer-generated landscapes, and we  
programmed the entire system from algorithm to end-user application. We  
could also test the environment in every way we wanted; in the right hands,  
these tools did \(and still do\) offer the trained engineer significant insights  
into crowd dynamics. But they were far from the goal of actually improving  
crowd safety, since these were complex systems to program and use, and  
expensive to purchase. Beyond the budget, and skills, of the event planners  
licensing/approval authorities and crowd managers.  
An end user of a crowd simulation requires a wide range of skills and  
a multidisciplinary approach to the scientific exploration of crowds.  
Simulation systems can include behaviour \(psychology\), management and  
operational practices, crowd reactions, influence from external factors,  
physics, dynamics, computer programming skills and data analysis. This  
scientific skill set is not the same as that of the groups who work with and  
understand the fundamentals of crowd risks.  
Crowd management  
We have had the pleasure of working with crowd managers, planning and  
managing major events who have far more insight, knowledge and expe-  
rience than that provided by our computer simulations. A good crowd  
management team is both proactive and reactive to changing crowd situ-  
ations. Crowds can—and do—react to many different influences during  
an event; a good crowd safety operations manager, with specific \(and  
often expansive\) knowledge of their specific environment, would adapt  
to the dynamics of the environment. Crowd safety operations managers  
tend not to be computer users \(beyond word processors or accounting  
spreadsheets\).  
We realised we had a problem\: how would our computer simulations  
achieve the objective of improving safety if the people who needed them  
most were not computer users? This was a difficult paradigm to change. 14 Introduction to crowd science  
WHO ARE THE END USERS OF CROWD  
SIMULATION?  
Using and understanding a crowd simulation system requires a significant  
capital investment—first to buy the system, and then to train people to use  
it effectively. The problem was cost. Event planners, the approval authori-  
ties and crowd safety operations managers—the people who have the most  
influence on crowd safety—simply did not have the budget for crowd simu-  
lation systems.  
Crowd simulations were therefore not going to achieve our goal of  
improving crowd safety.  
Realising the problem  
The simulations we designed had only one real purpose\: to understand the  
nature of crowd risks. Ultimately, we were aiming to define better opera-  
tional guidance for places of public assembly. That guidance would only be  
of use to the crowd safety operations manager if they understood how to  
apply it and understood the risk analysis process. To improve crowd safety,  
we needed first to understand the needs of the crowd safety operations man-  
agers—the people who would have most impact on crowd safety.  
If we were going to succeed in our objective, it would have to be redefined\:  
we would have to make it compatible with the events industry business model.  
Safety budget  
The events industry is a business, and businesses need to make profit in  
order to exist. Worldwide, the events industry is worth an estimated \$500  
billion, with profits rising annually. Businesses survive on profit margins  
and need to maintain cost controls in order to do so; safety does not con-  
tribute to the profits of the business.  
The events safety officer and/or crowd safety operations manager are  
often fighting for resources and budget \(cost\). Event organisers typically  
want as many people as possible at their event in order to maximise their  
investment \(profit\). Safety managers want to maximise safety at the event  
\(cost\). There is a long list of resources required for safe event management,  
such as staff costs, staff equipment and clothing, appropriate staff training,  
radios and control room hardware, and larger events require significant  
investment for safety infrastructure \(cost\). Crowd safety systems are like  
insurance; you need to invest in safety \(cost\) but there is no return on that  
investment \(cost but no profit\). Investment in safety always has the same  
outcome\: nothing untoward happens! All cost, no profit.  
With event organisers looking to maximise returns and save on costs, the  
safety budget is always under pressure. This creates a problem with cost Introduction 15  
justification for further safety measures\: typically the comment ‘If nothing  
happened last time, why do we need further investment?’ This attitude  
made computer systems difficult to both define and justify as a cost-versus-  
benefit analysis. If you cannot measure the improvement to crowd safety  
when there are no tangible elements to measure, the best you can hope for is  
no incidents; how could the business justify improved safety systems using  
computer simulations?  
There are only a few super-events worldwide per year; they are a consul-  
tants’ market, and an expert using expert tools can \(and will\) provide valuable  
services in this market. But this was not the market where the most improve-  
ments could be made towards risk reduction and safer crowd management.  
Prices driven down  
There are several companies around the world selling crowd simulation  
systems \(and high-level training\) to consultants. These are of use in major  
projects, during the design phase and for value engineering purposes. It  
takes an expert to understand the crowd simulation output as well as the  
input; this expertise comes from experience of crowds and not from the  
computer simulations. We have noted many so-called ‘crowd experts’ pub-  
lishing materials relating to crowds—specifically design-related concepts—  
that a crowd safety operations manager would reject.  
If not used correctly by experts, with crowd management experience,  
who can understand both the limitations to their simulation and the risks  
in the real world, crowd simulations can be dangerous. More worryingly,  
there are also many competing crowd simulation developers with no crowd  
management experience\: computer scientists who sell consulting services  
to the events industry. These services compete for the same clients and  
prices are driven down, margins become tighter and shortcuts are inevita-  
bly taken. This is a problem lying in wait when those sites and events are  
realised, when the computer simulations of crowds are evaluated against  
real crowds. We know of two sites where this has already occurred and the  
consequence was mass fatalities.  
Not the way to go  
Crowd simulations seemed to be going against everything we had worked  
towards, which was to produce a tool that could be used to improve crowd  
safety. Furthermore, the area we could make most impact was not at the  
top end of the market \(the so-called ‘mega-events’ such as the Olympics\)—it  
was the lower-budget events that we saw the accidents occur, where safety  
is perceived as a cost and not as a benefit. A review of the history of acci-  
dents and incidents shows that those were the events where risk analysis  
and safety improvements are most required. 16 Introduction to crowd science  
It was obvious that in order to improve a global understanding of crowd  
risks, and to develop safety engineering standards, we had to aim at a low-  
cost solution. Training is one method of achieving that goal, and we con-  
tinue to develop workshops and courses to help improve understanding  
of crowd risks. At the time of writing this book we are loading eLearning  
materials for an international audience.  
New objective  
We have outlined the financial drivers for crowd safety in the event industry  
and the reasons why cost–benefit analysis is a barrier to software simula-  
tions being universally adopted across the industry. Our new objective was  
to improve crowd risk analysis and safety engineering at a minimum cost  
to the end user.  
UNDERSTANDING THE PROBLEM  
Over the last decade a more worrying factor was arising. As we continue  
to deliver methods of both reaching and teaching the principles and appli-  
cations of crowd science to the industry, we have observed many different  
types of simulations appearing on the market. Some of the new generation  
of tools are good, others are not so good. Some of these simulations should  
be avoided at all cost. We also see many new consultants offering safety  
services; the market is becoming competitive, driving prices downwards,  
and this is leading to inexperienced users investing in the cheapest possible  
simulation and/or services. In general, where costs come down, quality also  
declines.  
These factors are driving the industry towards a false sense of safety.  
We need to expand on this specific point in order to raise awareness of the  
underlying risks of using a crowd simulation for safety applications.  
Safety-critical systems  
A fundamental problem with crowd simulation is that the user needs to  
understand the tools, models and simulations and the underlying princi-  
ples of crowd safety. If the subject materials have been studied extensively  
\(crowd risk analysis\), in the right hands these can be very useful tools. In  
the hands of an inexperienced end user, these tools could be used indis-  
criminately, with little or no understanding of risk or consequences. In such  
cases, a little knowledge can be a dangerous thing; the illusion of safety  
and certainty is projected. There can be no uncertainties in safety-critical  
systems. Introduction 17  
For any engineering discipline—specifically where safety is concerned—  
we need to define standards, acceptable validation limits, tolerances and  
safety criteria for operation. We also need to understand and adopt rigorous  
risk management standards, and exposure to risk should be mitigated with  
appropriate, qualified training and risk assessment procedures. Typically,  
in the engineering disciplines, standards are tested, reviewed, published,  
adopted, utilised and reviewed by professional engineers, who have been  
assessed in their discipline and have attained the required standards recog-  
nised by their peers, and who have demonstrated their competence by regu-  
larly having their skills tested and confirmed by a regulating authority. This  
is a professional approach, but we find no such approach related to crowd  
safety, to event planning or to crowd management. The events industry is  
not formally regulated in the same way as other safety-critical industries.  
We argue throughout this book that crowd safety and crowd risk analysis  
should be considered in a similar way to other engineering disciplines, with  
appropriate standards, validation and regulation.  
Towards a standard  
For decades, we have used a combination of many skills—not just computer  
simulation—to assess risk and to engineer solutions. Two key processes  
have been common to all projects\: the first relates to capturing the project  
information, extracting the fundamentals of the project, and interview-  
ing and distilling the basic information about specific events and incidents  
from the crowd safety operations manager’s experience of those events.  
The second relates to visualising that information in order to process all  
the available data in a systematic and methodical manner. We were using  
several different techniques to capture information, and a question began  
to formulate\: could this approach, this knowledge and experience, be cap-  
tured and passed on to other end users?  
The tools we used were designed to assimilate crowd risks from multiple  
perspectives and to provide us with a framework for crowd safety engineer-  
ing. We had to design these methods to capture and process the unique  
characteristics of specific events \(both in the United Kingdom and abroad\)  
from a wide range of sources, cultures, backgrounds and educational levels.  
We had to arrange this information in such a way that it could later be com-  
pared and contrasted to other events, other risks and other solutions for  
crowd safety and event analysis. It became our template for any/all events  
as we refined the methods and integrated them across a wider range of  
projects. This developed as a consequence of working across many events,  
in many countries, over many years.  
We noticed that the key problems of crowd risk analysis and safety engi-  
neering had a common set of characteristics; there was a set of best prac-  
tices, but these were neither captured nor documented. Either globally, or 18 Introduction to crowd science  
event by event. The information remained, by and large, retained only in  
the memory of the crowd safety operations manager, for specific problems.  
There were other noticeable event issues\: crowd management is a combi-  
nation of many different facets and skills, not least of which is the knowl-  
edge learned through practical experience. There was no adequate process  
of capturing the previous managers’ knowledge and experience, other than  
on-the-job training or word of mouth. These methods were inconsistent  
and often left gaps in knowledge of specific sites or events, especially in  
an industry that has a high staff turnover. Valuable experience was lost,  
mistakes were remade and important safety lessons missed. There had to  
be a better way!  
Capturing information  
There is a lot of information required for crowd risk analysis and safety  
engineering for events, but it can be very specific to a particular event, loca-  
tion or time of year. Much of this information is required during planning  
and approval process but may not feature for the operational requirements  
of the control room during an event. For example, planning functions that  
include crowd intelligence; risks that may be specific to a history of crowd  
behaviour; localised behaviour—these are planning and approval issues.  
They are not specifically operational issues for running an event, but they  
are necessary for planning it.  
Crowd management is a function of both the planning and operational  
issues for an event. Developing a crowd management plan without crowd  
management experience can be a dangerously misguided task. As a conse-  
quence, there are a wider range of skills and knowledge required for the  
development of a good crowd management process. As such the ‘model-  
ling’ process \(not crowd simulations\) can be very useful in understanding  
the spectrum of crowd dynamics and the crowd risk analysis for planning,  
approval and operations of an event.  
Understanding the audience  
There is a potentially dangerous niche market for crowd simulations.  
Company executives, looking to have their egos stroked and confidence  
assured in their investment; computer scientists who have played with crowd  
computer systems in their safe little world, free from anxiety and far from  
the crowds. The latter are consultants selling services to the former\: it is an  
interesting cycle to observe.  
Both groups are far removed from the heart of crowd safety issues. Neither  
consultants nor executives attended our crowd safety workshops—those  
were entirely populated by crowd safety operations managers, operators, Introduction 19  
emergency services and licensing \(approval\) officers from local authorities,  
who needed to understand risk but had no budget for computer simulations.  
To achieve our objective \(to improve crowd risk analysis and safety engi-  
neering at minimum cost to the end user\), we needed to develop tools that  
could be used by crowd safety operations managers, not computer simula-  
tions that could only be used and understood by computer scientists. What  
was needed was an pragmatic solution—something that could be taught,  
that could capture vital knowledge and experience of crowd safety opera-  
tions managers, that could capture best practises and define causes of major  
incidents. Something that could actually improve crowd safety. Training  
was proving to be beneficial, so we began to introduce modelling to the  
training programmes. This approach was far more readily accepted, being  
both low-cost and quick to implement. It also put the tools where they  
could be best utilised.  
LEARNING FROM PAST EXPERIENCE  
The skill set required for safe crowd management is understated, typically  
learned through experience, and can be site- or event specific. Near misses  
are rarely documented, and neither are things that work well. The events  
industry has no requirement for formal qualifications—either for crowd  
risk analysis or crowd safety engineering; the risk assessment process is,  
at best, subjective and in general unstructured. There is a clear need for a  
more formalised approach to best practices, and for the continual devel-  
opment of information capture systems to improve our understanding of  
events and, specifically, to reduce accident potential.  
We have observed crowd safety operations managers; worked across a  
range of events; attended many hundreds of event and site briefings and  
reviewed over 100 years’ worth of crowd accidents and incidents from  
around the world. There were three common elements to the fundamental  
causes of major incidents, yet there was no process of capturing this infor-  
mation, no formalisation of best practices and no methods of recording or  
reporting near misses beyond the event teams at that event. The knowl-  
edge of each site, each event, appeared to be unique to that event or site, so  
there was no perception of common modes of failure across the industry and  
around the world.  
There are, of course, many elements common to planning and crowd  
risk analysis, specifically with respect to crowd dynamics and crowd safety,  
but it became obvious that capturing information, best practices and near  
misses—and understanding the underlying causality of major incidents—  
was essential to improving crowd risk analysis. Safety engineering for the  
events industry is a challenge that it has taken over a decade to come to 20 Introduction to crowd science  
terms with, to formulate and to understand; making information widely  
available to the events industry is equally challenging.  
Event planning  
The process of event planning has many facets, and they are the subject of  
many books, courses and published guidance on the topic. A good event  
planner \(organiser\) will be well versed in economics, marketing, schedul-  
ing, contracts and many other subjects. They will be familiar with building  
codes, fire codes, licensing and health and safety regulations. Although  
there is much useful and easily accessible information on the topic of safety,  
most of it focuses on the physical environment \(such as number of exits, fire  
suppression systems, rates of passage, etc.\).  
Typically, crowd safety is divided into two main areas\: risk assessment  
\(prior to the event\) and crowd management \(during the event\). Risk assess-  
ment should be a process of assessing a site for its unique attributes and  
applying a rigorous methodology to either mitigate and/or manage risks.  
Many event plans are simply ‘cut and paste’ from previous plans; last year’s  
plan dusted off, a new date put on the front, or, worse still, a previous event  
plan re-titled and presented to the approval authority for a different event!  
We ask that question time and again during the workshops. Cut and paste  
is a common practise.  
The approval process for evaluating the event plan and risk assessment  
must be equally stringent in its analysis before they can be signed off as ‘fit  
for purpose’. This, of course, means the approval authority must under-  
stand crowd risk analysis in order to perform their duties to an appropri-  
ate standard and avoid crowd accidents. It needs to be highlighted that  
approving a plan that may not work allows risk to materialise and can  
result in criminal charges of negligence.  
Although there are many courses on event planning, there are few  
courses dedicated to crowd risk analysis and to risks that relate to the  
crowd dynamic—and specifically to the identification, classification and  
resolution of crowd risks from first principles. We ask the same question  
on every workshop we run \(over 6000 people so far\)\: ‘How many people  
have had a close call at an event?’ Every time we ask this, and hear the  
‘near miss’, ‘close call’ and ‘almost a disaster’ replies, we are reminded that  
there are many more sites and events that are potentially incidents waiting  
to happen, due to the lack of a rigorous approach to crowd risk analysis.  
Risk assessments  
The risk assessment is a key document in both understanding and managing  
crowd risks. When we run crowd safety workshops we often ask, ‘Who has  
read their risk assessment?’ One delegate once replied that this function was Introduction 21  
subcontracted, outsourced and that company X did all of the risk analysis  
work for them! The delegate neither understood the process, nor realised that  
the risk analysis is only one small part of the safety process. This was not a  
unique situation; we have asked the same question many hundreds of times  
around and received similar replies. Often we ask, ‘Who has written a risk  
analysis?’ Most confess they had simply copied the bulk of their risk assess-  
ment from another event, ignoring the process of risk analysis altogether.  
Risk mitigation, risk reduction, risk management, risk reporting\: these  
are all required for both large and small crowds at events. It is a process  
that should not be neglected, either during the event planning or the event  
approval phase. The bigger the event, the greater the range of risk consid-  
erations that may apply—the more detailed and meticulous you need to be  
in the risk analysis process. It is not a checklist; it should be an active docu-  
ment that engages both the person/team that performs the analysis of risks  
and the person/people who have to mitigate those risks during an event.  
Yet it generally appears to be a poorly understood and poorly implemented  
process. We do have experience of excellence in this process—specifically  
during the crowd management planning for the Royal Wedding in 2011,  
where the risk analysis, management and risk ownership were the major  
topics for most of the planning meetings—but this is the exception rather  
than the rule.  
An important aspect of our research objectives \(how to improve the risk  
analysis process\) became clearer over the years. Both crowd management  
and risk analysis were mostly based on ‘on-the-job’ experience and not a  
formalised methodology. Ask two people to assess the same risks and you  
will often get a very different set of results. As such the current risk analysis  
process is highly subjective, being based on personal perception and experi-  
ence. Budgets for this aspect of event planning and the subsequent approval  
process are typically hard-fought, and the whole process is underfunded  
and subject to time constraints due to the lack of formal crowd risk analysis  
methodologies. Which is one of the many reasons why ‘cut and paste’ of the  
risk assessment and the event plan, is so common, and so terribly wrong.  
Reality hits home  
The delegates who came on the courses and workshops were providing us  
with a wide range of event issues and concerns. We began cataloguing the  
key issues—near misses, things that worked, industry best practices—and we  
began to get a clearer understanding of the crowd safety operations manag-  
ers, who struggled for budget and were looking for solutions. We also devel-  
oped a faster approach to collecting the relevant information about crowd  
dynamics and crowd risk from the workshops. Furthermore, we were gain-  
ing insights across the whole industry, from different perspectives \(the police,  
event organisers, licensing officers and crowd safety operations managers\). 22 Introduction to crowd science  
The main problem was, and still is, an inconsistent approach to the event  
planning process. There are no standards for an event plan, no criteria to  
determine whether or not it is fit for its intended purpose—unless it fails and  
comes under scrutiny. Therein lies another issue with the events industry\: as  
previously stated, near misses are not recorded or passed on to others; best  
practices are not learned and then have to be relearned. There was no pro-  
cess of either capturing or sharing best practices. Information was simply  
lost. Introducing standards and improving crowd safety conditions needed  
to be linked to the event planning process; we appeared to have a methodol-  
ogy, and we had experience of teaching that methodology, but we could not  
reach the whole industry, around the world, by teaching small classes.  
THE ROOT OF THE PROBLEM  
Event safety plans are a requirement for licensed UK events, but they are  
subjective documents, presented in a wide range of formats,\* reviewed by  
local authorities who may or may not have had appropriate training in risk  
analysis and safety certification. This extract from the Health and Safety  
Executive \(HSE\) gives some idea of the types of things an event planner  
requires for safe crowd management†\:  
As an organiser you must as far as reasonably practicable ensure the  
safety of visiting crowds. While certain aspects of crowd safety can be  
allocated to contractors, for example stewarding, you will retain over-  
all responsibility for ensuring the safety of the public.  
Hazards presented by a crowd\:  
Crushing between people, crushing against fixed structures, such as  
barriers. Trampling underfoot. Surging, swaying or rushing. Aggressive  
behaviour.  
Dangerous behaviour, such as climbing on equipment or throwing  
objects.  
Hazards presented by a venue\:  
Slipping or tripping due to inadequately lit areas or poorly maintained  
floors and the build-up of rubbish. Moving vehicles sharing the same  
route as pedestrians.  
Collapse of a structure, such as a fence or barrier, which falls onto  
the crowd. People being pushed against objects, such as unguarded hot  
\*  
We have reviewed over 1000 event plans, crowd management plans and safety plans over  
the last decade.  
† Source\: http\://www.hse.gov.uk/event-safety/crowd-management.htm Introduction 23  
cooking equipment on a food stall. Objects, such as stalls, which obstruct  
movement and cause congestion during busy periods. Crowd movements  
obstructed by people queuing at bars, etc. Cross-flows as people cut  
through the crowd to get to other areas, such as toilets. Failure of equip-  
ment, such as turnstiles. Sources of fire, such as cooking equipment.  
It is an unstructured list, and similar lists are presented in various guid-  
ance documents in the United Kingdom \(and around the world\). The unstruc-  
tured nature of the list leads to an unstructured approach to the problem of  
risk analysis. As a result, risk and hazard identification is treated in the same  
unstructured manner, typically as a checklist in a risk assessment document.  
The guidance needs to be updated to include a formalised structure to the risk  
assessment process; otherwise, the haphazard nature of the above list is read  
as a methodology. The approval process needs to be similarly structured, and  
competent individuals need to check the event risk assessment, otherwise, the  
process is failing the ultimate duty of care to the public attending events.  
Vision and imagination  
From the Hillsborough Stadium Disaster Report, there is a specific com-  
ment in Chapter 2, section 59 that reads\:  
It is not enough to aim only at the minimum measures necessary for  
safety. That has been, at best, the approach in the past and too often  
not even that standard has been achieved. What is required is the vision  
and imagination to achieve a new ethos…  
Although the aforementioned comment was written for football, it is true  
of any event where the minimum measures necessary and the lowest pos-  
sible cost approach are adopted. Where a new event is planned on a green-  
field site,\* crowd safety and crowd risks may be unknown. A first principles  
approach was required to replace the ‘cut and paste’ from old event plans.  
As events have grown over the years and crowd numbers have increased,  
new risks have developed. For example, sites that may function safely for  
5000 people may not work at all for 5500 people. In addition to the need  
for a first principles approach was the need for vision and imagination in  
order to achieve this new ethos.  
The computer tools we had designed, developed and used for test-  
ing the site design and areas of the built and complex environment had  
provided some valuable insights into the nature of crowd dynamics and  
\*  
The term ‘greenfield’ was originally used in construction and development to reference land  
that has never been used \(e.g. green or new\), where there was no need to demolish or rebuild  
any existing structures. In relation to events, the term is used where an event will occur on  
a site not previously used for that event. 24 Introduction to crowd science  
crowd risks. These tools, as we had discovered during our research, were  
not the right tools for the problem in hand. We wanted to capture the  
information, knowledge and experience of crowd safety operations man-  
agers, because the industry needed a structured, pragmatic and, above  
all, easy-to-use approach to the problem. Without structure, you cannot  
be either efficient or effective—and that is where the next chapter of the  
research began.  
A new approach  
As stated in the PhD thesis\:  
Safety design methodology based on the present guidelines using unit  
widths of exit with no consideration to familiar routes, focal routes,  
psychological factors or crowd dynamics require reconsideration. \[…\]  
Taken without the interpretation of a competent person, the relevance  
of exit widths may meet the statutory requirements. However, as we  
have demonstrated, they may not meet the physical needs to safely and  
efficiently evacuate the occupants in an emergency situation.  
\[…\]  
The ultimate goal is to drive through the legislature that a simulation  
should be a statutory requirement of a licence to operate a place of  
public assembly. We propose replacing the need for a competent person  
with the need for a competent simulation. It is hoped that the crowd  
simulation tool represents a new wave in the understanding of crowd  
safety through the study of crowd dynamics.  
With the benefit of hindsight, this last statement was naive. At the time  
we did not appreciate the necessary expertise required for the end users, nor  
did we appreciate that a successful crowd simulation required of the user a  
combination of operational experience, understanding of crowd safety and  
human behaviour, and computer science.  
Simulations were not the way forward  
As previously stated, simulation tools were only useful in the hands of  
experts, and only for a few major events where both ample time and bud-  
get were available. What we failed to understand at that time was the pro-  
cess around planning—the people involved in the approval and delivery of  
a major event. This is a low-cost process; the individuals involved in these  
processes have no scientific training for event planning or the event approval  
process. They are low-paid employees, not computer scientists. The solution  
must fit the problem in order to be accepted, and cost is a major factor in that Introduction 25  
acceptance. The simulation-based solution was a dead-end. The model-based  
solution is low-cost, easy to implement and can be readily adopted with some  
basic training. We have delivered that type of training, and the impact on the  
industry is clear in both the planning and approval processes.  
RESEARCH REVISITED  
We have catalogued and reviewed accidents and incidents, looking for the  
common denominators \(the DNA of mass fatalities and major incidents\),  
evaluating which factors lead to incidents and how these factors may be  
avoided in the future. We have also listened to the delegates at our work-  
shops, noted their event-related problems and catalogued the different  
types of models we could use to help solve these problems. Over the last  
fifteen years we have never had to use a computer simulation in the class-  
room to solve a delegate’s problem regarding crowd risk analysis and safety  
engineering. We use some tools to help us visualise crowd density and risk,  
to measure spaces and to define routes, but these are not simulations—they  
are measurement tools. These simpler computerised tools were far more  
useful for developing lecture content, examples, illustrations and video  
clips to help improve the understanding of crowd risks and safety issues.  
We also continued to develop many new simulation systems to help  
us understand risk, but the output was always the same—better educa-  
tional materials. These materials are all relatively straightforward to teach,  
quickly adopted into working practices and, most importantly, designed to  
capture important knowledge and best practices in a form that others can  
pick up, understand and use. In short, we had found a much simpler solu-  
tion—a low-cost solution—and an easy way of implementing this solution.  
It was an effective means of crowd risk analysis and safety engineering.  
Cost-effective crowd safety modelling  
In all our project work we were using a first principles approach, mapping  
and sketching the fundamentals of complex environments, developing a  
robust method of analysing the key elements of risk and safety, using a wide  
range of analytical techniques to break complex systems into manageable  
segments, and then \(and only then, if we were to gain a deeper understand-  
ing of possible failures in very complex environments\) would we apply a  
computer simulation. We were only using simulations to understand failure  
modes—never for proof of concept. The principles we adopt are simple\: once  
you understand how crowds are exposed to risks, you can use operational  
information and management system to minimise the exposure to those  
risks. You can change design, retest the site and, by a process of an inte-  
grated design, information and management solution, outline how complex 26 Introduction to crowd science  
environments need to work. Control, monitoring and ongoing evaluation of  
crowd flow needs to take place in order to minimise crowd risks.  
Our main research tools were pen and paper. This has been reflected, and  
refined, in the workshop teaching methods over the years. What the work-  
shop delegates needed was an insight into modelling process, and access to  
the tools that would help them develop the skills required to perform their  
own analysis. They needed a method of understanding the fundamentals  
of risk, and hence they needed to define the principles and applications of  
these tools towards crowd safety.  
There were, of course, significant challenges in developing a practical  
solution. The tools we used needed a training course that would benefit  
\(not confuse\) the target audience. These tools needed, by definition, to be  
low-cost, easy to understand \(and hence implement\) and to adopt into the  
mainstream of the phases of event planning and event approval. The design  
and delivery of the crowd science workshops needed to focus on the pri-  
mary objectives of the original research; to understand the nature of crowd  
dynamics with respect to crowd safety; to develop a more appropriate risk  
analysis methodology for the design and management of places where  
crowds gather; to create tools that allow others to understand the nature,  
problem and components of crowd safety.  
Give a man a fish and you feed him for a day. Teach a man to fish and  
you feed him for a lifetime.  
Chinese Proverb  
The direction was clear\: information, education and application.  
Conclusions  
This chapter stresses the following point from several different perspec-  
tives\: it is essential to understand the needs and frustrations of the end  
user—the crowd safety operations manager. There is a need for a simple,  
back-to-basics approach to the domain of crowd risk analysis from a sci-  
entific perspective. There is a need for standards and for qualifications in  
crowd risk analysis and safety engineering; there is a need to capture and  
share best practices through the application of standards and appropriate  
training. One only needs to look at the scale of major events around the  
world and historical records of crowd-related incidents to understand that  
need. Which is why, after years of being asked the same question—‘Why  
don’t you write a book?’—we present here the principles and applications  
of crowd science, with a focus on risk analysis, risk dynamics and crowd  
safety as it relates to event planning, event licensing and event operations  
around the world. Chapter 2  
# Crowd risk analysis  
A wise man learns by the mistakes of others, a fool by his own.  
Latin proverb  
Human beings, who are almost unique in having the ability to learn  
from the experience of others, are also remarkable for their apparent  
disinclination to do so.  
Douglas Adams  
INTRODUCTION  
One of the most interactive demonstrations we use in the workshops is a  
series of density experiments using a fixed area. These are important ele-  
ments in understanding both the physical \(number of people per square  
metre\) and psychological \(how it feels\) factors in crowded spaces. These  
experiments illustrate a common failure in event planning\: the temptation  
to focus on capacity and not on how the spaces are going to be used. We  
may, for example, have a vast area available for the event but still experience  
crowd-crushing problems if the stage area is badly designed, or entry and exit  
systems are not suitably arranged/wide enough. In this chapter, we shall con-  
struct up a series of models for both static and dynamic spaces, areas where  
the crowd is predominantly stationary \(static\) and where it is predominantly  
moving \(dynamic\) to assist the event planner, licensing officer and opera-  
tion teams to recognise crowd risks. This is important because the event will  
shape the crowds behaviour, specifically how crowds react to risks.  
An issue we have encountered over the years, specifically during the  
investigation into personal injury and mass fatalities for expert witness tes-  
timony, is how poorly defined the legal terms are in the context of crowd  
problems. One factor that influences this is the ‘blame the crowd’ attitude  
of the media. In the Daily Telegraph \(2001\), an article describes a short his-  
tory of football disaster. The language used is both emotive and misleading;  
27 28 Introduction to crowd science  
the emphasis, as you can see from the language used in the headlines below,  
is to blame the crowd\:  
1955—Santiago, Chile\: Six people died when 70,000 tried to jam into  
the stadium.  
1964—Lima, Peru\: More than 300 people die in a riot between fans of  
Argentina and Peru.  
1967—Turkey\: A disallowed goal in a Turkish game provokes a riot in  
which 41 die and 600 are hurt.  
1968—Buenos Aires, Argentina\: 74 fans die trying to escape burning  
newspaper being thrown down from an upper tier, after rushing  
towards a gate pushed shut by fans on the other side.  
1971—Ibrox Park, Glasgow\: 66 people die in a crowd crush when fans  
leaving the stadium are met by a group trying to return after Rangers  
scored an equaliser.  
1971—Cairo, Egypt\: 48 die and 50 injured when crowds break down  
barriers.  
1981—Piraeus, Greece\: 24 people die in a stampede as fans rush to leave  
the ground.  
1982—Moscow, the USSR\: 340 die when fans leaving the stadium try to  
re-enter the stands after a late goal.  
1982—Cali, Colombia\: 24 people die and 250 are hurt when drunken  
fans provoke a stampede.  
1985—Valley Parade, Bradford\: A fire, which fans started in rubbish  
underneath a stand, kills 56 fans.  
1985—Brussels, Belgium\: 39 fans die in rioting at Heysel Stadium.  
1988—Kathmandu, Nepal\: A stampede towards locked exits in a hail-  
storm at the stadium, 70 fans are killed.  
1989—Hillsborough, Sheffield\: 96 die and 200 injured after a crowd  
surge crushes fans against barriers.  
1992—Bastia, France\: 8 people are killed and 400 injured when a  
crowded temporary stand collapses.  
1996—Lusaka, Zambia\: Nine soccer fans were crushed to death and 78  
others injured during a stampede.  
1996—Guatemala City, Guatemala\: 84 people died and about 150 oth-  
ers were injured during a stampede.  
1997—Lagos, Nigeria\: Five crushed, dozens injured when the crowd of  
40,000 head for exits.  
2000—Monrovia, Liberia\: Three people suffocated to death and ­ others  
were injured as thousands of fans forced their way into an overcrowded  
stadium for a World Cup qualifier between Liberia and Chad.  
2000—Harare, Zimbabwe\: 13 fans died after police fired tear gas into  
a crowd estimated at 50,000 to quell growing unruliness. The fans  
were killed in the stampede exiting the stadium. Crowd risk analysis 29  
2001—Johannesburg, South Africa\: 43 people die and 155 injured when  
people outside tried to push into Ellis Park stadium. Police had earlier  
fired tear gas at people stampeding outside the stadium.  
This language also fogs the investigation of such events, as it deflects the  
blame away from the event organisers and towards an amorphous entity \(the  
crowd\) where no single individual is to blame. When emotive \(and incorrect\)  
words such as ‘stampede’ or ‘panic’ are used to describe the crowds’ behav-  
iour it directs the blame towards the crowd and it can be difficult to attribute  
causality, and reality, of the situation. The language permeates the media,  
enters our vocabulary and the crowd is thought of as a ‘dangerous entity’. We  
have seen events where the organisers immediately appear on the media \(TV/  
radio\) and blame the crowds’ behaviour; this deflects responsibility away from  
poor planning, negligent risk assessment and incompetent management.  
Since 2001, the headlines continue to attribute blame by the constant, and  
inappropriate, use of the words ‘panic’ and ‘stampede’.  
2001—Ghana, West Africa. A stampede at a packed soccer match  
between two of Ghana’s leading teams killed at least 100 people.  
2002—Japanese Mall Event, Crowd Craze—Ten injured.  
2003—India. Stampeding pilgrims have trampled at least 32 people to  
death, many of them elderly women.  
2004—Saudi Arabia. Two killed in IKEA stampede.  
2005—Wai, India. Thousands of Hindus panicked during a religious  
procession, triggering a stampede that killed at least 150 people.  
2006—Saudi Arabia. Deadly stampede kills 363 pilgrims in this year’s  
otherwise successful Haj operation.  
2006—Manilla, Philippines. Stampede at stadium kills 79 people.  
2008—Jakarta, Indonesia. Ten young people were crushed to death as  
hundreds of music fans tried to force their way out of a rock concert.  
2008—India. As many as 150 Hindu worshippers, many of them women,  
children, and the elderly, were crushed to death in a stampede.  
2008—Pasuruan, Java. At least 23 people were killed when a stampede  
broke out as crowds of poor Indonesians fought over alms handed out  
as a Ramadan gift.  
2008—India. At least 147 people have been killed in a stampede at a  
Hindu temple.

between Ivory Coast and Malawi leaves 22 dead and 132 wounded.  
2010—Morocco. Twenty-six dead in stampede at Timbuktu’s oldest  
mosque.  
2010—India. Sixty-three die, dozens injured in Indian temple stampede.
