Love Parade music festival. The crush happened when hundreds of 30 Introduction to crowd science

2012—Port Said, Egypt. At least three people were suffocated as thou-  
sands of Coptic Christians packed an Egyptian cathedral.  
2013—Ivory Coast, West Africa. Stampede death toll rises. Deaths from  
crush at New Year’s Eve fireworks display rose to 62 as scores of  
injured go to hospital.  
2013—Kumbh Mela\: India. A stampede at a railway station in the north-  
ern Indian city of Allahabad in which at least 36 people died.  
2013—India. Over 50 killed and 100 injured in stampede at Datia dis-  
trict in Madhya Pradesh.  
2013—Church stampede in Anambra, Nigeria, ‘kills 17’. At least 17 people  
have been killed and many more injured in a stampede at the end of a  
religious vigil in eastern Nigeria.  
2014—Ningxia, China. Fourteen people have been killed and ten injured  
in a stampede at a mosque.  
2014—Mumbai, India. Eighteen killed in stampede ahead of the funeral  
of the spiritual leader Syedna.  
BASIC PLANNING/APPROVAL REQUIREMENT  
If you review the history of accidents and incidents you will find a mis-  
match between the crowd size/numbers attending the event and the area  
available for that crowd. This implies that there may be some fundamental  
errors in the design and/or use of crowded spaces, or a failure to apply an  
appropriate risk analysis methodology. To establish whether or not there  
were errors in the calculation of either the capacity or the throughput, of  
major incidents, we need to understand the principles of crowd density Crowd risk analysis 31  
and the nature and dynamics of overcrowding incidents. We also need to  
understand the core competency skills required for both risk analysis and  
site design for major events. We can define these as the basic skills required  
for an event planner and the license/approval authority.  
Missing skills  
Over many years of teaching the basic principles and applications of crowd  
modelling, we have noted that the basics are poorly \(if at all\) understood.  
Mathematical skills, such as being able to calculate area and density, were  
very low across the industry. That appeared to be at the core of the prob-  
lem; the relevant skill set required to conduct an adequate risk assessment  
was poor, and therefore, the risk assessment was inadequate. This applied  
to both the planning and approval phases of the event process.  
To address this missing skill set, we developed a range of simple model-  
ling tools that not only helped delegates at our workshops understand crowd  
space requirements, but also provided a much more rigorous approach to  
risk analysis. From the workshops, expert witness cases we have been  
involved with, and the many tales of near misses, one thing was clear; in  
all cases, the basic skills of risk assessment were poor or altogether lacking.  
Basics  
As previously stated, in our research and teaching, we have found a lack of  
awareness of the basic skills \(key competencies\) relating to event planning  
and event approval/licensing/permitting and risk assessment. These basic  
skills are as follows\:  
1\. Understanding the standing crowd density limits  
2\. Understanding the moving crowd density limits  
3\. Understanding crowd risks associated with standing and moving  
crowd density  
4\. Understanding areas within the site that are static \(standing crowds in  
those areas\) and dynamic \(moving crowds in those areas\)  
5\. Understanding crowd flow rates, throughput rates and fill rates  
6\. Understanding the risks associated with high/low flow rates in com-  
plex spaces  
To perform an adequate risk assessment with specific focus on the crowd  
dynamic, we have found that once the basic elements of understanding and  
application are outlined, typically over 2 days, the delegates can apply these  
principles to any event, large or small. The key to this is building a solid,  
visual image of the risks in complex and dynamic environments. We take  
the delegates through that process. 32 Introduction to crowd science  
UNDERSTANDING THE BASIC SKILLS  
In this section, we will demonstrate how to calculate and assess crowd density.  
From first principles, we will demonstrate how to calculate and assess crowd  
flow and throughput, and hence how to assess overcrowding and crushing  
risks. We will then examine historical incidents using this technique, to illus-  
trate how—if these basic skills had been applied ­ correctly—the risks could  
have been foreseen, anticipated and hence incidents prevented. We outline  
how to apply these techniques for future events and how to define a more  
appropriate technique for crowd risk assessment in order to improve crowd  
safety. The first of these is the calculation of the standing crowd density.  
How much space do crowds need?  
The key to understanding the space requirement for a crowd lies in under-  
standing how much space is required for each person. This sounds simple,  
but there is a problem\: some documents refer to density \(people per square  
metre\) and others refer to the area per person \(which is the inverse of den-  
sity\). This confusion needs to be both highlighted and avoided.  
Why is this a problem?  
Density values \(people per square metre\) are easier to visualise than its  
inverse \(area per person\). Here is a simple experiment to illustrate this point\:  
consider how much space there is around you right now. That is your area  
per person. Now try and estimate that area. As you can see, it is not easy to  
make a quick estimate of your area per person—especially if there are other  
people around you. Now think of 1 square metre, marked on the floor, and  
two people standing in that box. This is two people per square metre; the  
same density as 0.5 square metres per person. The people per square metre  
value—is easier to visualise than the area per person. We have also found  
that when delegates were asked to explain, say, 0.3 square metres per person  
as a density, they struggled to both visualise and express this as a density.  
Engineers and mathematicians have no difficulty in understanding that  
the area per person is the inverse of density; the problem is that crowd man-  
agers are neither engineers nor mathematicians. We keep it simple—density,  
people per square metre, is easier to understand.  
Standing and moving space requirements  
A person standing at an event occupies a certain area \(floor space\). A mov-  
ing person occupies a larger area, as they need to extend their limbs to  
walk. This should be intuitively obvious. We need to understand both the  
standing and the moving space requirements in order to evaluate whether Crowd risk analysis 33  
the design of a system can accommodate the crowd. Within the event, there  
will be areas that the crowd will be predominantly static \(standing\) and  
other areas where there will be a lot of potential movement \(dynamic\).  
These two areas need to be assessed differently.  
This provides us with the basic definitions for crowd risks in the event  
space\: the static and the dynamic risks. The space \(static or dynamic\) is  
either sufficient for the crowd’s needs or there is a risk of overcrowding  
and subsequent injury. This needs to be the first consideration in the event  
planning and approval phases. Is there sufficient space for the crowd? Is the  
space fit for the purpose?  
If not, there are risks to the crowd, and these risks need to be managed  
in order to prevent accident or injury. Although this sounds trivial, the real-  
ity is that many crowd risks are poorly understood, neglected or ignored.  
The fundamental principles of crowd space requirements, crowd throughput,  
flow rates, density, ­ overcrowding, risk monitoring and risk management were  
either neglected or poorly applied in all the major disasters. For example, Love  
Parade \(Duisburg, 2010\); Hillsborough \(UK, 1989\); Mihong Park, Beijing  
\(China, 2004\) and the Jamarat Bridge \(Saudi Arabia, 1990, 1994, 1997,  
1998, 2001, 2004 and 2006\) all feature similar mismatch between the capac-  
ity \(area\) of a space and the number of people trying to move into that space.  
Hillsborough, for example, in the United Kingdom, had a wide approach  
route \(Leppings Lane\) and only a few turnstiles designated for the approach-  
ing crowd. The crowd flow exceeded the throughput \(process rate\) at the  
turnstiles. Consequently, a queue developed, and over time, this became a  
crush. To alleviate the pressure, an exit was opened and the crowds flowed  
into an area that was already overcrowded, resulting in mass fatalities.  
Love Parade \(in Germany\) had similar characteristics\: the arrival profile  
\(the rate at which crowds arrive\) exceeded the throughput of the entry sys-  
tem and the combined ingress/egress rates were predictably greater than the  
physical limit of the system, its throughput. Again a fatal crush developed.  
These incidents share similar characteristics; simple mathematical analysis  
shows the underlying failure modes of the systems, and simple mathematics  
would have proven this was the case before the accident claimed lives.  
To expand on this key point, we need to take a first-principles approach  
to defining the size of people, their spatial requirement and the required  
space for safe crowd standing and moving at events.  
BODY SPACE  
There are published data\* available on the breadth and depth of individuals  
of the world’s population \(shown in Table 2.1\). We can use this to illustrate

the principles of crowd density and then build a fundamental model of risk  
from overcrowding. From this risk analysis, we can go on to review the  
existing guidance and building codes.  
We can see that the maximum area of a person is 0.26 square metres,  
and the average is 0.20 square metres. The maximum width of a person is  
51.5 cm, the average width of a person is 46.06 cm. These area calculations  
include space around the person\: this is called the body ellipse.  
Body ellipse  
The data from Bodyspace were calculated using the body ellipse, shown in  
Figure 2.1, which includes some space around a person.  
EVENT GUIDANCE  
The UK Events Guide \(similar documents exist in other countries\) indi-  
cates that the event capacity space must be calculated at two people per Crowd risk analysis 35  
Figure 2.1  Human profile \(average-sized human frame seen from above\). This is a down-  
ward view of a standing person.  
square metre. Although this is a simple enough calculation—for example,  
to calculate event/site capacity, take the available area and multiply by  
two—it has proven to be oversimplified, and there are hidden risks in this  
oversimplification as it assumes that all spaces are used equally within  
the site.  
Standing crowd density  
To understand crowd density \(people per square metre\) and hence crowd  
risk, we use several visual aids and classroom experiments. The reason we  
developed this was twofold, the first was to provide some control room  
visuals, specifically for closed-circuit television \(CCTV\) operators, to assist  
them in visualising crowd density from a range of angles and the second  
was to assist delegates in understanding both the physical and psychologi-  
cal effects of crowd density. Over the years, these experiments have proven  
to be memorable and popular and they help delegates/students understand  
the principles of standing crowd density and risk.  
Average area for a person  
If we assume that the average area per person is 0.20 square metres \(this  
includes a little space around the person, as previously explained\), it fol-  
lows that five people could occupy approximately 1 square metre. Firstly,  
0.2 square metres per person should be converted into a density value.  
The calculation would be 1 square metre divided by 0.2 square metres  
per person, which is the equivalent of five people in 1 square metre. We  
could, in theory, accommodate five people per square metre for any given  
area; we could, in theory, take 100 square metres and fit 500 people into  
that space. They would be packed together with little space between  
them. 36 Introduction to crowd science  
We can observe events \(e.g. front of stage at a concert or along a parade  
route\) where the crowd appears to be packed at high density. In these cir-  
cumstances, the maximum density \(five people per square metre\) may seem  
reasonable. Yet, the guidance only relates to the overall site capacity and  
does not refer to high-density \(static\) spaces or lower-density \(dynamic\)  
spaces. These two types of spaces need to be risk assessed differently, and  
we need standards for assessing these risks.  
So, would maximum packing, at five people per square metre, be an  
acceptable standard? The UK Guide to Sports Grounds \(the Green Guide\)  
indicates that 47 people in 10 square metres are the maximum number of  
people for a standing crowd at a sports event. This appears to be in line  
with the international data for area per person, but there are no references  
to the shape of the space, the size of the crowd \(the number of people\) or  
the nature of the event. Subsequently, there are a number of potential hid-  
den problems in this notion of maximum packing; it assumes that everyone  
is of ‘average’ size, standing still and evenly distributed across the space.  
If we were to pack an area to this level \(five people per square metre\), the  
crowd would have to remain static. There would be very little space for any  
individual to leave a packed area. Individuals would find it difficult to move  
around the area, to get to the facilities and so on.  
If someone falls in a maximum-packed crowded space, it may be very dif-  
ficult for him or her to stand up again. It should be clear that, at the maxi-  
mum packing density of five people per square metre, the crowd would not  
be able to move freely or easily in an emergency, and so, there are risks.  
The size of the crowd, the complexity of the event, the shape of the area,  
its entry and exit points, the nature of the event, the location and capacity  
of the facilities and exit routes … all these factors need to be assessed when  
considering a maximum packing area.  
In simple terms, as crowd density increases, the ability of individuals to  
move in the crowd decreases. We can state that as crowd density \(number of  
people per square metre\) increases, there is an increasing risk to individu-  
als \(and hence to the crowd\). Increasing crowd density also decreases both  
comfort and individual mobility.  
Maximum packing \(five people per square metre\) would have some iner-  
rant risk and may be uncomfortable, depending on its location and dura-  
tion. Although it may sound obvious—that the risk of incidents increases  
as crowd density increases—there are no clear definitions of this level of  
risk.  
We regularly observe high-density crowds at the front of stage at a con-  
cert or during a parade with no incident, and that is a fundamental problem  
with the concept of risk. A risk is a situation involving exposure to danger;  
an event may pass without the incident. That does not lessen the risk, but  
it does alter the perception of risk—and this concept can be difficult to  
explain. Crowd risk analysis 37  
The toss of a coin  
Imagine you toss a coin three times, it is a fair coin—a coin that has an  
equal probability of landing on either heads or tails. Supposing the coin has  
fallen three times on heads\: what do you assume about the coin? That it is  
not fair, that it has some bias towards falling on heads, that some cheating  
has occurred or that the laws of probability now dictate that the next toss  
must be tails in order to even up the odds? \(After all, it is a fair coin.\) If we  
ask delegates to predict the next toss, they often say\: ‘Probability says it is  
more likely to come up tails next’ but they are wrong. Each toss is fair; each  
outcome \(heads or tails\) is equally likely \(50\:50\); no matter what pattern  
has occurred in the previous coin tosses, it does not, will not and cannot  
affect the next coin toss.  
This example illustrates the problem of risk and frequency of occurrence;  
if you assume that the next toss of a coin will be tails because heads have  
occurred three times in a row, you would be wrong. The result is equally  
likely to be heads or tails; each toss of the coin is independent of the last  
toss, no matter how many times the previous tosses have come up as heads.  
The reason why this principle is so important is best illustrated when we  
look at risk analysis for major events\:  
Supposing you run an event that includes a large crowd, packed at high  
density, watching a concert. Let us assume that you have compiled your  
risk assessment and taken the necessary precautions for crowd safety  
\(installed pit crews and crowd monitoring, invested in steward training and  
arranged emergency crews on standby\) and… nothing happens. There are  
no incidents.  
This does not change the underlying risks but it does change the percep-  
tion of risk—and that can have an impact on the preparedness, effort and  
budget for the safety systems for the next event. Just as the succession of  
heads in the coin toss experiment encouraged a belief that tails were bound  
to come next, a run of successful events without the incident will lead some  
operators to believe that there is less of a risk for the next event.  
This confuses the relationship between crowd density and crowd risk  
for the crowd manager or event organiser, since a risk is an underlying  
threat and not a manifestation of that threat. The next event has the same  
risks as the last event; it requires the same management effort for planning,  
the same resources and the same budget. As events are staged around the  
world, with larger and larger crowds, there is a perception that they can be  
run with less effort and less cost. When you monitor the impact a major  
disaster has on crowd safety engineering, you see a clear pattern emerging\:  
the costs of protecting the crowd have no return and no profit. The bud-  
gets become harder to fight for and justify, the standards for safety decline  
and another incident occurs. Without the key competency skills, standards  
for event planning, improvements to the guidance and licensing of major 38 Introduction to crowd science  
events and the appropriate risk analysis techniques, major incidents and  
crowd disasters are inevitable.  
DEFINING RISKS  
Applying the event guidance of two people per square metre does not clas-  
sify an event as safe. Crowds move into a site, move around a site, stand  
and watch events, queue for bars and toilets, enter and exit attractions…  
events can be very dynamic in nature.  
To understand the crowd risks, we need to understand the location, dura-  
tion and severity of potential risks to the crowd during an event. We also  
need to understand that there are two different values of crowd density, a  
static \(stationary\) value and a dynamic \(moving\) value. If we consider two  
primary states of the crowd \(standing and moving\), then we can define  
event spaces with static areas \(where the crowd will be typically standing,  
watching and observing\), and dynamic areas \(where the crowds typically  
pass through, such as entry systems\).  
Although the UK Event Guidance states the capacity should be defined  
as two people per square metre, this value is not sufficient to define a  
‘safe’ event capacity. Emergency egress capacity \(defined as the time it  
would take to evacuate the site\) may limit the site capacity and there  
are other factors such as the physical condition of the site, the security,  
the site perimeter and the nature of the crowd. In short, we need more  
information to understand, classify and mitigate potential crowd risks.  
We need to understand the time—the duration—of the crowd dynamic  
in event spaces.  
To understand these elements, and why they are important for crowd  
safety, we need to understand the two primary density values \(static  
and dynamic\) and how they relate to the crowd dynamic during events.  
Although these principles are relatively simple to explain and illustrate,  
our experience shows that a lack of basic understanding has been one  
of the primary failures \(in understanding risks\) in major incidents. The  
problem is compounded by inadequate guidance that states an oversim-  
plified value \(two people per square metre\), and this is the root of the  
problem\: two people per square metre are not sufficient to highlight  
the potential for harm at a major event. Furthermore, to argue a case  
for lack of appropriate risk analysis, space calculations, differentiating  
spaces and static/dynamic area calculations, we often hear this claim\:  
‘But the guidance states two people per square metre’, as if that value is  
the only calculation required to guarantee crowd safety. This is a naive  
and negligent approach to crowd risk analysis. However, if you read the  
relevant section in the UK Event Guidance, it implies that the minimum  
is enough! Crowd risk analysis 39  
To illustrate this point, below are extracts from event guidance from  
Australia, the UK Sports Guide and by comparison, the UK Event Guidance  
\(1999—date of publication\). The Australian Event Guidance Worksafe—  
Crowd Control at Venues and Events\: A Practical Occupational Health  
and Safety Guide \(second edition, January 2007\) states\:  
1.1 WHAT DOES THIS GUIDE AIM TO DO?  
Managing crowds involves significant risk. The extent, severity and  
exposure to risk will vary depending on\: the circumstances crowd con-  
trollers find themselves in; how well crowd control staff have been pre-  
pared; and how effectively risks have been controlled.  
This Guide has been developed to support and assist crowd con-  
trol agencies and host employers \(venues and events\) who use crowd  
control staff to understand and fulfil their responsibilities under the  
Occupational Health and Safety Act 2004 \(the OHS Act\).  
This Guide identifies common safety problems and suggests solu-  
tions to ensure crowd control work is conducted as safely as it reason-  
ably can be. It also provides numerous recommendations and tools to  
effectively secure the health, safety and welfare of crowd control staff,  
as well as other staff and patrons, when\:  
•  
•  
•  
•  
•  
Controlling entry into venues or events  
Monitoring and communicating on crowd and individual  
behaviour  
Dealing with potentially aggressive, abusive or violent behaviour  
Physically managing aggressive, abusive or violent behaviour  
Administering and coordinating ‘first response’ first aid or criti-  
cal care or  
•  
Coordinating emergency evacuation of a venue or event  
From the UK Guide to Safety at Sports Grounds \(fifth edition, 2008\)\:  
The Guide to Safety at Sports Grounds is an advisory document for use  
by competent persons. It is the distillation of many years of research  
and experience of the safety management and design of sports grounds.  
The Guide has no statutory force but many of its recommendations  
will be made statutory at individual grounds by their inclusion in safety  
certificates issued under the Safety of Sports Grounds Act 1975 or the  
Fire Safety and Safety of Places of Sport Act 1987. \[…\] The advice given  
in this Guide is without prejudice to the application of the appropri-  
ate Building Regulations, the Health and Safety at Work etc. Act 1974,  
and any other relevant legislation. The information in this Guide is  
intended to provide useful guidance, but it is not a definitive statement 40 Introduction to crowd science  
applicable in all circumstances. Independent professional advice should  
be obtained before taking any action or refraining from taking any  
action based on this information.  
Both of the above documents point to specific legal requirements \(in  
Australia and the United Kingdom\). In contrast, the UK Event Guidance  
\(HSG 195 1999–2013\) states\:  
Following the guidance is not compulsory and you are free to take  
other action. But if you do follow the guidance you will normally be  
doing enough to comply with the law.  
At the time of publication of this book, HSG 195 was under review. The  
proposed wording reads\:  
1.5 Event organisers, concert promoters, contractors, subcontractors,  
freelancers and venue owners all have a statutory duty to comply with  
the Health and Safety at Work Act \(HSAW\).  
This obviously changes the emphasis from ‘doing enough to comply with  
the law’ to a direct reference to the Health and Safety at Work Act, which  
has been in place in the United Kingdom since 1974. This was one of the  
primary motivations for developing the crowd safety courses, and why we  
still need to define a more appropriate method of understanding crowd  
risks and legal liabilities.  
THE MINIMUM IS NOT ENOUGH  
We need to explore the space limits for both static and moving crowds, and  
to define standards and practical guidance for event planning and approval  
phases. For that, we need to define, from first principles; static and moving  
crowd density—to differentiate event spaces into either static or dynamic  
spaces. We need to define the relative risks in both spaces, and define the  
overall event dynamic. We have found that when delegates understand these  
basic principles, and can apply them in conjunction with the various guid-  
ance documents, they report that they feel better equipped to understand  
the principles of crowd risk analysis. We do not aim to replace the guidance,  
only to develop a clearer understanding of crowd risks from first principles.  
Illustration of crowd density  
We created a series of Figures 2.2 through 2.4 showing crowd density from  
one to six people per square metre. Crowd risk analysis 41  
Figure 2.2  \(a\) One person per square metre. \(b\) Two people per square metre. These  
are downward views of standing people. \(Author’s graphic.\)  
Figure 2.3  \(a\) Three people per square metre. \(b\) Four people per square metre. These  
are downward views of standing people. \(Author’s graphic.\)  
In Figures 2.2 and 2.3, the graphics show characters of 50 ×  
30 cm from  
a top-down view. The characters are standing still, and are all facing in the  
same direction and standing in the same way. The distribution of charac-  
ters is uniform \(evenly spaced\) and all characters in the graphics are exactly  
of the same size. Obviously, this is nothing like a real crowd of real people,  
but it does help visualise crowd density.  
Higher-density crowds  
At higher densities, the crowd packing obviously becomes closer, to the point  
of people being in physical contact with each other. We have illustrated this 42 Introduction to crowd science  
Figure 2.4  Five and six people per square metre. These are downward views of standing  
people. \(Author’s graphic.\)  
using the same individual graphic in Figure 2.4. It should be clear that a  
standing crowd might be able to pack to high density at concerts if they are  
all facing the same direction and are packed in rows.  
Crowds are rarely this well packed or ordered. We highlight that Figure  
2.4 \(five and six people per square metre\) shows an average set of identical  
individuals, standing in exactly the same way; facing forwards and packed  
as closely as possible. We can see that although it may be possible to pack  
these many people into this space, it is not realistic. Furthermore, Figure  
2.4 gives no indication of risk or comfort within the crowd. The graphic  
is misleading; it looks possible, but it is neither practical nor desirable.  
At concerts, where free standing is allowed, the crowd may pack to even  
higher densities, where individuals are physically squeezed together and  
moving to the music.  
Freedom of choice  
In workshops and lectures, we illustrate crowd density, comfort and risk  
using the following experiment\: first, we provide delegates with cut-out  
characters and squared paper. The delegates can experiment with the cut-  
outs in different packing configurations, such as random packing, front-of-  
stage crowd packing, queuing, packed elevator, Christmas sales and so on.  
Then we mark out an area of 1 square metre on the ground. We have a  
piece of string of 4 m long, tied in a loop; we mark every metre on the string  
with a pen and simply tape the string to the marks on the floor \(in a square\).  
We then ask people to step into the square one at a time. Unlike the cut-out  
figures and graphics in this chapter, the delegates are all of different sizes  
and they stand in different postures/positions. Where there are two people Crowd risk analysis 43  
in the square, we ask them to move around and to tell us how it feels. This  
illustrates, albeit using a small area, that two people per square metre can  
safely occupy that space and move around. We then ask another person to  
step into the square; again, we ask them to move around and to describe  
how they feel. Another person is then asked to step into the square.  
With four people per square metre, there is normally enough space for a  
fifth person to walk through the group \(even with larger-framed individu-  
als\). This gives us a useful crowd indicator that can assist CCTV camera  
operators\: if we are observing a crowd, and we see people walking through  
the crowd, we know the density must be less than four people per square  
metre, and is therefore relatively at low risk.  
With five people in the square metre, we ask the individuals how this  
level of density feels and they report beginning to feel a little uncomfort-  
able. The level of discomfort will depend on many factors, such as gender  
mix, body sizes, room temperature and so on.  
Upper limit established  
With five people in the square, we ask a sixth person to try to move through  
the group, and we find that the sixth person cannot easily pass through.  
With larger-framed individuals occupying more space than others, it can  
become very uncomfortable for the person in the middle of this experi-  
mental setup. We let the group stand in position for a few minutes and ask  
them to describe, to the rest of the class, how it feels. This is a very simple  
experiment, easily set up and very low cost.  
We recommend that you, the reader, may want to replicate this simple  
experiment and draw your own interpretation of the group comments. You  
will note some interesting observations \(e.g. males and females will report  
different degrees of comfort, specifically at higher densities when bodies  
are in close contact\). This experiment gives us an indication that our physi-  
cal and psychological comfort limit appears to be at between four and five  
people per square metre. This is, of course, dependant on the individuals  
in the square metre, for example, some people can be considerably larger  
than others.  
Crowd risks  
To demonstrate risk, we tell the five people in the square that we are going  
to give the person on the corner a push. We then push that individual diago-  
nally across the group. We typically observe a shuffling movement as the  
individuals counter the push by moving their feet. From this, we can note  
that, at five people per square metre, the crowd has some resilience; it can  
absorb some pushing. 44 Introduction to crowd science  
We then ask a sixth person to step into the square, then a seventh, until  
the people in the square are physically touching, shoulder to shoulder, chest  
to back. This proximity generally occurs at around seven or eight peo-  
ple per square metre with a typical audience, but that number can vary  
depending on the individuals’ sizes. \(Note\: If you replicate the experiment,  
you may need to be tactful in selecting your target group.\)  
Shockwaves  
At six people \(or seven, depending on the sizes of the people in the square\)—  
when the individuals are surrounded on all sides and are in physical con-  
tract with each other—we repeat the push experiment. This time, we find  
the whole group moves; the person on the opposite corner is often pushed  
out of the square. As the group is packed together \(we call this ‘close pack-  
ing’\), the individuals cannot move their feet to counter the push; so, the  
energy of the push is transmitted through the crowd, like dominos fall-  
ing. We have several examples of this effect \(Love Parade, Jamarat Bridge,  
Hillsborough and various concerts\), in which we observe a wave passing  
through the crowd, gathering momentum as it progresses.  
This illustrates the nature of a specific type of high-risk situation—  
namely that at high density \(six or seven people per square metre\), a small  
push can transmit through the crowd. This is called a compression wave—  
or shockwave—and only occurs when the crowd density is such that there  
is no space between individuals. Over the years, we have seen many com-  
plex explanations, formulae, video-processing system, papers, models and  
simulations that relate to the detection of shockwaves in crowds. It is an  
interesting research, but it misses the key concept of crowd safety. Our  
classroom experiment illustrates the point clearly and simply.  
Shockwaves are dangerous; people can \(and do\) get hurt. In the Love  
Parade disaster \(and others\), there is clear evidence of shockwaves prior  
to the mass fatalities. By the time these shockwaves would be detected, by  
video processing or otherwise, it would be too late to prevent serious harm.  
As the primary objective of crowd safety engineering is to understand and  
minimise risks, it follows that we should act to prevent the shockwave  
occurring in the first place.  
KEEP THE CROWD DENSITY LOW  
It should be obvious that the shockwave can be prevented if the crowd  
density is kept lower than six or seven people per square metre. To focus  
on the detection of shockwaves is to fail to understand the nature of crowd  
density, crowd flow and crowd safety at its most fundamental level. We  
have always found it curious that research and video detection systems for Crowd risk analysis 45  
high-density crowds enter the literature as ‘safety systems’. The objective is  
to prevent shockwaves—not to measure, monitor or predict them, but to  
prevent them from occurring.  
Do we need to understand the physics of the crowd shockwave? There  
is some value to this research, but as shockwaves can only occur in high-  
density crowds, controlling crowd flow and crowd density prevents these  
dangerous phenomena from occurring in the first place. Regardless of  
the results of the scientific study and modelling of shockwaves, the UK  
Guide to Safety at Sports Grounds \(2008\) guidance documents suggest  
‘no more than 47 people in 10 square metres’ for a standing crowd, and  
‘no more than four people per square metre for a moving crowd’. By  
adhering to the guidance, this crowd manager would prevent the risk of  
shockwaves.  
Average individuals  
The average-sized individual in our graphics \(Figure 2.4\) still had some space  
at six people per square metre. It is important to highlight the problem with  
the concept of an ‘average-sized’ person\: in the illustrations, and in many  
computer simulations, the ‘average’ is not a realistic representation of the  
individuals that form a crowd. People come in all shapes and sizes; our simple  
classroom example of crowd density, using a piece of string, illustrates the  
basic limits of density, comfort and risk. As we increase the crowd density  
above five people per square metre, the crowds become packed and are sus-  
ceptible to shockwaves. The risk of personal injury dramatically increases at  
high density.  
Guidance\: Single values  
The difficulty comes in trying to define safety limits as a single number for  
all crowds and all spaces within an event with no differentiation between  
static and dynamic spaces. The UK Guide to Sports Grounds \(the Green  
Guide\) indicates 47 people per 10 square metres as the upper limit for  
crowd density \(4.7 people per square metre\). This is less than the limit we  
established in our classroom experiment, which was five people per square  
metre, but it is always difficult to find that 0.7 person! Of course, these  
values are entirely dependent on the size of the individuals. ‘Five people per  
square metre for a standing/static crowd, assuming they are all of reason-  
able size’. This may not, of course, fit the description of all crowds attend-  
ing all events; the presence of backpacks, family groups, picnics and so on,  
can affect the space occupied by the crowd. These factors, and the ever-  
increasing body mass index of the Western population, suggest the figures  
in the demographic table \(Table 2.1\) and may need to be reconsidered with  
regard to certain countries and certain events. 46 Introduction to crowd science  
Control density to reduce risks  
Obviously, at lower densities, the shockwaves simply do not propagate; we  
can state that increasing crowd density beyond five people per square metre  
will increase the risk of shockwaves. We can also state that the shockwave  
potential \(or susceptibility\) begins when there is no space between individu-  
als in a crowd of between six and seven people per square metre. At this  
density, a static crowd is susceptible to risk of serious harm. Simply put, if  
you keep the static crowd density to five people per square metre or less,  
you can prevent shockwaves. This is so fundamental to crowd risk analysis  
and safety engineering that it needs to be writ large. The upper limit to  
crowd density is defined for a good reason\: it prevents the risk of shock-  
waves. Why this is not stated in guidance or in scientific literature is not  
clear. We also need a different value for a moving crowd; we will review  
that later in this chapter.  
Calculating area\: A problem  
To control crowd density, the operator must know the event areas. He or  
she must understand how they are used and whether they are static or  
dynamic spaces. This leads us to an interesting problem we found with  
many delegates; they found it difficult to understand the calculation of  
area.  
Density is measured as people per square metre. Over the years, we have  
asked a wide range of practitioners, ‘What are 10 square metres’? It may  
come as a surprise that many reply, ‘10 metres by 10 metres’. This gives  
us some insight into the need for going back to the very basics of area and  
density analysis for static and moving crowds. It is difficult to visualise an  
area of 47 people per 10 square metres \(as per UK Guide to Safety at Sports  
Grounds, 2008\), and hence, mistakes are often made in calculating crowd  
density limits.  
One square metre is easy to visualise\: it is a box measuring 1 by 1 m.  
We can visualise two, three, four or more people standing in that box,  
and that is a good visual reference for crowd density. Larger areas start  
to become more difficult to visualise. If you think 10 square metres is a  
square measuring 10 by 10 m, you will be visualising 100 square metres—  
not 10 square metres. Ten square metres are 5 by 2 m \(5  
×  
2  
=  
10\). This  
may seem obvious to the reader, but we have found this is a fundamental  
problem with delegates in the workshops.  
Events are rarely held in perfect square or rectangular areas; areas can be  
difficult to calculate. Since area calculations determine the crowd capacity,  
an area error will lead to an event capacity error and this may lead to an  
increased crowd density, which means increased risk. Event spaces need to  
be individually assessed for their usage during an event. Crowd risk analysis 47  
Two types of space  
An event area can be broken into two different types of space\: those where  
the crowd is typically standing, and those where it is typically moving.  
The maximum density is different for these two spaces. For static spaces,  
we have established that five people per square metre are an upper limit, a  
maximum for comfort, and that this applies to a crowd without backpacks,  
of average, uniform size, etc. Again, we stress that guidance does not dif-  
ferentiate these two classes of space and only refers to one value, which is  
two people per square metre. So we need to break the event space into at  
least two types of space, static and dynamic. First, we need to estimate the  
overall area.  
When we ask delegates to estimate both crowd density and event areas  
using a wide range of photographs, maps, plans and diagrams, we often  
get a wide range of answers. Most delegates—and these are typically peo-  
ple with crowd management experience—over- or underestimate area and  
crowd numbers. In several examples, we have noted that crowd numbers  
grossly inflated by a factor of 10 \(1,000,000 people, where the correct num-  
ber was 100,000\).  
This is a fundamental calculation error, and the one we see repeated  
around the world; this kind of error increases the chance of incidents due to  
overcrowding. To illustrate the point\: one of our former projects involved  
working on a major event where the crowd numbers were originally esti-  
mated at 250,000 people viewing along a route. After working with the  
client and measuring all the available viewing areas, the realistic estimate  
proved to be 50,000 people viewing along that route.  
This illustrates the common failures in event planning\: failure to esti-  
mate the areas correctly, failure to estimate the safe capacity and failure to  
understand the space requirements for both standing and moving crowds.  
It is essential that we develop clearer, simpler methods for area and capac-  
ity analysis.  
A BIGGER PICTURE  
Reference graphics are a good way of analysing area and capacity analysis.  
To illustrate a large crowd, we produced a range of images of randomly  
distributed crowds, pictured over two tennis courts to provide a reference  
dimension. The following series of Figures 2.5 and 2.6 show 625 square  
metres \(25 by 25 m\).  
To calculate the area of a square or rectangle, you multiply the length  
by the breadth. Non-rectangular shapes have different area calculations.  
There are a number of potential pitfalls where the area for crowds is con-  
cerned; for example, the area of a circle of 1 square metre would have a 48 Introduction to crowd science

Two people per square metre  
As we can see from the graphics \(Figures 2.6, 2.7, and 2.8\), it is difficult to  
estimate crowd numbers. The problem is compounded when viewing this  
type of crowd via CCTV \(closed circuit television\) in a control room. Figure  
2.9 shows the crowd in 625 square metres at a density of five people per  
square metre. It appears that the density is higher towards the top of the dia-  
gram; this is an illusion, due to the angle of the camera as it looks towards  
the crowd. The density is in fact even throughout. This illusion has conse-  
quences for event management\: angles and lines of sight can be misleading.  
Density perspective illusion  
The importance of modelling cannot be overemphasised. In Figure 2.9, we  
see what appears to be a density gradient \(higher density towards the top  
of the image\). There is no gradient; it is only the illusion of the perspec-  
tive from the angle of the camera position in our virtual environment. This  
picture illustrates the problem a crowd manager has during an event; the  
inexperienced operator can make incorrect assumptions of crowd density  
based on poor understanding of key lines of sight—it is one of the problems  
relating to over- or underestimation of crowd numbers.  
To illustrate this point, let us consider a ticketed concert/event that has a  
total capacity of 10,000 people, based on 5000 square metres at two people  
per square metre. We would argue that this is over simplistic and that it  
fails to address the key areas of crowd risks. For example, if all 10,000  
Figure 2.9  Five people per square metre \(3125 people in 25  
×  
25 m  
=  
625 square metres\).  
The image is from the modelling tool, developed by the author, to visualise

people want to be as close as possible to the front of stage, crushing/shock-  
waves may develop. This would be at high risk, even though it would still  
comply with the ‘two people per square metre’ capacity guidance.  
Space filling  
Static crowd density can be impacted by the manner in which space fills. If,  
for example, a crowd rushes towards the front of stage when gates open,  
they will pack more tightly than a slower-moving crowd. Crowd movement  
during entry can alter the risk of shockwaves during an event, and its analy-  
sis should feature as part of the best practices for event management. It can  
become labour intensive where lines of stewards may need to control entry  
rush for a more popular performer and a more excitable crowd, but it has  
a significant safety impact on the pressure at the front of stage during the  
performance. To understand this, we need to understand the space require-  
ments of a moving crowd and develop a better understanding of the ways in  
which crowds fill spaces.  
Moving crowds  
The profile \(downward view\) of a person walking is going to be larger than  
that of a person standing. Figures 2.10 through 2.13 illustrate a typical  
walking profile.  
This is a sequence of images illustrating the typical space used when  
arms and legs move during normal, unimpeded walking. We can use the

largest profile \(downward view\) of the man walking \(Figure 2.13\), as the

and dynamic areas\).  
Sounds too simple!  
Basic information can assist in the planning of an event. The problem is  
that the guidance for events does not cover the basics; it does not build up  
these key-learning points or support the fundamental elements to build a  
case for crowd risk analysis. The industry is, as previously stated, minimum  
cost, minimum wage, with a high staff turnover. So, to support the case for  
crowd and event risk analysis in relation to static and dynamic space, we  
need to go back to the first-principle analysis and build up the picture using  
simple tools—the tools we have developed over a decade of researching,  
advising and teaching. These tools have proven, repeatedly, to provide vital  
insight into the nature of risk and the crowd dynamic at minimum cost to  
the event process.  
Once the basic principles of individual static and dynamic space require-  
ments are understood, the development of a more detailed flow model has  
both context and application. From that, we can build a clearer under-  
standing of the crowd risks across events and in the built and complex  
environment.  
An important point we have come to understand—and to develop bet-  
ter teaching methods for—is the nature of crowd flow and how it relates 54 Introduction to crowd science  
to individual speeds. In other words, the time it takes areas to reach crush  
density. The overall flow–density relationship is vital in understanding risks  
in crowded spaces. First, we need to illustrate crowd density for a moving  
crowd.  
Standing and walking profiles\: Another string  
experiment  
Figures 2.14 through 2.16 show one, two and three people per square  
metre, both standing \(on the left\) and walking \(on the right\).  
These graphics are again artificial in that we have set an even density  
\(space between characters\) and allocated the maximum space between  
Figure 2.14  One person per square metre standing and walking. \(Author’s graphic.\)  
Figure 2.15  Two people per square metre standing and walking. \(Author’s graphic.\) Crowd risk analysis 55  
Figure 2.16  Three people per square metre standing and walking. \(Author’s graphic.\)  
the walking characters, who are all marching in a perfect step. This serves  
to illustrate the definitions of space required for standing and moving  
crowds.  
We illustrated static/standing density in the classroom using volunteers  
and a 4-m length of string laid out on the floor in a 1-m square; this  
gave us some insight into the nature, influences and risks associated with  
standing crowds and shockwaves. The moving/dynamic crowd requires  
more space. To understand the moving crowd space requirements, the  
potential for risk to the moving crowd and the comfort factors for the  
individuals in the crowd, we use another string experiment—this time  
with people walking.  
The string needs to be 3.54 m long, which is an area of 1 square metre  
\(an approximation for the purposes of this experiment\), and tied in a loop.  
We put a person inside the loop and ask them to walk 10 paces forwards;  
then we put a second person in the loop and ask both to walk 10 paces  
forwards. For one and two people per square metre \(see Figure 2.17\), there  
is no impediment to normal walking, providing the two individuals possess  
approximately the same build and level of mobility. When we put a third  
person into the loop, the only way the three can walk normally is to stand  
side by side. A fourth person in the loop means the subjects can only be  
arranged in a two-by-two configuration, and they start to experience diffi-  
culty in walking. A fifth person in the loop reduces movement dramatically  
\(to a slow shuffling\).  
We then ask the five people in the loop to march in a step. There is  
an increase in the speed of movement, but not up to the normal pace.  
Introducing a sixth person reduces the movement to a very slow shuffling.  
We have videoed this experiment for educational purposes \(http\://www.  
gkstill.com/Support/crowd-flow/index.html\). 56 Introduction to crowd science  
Figure 2.17  Two people per square metre walking experiment. \(Author’s photograph.\)  
We stress that this experiment only illustrates the principles of how den-  
sity and crowd movement are related. We can derive a simple graph of  
crowd density and crowd flow—illustrated at the end of this section. This  
is an important teaching point that helps to build a clearer understanding  
of both the function of density and crowd flow, along with the experience  
of crowd density in a controlled environment.  
In Figure 2.18, we can see three people per square metre, in the string loop,  
walking side by side, unimpeded and in relative comfort. In Figure 2.19, we

Figure 2.19  Four people per square metre walking experiment. \(Author’s photograph.\)  
see four people per square metre and the movement becomes difficult; the  
individuals are in a closer packing configuration.  
As with the standing experiment, we add another person to the group to  
assess the effect of density, asking the group how it feels, and we time their  
progress walking over a measured distance.  
At five people per square metre, the movement is severely restricted, and,  
as you can see in Figure 2.20, the group is packed together. Unlike in the  
Figure 2.20  Five people per square metre walking experiment. \(Author’s photograph.\) 58 Introduction to crowd science  
Figure 2.21  Six people per square metre walking experiment. \(Author’s photograph.\)  
standing experiment, the group cannot spread out to the corners. This  
experiment illustrates the dynamics of a constrained crowd.  
At six people per square metre, movement is very slow and awkward  
\(Figure 2.21\). The subjects typically describe this level of density as uncom-  
fortable and unsafe, as they have no real control over their balance.  
GRAPHING THE RESULTS  
The two string experiments demonstrate risk and comfort in relation to  
crowd density. These simple classroom exercises provide us with both  
the physical experience of crowd density and the insight into the nature  
of standing and moving crowd space requirements. To provide further  
insight into crowd flow, we time the dynamic group walking over 20 m and  
note that as the density increases above three people per square metre, the  
crowd’s speed of movement drops dramatically.  
Figure 2.22 shows the relationship between crowd density \(people per  
square metre\) and crowd flow \(people per metre per minute\). From this,  
we can see that as crowd density increases above three people per square  
metre, the flow rate \(number of people past a specific point per minute\)  
drops. So, the congestion, efficiency and rate of passage will become dif-  
ficult above the threshold value. At the guidance value of four people per  
square metre, as we observe in the string experiment, the crowd is at its Crowd risk analysis 59

maximum packing density. With five people per square metre, movement is  
difficult, and increases the potential risk of trips, slips and falls.  
Nothing new  
The graph is not ground breaking. The relationship between crowd flow  
and crowd density has been published in many different forms for many  
years, from Fruin’s original thesis in 1971 and the subsequent papers; inter-  
national guidance \(such as the USA Highways Capacity Manual, which  
outlines pedestrian movements in places of public assembly\) and with  
guidance for transportation system designs \(e.g. railway transit systems\).  
Berlonghi\* describes the effects of high density as follows\:  
A dense crowd is one in which individual physical movement is rap-  
idly becoming less likely or possible due to the density of the crowd.  
People are attempting to move but they are either swept along with the  
movement of the crowd or are falling on top of each other. The results  
of this compression of people are fatalities and serious injuries due to  
suffocation.  
\*  
From seminar paper Understanding and Planning for Different Spectator Crowds\: in  
Engineering for Crowd Safety, Elsevier Science Publishers B.V., 1993. 60 Introduction to crowd science  
Doomed to repeat past mistakes  
We have asked many delegates over the years whether they were aware of  
the above references \(Fruin, Berlonghi et al.\). The answer is generally ‘No’,  
and the reason for this is that the text, although widely available, is not  
widely read or understood, too technical, not required reading, not part of  
the job specification. The message should be clear; mistakes at major events  
are doomed to be repeated unless this material becomes both understood  
and applied.  
Site design  
To control entry to an event, we would erect some sort of barrier or fence  
around the site. The crowd needs to enter the space \(typically by gates or  
ticket-checking system\) and leave by the same \(or different\) points in the  
system after the event has finished. It may not be immediately obvious,  
but the entry and exit points will be of limited throughput, and they need  
to be of sufficient capacity \(throughput\) to minimise the risk of crushing  
on entry or exit. These entry/exit points dictate the rate of fill, and if the  
high-density areas fill too quickly, shockwaves develop and the situation  
becomes high risk.  
Space utilisation  
An important feature of crowd density and risk assessment is evaluating  
which areas within the space will be of high density and which areas will be  
of lower density\: which areas are predominately going to be standing and  
static, and which areas are going to be dynamic. For example, entry and exit  
systems would be predominately of higher density during ingress and egress,  
but low density at other times. As an area \(say the front of stage\) fills with  
people, the crowd is unlikely to be evenly distributed around the site. There  
will be a higher density of people nearest to the point where the performers  
are expected on stage, and the density will decrease further away from the  
stage barriers. This can make it difficult to estimate the point at which the  
space is reaching its capacity limit, as the packing density will have a range  
of values. This is why it is important to monitor the crowds moving into  
these spaces.  
We know that before the crowd reaches close packing \(six or seven  
people per square metre\), we can see spaces between people \(with a good  
downward CCTV angle\), but this is not always a practical solution to con-  
trolling crowd density, there may not be time to react between the crowd  
entering the space and the space becoming too crowded. Monitoring and  
managing crowd flow and crowd build up \(density\) is essential for crowd  
safety. Crowd risk analysis 61  
BETTER REFERENCES  
We can develop a better method of assessing crowd density and event capac-  
ity, and a clearer understanding of the rates at which spaces can fill with  
training and practise. This is vital in order to understand and avoid dan-  
gerous overcrowding. To do that, we need some basic information about  
the space a moving crowd occupies, the rates at which crowds can move  
and the rates at which spaces can fill. One solution has been to use crowd  
simulations. As we outlined in the previous chapter, this method is used for  
some major events.  
DANGEROUS CROWD SIMULATIONS  
Although movement is possible at six people per square metre, it is nei-  
ther desirable nor safe. Although this may seem obvious, many computer  
simulations display crowd movement at this density—or even higher. As  
evidenced in our string experiments, a packing density of six people per  
square metre carries significant personal risk due to shockwaves. It would  
seem an obvious safety oversight to have a computer simulation operating  
and demonstrating safe passage at such high density, and yet, we have seen  
many such simulation outputs presented as a safety case.  
Crowd density, as defined in safety guidance, should not exceed four  
people per square metre for moving/dynamic areas \(queues, slow-moving  
crowds\), and should not exceed 4.7 people per square metre for standing/  
static areas. A crowd simulation that displays high-density crowd move-  
ment, without highlighting the significant risk to life and limb, is both mis-  
leading and potentially dangerous.  
DEFINING ‘RISK’ DUE TO DENSITY  
The question we need to ask is\: ‘What are the risks associated with crowd  
density’? If we translate the previous images and string experiments into  
a graph, we can develop a template for risks to both static and moving  
crowds \(see Figure 2.23\).  
Using the string experiments, we can demonstrate that the shockwave for  
a static crowd becomes dangerous at densities greater than five people per  
square metre, and that a moving crowd becomes unstable above three peo-  
ple per square metre. Planning an event, a site design, to maintain crowd  
density below these levels will reduce the risk. This may sound trivial when  
explained in black and white, but an examination of the fundamental  
points of failure of major crowd disasters has shown that these basics were  
poorly understood and not implemented in practice. 62 Introduction to crowd science

Conventional risk assessment  
A risk assessment is typically presented in a format similar to that shown  
in Figure 2.24, in which a list of risks—often unstructured—is described, a  
likelihood and consequence value assigned, and an overall score attributed.  
Figure 2.24  Typical risk assessment tabular format. \(From one of the author event plans,  
fuzzed to remove specific details.\) Crowd risk analysis 63  
In Chapter 1, we described the unstructured nature of this type of list  
induces an unstructured approach to the problem of risk assessment. Risk  
assessment can be highly subjective; one person’s assessment can vary to  
another person’s assessment of the same risk. This process is fraught with  
potential for misinterpretation for two reasons\: the first is that crowd risks  
are dynamic in nature and one number may not be sufficient to designate  
the risk; the second is that the risk assessment process—a list—does not  
lend itself to description and hence understanding of the dynamic nature  
of events.  
Risk is dynamic; crowd risks can change over time. They are dependent on  
a number of factors and on a number of external influences. Understanding  
how risks change with time is fundamental to the development of a robust  
crowd management plan.  
Qualitative and quantitative risk analysis  
The existing risk-analysis process can be described as quantitative\: a pro-  
cess in which a risk is identified and a value is assigned to that risk. As  
crowd risks are dynamic in nature, they do not lend themselves to this kind  
of over simplification. We can apply a qualitative risk-mapping process to  
assist in identifying the time, location and relative severity of a risk. To  
understand this process, imagine a site that is about to open, where the  
crowds are queuing outside the event. The crowd risks are now located in a  
specific space \(the entry points\) and during a particular time period \(prior  
to gates opening\), and it may be anticipated that the crowds will cover a  
specific area—which would be based on the number of people expected at  
the event—while queuing.  
This is very simple to illustrate on a map. It shows the location of the  
risk, the size of the crowd, the relative risk \(we typically suggest red/orange/  
green to indicate these types of risk\) and the time of the risk. If a series of  
maps/diagrams are produced for different times during an event, we can see  
that the risks move around the site, appear at specific locations and times  
and for different durations. We recommend drawing several diagrams that  
represent the site at different times, thereby highlighting crowd risks in  
key areas and at key times. This will make it easier for event organisers to  
focus resources on the monitoring or management of these risks. This is a  
qualitative risk-mapping process, as we are visualising the location, dura-  
tion and area of potential crowd risks. It assists in communicating risk  
to all parties associated with the event planning, approval and operation  
processes. Chapter 3  
# Causality  
Shallow men believe in luck or in circumstance. Strong men believe in  
cause and effect.  
Ralph Waldo Emerson  
Essayist, 1803–1882  
We learn from history that we do not learn from history.  
Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel  
Philosopher, 1770–1831  
INTRODUCTION  
In this chapter, we examine causality of crowd-related incidents with spe-  
cific focus on reasonably foreseeable crowd risks for planned events. In  
expert witness cases, we would initially be asked these questions\: ‘was the  
incident reasonably foreseeable?’ and ‘at what point in time could the inci-  
dent have been avoided/averted?’.  
As we defined in the opening chapters of this book, the event process  
can be split into three distinct phases, planning, approval and operations.  
To answer the aforementioned questions, we need to review the concept  
of ‘reasonably foreseeable’ in the context of a layperson, an adequately  
trained crowd manager, a licensing officer/inspector and an expert. Then  
we need to review the question of averting the incident. Clearly, if the risks  
are identified during the planning and/or approval phase, and are either  
designed out of the system, or managed appropriately, then the risk may  
never materialise during the event. As an expert witness for major inci-  
dents, this is a key part of accident analysis as it identifies core competency  
and/or negligence during the planning and/or approval \(licensing\) process.  
To make crowd risk analysis more accessible to the layperson, we have  
tried to review the concepts of causality in the context of ‘reasonably fore-  
seeable’ as they would relate to crowd incidents. Our intention is not to  
65 66 Introduction to crowd science  
challenge the legal definitions, but to provide a framework, in the specific  
case of crowd incidents, of those moments in time when the incident could  
have been avoided. For this purpose, we have defined ‘distal’ and ‘proxi-  
mate’ causality in the context of planning, approval and operations of an  
event.  
Crowd disasters  
During the PhD research, we developed an incident database and began to  
categorise major crowd disasters \(excluding terrorist or structural collapse  
incidents\) to see whether there were patterns of failure.  
Notes on incidents  
In Table 3.1, we have defined, in the broadest of terms, the characteristics  
of some of the incidents over the last 25 years. A more complete form of  
these incidents appears in the appendix. Some terms may not be familiar  
to the reader, such as ‘crazing’—a term used to describe a situation where  
a crowd has been excited by particular circumstances, such as a celebrity  
appearance or a marketing promotion—where the excitement in the crowd  
is a function of media influence. ‘Hand-outs’ refers to items distributed to  
the crowd, creating a sudden interest and a subsequent crowd surge. While  
classifying these incidents, we found that they fell into three main headings\:  
design-, information- and management-related issues. Before we address  
the specific underlying attributes of crowd incidents, we need to review the  
event process and the definitions of causality.  
PLANNING, APPROVAL, OPERATIONS  
There are two types of events\: planned and unplanned. Unplanned events—  
such as spontaneous gatherings, flash mobs, riots, protests, civil disorders  
and the like—are not the subject of this book. Unplanned events are reac-  
tive in nature, and problems during these events fall into a category that  
can be defined as public order issues. Although some of the techniques  
described in this book are applicable to certain unplanned events in specific  
locations \(e.g. as desktop planning exercises in advance of known problem  
gatherings\), we will focus on planned events.  
As described in Chapter 1, planned events have three distinct phases\: the  
initial planning phase, the event approval \(licensing or permitting\) phase  
and the event operations phase. Here we will outline the issues of predict-  
able crowd risk, the analysis of event sites and the development of crowd  
management plans; specifically, we will focus on ways of improving crowd  
safety. Causality 67

Planned events  
Major events, such as citywide events, concerts, festivals and the like,  
require a significant amount of planning. This process may engage a wide  
range of partners, such as the emergency services, local authorities, build-  
ing control, licensing, promoters, contractors, landowners and performers.  
It is the responsibility of the event organiser to develop and submit an event  
plan to a licensing \(or permitting\) authority.  
In the United Kingdom an event plan is typically subject to a review pro-  
cess, often by an independent safety advisory group. Expert safety advisors  
may also be engaged to assess and advise on whether the event plan and  
safety concepts are ‘fit for purpose’.\*  
An approving authority—for example, a licensing authority in the United  
Kingdom, or a permitting authority in the United States—will then review  
the plan so that the event may be granted a licence/permit to proceed.  
For both of these critical phases \(planning and approval\) a competent  
person must compile the risk analysis, and an equally competent person  
must check it. Careful review of the risk analysis is critical; any risks  
missed during the planning process must be identified. If both planning  
and approval phases neglect risk assessment, risks may be realised during  
the operational phase of the event.  
Crowd risk analysis should include the necessary examination of spaces  
for both static \(standing\) and dynamic \(moving\) crowds. These are spaces  
such as queuing systems, entry points, exit points, emergency access and  
egress systems, connecting corridors, tunnels, turnstiles, front of stage,  
bars, concourses and the like.  
\*  
In this context, the term ‘fit for purpose’ means all parties are confident that the plan will  
work. 70 Introduction to crowd science  
For many large events, there also needs to be crowd monitoring and  
continual risk assessment during the operational phase of the event—for  
example, assessment of crowd flow rates \(dynamic spaces\) for congestion  
during queuing, at entry points, turnstile operation; congestion in critical  
locations, and whether the system is performing as planned. We would  
define this as a dynamic risk assessment and risk management process.  
Historical review  
If we review the list of incidents and accidents from around the world, we  
see an underlying theme that is common to several major incidents, pointing  
towards deficient planning and approval before events and unsatisfactory  
risk management during events. In other words, there are common causes to  
major incidents that should be recognised during planning and/or approval  
phases of the event process. We will focus in this book on these elements\:  
planning, approval, operational risk analysis and risk management. Our  
aim is to classify accidents and incidents, to identify and document the key  
points of failure, and to provide methodologies that will improve crowd risk  
analysis and safety engineering for places of public assembly. It is worth not-  
ing that of these elements, only one is truly independent of the others; plan-  
ning and event, submitting the initial documents and risk assessment, and  
managing or subcontracting the event operations are generally undertaken  
by those with event profit in mind, while approval is generally undertaken  
by authorities with no interest in the overall profit of the event, and hence  
independent of the financial drivers for the events success.  
Patterns of failure  
Major incidents are often misreported in the media in emotive headlines  
that use phrases such a ‘crowd stampede’, ‘panic’ and ‘riot’. This type of  
language implies that the crowd was at fault. However, when you examine  
the failures of each incident, a different picture emerges. The most common  
element of failure we encountered when reviewing the incident database  
was the fact that design capacity and throughput limits were exceeded. In  
many cases they were unchecked or unknown before the event, and the  
result of exceeding these limits was overcrowding. There were too many  
people and there was not enough space. This resulted in serious risk to life  
and limb, crowd crushing, injuries and mass fatalities.  
Design capacity and throughput should be understood and risk assessed  
during the planning, review and approval phases of an event. Neglect of  
this process is an underlying \(distal\) failure; to carry out the process and  
then neglect the management of the risks that have been identified is an  
operational \(proximate\) failure. This occurs during the event. The crowd is  
rarely, if ever, the actual cause of the incident. Causality 71  
CAUSALITY  
Causality is a very complex legal question; our aim is not to challenge legal  
outcomes, but to try to understand, classify and define the elements that  
have given rise to major incidents and mass fatalities in crowd-related cases,  
and to prevent history repeating itself. The aim here is to learn from past  
incidents and to improve best practices in the crowd management and event  
planning processes.  
To understand the context of causality, we have outlined some basic defi-  
nitions, taken from a range of sources. We use these definitions to clarify  
the discussion points in the context of crowd risk analysis in this book.  
Definitions  
Our focus is on the crowd dynamic. We excluded from our analysis inci-  
dents that are governed by engineering standards \(such as collapsing struc-  
tures\), criminal and terrorist activities, fires and natural disasters. We also  
excluded spontaneous \(unplanned\) events, for reasons given at the begin-  
ning of this chapter. We focussed on the areas where reasonable foreseeable  
risks could be predicted during the planning and approval phases of the  
event process, and where there may be negligence, incompetence and hence  
a direct link to an identified ­ causality associated with the crowd accidents  
or incidents. We outline where crowd risk analysis and safety engineering  
need to be introduced in order to prevent future problems.  
Causation\: Comparing scientific and legal definitions  
There are many similarities between a scientific and a legal definition of  
causation. Both attempt to provide a reasoned process with the objective of  
determining the underlying causality of observed phenomena. The major  
distinction between the scientific and legal definitions lies in the concept of  
certainty.\* The legal concept of causation is deterministic\: it is an expres-  
sion of certainty as an absolute concept. The scientific concept of causation  
is probabilistic\: it is an expression of truth.  
Risk and certainty  
Most major events run without serious incidents. We know from years  
of working in the industry that there are many unreported near misses  
and that narrow escapes are seldom published to a wider audience. There  
are numerous published materials on crowd risks, including accident and  
incident reports, court cases and public enquiries, and histories of major  
\*  
Source\: http\://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Causation\_\(law\) 72 Introduction to crowd science  
incidents. High-density crowd risks are not recent—or unknown—phe-  
nomena. From this, we can assert that the information relevant to crowd  
risks is readily available for the practitioner, but only for those incidents  
that have failed and have been correctly identified and duly reported.  
Furthermore, the existence of a crowd risk does not mean it is certain to  
occur, but it does identify the outcome should it happen to occur. Crowd  
risks can be difficult to understand, they can be dynamic, have changing  
values. Crowd risks can have a relationship to the numbers of people and  
the time/duration of the event. For example, imagine a four-metre-wide  
corridor leading to a two-metre-wide door. We can say, with certainty,  
that so long as the number of people moving along the corridor towards  
the door is less than the number of people that can get through the door,  
the system will function with minimum risk. If the number of people  
moving along the corridor exceeds the number of people trying to get  
through the door, the risk of overcrowding \(and hence the risk of crush-  
ing\) increases. We can reach a situation where twice as many people are  
passing along the corridor than can get through the door. A crush can  
develop, but that will depend on how many people, over what period of  
time, are passing along the corridor trying to get through the door.  
The risk can be calculated in this way\: narrowest point in the system and  
flow rate towards that point, minus the flow rate past that point. A queue  
will develop, but the queue, provided it does not grow too large, is not a  
certainty of crushing. The risk will result from the number of people who  
continue to arrive, in excess of the number of people who can pass through  
the system. In this example, the risk of injury \(trips, slips and falls\) can start  
at zero \(no-one in the system\), will rise as the number of people begin to  
move through the system. When there is a sufficiently large queue of peo-  
ple, the risk of crushing exists, but the individual desire to proceed through  
the door must also be present to develop a crushing incident. Hence, there  
is an element of human behavior to consider in these situations.  
Until the point at which the number of people arriving at the door is equal  
to the number of people that can pass through the door, there is a minimal  
risk. Once that limit is reached, the risk of crushing rises at the rate at which  
more people arrive at the door with a desire to pass through it.  
This is a product of the number of people, the difference in the rates of  
passage \(door and corridor—the physical factors\), the desire to proceed  
and the time \(duration\) of exposure to the overcrowding \(the psychologi-  
cal factors\). The certainty of crushing, injury and the potential for fatali-  
ties will increase because of the time people are exposed to this situation,  
and of the number of people in the system at that time as will the desire  
to get through the door, thereby accelerating the rate of failure. This may  
appear complicated in writing, but there is a simple method of qualifying  
the possible risks using a diagram \(as demonstrated in Chapter 5, RAMP  
analysis\). Causality 73  
If the risks are recognised, and appropriate measures are deployed or  
implemented to manage those risks, then the likelihood that they will  
develop into an incident is controlled but the underlying risks have not  
changed. Underlying risks must be understood, and the process of identify-  
ing and managing \(or mitigating\) risks must be appropriately documented.  
There is a range of risk assessment, reporting and documentation pro-  
cesses and procedures available from many different, freely available  
sources. These rely on subjective \(personal\) assignments of a set of numbers  
\(likelihood and consequence known as quantitative risk assessment\) and a  
brief description of the risks and how they will be managed. Risks can, as  
we demonstrated in the corridor/door thought experiment earlier, have a  
range of values during an event.  
Risk of crushing is a function of the number of people in a given area;  
it is related to the rate at which a space becomes overcrowded. This is not  
a single, static number\: it has a dynamic property that needs to be both  
defined and understood.  
The rate at which a risk of overcrowding develops into a likelihood of  
crushing does not feature in the safety guidance \(e.g. in the UK HSG 195  
document The Event Safety Guide\). This potentially misleading and danger-  
ous oversight needs to be addressed. What is required is a method of identify-  
ing and documenting risks that are dynamic in nature; a workable definition,  
and a basic understanding of how quickly overcrowding becomes hazardous  
to life and limb. What is required is a method of assessing these types of risks  
during the planning and approval process, to prevent them from developing  
during an event.  
Problems lying in wait  
Risks that go unnoticed are called ‘problems lying in wait’. These are prob-  
lems that have not been considered or dealt with appropriately; risks that  
people knew about, or should have known about. Failure to identify risks  
that prove to be the underlying \(distal\) causes of major incidents may be  
defined as negligent. If these risks are not recognised during the planning  
and approval phases of an event, and the event then proceeds and the risks  
are realised, there may be questions of negligence relating to the risk assess-  
ment and the relevant parties may be held accountable.  
Accidents do not just happen; there is always a cause. Causation can be  
defined as follows\:\*  
Causation refers to the enquiry as to whether the defendant’s conduct  
\(or omission\) caused the harm or damage. \[…\] Causation in criminal
