liability is divided into factual causation and legal causation. Factual  
\*  
Source\: http\://www.e-lawresources.co.uk/Causation-in-criminal-liability.php 74 Introduction to crowd science  
causation is the starting point and consists of applying the ‘but for’ test.  
In most instances, where there exist no complicating factors, factual  
causation on its own will suffice to establish causation. \[…\] This asks,  
‘but for the actions of the defendant, would the result have occurred’?If  
yes, the result would have occurred in any event, the defendant is not  
liable. If the answer is no, the defendant is liable as it can be said that  
their action was a factual cause of the result.  
Wikipedia\* defines causation as follows\:  
\[…\] Causation proves a direct link between the defendant’s negligence  
and the claimant’s loss and damage. For these purposes, liability in neg-  
ligence is established when there is a breach of the duty of care owed  
by the defendant to the claimant that causes loss and damage, and it is  
reasonable that the defendant should compensate the claimant for that  
loss and damage.  
Proximate and distal causality  
The judicial system often does not distinguish between actual and proxi-  
mate cause, using the term ‘proximate cause’ to refer to both the actual  
cause and the underlying or ultimate causes of an incident. Part of the dif-  
ficulty in distinguishing the immediate and underlying causes \(and hence  
liability\) lies in the fact that the fundamental issues of proximate cause have  
little to do with the scientific definition of causation, and focus more on the  
social limits of liability. For the purposes of clarification, in the context of  
this book we are using the following definitions\:  
A proximate cause is an event that is closest to, or immediately respon-  
sible for causing some observed result. This exists in contrast to a higher  
level ultimate cause or distal cause that is usually thought of as the funda-  
mental reason for an occurrence. Separating proximate and distal causality  
leads to a better understanding of the incidents and systems concerned. For  
example, why did the ship sink? The proximate cause might be because it  
hit a rock, but the underlying, distal cause may be because it was on auto-  
pilot and the radar system was switched off.  
A proximate cause may be defined as the action or event that is closest  
to, or immediately responsible for, causing an observed result. It is the most  
direct, effective or substantial cause of a tort—relevant where the negligence  
of more than one person contributed to the outcome. The distal cause is the  
larger context in which individuals carry out their actions, for example,  
where an agency substantially increases the probability of harm through  
conduct that falls below the standards of behaviour established by law for  
\*  
Source\: http\://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Causation\_in\_English\_law Causality 75  
the protection of others. To put this in context, we need a brief review of  
the UK\* legislation and guidance regarding crowd safety at events. Similar  
definitions exist in other countries, we refer to UK legislation as an example  
of these definitions.  
EVENT LEGISLATION  
In the context of a UK legal framework, an event falls under aspects of the  
Health and Safety at Work Act† 1974\. The following definitions apply\:  
The purposes of the Health and Safety at Work etc. Act 1974 \(the HSW  
Act\) include protecting people other than those at work from risks to their  
health and safety arising out of or in connection with the activities of peo-  
ple at work. Section 3 of the HSW Act places general duties on employers  
and the self-employed towards people other than their employees.  
There are specific guidelines‡ from the UK Health and Safety Executive  
\(HSE\) for events in relation to the aforementioned act\:  
Planning  
The level of detail in your planning should be proportionate to the  
scale of the event and the degree of risk. \[…\] Whatever the scale of the  
event, make sure there is a clear understanding within the organising  
team of who will be responsible for safety matters.  
Safety plan  
As an event organiser, identify the\: scale, type and scope of the event,  
type and size of audience, location, duration of the event, time of day  
and year the event will be held. These factors will help you to determine  
what resources and facilities will be required. Translate this informa-  
tion into an appropriate safety plan. The key tool for creating a safety  
plan is the process of risk assessment.  
The safety plan must include an appropriate risk assessment. This must  
be in such a form that the licensing \(approval\) processor can understand  
and approve the plan as fit for purpose and, if appropriate, grant a licence  
for the event. The plan can, of course, be rejected as being unfit for pur-  
pose. As previously stated there are a number of events that do not require  
approval \(licensing\) and miss the crowd safety checking process.  
\*  
In a negligence claim \(at least in the United States\), there are actually two causation ele-  
ments that the plaintiff has to prove\: direct causation and proximate causation.  
† Source\: http\://www.hse.gov.uk/enforce/opalert.htm  
‡ Source\: http\://www.hse.gov.uk/event-safety/getting-started.htm 76 Introduction to crowd science  
The Licensing Act 2003  
The Licensing Act 2003, Chapter 17 is an Act of the Parliament of the  
United Kingdom and sets out four licensing objectives, which must be taken  
into account when a local authority carries out its functions. They are\:  
1\. The prevention of crime and disorder  
2\. Public safety  
3\. Prevention of public nuisance  
4\. The protection of children from harm  
In Scotland there is a fifth licensing objective, which is\:  
5\. Protecting and improving public health  
Since the introduction of the Licensing Act 2003, licences are required  
for sale of alcohol and the provision of regulated entertainment. As an  
event planner, you will need some form of licence if you wish to carry out  
any of the following activities\:  
•  
•  
The sale of alcohol by retail anywhere, whether for consumption on  
or off the event premises  
The provision of regulated entertainment, namely\:  
•  
•  
•  
•  
•  
•  
The performance of a play  
The showing of a film  
An indoor sporting event  
Boxing or wrestling  
A performance of live music  
Any playing of recorded music \(excluding incidental background  
music unless played from a moving vehicle—carnivals/proces-  
sions, etc.\)  
•  
•  
A performance of dance  
Entertainment of a similar description to live music, recorded  
music or dance  
Where the entertainment takes place in the presence of the public, or a  
section of the public, you may also need a licence for the provision of hot  
food or hot drink.  
The role of checking whether an event is compliant with the Licensing  
Act is a function of a local licensing officer or committee. Their duties  
include providing information and guidance concerning the operation  
of the Licensing Act, and supervising the compliance by the holders of  
premises and occasional licences in respect of the licences that they hold.  
This monitoring aspect will ensure an ongoing check is in place and that  
licence holders are observing the licensing principles as well as the condi-  
tions placed upon the grant of their licences. A licensing officer may have Causality 77  
a statutory right to enter and inspect licensed premises for the purposes of  
determining whether the activities on the premises are being carried out in  
accordance with the Act. The licensing officer may also request to see any  
substances, articles or documents they see fit to examine.  
In summary, the licensing officer needs to be a competent person and  
should be able to understand the risks to the crowd in order to deem an  
event safe \(fit for purpose\) and grant a licence. Similar requirements exist  
in other parts of the world yet there is no mandatory minimum skill set or  
training for such officers or teams in relation to crowd safety.  
Negligence  
When we focus on crowd risk analysis during the planning and approval  
phases of an event, both the concept of a ‘duty of care’ to the public and  
the legal framework definitions may apply. In English law, the definition\*  
of negligence is\:  
Conduct that falls below the standards of behaviour established by law  
for the protection of others against unreasonable risk of harm. A person  
has acted negligently if he or she has departed from the conduct expected  
of a reasonably prudent person acting under similar circumstances.  
Our aim is to learn from the history of crowd accidents and to catego-  
rise the ‘things that can go wrong’. We do this so others can benefit from  
the information and improve working practices; to reduce crowd risks and  
improve crowd safety for future events. We do not aim to challenge the  
legal definitions, or previous legal outcomes—only to define terms of refer-  
ence in the context of crowd risks.  
We will outline, and demonstrate, some simple checks and measures that  
help identify the risk of crowd-related incidents, accidents or crush poten-  
tial. These risks can, and should, be recognised during the planning and  
approval phases of the event process. The objective of this book, and the  
courses we run around the world, are to inform, to educate and to demon-  
strate these principles, and their application, to planners, approval/licensing  
authorities and operators. These techniques are a low-cost way of assessing  
crowd risks for major events and complex sites.  
Definitions  
We will use the terms proximate and distal causality in relation to crowd  
accidents and incidents. In this context, distal causality, where an agency  
substantially increases the probability of harm by failing to identify—and  
\*  
Source\: http\://legal-dictionary.thefreedictionary.com/negligence 78 Introduction to crowd science  
mitigate—substantial risks to the crowd during the planning and approval  
phases of the event. This failure may include, but is not limited to, recognis-  
ing the potential for overcrowding. This is a fundamental requirement; a  
basic skill set that requires some core \(but not complicated\) mathematical  
skills, including calculation of the space requirement for both static and  
throughput for a moving crowd.  
There is an apparent lack of rigour in applying basic scientific and engi-  
neering principles for crowd risk analysis; a failure to understand crowd den-  
sity, crowd flow rates and the time it can take for areas to reach critical crowd  
densities. There are also fundamental failures in understanding how crowds  
move towards, within and away from a site under normal and emergency  
situations. There are simple calculations for this type of analysis, and they  
must be applied with the same rigour as other engineering disciplines. We  
highlight that for all other parts of an event—such as the provision of suit-  
able electrical supplies, temporary demountable structures and food prepara-  
tion/hygiene—rigorous standards should be applied, checked and enforced.  
We will outline, from first principles, a more robust scientific method for  
crowd risk analysis and safety engineering for planned events and places of  
public assembly.  
Defining crowd disaster causality  
From our disaster database research, we defined three fundamental ele-  
ments that were common to major incidents\:  
1\. Design-related incidents, where there appears to have been a miscal-  
culation relating to the crowd flow and the space capacity.  
2\. Information-related incidents, where there appears to have been an  
external influence on the crowd behaviour that induced an accident/  
incident.  
3\. Management-related incidents; where there appears to have been one  
or more management decisions that were responsible for the accident/  
incident.  
A design-related incident, where an element of the site proved appropri-  
ate for its intended use, is a distal failure and should have been recognised  
and addressed during planning and approval. Information and manage-  
ment failures are proximate failures as they may have occurred due to  
unexpected unfolding events. The risk of these events unfolding should  
have featured in the event plan, the contingency plan and the emergency  
plan, which should have been approved by the relevant approval/licensing/  
permitting authority.  
Information and management failures should be the subject of ‘what if’  
scenarios during the planning stage. If these are missing, or poorly devised, Causality 79  
we would argue that these too are distal failures—failures in the underlying  
safety culture during the event planning and approval processes.  
Causality is one of the most complex legal issues for case law, but we  
would argue that failing to identify risk is a distal failure by definition. By  
failing to identify and mitigate risks that are reasonably foreseeable, ‘an  
agency substantially increases the probability of harm’.  
Causes of accidents/incidents  
We have acted as expert witness for several cases involving crowd-related  
incidents. Typically, these feature mass injuries and fatalities; in every case  
our first indicators of negligence were found in the risk assessment and in  
the event safety plan. Failure to recognise risk is evidence of either negli-  
gence or incompetence in event planning and approval phases of the event.  
This is not a new or novel concept.  
SITE DESIGN\: THEORY  
From John J. Fruin’s Causes and Preventions of Disasters, published in  
Engineering for Crowd Safety in 1993 \(p. 99\)\:  
Crowds occur frequently, usually without serious problems. Occasionally  
venue inadequacies and deficient crowd management result in injuries  
and fatalities.  
In the same paper, Fruin describes ‘The view from the crowd’ \(p. 102\)\:  
\[…\] When crowd density equals the plan area of the human body, indi-  
vidual control is lost, as one becomes an involuntary part of the mass.  
At occupancies of about seven persons per square metre, the crowd  
becomes almost a fluid mass. Shock waves can be propagated through  
the mass, sufficient to lift people off of their feet and propel them dis-  
tances of 3 m \(10 ft\) or more.  
Later in the paper, Fruin describes the forces involved in crowd-related  
disasters \(p. 103\)\:  
Crowd forces can reach levels that \[are\] almost impossible to resist or  
control. Virtually all crowd deaths are due to compressive asphyxia  
and not the ‘trampling’ reported by the news media. \[…\] Forces are  
due to pushing, and the domino effect of people leaning against each  
other. \[…\] Horizontal forces sufficient to cause compressive asphyxia  
would be more dynamic as people push off against each other to obtain  
breathing space. 80 Introduction to crowd science  
Again from the same paper, under the title Prevention of Crowd Disasters  
by Crowd Management, Fruin states\:  
Most major crowd disasters can be prevented by simple crowd man-  
agement strategies. The primary crowd management objectives are the  
avoidance of critical crowd densities.  
Fruin’s concept of prevention outlines the basic issues of crowd disasters,  
and we have expanded on these concepts. We first define the fundamental  
causalities and then we review the incidents to identify where crowd risks  
occur \(where in the event process they should be identified\) and then we  
outline tools and techniques to work through crowd risk analysis for events.  
To understand the reasoning behind this approach, we need to understand  
how major incidents have occurred.  
Investigating a major incident  
During the investigation phase for crowd-related incidents, we first exam-  
ine the event planning and site design to understand whether there was an  
inherent risk of overcrowding, and whether that risk was assessed and/or  
managed appropriately. Typically, this investigation reveals a poor or defi-  
cient risk analysis process.  
We then examine the site design from a theoretical perceptive; using prin-  
ciples defined by Fruin \(Pedestrian Planning and Design, 1971\) and then  
apply any calculations used by the event planner to test their competency  
in the risk assessment process. We compare our analysis with the event  
planners’ analysis and note any deficiencies in their planning process. From  
this point, we review the approval process to see whether these deficiencies  
were detected. Clearly if both processes failed to identify risk, and yet the  
plan was approved, then the conduct fell ‘below the standards of behaviour  
established by law for the protection of others against unreasonable risk  
of harm’.  
A primary investigation typically revolves around key questions\: Did the  
organiser and approval authority understand the risks? If not, why not?  
Would a reasonably competent practitioner understand those risks? If so,  
were the event planning and approval authorities competent in the execu-  
tion of their duty of care—to provide a safe environment for their event?  
We appraise the design using internationally recognised safety crite-  
ria in order to establish whether there were inherent risks in the design  
\(pointing to distal causality\), and whether those risks were predictable and  
avoidable \(reasonably foreseeable\). We then establish whether there were  
risks that went unnoticed and subsequently passed both the planning and  
approval stages. This gauges competency and the potential for charges of  
negligence. Causality 81  
Probability of harm  
If event plans contain inerrant risks that have not been identified during  
planning and approval, these risks may still be detected and rectified dur-  
ing the event \(the operations phase\). Good crowd management practices  
can keep an event safe, but if design-related risks are not identified before  
the operations phase, any failure in management can be catastrophic.  
Management of a risky environment, without knowledge of the risks, ‘sub-  
stantially increases the probability of harm’.  
CROWD FORCES  
In Causes and Preventions of Disasters, Fruin states \(p. 103\)\:  
Evidence of bent steel railings after several fatal crowd incidents shows  
that forces of more than 4,500 N \(1,000 lbs\) occurred. \[…\] Compressive  
asphyxia has occurred from people being stacked up vertically, one on  
top of the other, or horizontal pushing and leaning forces. In the Ibrox  
Park soccer stadium incident, police reported that the pile of bodies  
was 3 m \(10 ft\) high. At this height, people on the bottom would expe-  
rience chest pressures of 3,600–4,000 N \(800–900 lbs\), assuming half  
the weight of those above was concentrated in the upper body area.  
In the Cincinnati rock concert incident, a line of bodies was found  
approximately 9 m \(30 ft\) from a wall near the entrance. This indicates  
that crowd pressures probably came from both directions as rear ranks  
pressed forward and front ranks pushed off the wall.  
Experiments to determine concentrated forces on guardrails due to  
leaning and pushing have shown that force of 30% to 75% of partici-  
pant weight can occur. In a US National Bureau of Standards study of  
guardrails, three persons exerted a leaning force of 792 N \(178 lbs.\) and  
609 N \(137 lbs.\) pushing. In a similar Australian Building Technology  
Centre study, three persons in a combined leaning and pushing posture  
developed a force of 1,370 N \(306 lbs.\). This study showed that under  
a simulated ‘panic’, five persons were capable of developing a force of  
3,430 N \(766 lbs.\).  
At around 3000 N,\* there is a potential for a five-person-deep ‘push’ to  
break ribs. Gross bone fractures are noted at forces of around 6920 N.† We  
\*  
From Biomechanics of chest and abdomen impact in The Biomedical Engineering  
Handbook\: Second Edition. \(David C. Viano, Albert I. King, 2000, Boca Raton\: CRC  
Press LLC\).  
† From Patellofemoral Joint Fracture Load Prediction Using Physical and Pathological  
Parameters—Proceedings of the 1998 SAE International Congress and Exposition. SAE  
980358 by P.J. Atkinson, C.M. Mackenzie and R.C. Haunt. 82 Introduction to crowd science  
can conclude that the crowd, by itself, can produce sufficient force to cre-  
ate serious risk to life and limb, and therefore an emphasis on appropriate  
planning, crowd risk analysis, approval \(licensing\) and event operations is  
vital to crowd safety. What we need is a methodology to identify whether  
there are elements of risk we can identify—and eliminate—during the plan-  
ning and approval phases of the event process.  
Reasonably foreseeable  
One could argue that many major incidents were ‘reasonably foreseeable’ if  
the event organiser failed to identify a risk that a competent person could  
identify using information that is freely available \(e.g. Fruin\). The event  
organiser would be deemed negligent in the executions of one’s duty of care  
and in failing to provide a safe environment under the legislation \(HSW Act\).  
This relates to all parts of an event, but specifically to the event ingress/  
entry system, where the crowd pressure and frustrations are often greatest.  
It also covers the site capacity \(number of people that the site can safely  
accommodate\) and intended usage and egress capacity \(rate at which a  
crowd can enter or leave a site\). Risk analysis also needs to consider both  
normal and emergency situations. Risk analysis should not be an ad hoc  
process; it must be structured in a systematic and methodical manner to  
ensure that all elements of risk as considered. A structured and methodical  
approach also facilitates a thorough approval process, which could other-  
wise be difficult to read and understand.  
For the crowd risk analysis, there are several considerations\: site capac-  
ity, movement pathways, entry and exit systems, utilisation and facilities  
management during normal and emergency situations.  
Site capacity  
Site capacity is typically calculated based on the available area, the suit-  
ability of that area and the rates of egress in an emergency. Site capacity  
is also based on physical and safety considerations for a site. International  
guidance also recommends assessment of ingress \(entry rates\) as an impor-  
tant safety factor that may reduce the overall site capacity if not sufficient  
to meet the arrival profile of the crowds. All parts of the system need to  
be considered; failure to do so may be classed as ‘conduct that falls below  
the standards of behaviour established by law for the protection of others  
against unreasonable risk of harm’, and would qualify as a distal failure in  
the planning and approval processes.  
Entry and exit points  
Although the crowd may be on public ground \(approach routes\), they may  
be exposed to risk while trying to enter an event. To illustrate this point, Causality 83  
consider a crowd moving towards the entry system with the expectation  
of entering the site. If the arrival flow \(people moving towards the entry  
system\) exceeds the capacity of the entry system \(number of people moving  
through the entry system\), a queue will develop.  
If the arrival flow rate exceeds the entry system capacity, then people  
arriving at the back of the queue arrive more quickly \(people per minute\)  
than the people at the front of the queue \(the people nearest the entry sys-  
tem\) can be processed. This results in a gradual build-up of crowd density  
\(people per square metre\), over time, at the point immediately in front of  
the entry system. As the crowd/queue size grows, the density at the front  
part of the queue will be compressed, increasing the crowd density and  
exposing the crowd to risk of crushing.  
As crowd density \(people per square metre\) increases to above six or seven  
people per square metre, the crowd reaches a point at which individuals  
experience physical contact and pressure. Prolonged exposure to this pres-  
sure has a detrimental effect on crowd behaviour, and there is an inherent  
risk to life and limb. Those individuals at the front of an entry system queue  
\(as described earlier\) can become trapped. They cannot remove themselves  
from the system, as the back of the queue is moving towards the entry point.  
There is a fundamental requirement to understand this specific problem  
and to ensure that this kind of situation does not develop.  
Movement pathways  
In Causes and Preventions of Disaster, under the heading Movement  
Pathways, Fruin states \(p. 106\)\:  
Arrangements that result in unbalanced use of egress or ingress routes,  
dead ends, or similar confusing and irregular pathway choices, are  
not acceptable. \[…\] Dispersed and equally balanced ingress and egress  
points are preferred over a single centralized location. The influence of  
external facilities on the volume and direction of movement must be  
considered.  
A balanced ingress and egress system would be a system in which the  
entry and exit points are distributed around a site and not focused on any  
one point. These are neither new nor novel considerations for a major event.  
These are based on the principles and applications of risk analysis and risk  
management.  
Site design\: Pinch points  
To facilitate the movement of large crowds over several hours at a major  
event, the routes around the site should not have any significant narrow 84 Introduction to crowd science  
sections—or ‘pinch points’—that can create congestion \(crowding\) and  
lead to crushing. As with the entry system described earlier, any part of an  
event, stadium or complex space with a pinch point means the crowds can  
potentially flow into a specific area faster than it can flow out of it. Pinch  
points can quickly develop high-density crowds, and there is subsequently a  
high risk of slips, trips and falls, crowd surges and pressure on those parts  
of the system build up. Again, these need to be identified in the risk assess-  
ment process by all parties involved in the planning and approval/licensing/  
permitting processes.  
Risk analysis and risk management feature in all engineering disciplines.  
They are generally regulated by governing authorities and legislated accord-  
ing to accepted engineering standards. These same standards need to be  
applied to crowd safety. At the time of writing this book, no such standards  
exit for the event planning and/or event approval process.  
UNDERLYING CAUSALITY  
Three primary failures, or underlying causes, emerge from the analysis  
of past incidents. The first relates to space requirement \(design failure\)—  
namely where the size of the space \(area\) is smaller than the total area  
required for people who are trying to occupy that space, resulting in  
crowd crush incidents and loss of life. Central to this are two relatively  
simple bits of information, the available area for the crowd, and num-  
ber of people trying to get into that area. We can look at this in several  
different ways—for example, consider a system with gates \(entry points\)  
leading to an event space, and a crowd trying to enter the system through  
the gates. This system will have a finite \(limited\) crowd flow rate based on  
the width \(or number\) of gates. In nontechnical language, the wider the  
entry system, the greater the number of gates, the greater the crowd flow  
into the system. An event designer, or site planner, should consider these  
elements to ensure the space is big enough to accommodate the crowd’s  
space requirements.  
Failure to provide sufficient space leads to overcrowding and the risk of  
crowd crushing or personal injury. This same rationale applies to all parts  
of a system where crowds move through complex spaces. Corridors, roads,  
entry points and event spaces all need to be large enough for the crowd try-  
ing to occupy that space. If they are not, there will be a crush. On review of  
the historical accidents and incidents, we can recognise, as a primary point  
of failure, this lack of sufficient space for the crowds.  
Another key element to understand is the rate of fill, how quickly spaces  
are occupied. Faster rates of filling can result in other problems, which we  
outline in later chapters. Causality 85  
Plan for the worse case  
Organisers of free events often protest, ‘But we don’t know how many peo-  
ple may come to this event!’ To which the reply is always, ‘What is your  
site capacity?’  
The first step in such cases would be to assess the maximum capacity \(the  
maximum number of people using the event space\). The next step would  
be to assess how the crowds will enter and exit that space. Separate plans  
for 50% occupation, 100% occupation and 500% occupation of the space  
should be considered. Then a comparison of the risk for each option—and  
a separate plan for each option—needs to be considered. This is a methodi-  
cal approach to risk assessment; the excuse ‘we didn’t know how many  
people might come’ only points to incompetency in planning.  
Overcrowding incidents  
In our research, we defined incidents that appeared to be design-limited,  
or space requirement failures, as ‘overcrowding incidents’ and reviewed the  
areas involved in those incidents. We found that by applying simple calcu-  
lations—such as how much area was available to the crowd, how the area  
was used, what the entry points were, how quickly the area could fill with  
people—the risk of serious injury could be anticipated prior to the event.  
Failing to provide a safe environment for the crowd—failing to anticipate  
the crowd numbers, crowd arrival rates and crowd throughput rate—is a  
common element linking many disasters. It points to an underlying system-  
atic failure in the process of the planning and approval/licensing/permitting  
processes; it is a failure to understand the crowd risks in the complex and  
built environment. During investigation of the historical database, we found  
no evidence of competency in applying those relatively simple calculations.  
Throughput incidents  
We also noted that there were throughput incidents, where the system could  
not cope with the demand\: in other words, there were too many people try-  
ing to get into/out of/through a space, and the result was crushing. Again,  
this pointed towards poor planning, failure to anticipate the crowd dynamic  
and failure to apply the necessary calculations for the crowd’s space require-  
ments. Again, the news reports would blame crowd behaviour for the failure  
and not a planning, approval \(permitting/licensing\) or operational failure.  
To a lesser extent, there were incidents that involved information fail-  
ure. These are where something changed in the environment and created  
a crowd reaction \(such as a sudden downpour of rain, or crowd crazing\),  
and could therefore be identified as a root cause of the accident. Failing to 86 Introduction to crowd science  
anticipate that the crowd would potentially ‘run for cover’ if it suddenly  
began to rain, or be attracted to a specific point of interest, also points  
towards planning/approval failure. In summary, the information failures  
were all related to a lack of understanding of the human factors.  
Crowd and event modelling  
We have developed a range of modelling techniques that do not use com-  
puter simulations. We will outline these in Chapter 5, as well as explaining  
how they can be applied during the planning process for major events. We  
break down crowd problems into phases \(ingress/circulation/egress\) and  
demonstrate how to arrange the subsequent information in an event man-  
ual. We explain how to divide an event into time zones \(as different factors  
will occur as the event plays out\). We also outline how to structure an event  
plan like a storyboard, describing the event as it is anticipated to evolve, to  
create a readable, useable and understandable document. The reason this is  
essential to the event process is that it reduces the potential for harm. Chapter 4  
# Crowd science  
The sciences do not try to explain, they hardly even try to interpret, they  
mainly make models. By a model is meant a mathematical construct,  
which, with the addition of certain verbal interpretations, describes  
observed phenomena. The justification of such a mathematical con-  
struct is solely and precisely that it is expected to work—that is correctly  
to describe phenomena from a reasonably wide area. Furthermore, it  
must satisfy certain aesthetic criteria—that is, in relation to how much  
it describes, it must be rather simple.  
John von Neumann  
Hungarian-born mathematician, 1903–1957  
INTRODUCTION  
In Chapter 5, we will introduce several modelling techniques for crowd and  
event risk analysis. These techniques have been developed over a decade of  
working on many different projects and teaching programmes, and have  
been applied to crowd and event safety around the world. They are neither  
theoretical nor academic constructs; they are tried and tested methods. We  
have applied these techniques to a wide range of events, from small crowds  
to vast gatherings, and have taught over 6000 delegates the associated prin-  
ciples and applications for use at their own events.  
Initially, the techniques were developed in order to capture information  
for our consultancy projects—specifically in Saudi Arabia, where language  
differences made it difficult to read documents. The tools proved useful for  
structuring information for analysis, and for passing on that information  
to all levels of event operators. The process involved developing a stan-  
dardised template, or meta-model,\* of crowd and event dynamics for risk  
analysis and value engineering \(optimal use of space\). It has proven to be a  
useful and teachable methodology—one that could be used for all events  
and public gatherings.  
\*  
A meta-model typically defines the language and processes that are used to create a model.  
87 88 Introduction to crowd science  
It may seem strange that after years of development and significant finan-  
cial investment that we abandoned all but the simplest of visualising tools  
for crowd risk analysis and crowd modelling. The main reason for this  
relates to how the crowds simulations were being used, to prove safety.  
Not, as originally intended, to discover the risks associated with crowds in  
complex spaces.  
Not how we would do it!  
As we stated in Chapter 2, the programmer defines the core crowd behav-  
iours in a computer crowd simulation and the end user can alter the param-  
eters on those programmed behaviours. There are many variables in a crowd  
simulation and each must be tested against the others to assess its overall  
impact against the result. Suppose a system had five variables\: it would be  
necessary to conduct between 5 and 15 tests \(5 + 4 + 3 + 2 + 1\) in order to test  
each variable against all the others, and the number of tests increases more  
as we add more variables. This is called ‘analytical intractability’\: effectively  
there are so many potential variations that it would take too long to test them  
all. So, computer scientists develop models to simplify the overall simulation  
process in order to obtain a result in a reasonable time.  
While programming the code for crowd and environment modelling, we  
were finding that crowd routes had to be programmed into the system,  
and that meant the outputs were direct functions of the end user’s inputs.  
This again highlighted that the skill of the end user determined the value  
of the simulation output. Single-value outputs \(such as the time taken for  
a crowd to egress a building\) indicated that only one simulation had been  
evaluated. Multiple runs would have \(or should have\) produced a range of  
values with mean and standard deviations—a measurement of how much  
variation there was in the simulation’s performance, which of course takes  
time to assess the variations.  
A computer scientist would test the sensitivity of key variables. Where  
the ‘new’ crowd simulations deviated from the original PhD crowd simula-  
tion was in the fact that we had designed the original simulation system  
to explore the ways crowded spaces may fail, as a risk analysis tool. The  
‘new’ crowd simulation system appeared to be developed along a different  
methodology, and the users were validating complex spaces, proving them  
to work!  
The reason for this was simple\: it was expensive to buy simulation sys-  
tems and to train operators, and it was time consuming to build models.  
The cost drives the market, and the market was the consultants and the  
project executives, who needed to be reassured that their investments were  
sound—just as we had anticipated. No one wants to spend the significant  
capital investment to discover problems. Crowd science 89  
Lessons from the workshops  
Delegates were coming to the workshops with printouts and simulation  
movie clips, and they were questioning the outputs. Clearly, this issue was  
of concern to the people on the front line of crowd management—the peo-  
ple who could actually make a difference to crowd safety. We needed to  
understand the nature of their concerns, and why so many delegates were  
questioning these simulation tools.  
The opening statement of the concluding section of the PhD asserts\:  
There were three objectives to this research\: Firstly, to understand the  
nature of crowd dynamics with respect to crowd safety; secondly, to  
develop a more appropriate risk analysis methodology for the design  
and management of places where crowds may develop. Finally, to cre-  
ate a tool that allows others to understand the nature, problems and  
necessary components of crowds and crowd dynamics.  
G. Keith Still, PhD thesis\: Crowd Dynamics, 2000, p. 262  
The tools we had developed sought to address the first and last objectives  
\(i.e. Legion, Paramics UAF, Myriad, Shepard, Progressive Crowd Collapse,  
Spatial Analysis, Network Flow, Tawaf models\). However, as we observed,  
when others were using these tools \(and many other simulations in the mar-  
ket\) the results were seldom focussed on risk analysis. We noted that often  
crowd simulations were focussed on proving that a system will work, often  
with unspecified assumptions and little or no validation. This was a worry-  
ing development—a focus on proof of concept and not on the investigation  
of how systems may fail. Of more concern was the fact that the risk analysis  
element seemed to be omitted from the consulting reports we were observing  
during the workshop sessions. Using these tools, it was possible to under-  
stand the nature of the crowd dynamic with respect to crowd safety, and it  
was possible to test the sensitivity of certain psychological parameters, and  
to assess different types of behaviour under computer laboratory conditions.  
Nevertheless, that was not how they were being used for major projects.  
The first objective, ‘to understand the nature of crowd dynamics with  
respect to crowd safety’, was not being met. The second objective was ‘to  
develop a more appropriate risk analysis methodology’, although possible  
with the aforementioned simulation systems, was also failing to make any  
significant impact on improving crowd safety. Computer simulations were  
simply not reaching the target audience; not the computer scientists, but those  
groups who had a direct impact on crowd management. The third objective,  
‘to create a tool that allows others to understand the nature, problems and  
necessary components of crowds’, was clearly not being achieved, as simula-  
tions were proving too expensive for the business end of event safety. 90 Introduction to crowd science  
The problem with simulation  
Some delegates were coming to the workshops displaying computer mod-  
els they simply did not understand; their company had paid huge con-  
sulting fees to have these simulations produced, and had paid for reports  
and analysis that merely told them the system works, when they knew,  
instinctively, they had a problem. These delegates were still looking for  
solutions. It appeared that the crowd simulation route was all cost and no  
real benefit.  
Why had this happened?  
Clients do not want to be told their investments may fail; they want to have  
their confidence in the project reassured, and they often hire consultants  
who produce simulations that will show the positive aspects of their proj-  
ect. Consultants using crowd simulations that constantly point out how  
things will fail do not win business contracts. This also drives the process  
towards ‘proof of concept’, where simulations are used to demonstrate how  
things work, not stress testing them to understand how things may fail.  
The approach we take, in the workshops, was the same we developed  
to capture information prior to inputting the relevant data to a computer  
simulation. That approach focuses on modelling.  
MODELLING  
A model—be it a physical model such as a model aeroplane in a wind tun-  
nel, or a complex digital simulation of a crowd—captures only part of the  
reality for analysis. That is a fundamental principle of modelling; a simpli-  
fication of the system that is under examination.  
Crowds are complex; they consist of many individual human minds,  
sometimes behaving in unison \(say at a concert, rally or protest\), sometimes  
opposing each other \(say as opposing fans at a football or sporting event\),  
sometimes as random elements in a complex space \(at shopping centres/  
malls and in streets\). To understand the entire range of human behaviour  
and possible reactions would require comprehensive knowledge of those  
things. It would also require extensive testing in order to understand all the  
possible outcomes of any given set of initial conditions. This would make it  
a very expensive and time-consuming exercise. The costs would need to be  
passed onto the client, and this has, in certain commercial crowd simula-  
tions, restricted information published for other researchers to use in their  
competing crowd simulations. Crowd science 91  
External influences on crowd behaviour  
Further complications arise in building a computer simulation of a crowd,  
as the individuals are also influenced by external events such as weather  
changes, announcements, management styles and information systems,  
design of buildings, barriers and entrance foyers. The list is endless and  
the models are simplifications of the real world. Simulations are, by defini-  
tion, only part of the overall analysis of crowds in the complex and built  
environment.  
When we were using simulations to test how a system might fail, we  
were testing a limited set of variables in order to determine how risks  
developed, to understand the nature and rates of failure. We noticed that  
many others \(crowd scientists, consultants and the like\) were using com-  
puter simulations to prove that a system would work, but in order to prove  
whether or not a system will work you need to test all possible initial  
conditions.  
The emphasis is on the word ‘proving’. This is an onerous, costly and  
time-consuming function. It requires far more data and inputs to be vali-  
dated. To prove something means to have tested all possible combinations  
and provide a measure of certainty that A, B, C inputs will produce, consis-  
tently, X, Y, Z outcomes. With so many variables and uncertainties across  
a wide range of possible crowd behaviours—under many different and  
dynamic changes to the environments—this may be an intractable prob-  
lem. It may never be defined as a proof.  
To clarify this, and in the light of our own use of simulation systems  
mentioned in the previous pages, we will illustrate the case in point using  
the Jamarat Bridge project. The pilgrims’ ritual \(‘stoning the devil’\) has  
a series of phases\: approach the pillar, throw the pebbles, move to near-  
est space, spend time making a prayer, move to the second pillar, throw  
the pebbles, move to nearest space, spend time making a prayer, move to  
the third pillar, throw the pebbles, leave the system. The objective of the  
model was to determine the sensitivity of three critical spaces\: the entry  
ramp width, the throughput capacity of the system and the egress capac-  
ity. To that aim, a fundamental model of the system was produced and the  
simulation was used to assess the critical input, throughput and output  
components \(widths\).  
As with all engineering disciplines and associated methodologies, the  
objective was to understand the limits of a system—to test it to destruc-  
tion, understand how and when it breaks, and then define the construction  
parameters in order to minimise the risk of the system breaking. We used  
the simulation to understand how the system might fail, then to inform the  
design teams and provide training outlines and management courses on  
how to manage that system safely. 92 Introduction to crowd science  
We then designed into the Jamarat Bridge safety margins, additional  
spaces to allow for variance in the crowd behaviour and contingency spaces  
for medical or other emergencies. We did not use simulations merely to  
prove the system works!  
By creating a balanced system, where ingress flow rate was less than  
throughput flow rate, and where egress flow rate capacity was greater than  
throughput flow rate capacity. In doing this, we ensured that there were no  
design bottlenecks in the system. We provided a fundamental design input  
by reducing the potential of design-related problems. In any crowded envi-  
ronment, the system can still fail under certain conditions; those conditions  
need to be understood and managed appropriately in order to prevent such  
a failure.  
That should be the objective of crowd modelling\: to understand how  
crowd risks develop and to inform the design, information and manage-  
ment processes of the nature of those potential failures. Crowd simulations,  
where the environment or the crowd parameters are changed to assess a  
design change inform the design process, but this does not evaluate the  
whole spectrum of crowd stimuli; crowds also were influenced by informa-  
tion and management processes.  
Deadly consequences  
Sadly, we know of two specific examples where people have misused com-  
puter simulations of crowds. In those cases, a poorly designed crowd simu-  
lation was used to assess how crowds might pass through a complex system.  
The system was subsequently approved as safe in the simulation. Sadly, in  
the real world mass fatalities resulted during operation.  
With the proliferation of crowd simulations, and the competitive nature  
of the market, it is inevitable that this will happen again. Part of the aim  
of this publication is to make end users aware that there is an inher-  
ent danger in the misuse of crowd simulations, and that there are lower  
cost, more effective and appropriate tools, techniques and methodologies  
available.  
CROWD DYNAMICS AND CROWD SCIENCE  
In Chapter 1, we described noting at workshops that crowd managers had  
a deep distrust of crowd simulations. To understand why this is the case, we  
need to outline the core problem with crowd dynamics simulations. Crowd science 93  
In the PhD thesis, we described crowd dynamics as follows\:  
Crowd dynamics can be defined as the study of how and where crowds  
form, and move, above the critical density threshold of more than  
one person per square metre. At low density \(below one person per  
square metre\) individuals have freedom to choose routes, directions,  
read information and react to their environment. At high density \(more  
than six or seven people per square metre\) there is the potential for  
overcrowding and personal injury. It is important to understand the  
dynamics of crowds, how crowds understand and interpret informa-  
tion systems, how management systems affect crowd behaviour and  
how, where, when and why accidents occur.  
The simulations we developed allow the study of simulated crowd move-  
ments; how and when the crowd reaches certain critical densities; how long  
it will take to fill spaces and then empty. Typically, this involves the study  
of crowd limits; how fast the crowd can move under certain conditions  
and when an area may reach capacity in built and complex environments.  
Although there are many variables—such as individual characteristics,  
external influences, environment, terrain and the weather \(there is a long  
list\)—we can determine the fundamental dynamics of crowds and the  
­ relationship between the model and the model environment.  
The original PhD research goal was to understand crowd dynamics as  
it related to crowd risks, and to use that information to inform those indi-  
viduals who manage crowds. As a result, the original software modelling  
and simulation systems were designed to understand crowd risks by experi-  
menting with the simulation—for example, by testing combined flows over  
different times through complex spaces to determine how, when and where  
areas may become problematic, the phrase used is sometimes ‘has the  
potential to become challenging’. We used these simulations to understand  
crowd risks, and to explore a range of parameters in order to understand  
the underlying components of those risks.  
We also explored the different types of geometry that could lead to crush-  
ing incidents, and developed progressive crowd collapse models to study  
the phenomena. Shockwave models help us understand the pressures and  
potential consequences of high-density compression, the waves of motion  
through the crowd and what elements may gives rise to a shockwave. With  
our wide range of crowd modelling tools, we could explore the potential  
causality of accidents and incidents. This is not possible with a real crowd  
as the risks to life and limb would be significant.  
Computer simulations that allow the user the opportunity to explore the  
movement of crowds, and to understand the crowd dynamic, are also very  
useful for problems that require optimisation of time and use of space \(value 94 Introduction to crowd science  
engineering\). But they need to be treated with care when applied to accident  
analysis.  
Simulation danger  
Unless a simulation can mimic the behaviour and reactions of crowds under  
extreme conditions, it may be misleading—and in some cases dangerously  
misleading. We have witnessed several major incidents where the event  
‘passed’ a simulation test. For example, a simulation that does not exceed  
6.25 people per square metre, used to model a high-density, high-throughput  
system, would show a slow progression through that system, but the reality  
of the situation would be serious risk to life and limb. The results may be  
ambiguous to a layperson and need to be interpreted by experts.  
We need to stress that the development of crowd simulations has been  
rapid and widely distributed around the world, and there may be many  
more serious incidents ‘lying in wait’. Enhanced computer graphics may  
look nice, but experts need to interpret the results.  
Nice to look at\: But is it real?  
High-resolution graphics—specifically the effects that are seen in movies—  
have set a high standard for computer simulations. Hollywood crowd  
simulations focus on how the movement of crowds will look to the viewer  
and, depending on the core algorithm \(the numerical formulae within the  
computer system\), they can look very realistic.  
The real world is not the same as the computer-generated world; there  
are many aspects of crowd behaviour that can impact on safety, such as  
individual aggression, interactions between rival factions at a protest, the  
influence of criminal behaviour, weather, sudden unexpected noises and  
the like. Although the simulations can be enhanced through the under-  
standing of certain behavioural characteristics, they are still based on lim-  
ited sets of untestable assumptions relating to human behaviour in complex  
spaces. For example, how do you validate/calibrate human ‘aggression’?  
How would the influence of a drunk behaving badly affect the crowd?  
What other environmental changes, that may be untested in a simulation,  
might affect safety? We once observed a sudden crowd crush, for reasons  
unknown, near a wide tunnel entrance; on inspection after the event, we  
noted a large horse ‘deposit’ in the tunnel. The smell was unpleasant, but  
the resulting crowd congestion could have proven to be hazardous.  
To expand on this point\: crowd simulations facilitate the study of the  
fundamentals of crowd movement and interactions \(dynamics\) through  
space, over time. They test a very narrow range of human behaviour under  
an assumed set of conditions. Those assumptions may be severely limited  
and may not consider a wide range of potential scenarios. An authentic Crowd science 95  
simulation report should state all the input assumptions to be truthful  
and honest with regard to the simulation’s limits. These are scientific and  
engineering principles that need to be rigorously applied for safety-critical  
environments. Furthermore, the outputs of a crowd simulation must be  
understood in the wider context of crowd behaviour and crowd manage-  
ment. Crowd simulations can only model a small part of the overarching  
complexity of crowds and human behaviour; this should be clearly stated  
whenever these tools are used for safety-critical applications. We cannot  
stress that point enough.  
CAVEAT EMPTOR  
The typical waiver on almost all software will normally contain a caveat  
that absolves the software company of all potential liability for use of  
application\:  
Under no circumstances will XYZ be liable for any losses or damages  
whatsoever, whether in contract, tort or otherwise, from the use of, or  
reliance on, the software. Neither XYZ nor any of its subsidiaries or  
licensors shall be liable for loss of profits, loss or inaccuracy of data, or  
indirect, special, incidental or consequential damages, even if advised  
of the possibility of such damages. Nothing in this limitation of liabil-  
ity shall limit XYZ’s liability for death or personal injury caused by its  
negligence or the negligence of its employees.  
You see the same disclaimers on any product that could be used incor-  
rectly; the user is responsible for the inputs and the interpretation of the  
resulting data. Caveat emptor\: let the buyer beware.  
Crowd science\: The bigger picture  
We still use crowd simulations \(where the situation dictates\), but they gen-  
erally only help us identify or quantify a risk. The simulation process is  
only one small part of a much larger picture. The wider objective is to  
understand and evaluate the spectrum of risks associated with crowds in  
public places, and to apply engineering standards and solutions to the pro-  
cess. These risks have many different dimensions; we need to understand  
their form and nature, their location, duration and severity. To that aim  
we developed a much simpler approach to the problem of risk analysis, and  
built a range of crowd models, for crowd managers to use and understand.  
The philosophy of a crowd model should be that it is simple to use, sim-  
ple to understand, simple to communicate the problems to the user and  
simple to apply to the event planning and approval process. The objective 96 Introduction to crowd science  
of a model is to identify risks \(or hazards\) once a model is built, the risks  
do not magically disappear.  
Risks and hazards  
It is worth noting that the UK Health and Safety Executive \(HSE\) defines\*  
risk and hazard as follows\:  
Hazard and risk are used interchangeably in everyday vocabulary.  
Nevertheless, it has proved useful to HSE to make a conceptual distinc-  
tion between a ‘hazard’ and a ‘risk’ by describing a hazard as the potential  
for harm arising from an intrinsic property or disposition of something to  
cause detriment, and risk as the chance that someone or something that is  
valued will be adversely affected in a stipulated way by the hazard.  
HSE—as far as the health, safety and welfare of people is concerned—  
frequently makes use of the above conceptual distinction in its guidance  
by requiring that hazards be identified, the risks they give rise to are  
assessed and appropriate control measures introduced to address the  
risks.  
The term ‘hazard’ is absent in the HSW Act. However, the Courts  
have ruled that as far as section 3 of the Act is concerned, ‘risk’ means  
‘possibility of danger’ rather than ‘actual danger’ \(see paragraphs  
41–42\). Conceptually, HSE will therefore regard anything presenting  
the ‘possibility of danger’ as a ‘hazard’.  
Moreover, since in any given workplace there would be a large num-  
ber of hazards that duty holders could address, requiring duty hold-  
ers formally to address them all would place an excessive and largely  
useless burden on them. So as not to impose unnecessary burdens on  
duty holders, HSE will not expect them to take account of hazards  
other than those that are a reasonably foreseeable cause of harm, tak-  
ing account of reasonably foreseeable events and behaviour.  
There are several key points pertaining to the event planning and approval  
process, relating to the aforementioned quote. The first is that the definition  
of hazards as ‘the potential for harm’ and risks as ‘the chance that someone  
\[…\] will be adversely affected \[…\] by the hazard’ are differentiated.  
Crowd hazards  
A ‘hazard’, in the context of crowd safety for events, may be a bottleneck  
or some part of a system that may constrain crowd flow, leading to conges-  
tion and potentially crushing. By definition, these may not be recognised  
\*  
Reducing Risks, Protecting People \(2001, ISBN 0 7176 2151 0\). Crowd science 97  
as hazards as defined by the HSE. A turnstile, for example, is not a hazard  
until the crowd arrival rates exceed the turnstile throughput or operating  
rate. At that time, a queue may develop; if the queue is left unchecked and  
is not managed; a crush may develop and there is now risk to life and limb.  
Similarly, bottlenecks \(or pinch points\) identified by a modelling process  
may not be a hazard until the crowd interacts with the geometry\: at that  
point risks can be qualified and appropriately managed. The pinch point  
is not, in itself, a hazard. It will function without risk until the arrival rate  
exceeds the throughput rate. The importance of this, in the context of crowd  
modelling and our original use of simulations, is that the objective was to  
identify, classify and understand these types of risks.  
On the value placed on risk assessment\:  
… requiring that hazards be identified, the risks they give rise to are  
assessed and appropriate control measures introduced to address the risks.  
This may also be confusing in the context of an interacting and dynamic  
system, such as combined crowd flows, congestion and sudden environmen-  
tal changes creating a crowd reaction. These can be difficult to describe in  
words in a conventional risk assessment; they can be equally difficult to  
classify and quantify.  
The final statement in the aforementioned extract\:  
So as not to impose unnecessary burdens on duty holders, HSE will  
not expect them to take account of hazards other than those that are  
a reasonably foreseeable cause of harm \[…\] of reasonably foreseeable  
events and behaviour.  
This implies there is no requirement for a rigorous, systematic, methodi-  
cal approach to risk assessment, risk classification and risk mitigation. It is  
important to note that even the most basic requirements for quality assur-  
ance, necessary for other, safety critical engineering disciplines, are not a  
requirement, for crowd risk assessment. The HSE statement “so as not  
to impose unnecessary burdens on duty holders…” has been used, by the  
defense, as the proverbial ‘get out of jail free’ card in several HSE prosecu-  
tions. If you compare two risk assessments from two different individuals  
who are asked to review the same event site, they may define and quan-  
tify the risks in very different terms and values. Similarly, if you read the  
accident reports and near miss accounts from crowd managers, the most  
common defense is\: ‘This was totally unexpected’. The risk assessment and  
classification process is experiential and very subjective; if we cannot define,  
classify, structure and quantise the risk in a consistent and structured man-  
ner, it follows that we cannot quantify the level of risk we may be exposing  
the crowd to in a complex and built environment. 98 Introduction to crowd science  
The purpose of a model  
The goal is to improve crowd safety through the application of crowd and  
event risk analysis models. One reason why the concept of simulations fails  
in this specific domain is that the computer systems require a high level of  
training to develop and use, which may be construed as an ‘unnecessary  
burden on duty holders’. As we pointed out in Chapter 1, the people with  
the most experience in crowd safety were not the users of crowd simulation  
systems. The simulation experts were computer scientists, often with little  
or no real crowd management experience, and the cost of hiring this exper-  
tise is a burden on budget and time in the event process.  
This was our primary reason for establishing simpler and more useful  
technologies for crowd risk analysis and crowd safety engineering\: to avoid  
that ‘unnecessary burden’, to bring about a step change in both the clas-  
sification and understanding of crowds risks.  
ARE ALL CROWD SIMULATIONS BAD?  
We need to examine the nature of the crowd simulation in a wider and  
deeper context in order to understand its application and limitations.  
A crowd simulation is expected to work, to be fit for purpose. There are  
dozens of crowd simulations on the market, ranging from good to bad to  
outright dangerous. So how do we differentiate a good simulation from the  
bad simulation?  
To put it bluntly, you would need a degree in computer science to under-  
stand the differences and subtleties of different crowd simulations. If people  
are injured, if lives are lost, then the event organiser, the licensing/approval  
offices and the event operators \(stewarding/security/police\) may be faced  
with a lawsuit, not the computer simulation of the consultant. Remember  
the modelling/consulting disclaimers\: caveat emptor.  
That is the core of the problem.  
After the event!  
It is only when an incident occurs in the real world that the crowd simula-  
tion can be questioned. Did the simulation predict the problem? It is only  
when a simulation has proved not to work—when lives have been lost—  
that those vital questions are asked, and by then it is too late. That is why  
we felt there was a need to go back to the basic principles and applications  
of the crowd dynamics PhD concepts—specifically to the use and misuse  
of simulations—to understand the core problem of crowds in the complex  
and built environment; to understand the crowd risks. We had to develop  
an approach that the crowd managers could use, at low cost, which would Crowd science 99  
be evident to the planning, approval and operations phases of the event  
process. To justify this principle, we need some essential descriptions of the  
crowd dynamic within the context of a simulation.  
What is a crowd?  
A crowd is, by definition, a collection of individuals. We cannot know what  
goes on in the mind of an individual, how their mood may shape their  
behaviour. We cannot predict every factor that may influence behaviour.  
The combination of elements and composition of crowds makes this an  
analytically intractable\* problem. To reiterate this specific point, we need to  
know all the potential variables, and satisfy a sufficiently rigorous test cri-  
terion to establish the sensitivity analysis of those variables on the potential  
outcome. The following example illustrates this concept.  
Imagine a little old lady in a crowd. If she is at the front of the crowd,  
her pace of movement may influence everyone behind her. This, of course,  
relates to the geometry of the environment \(say a corridor or narrow stairs\).  
In other words, her movement can slow the progress of the entire crowd  
under certain conditions. If the little old person were at the back of the  
crowd in the same environment, her movement would have no impact on  
the crowd’s progress. The same principle applies to a slow-moving vehicle  
on a busy road\: traffic slows to the pace of the slowest-moving vehicle at the  
front of the traffic on the narrow road. An individual’s position, under cer-  
tain key conditions, can have a significant impact on the outcome of such a  
situation. With regard to simulations, this effect requires careful filtering of  
the set-up and numerous sensitivity tests to establish whether condition ‘A’  
\(little old lady\) has any impact on the outcome of a simulation run. It may  
take a number of simulation runs to establish whether or not there is any  
impact—we call this a sensitivity analysis. Is the simulation output sensitive  
to a specific initial condition?  
‘One size fits all’  
There is a wide range of human shapes, behaviours and levels of fitness  
in the population. Some people may walk as a group, with children, be  
carrying bags, have varying degrees of mobility, be more \(or less\) aware of  
their surroundings. There are many variables to consider when building  
and using a crowd simulation. Most of these human factors \(variables\)  
are ignored due to the problem of testing all combinations \(where in the  
crowd is the little old person—at the front or at the back?\) and the inevi-  
table cost of performing these tests. As a result, simulations only really  
\*  
Too many unknown elements, parameters and interactions in the system to facilitate math-  
ematical analysis. 100 Introduction to crowd science  
test a very limited set of crowd variables, generally using a single size of  
character.  
There is a second—and more dangerous—element in crowd simulations,  
which is related to packing density \(the number of computer-generated  
characters that can occupy the computer equivalent of the space\). The  
computer needs to process the shapes of the characters and the individual  
calculations for overtaking and passing \(one- and two-way crowd flows\).  
There are many layers of calculations, both for the crowd dynamic and the  
graphics, in a display of a crowd on the computer screen. This complexity  
is dealt with by way of simplification. One such simplification is to move  
the computer-generated crowd as if it is moving on a chessboard, from  
square to square. This limits the packing density in the simulation. Because  
of this kind of simplification \(limiting packing density to, say, 6.25 people  
per square metre\), those simulations will never show the crowd at risk of  
increased pressure and crushing \(which occurs at 7–10 people per square  
metre\). The limits to some crowd simulations can be dangerously mislead-  
ing, for example, where a density-limited simulation is applied to assess  
risks in an environment where the crowd may exceed the simulation’s limit.  
The simulation shows that ‘all is good’, when a real crowd could develop  
substantial crowd pressure and significant risk to life and limb. This has  
occurred at least twice, to our knowledge \(Jamarat Bridge 2006 and Love  
Parade 2010\), resulting in mass fatalities.  
A crowd simulation applied to this kind of risk analysis—without knowl-  
edge or understanding of sensitivity analysis, with no one to challenge the  
inputs or outputs, and with no voice of experience in the process \(i.e. peo-  
ple with real crowd management experience\)—can be fatal. The continued  
blind use and application of computer simulations will inevitably lead to  
further problems. Simulations can look very realistic, but they are only as  
good as the rules within the underlying models, assumptions, computations  
and formulae.  
Using a crowd simulation  
In general, when used correctly, simulations have two main purposes\: to  
prove or disprove a potential feature or characteristic of an event, or to  
provide some insight into the nature of an event. When simulating crowds,  
the psychologist may test the sensitivity of certain variables—for example,  
by increasing the aggression level for a character in a conflict situation.  
This would have no real-life parallel as one cannot measure aggression in  
individuals, or how it may change in a conflict or crisis situation and affect  
others. As such, this kind of modelling may have no practical applications,  
but it could provide valuable research insights into the relationship of \(say\)  
aggression and conflict resolution options. As previously outlined, scien-  
tific tools are used by scientists for the purpose of scientific study, to test Crowd science 101  
hypotheses and explore the sensitivity of the outcome in relation to the ini-  
tial assumptions. Valuable academic research, but, as with any safety criti-  
cal engineering discipline, these simulations need to be properly validated,  
with real crowds, before they could be used to prove a safety case.  
Simulations are based on a set of assumptions, and some \(not all\) of those  
assumptions may be tested and explored in a simulation run. Whether  
there is a tangible output or not, the exploration of assumptions relating to  
crowds can provide valuable insights. In our own research, we have found  
many interesting phenomena using simulations—some good, some useful,  
some interesting, some bad and some dangerous.  
APPLICATIONS OF A CROWD SIMULATION  
Over the last 25 years, we have worked with architects, site designers and  
transportation and structural engineers who were familiar with the limita-  
tions of simulations. At the same time, the crowd managers—the people  
who may benefit most from the uses and applications of crowd dynam-  
ics simulations—neither understood the data nor believed the results. This  
disbelief was a significant cause for concern in our work. Over the years  
of teaching the principles and applications of crowd modelling, the same  
questions relating to simulation credibility were asked time and again. We  
do not understand this? What does this report mean? We do not think this  
is right? It does not look like a real crowd!  
Furthermore, when we reviewed the historical database of accidents  
and incidents, we realised that causality had some common features, but  
near misses were not documented and there was a lack of understanding of  
crowd dynamics throughout the industry. This was common to incidents  
around the world. That was the missing link, the information the crowd  
managers knew and the computer programmers did not comprehend. That  
was why the simulations did not look real!  
An untapped wealth of information and experience exists in the minds of  
the crowd managers, and their experience had to be collated in a structured  
and accessible manner to make it useful and improve crowd safety across  
the industry. Typically, the experienced crowd managers were not program-  
mers, scientists or expert computer users; we could not use computer sys-  
tems to capture and disseminate that experience.  
Simulation versus experience  
We knew that, owing to the restrictions of computation and graphics capa-  
bilities, there are a number of programming shortcuts necessary to produce  
a reasonable model, and the computer programmers only knew these short-  
cuts. When crowd managers challenged the outputs of computer models, 102 Introduction to crowd science  
they were baffled by the science. These results were, indeed, very clever  
models, but they had little to do with reality. Crowd managers would bring  
simulation video outputs to the workshops, pointing out their concerns.  
These two groups—the computer scientists and the crowd managers—were  
not collaborating.  
We observed a growing distrust of crowd simulations from the crowd  
managers, and a growing disdain—a dismissive attitude—from several  
crowd simulations developers towards the crowd managers, who were  
questioning their simulations. The proceedings from various conferences  
purported, in two major publications, theories of crowds, behaviour and  
reactions that were patently incorrect and would be dismissed as dangerous  
by the experienced crowd manager and crowd psychologist. The power of  
the technology grew, and the graphics capabilities became more realistic,  
but that masked a deeper and more worrying problem.  
Graphics, not physics  
Photorealistic, human-like characters in the movies \(Avatar, for example\)  
and on the computer screen are an illusion. In the mind of the viewer if it  
looks like a human and moves like a human, then it surely has all the attri-  
butes of a human. That was the core of the problem in simulating crowds\:  
computer scientists were trying to emulate the way they believed crowds  
behaved. Crowd managers were seeing the simulations and saying, ‘That’s  
not how a real crowd behaves!’  
There is no value to spending tens of thousands of pounds \(or dollars\)  
on simulations if the results are neither used nor trusted. People with real  
crowd experience need to be integrated into the development process; oth-  
erwise, the scientist is in an isolated and unrealistic world. With such a gulf  
among the programmers, scientists and end users, a fundamental question  
kept coming up in the workshops\: were these crowd simulations reliable?  
Were they ‘fit for purpose’ when used to prove an environment is safe for  
the crowds, for assessing crowd safety? To simplify this complex problem,  
we need to explain more about the limits to a simulation.  
Limits to simulations  
To understand certain aspects of a system it helps to simplify that system as  
much as possible, and to include only those properties and characteristics  
that are essential for understanding the system under study. A simulation  
incorporates only those elements that influence the behaviour under study.  
If, for example, you want to understand how an object falls, you can conduct  
experiments by dropping different types of objects and measuring how fast  
they fall. From these studies, you can construct a formula \(a model of how  
fast an object will fall\). This formula predicts how all other objects will fall. Crowd science 103  
From this you can build a computer simulation—a computer model of  
falling objects \(physics\) based on your formula. It is important to note that  
in this process you do not concern yourself with whether the object is new  
or old, red or green, has odour or not. You focus on just two key properties\:  
the experimental results and your formula \(or model\). For a falling object,  
there are just two parameters\: time and the gravitational force. That is all  
you need to know in order to produce a very good model of how objects  
will fall. Just two parameters! All objects will fall at the same rate; the  
speed at which they hit the ground is a function of the time they fall, which  
relates to their initial height when they are dropped. All objects accelerate  
under the gravitation force at a predictable rate. The model is, to a scientist,  
a simple formula.  
Physics, not graphics  
A computer simulation is written using a series of mathematical recipes  
called algorithms. These recipes need to be based on some numerical for-  
mulae that can predict the property you want to study—for example, how  
objects fall. Two parameters determine the time it takes for all objects to  
fall\: the initial height and the gravitational constant. We can then use that  
basic model to determine the speed at which the object will hit the ground,  
and from that we can calculate how high an object may bounce, and for  
that we need to know something about the properties of a falling object. We  
can also determine whether or not air resistance will affect the size of the  
object. For example, although a tonne of coal may fall as fast as a tonne of  
feathers, the surface area of the tonne of feathers will be much larger and  
may be affected by the density of the air. And so it goes, we start with a  
simple model, mass and acceleration, and we add parameters to this as we  
dig deeper into the system under scrutiny. The more we need to understand  
about the phenomena under scrutiny, the more complex the model becomes.  
You can use a falling body formula without a computer and it will work  
equally well.\* If you need to produce a graphical display of objects falling,  
you suddenly need to consider the display elements, such as colour, texture,  
lighting, how the object may rotate during its fall and many other proper-  
ties. You may need to programme a background, its lighting, other objects,  
their lighting and texture \(rendering\) to make your simulation look more  
realistic to the viewer.  
It takes a lot more effort to perfect the display characteristics of an object  
falling than it might to understand the basic physics of the falling object.  
\*  
At least it has been done since the time of Galileo Galilei \(1564–1642\), who defied centuries  
of tradition and demonstrated his theory that two objects of different mass, when dropped,  
would hit the ground at the same time. The principles were always true, but it took an  
experiment to prove it! 104 Introduction to crowd science  
The phenomena is physics, the display is graphics; it is important to dif-  
ferentiate between these two as the end user can be easily fooled. Great  
graphics and poor/bad physics may look convincing to the eye, but they  
may be completely wrong!  
The processes of modelling \(understanding how things work\) and simu-  
lations \(testing how things work\) are very different to rendering an image  
on a computer screen. This is why it takes so long to produce an animated  
movie; there is a lot of time and effort required to create all the visual ele-  
ments to make them appear realistic.  
VALIDATION  
The physics of falling objects can be tested. We can test objects to the point  
of destruction; we can drop a glass, a vase, a piece of fruit and so forth,  
in fact, we can drop many objects in order to validate our model of fall-  
ing objects. Obviously, we cannot test a crowd to destruction, nor can we  
measure such intangible elements as mood, aggression or reactions. There  
are many such behavioural and psychological limits to understanding the  
individual, and the crowd may never be validated in the same way we can  
validate other engineering disciplines.  
Complex simulations  
As we develop an understanding of the object under scrutiny, we eliminate  
the things that needlessly complicate matters. This simplification process is
