called modelling; mathematicians sometimes call these ‘toy’ models. In this  
context, ‘toy’ means the simple analysis of something that is understood  
to be much more complicated. You can present a model as an actual repre-  
sentation of the system under scrutiny on a computer screen \(simulation\),  
or as a mathematical relationship \(e.g. a flow rate calculation\). Then, as  
you wish to know more and more about any phenomenon, or as the phe-  
nomenon becomes more and more complex, you need increasingly elabo-  
rate equations, increasingly detailed programming and you end up with a  
computerised simulation that is hard to understand. The more complex the  
object you are studying, the more complex the models need to be in order  
to establish the underlying behaviour. This process is necessary to both  
understanding and predicting outcomes.  
When to use a simulation  
We can make the following general observations about the simulation  
approach\: Crowd science 105  
•  
•  
•  
•  
•  
A simulation is most appropriate when the problem is too complex to  
solve using another method.  
A process such as random-number procedures must be employed to  
generate values for the probabilistic components of the model.  
A book-keeping procedure must be developed to keep track of what is  
happening in the simulation process.  
Because of the numerous calculations required in most simulations, a  
computer programme or simulator is required.  
The simulation process must be conducted for many days or periods  
in order to establish the long-run averages for the decision alternatives  
or other changes in the system.  
Through a series of experiments with the simulation, good values may be  
selected for the key design parameters of the system. This places the empha-  
sis on using a computer, and on the user having the ability to alter the initial  
parameters in a simulation and to test the outcome. The greater the number  
of parameters, the longer it will take to test them all.  
Least possible simulation  
The scientist would call the simplest model of a system a ‘least possible  
simulation’ \(LPS\), whereas a ‘full’ simulation gains in complexity faster  
than the simulated object does, and the simulation eventually catches up  
with the phenomenon. Plus the time and effort required in creating a realis-  
tic graphical representation of the object under study. The principles of LPS  
were established years ago and the science is called Information Theory.  
A simulations have less information than the system you are claiming to  
simulate. You need to understand a lot about the system you are simulating;  
you need to support validation with real-world measurements. Which begs  
a simple and direct question of a simulation vendor\: how much do we know  
about human behaviour? The answer is not a lot!  
Understanding human behaviour  
Crowd managers had experienced a much wider range of human behav-  
iours than the computers were capable of simulating. A further problem  
related to obtaining test data for crowds, as there was such a wide range of  
behaviours and variables. Returning to the falling-object model, we drop  
objects to develop a model \(a formula\), and then we can use that model to  
predict the behaviour of a different object and hence refine our model. To  
understand crowd risk and make appropriate measurements of risk reac-  
tions, we would need to study the crowd in a risky situation. This is neither  
ethical nor practical, so our crowd studies and subsequent crowd models 106 Introduction to crowd science  
are based on assumptions, on the few CCTV observations of risk-related  
crowd behaviour. These models are incomplete at best.  
Getting back to basics  
To get back to our original research objectives, a new approach was  
required. We had been using simulations to gain valuable insight into the  
nature and interactions of crowd movements in built and complex spaces,  
but in order to develop a more appropriate risk analysis methodology for  
the design and management of places of public assembly, a simpler, noncom-  
puter-based approach was required. What was needed was a set of tools that  
allowed others to understand the nature, problem and components of risk  
as it related to crowd safety. Modelling—not simulations—would provide  
the methodologies and tools for the people who manage crowds. Models  
are different to simulations in that we can construct a framework, an empty  
template, in which we can capture vital information and communicate it  
to others. One important element of this process is to provide a record of  
information in a form that others can read and understand, to prevent an  
event relying on the experience of one individual.  
The main reason for this change in approach was the fact that the events  
industry \(worldwide\) has had many close calls and near misses. Incidents  
that had the potential to become mass fatalities were simply not recorded.  
Unlike any other engineering practice, there were few standards.  
SUMMARY  
With little or no standard of competency for risk analysis and risk  
­ management, and with the events industry operating on a minimum budget  
for maximum return, there were significant limitations to improving stan-  
dards. The first of which is that no one wants to publicly state, ‘Phew! We  
were lucky that time!’ So information that was important to the process of  
improving crowd safety was not being captured. As a result, safety systems  
were not improving, and—as we have discovered over the last decade—the  
same mistakes were being made over and over again as experienced indi-  
viduals left their posts to move to other events, and the replacement crowd  
manager had to relearn processes and procedures, what worked and what  
did not work. Some safety systems required investment but may provide no  
immediate return on that investment. Furthermore, with no legal require-  
ment for adequate training, infrastructure or safety systems, the indus-  
try had no incentive to change. The challenge was to produce a low-cost,  
easy-to-implement methodology that operators could use with minimum  
training. It needed to be useful, informative and practical. In other words,  
crowd and event models—not computer-generated crowd simulations. Crowd science 107  
The task was defining itself\: to take the tacit \(experiential\) information  
from the crowd managers and turn it into an easy-to-understand model.  
This process is called ‘codification’; turning knowledge and experience into  
guidance, learning and the codes of best practice. Just as the computer pro-  
grammers struggled to understand the behaviour and dynamics of crowds,  
the crowd managers could not express their tacit knowledge \(experience\)  
into computer code. During the workshops, we heard many stories of  
similar incidents of ‘almost a disaster’ and we collated these in the search  
for more appropriate modelling techniques for crowd risk analysis and to  
develop better models of crowds and events.  
Fortunately, there was a wealth of existing techniques that could be  
mapped into the event industry. For example, matrix analysis \(a technique  
of evaluating two independent sets of variables\), flow diagrams to evalu-  
ate conflicted spaces, risk mapping and the dynamics of how and where  
spaces are used\: these all exist in the broad science of operations research  
\(the study of optimising certain manufacturing processes and engineering  
systems\). We could model crowds and events. Modelling is a very differ-  
ent process to simulation; simulations are based on models, but could we  
model crowds and events without needing to simulate them? Chapter 5  
# Crowd and event modelling  
Truth is ever to be found in simplicity, and not in the multiplicity and  
confusion of things. As the world, which to the naked eye exhibits the  
greatest variety of objects, appears very simple in its internal constitu-  
tion when surveyed by a philosophical understanding, and so much the  
simpler by how much the better it is understood.  
Isaac Newton  
Divide each difficulty into as many parts as is feasible and necessary  
to resolve it.  
Descartes  
INTRODUCTION  
A model is the simple representation of a complex process. Models contain  
the key elements of a system under study, and most good models begin with  
the modeller breaking down a complex process into a series of parts. These  
parts should be focussed on one or two issues and not be overly complex.  
Models should be simple to construct, simple to visualise, simple to manip-  
ulate and they should communicate the key elements of the model to both  
the modeller and other interested parties. In the context of crowd science,  
the models we develop are aimed towards understanding crowd risks; they  
should assist in identifying those risks with the aim of eliminating, reduc-  
ing, mitigating or managing them.  
In this chapter, we will cover the principles Fruin outlined for major  
design projects, and then we will look at specific tools we have developed,  
dedicated to crowd risk analysis and safety engineering for typical event  
planning and crowd management projects. For all of these projects, it may  
be necessary to define a crowd management plan. That process we define  
within the overall scope of crowd risk analysis and safety engineering.  
109 110 Introduction to crowd science  
THE CROWD MANAGEMENT PLAN  
You cannot develop a safe, robust crowd management plan without some  
form of crowd modelling \(i.e. some idea of how the crowd will behave at  
your event/venue\). Equally, you cannot keep a crowd safe without some  
form of crowd monitoring \(of arrival flow rates, how the queues build  
up, areas of high crowd density and different types of crowd behaviour,  
both normal and emergency\). Fruin defined the process in a flow chart  
\(in Pedestrian Planning and Design, 1971\) and it has proven to be a  
robust model for the process of systematically reviewing complex major  
projects.  
Major project analysis  
The key to good model building is in using a range of modelling tech-  
niques—and understanding the limitations of each specific technique. As  
previously outlined, unless you have expertise in crowd simulations, cau-  
tion is advised—especially if you believe one tool, one software system,  
one methodology or one technique can be used to solve all aspects of large  
complex events. If you build models for a major event \(such as an Olympics  
event\), you need to be aware of your simulation’s limits.  
The good, bad and crazy  
We have seen many bad modelling tools, built on poor or bad assumptions.  
We have seen some tools that produce dangerous and misleading outputs,  
which have subsequently been used to justify substandard event plans. We  
have also presented so many, inadequate crowd management plans and  
neglected event risk assessments that we have developed and run a course  
for the UK Police and Institute of Licensing \(IoL\), focussed on major events,  
subtitled ‘How to spot a cr\*p event plan’ and are never short of materials  
for that course.  
During the initial project definition for the Jamarat Bridge, we were pre-  
sented with over 5000 submissions, project ideas, concepts and proposals.  
We found it very simple to divide those into three piles, the good, the bad  
and the crazy.  
Modelling complex events  
We shall first explore a major project using the Fruin flow chart, and out-  
line the process involved for modelling large events. Then we will refine  
the process for smaller events. By large we mean events in the hundreds of  
thousands, by smaller we mean events in the tens, hundreds or a few thou-  
sand people attending. Crowd and event modelling 111  
First, you cannot hope to capture all the aspects of a complex dynamic  
environment and all the potential human factors are involved throughout  
the event. Any consultant/programmer who claims otherwise is deluded, or  
lying. Common sense tells you that any attempt to model the full scope of  
an event, and all its potential aspects and outcomes, would involve first cap-  
turing all the elements that may have an influence on that event. This would  
include all the possible human factors. We have highlighted the nature of  
this problem as intractable \(too many variables, too many unknowns and  
too many possible outcomes\). We stress that complete event modelling, in a  
single framework, is both flawed and analytically intractable.  
To illustrate this specific point, imagine this scenario\: it is the day of the  
event; it is sunny and warm. An individual wakes up after a good night’s  
sleep, gets on a train and makes their way to the event. That individual  
may not be behaving, moving, thinking, reacting in the same way if it were  
raining/if there were transport delays/if they had had a bad night’s sleep.  
Many factors may influence human behaviour. We are all different and our  
moods and behaviour are irrevocably linked to our environment and our  
experiences. The question is how many of these human factors will affect  
the overall crowd behaviour at an event? How many of these elements do  
we need to know of when modelling and planning for a major event?  
We outlined these problems in Chapter 4, but we stress that individual  
frustration, aggression and mood in general are potentially influenced by  
external factors, and hence so is crowd behaviour.  
Who goes first?  
We have noted that the order of people in the system may be significant \(the  
little old lady factor outlined in the previous chapter\). For example, three  
people in a row who have all had a very bad morning. How could this order  
affect overall crowd behaviour if there is a sudden change in the environ-  
ment—say, a person suddenly stopping, tripping or falling? There are so  
many unknown factors that the problem of testing all of these, understand-  
ing whether any single factor could influence the overall crowd behaviour,  
is by definition, intractable.  
Some of these factors may be irrelevant; some may combine to produce a  
small group with raised anxiety or aggression that can influence the crowd’s  
behaviour. Some may have a wide area impact \(such as a delay on entry or  
a change in weather\). When behavioural simulations are used to explore  
these relationships, we can begin to understand the complexity of behav-  
ioural influences, but we may never be able to measure these influences. We  
may never be able to anticipate the impact of certain influences, and hence  
we may never be able to predict or mitigate their effects on the crowd. Even  
if we could, somehow, measure the influences, it would be a costly exercise  
and, as explained in Chapter 1, crowd safety is a low-cost industry. 112 Introduction to crowd science  
However, influencing crowd behaviour can help to mitigate many poten-  
tial risks. For example, designing free-flowing entry and exit systems will  
reduce the potential for frustration, and hence crushing, regardless of indi-  
vidual moods.  
MODELLING FOR A MAJOR PROJECT  
Modelling individuals, with the aim of predicting their behaviour, is fraught  
with complexity and should be treated with caution. Tools that allow the  
exploration of human factors may serve useful purposes in appropriate cir-  
cumstances, but these are expert tools and do not fit the majority of event  
safety requirements.  
A more practicable and sensible approach is to analyse the components  
that create an event, such as the entry system, exit system, front of stage  
and the like, treating each element with an appropriate modelling tool, and  
then examining the whole system. This is opposed to trying to model the  
whole system using one tool.  
The choice of tools and techniques is as important as understanding and  
defining the event. Figure 5.1 is by Fruin.\* To outline its application we will  
describe each heading, and then outline how modelling tools fit within the  
context of event planning and the development of a crowd management  
plan.  
Define the objectives  
Clearly, every event should begin with a set of objectives—perhaps a desired  
result, a particular performer, raising awareness of a cause and so on. These  
objectives may include the scope, nature and ultimate aims of the event.  
We then need to understand the event site, its capacity, access and egress  
routes, the transportation infrastructure and the site constraints under dif-  
ferent weather conditions. There are a number of key questions related to  
this initial analysis, such as\:  
•  
•  
•  
•  
•  
•  
Why are the crowd coming to this event?  
How will they get there?  
How quickly can we process people into the event?  
How quickly can they leave \(normal and emergency considerations\)?  
Does the performer have a large following?  
Does the performer/celebrity use social media to inform his/her fans  
of a free event \(such as a book signing\)?  
\*

Environmental Planners. 1971. Library of Congress catalogue number 70-159312. Crowd and event modelling 113

Figure 5.1  Fruin—flow chart of the process for evaluating a pedestrian planning project.  
These are all important, and often neglected, questions relating to choice  
of performer or celebrity at free events. The event/site capacity is only one  
aspect of site suitability. The fundamental questions should be\: ‘Is this site  
suitable for this event?’ and ‘Is there sufficient ingress/egress and site capac-  
ity for this crowd?’  
When we work on major projects, all of the stages described below  
would be considered. From a consulting, modelling and potential client  
perspective, this flow chart, developed by Fruin, is a useful guide for crowd  
modelling.  
Data collection  
During the initial stages of a project, we would typically collate a wide  
range of photographs, CAD plans and information about the site. We would  
interview the organisers and other agencies \(police, security, architect, etc.\)  
to understand the key components of the event. If it is a major event we may 114 Introduction to crowd science  
well run a site workshop with a multiagency team, gathering a wide range  
of scenarios in order to build as much information as possible. On any proj-  
ect, it is important to devote time to gathering the appropriate information;  
again, the experience of what can be modelled and what techniques should  
be used on a specific project is invaluable. By spending time on data collec-  
tion and collation, you can assess which tools/techniques are most suitable  
for that specific project. There is no point in using a sledgehammer to crack  
a walnut; use the tools as appropriate and always, wherever possible, walk  
the site to gather local information that may not be available on maps,  
plans or diagrams.  
Analysis  
Analysis of the data is typically performed using a wide range of  
resources. This may be a review of the transport data, the capacity and  
frequency of trains. Head counting on any existing videos of the event  
can help assess crowd flow rate. Images/photographs and density analy-  
sis can help assess potential risks. Distribution analyses \(which routes  
do the crowds take to get to the event?\) are also helpful. There are many  
other aspects of crowd management and crowd control that may need  
to be considered for major events\: data analysis is often 80% of a major  
project—in other words, it can take 80% of your time to collect, col-  
late and analyse the relevant data. Developing crowd management plans,  
event plans and site plans takes time—but the objective is to assess the  
issues around crowd safety, and to do that you need to collect and collate  
as much data as possible.  
Contingency plans/simulations  
A simulation may help to assess congestion at an event with multiple  
entry/exit points. We would use a range of potential tools—some we have  
designed ourselves for specific risk analysis, others that are commercially  
available \(CAD, area and distance measuring tools\). It is important to note  
that running one type of simulation may not be enough to study many  
types of high-density pedestrian environments. Often we may need to test  
the extremes \(pedestrians not moving/all pedestrians moving\) to discover  
the ‘solution space’ of a problem. This is a reality check, and it provides us  
with a benchmark for further modelling techniques. One such technique is  
called ‘extreme value analysis’—where the worst possible cases may need  
to be explored.  
By considering the extremes, we can represent the conditions in which  
the risks of crowd injury may be anticipated. They can then be assessed,  
and remedial actions applied to mitigate them. These extremes may rep-  
resent two different types of management strategy \(low-density risks and Crowd and event modelling 115  
high-density risks\); it is important to formulate a contingency plan for both  
extremes. Models can assist us in both understanding a specific scenario \(as  
it develops\) and planning the appropriate crowd management strategy, pro-  
cess and procedures. The use of simulations in this context is to understand  
modes of failure. How could the risks develop, in which areas, over what  
period, and how long may a hazardous condition last? We must stress the  
difference between this process testing a system to understand how it may  
fail, and one that confirms a site is OK for use.  
By testing the extreme conditions, for example, no-one moving, crowds  
building up, rates of flow, everyone moving, trips/falls and so forth, we are  
reasonably confident of having contingency planned for the worst-case sce-  
narios. It is important to always test the plan and strategy for 200–1000%  
more people arriving over a shorter period than expected. This will test the  
robustness of the plan and the management process in case of larger crowds.  
Presentations/approval  
It is important to note that any issues relating to crowd safety may involve a  
range of agencies \(police, civil defence, architects, medical, waste manage-  
ment, security forces, etc.\) and that the presentation of plans/suggestions  
should be clearly understood by all parties. Given the language differences  
in the many countries in which we work, we have adopted a graphic pre-  
sentation\* technique to highlight potential crown problems, using line dia-  
grams, risk maps and decision support tools.  
Design phase  
Many elements of the design can change between plan and build. We are  
specifically concerned about the amount of information that is typically  
missing during the early stages of build—mostly involving the management  
and operations procedures. We are often presenting to the operations and  
management team during the build phase of projects. This allows us to act  
as a communications conduit for both the design and operations groups  
working on complex places of public assembly. For major events, such as  
planning for the Royal Wedding in 2011, a continual review process and  
risk analysis, were subject to weekly meetings and project review.  
Simulations/approval  
We test a wide range of issues relating to the design; often these tests  
are subject to approval by the operators, so we run workshops to facil-  
itate understanding of the various models and simulation results. It is  
\*  
We will illustrate these in Chapter 6. 116 Introduction to crowd science  
not enough to run a model and assume that all parties will understand  
it and be able to adopt a suitable operational procedure. We strongly  
advise that the process of modelling/simulation currently used should be  
checked independently, a model builder could be caught in their own little  
world and fail to spot obvious errors. Writing an operational/procedural  
manual must be a continual process during the construction phases of  
large complex spaces.  
Operations manual  
If a simulation is necessary, the process of creating and running a simula-  
tion allows a skilled operator to assess a wide range of contingency con-  
ditions. It is advised that modelling and operational procedures run as a  
joint project. The development of an integrated management response team  
improves crowd safety and is a common feature of large-scale events \(such  
as the Olympics\) worldwide. These involve multiagency teams \(police,  
medical, civil defence, waste management, security forces, etc.\). We have  
extensive experience of running and facilitating workshops of this type.  
The process involves educational materials, examples of similar sites,  
near misses and data capture. By coupling teaching/training within the  
consulting project, we can achieve a number of improvements. It is essential  
that the operation teams are integrated into the process, not left to ‘man-  
age’ the situation after the consultant has left the building.  
This was the key concern we highlighted in Chapter 1, that the opera-  
tional teams, the crowd managers, were not involved in the planning stages.  
Continuous revision process  
It is important to maintain a continuous process of modelling, simula-  
tion and writing/testing operational procedures to ensure the operational  
efficiency and crowd safety during the build phase of large complex envi-  
ronments. We have outlined the basis of our data analysis and initial con-  
clusions; one additional element is to evaluate the potential for an incident  
from a timeline analysis of previous incidents around the world. Learning  
from other people’s mistakes is a good way to prevent these from occurring  
at your own events.  
Fruin’s flow chart is typically used for large-event consultancy projects\:  
collecting information, processing it, testing a range of scenarios and then  
developing an event plan, a safety concept and a crowd management plan.  
By illustrating the stages we would adopt for major projects, you can adopt  
all, or part, of this process as required. Most events do not have all of the  
complexity of a major event so a simpler process can be applied. You can  
apply the elements defined in the Fruin’s flow chart to any scale of event, and  
it serves as a checklist for the entire event planning process. The objective, as Crowd and event modelling 117  
we continue to stress, is to understand the crowds risks in the specific event  
under scrutiny.  
The Fruin flow chart is a robust model and, as a checklist for a client  
and a consultant, it outlines the basic principles of the planning around  
the crowd. It applies to events in general terms, but the event has some  
unique characteristics which we can define in the context of building a  
risk assessment.  
WHAT IS AN EVENT MODEL?  
An event is a place where people gather to participate in and/or enjoy a per-  
formance. The crowd component of an event is simple to express\: the crowd  
enters a space, venue or arena \(ingress\), then it typically moves around that  
space \(circulation\) and finally it leaves that space \(egress\). Every event con-  
sists of these three phases, albeit for very different durations.  
As the crowd moves towards the event or venue, there are sets of expecta-  
tions that influence its behaviour \(about how to enter the venue, for exam-  
ple\). Those expectations change once inside the event/venue \(as members of  
the crowd try to find, for instance, their seat or the best position, or locate  
the toilets/bar/merchandising\). Finally, as the crowd leaves, another set of  
expectations is experienced\: ‘How do I get out of here?’ So in broad terms,  
for the purposes of defining a standard model or template, we have three  
primary phases \(expectations\) of crowd behaviour\: ingress—circulation—  
egress \(ICE\).  
From research into past accidents and incidents, we can determine causal-  
ity of major incidents as having three primary influences. Design can influ-  
ence crowd behaviour, for example, if the number of people trying to enter  
an event space over a period exceeds the capacity of that system. In this  
way queuing, pressure and potentially crushing can develop on an entry  
system. Design is therefore a primary influence on crowd safety. The second  
influence on crowd behaviour can be defined as information. Elements such  
as signage, public address \(PA\) systems and media \(websites, Twitter feeds,  
news items, press releases, maps, plans, diagrams, rumours, weather changes  
and so on\) can influence crowd behaviour. The third influence is the man-  
agement system; how the event is managed, how it operates on the day, the  
process and procedures, the monitoring and recording of crowd movements,  
risk and safety measures. So we can map these as the design, information  
and management aspects \(DIM\) that can influence crowd behaviour.  
The template of the primary phases of crowd behaviour \(ingress, cir-  
culation, egress\) and the primary influences on crowd behaviour \(design,  
information and management\) lends itself to the acronym DIM-ICE, which  
sounds like ‘demise’, and is hence a subtle reminder of the consequences  
of failing to understand crowd risk and crowd safety! For our model, we 118 Introduction to crowd science  
also need to consider two primary modes of crowd behaviour, for both  
normal and emergency situations. There are potentially many different  
types of emergency, and how the crowd might react to each will need to  
be understood. This is an important aspect of the modelling process; how-  
ever, despite the many different types of potential threats there are only a  
few types of reaction to those threats—to move away from the threat or  
move towards it \(to assist others\), or to stay put and do nothing. We will  
address these elements when we outline the modelling approach to emer-  
gency behaviour and crowd reactions.\*  
Event characteristics  
The need for a standardised approach to event modelling developed out  
of the common misconception that all events are unique. There are, of  
course, differences from event to event, but there are also many similari-  
ties. By focussing on these similarities, we have developed a range of tools  
that provides the ability to both capture and share best practices with all  
parties involved in event organisation. More specifically, we can capture  
information that relates to near misses, event risks and the crowd dynamic.  
We use this to collate best practices from around the world. We reviewed  
the process we had developed to produce simulation; it had all the key  
components necessary to address the modelling problem. We use these  
tools to collect and collate information, to model the crowd risks at events.  
DIM-ICE meta-modelling  
The first of these tools is a meta-model; a template for events that focusses  
the user’s attention on the time-related element of crowd risks. It is a uni-  
versal template for crowds attending any event, anywhere in the world.  
Although that claim may sound grandiose, this is nothing more than a struc-  
ture—an empty template that assists the user in collecting and displaying  
information in such a way that the details highlight the unique features \(or  
similarities\) of the event and the site characteristics. We originally designed  
this to capture information for major consulting projects, specifically dur-  
ing the work in Saudi Arabia where language and cultural differences made  
documents difficult to read and fully understand. By structuring an event  
into times \(ICE\) and taking the causes of major incidents from the database  
records, we can develop the template.  
This template is used for different types of events as a planning tool for  
the organisers and as a checklist for safety inspectors and event licensing.  
\*  
There are only four options\: total evacuation, directed evacuation, phased \(partial\) evacu-  
ation or stay-put \(shelter in place\). We cover this in more detail in Chapter 8. Crowd and event modelling 119  
It allows the user to compare and contrast different performers at the same  
venue, or different venues with the same performer. It allows users to cap-  
ture and structure information and facilitates a consistent approach to  
crowd risk analysis and crowd safety applications.  
Phases and influences  
The template relates the phases of an event and the influences of the envi-  
ronment on crowd behaviour. This provides a framework for evaluating  
how crowds may react under normal and emergency situations. It is a  
matrix analysis that, when populated, defines the crowd issues for a specific  
site or event, and the risk associated with each phase \(ICE\).  
Figure 5.2 shows the DIM-iCE template for events, this is a blank tem-  
plate which can be on a word process or spreadsheet, and begins with a  
blank space to capture the event characteristics. For example, how does  
ingress-design, function? Does the system use barriers, gates or turnstiles?  
What is the throughput?  
Typically, we would populate this template with a brief description and/  
or value \(people per hour\) to provide a reference table for the event infor-  
mation. Then we would colour that box red, orange or green to highlight  
any risks associated with that specific element of the template \(Examples of  
this, in colour, are shown on this webpage\: http\://www.gkstill.com/Support/  
WhyModel/dimice.html\).  
Why develop a matrix approach?  
The original reason for collating data in this way was to capture informa-  
tion that may be specific to an event or site in a methodical and systematic  
Figure 5.2  The DIM-ICE meta-model template. The blank template allows the user to  
systematically work through the crowd behaviour phases and influences of  
an event. 120 Introduction to crowd science  
manner. We defined, from disaster database analysis, the primary risks  
noted from past disasters, the influences on crowd behaviour—namely the  
design influences \(capacity, area, building, barriers, etc.\)—the informa-  
tion influences \(media, PA systems, etc.\) and the management influences.  
The reason for this is to focus our attention \(and that of anyone using this  
information\) on the areas that have been identified as the distal causes in  
crowd-related accidents and incidents. Colouring the specific grid/box red,  
if that particular element for that event had some specific risk, ensured we  
highlighted the risk in the most visual manner possible. This helps identify  
the phase of the crowd behaviour and the potential influence on crowd  
behaviour, highlighting the risks to the crowd.  
Matrix analysis  
Using a matrix such as the DIM-ICE meta-model, we can apply analytical  
techniques for tactical and strategic responses to crowd-related issues. That  
means we lay the information out in a tabular format \(a 3  
×  
3 grid\). Values  
such as cost, risk, flow rates and so forth can be assigned to each grid and this  
can provide a useful insight into the best use and deployment of resources.  
The matrix approach not only focusses attention on key elements of  
time-related risks—it also highlights what most crowd management plans  
fail to address\: that incidents can occur during ingress, mid-event and dur-  
ing egress. Typically, an event plan will only focus on emergencies during  
an event, yet the disaster database highlights that the majority of crowd  
accidents occur either during ingress or egress.  
It is important to stress that these are neither complicated nor time-consuming  
techniques; we have developed and taught this approach over  
many years, and found that the adage ‘keep it simple’ is not only a truism  
but also a necessity.  
Flow path analysis  
Another important concept for event modelling, which we introduced in  
the PhD thesis and later developed into a data capture and analysis tech-  
nique for major events, is that of crowd flow paths or routes. We outlined  
this as part of the computer code for the original crowd simulation system  
using the following definitions\:  
Least effort\: It is defined as the ‘easiest’ path that individuals take as they  
progress through their environment. This can be reduced to two simple  
algorithmic rules\:  
1\. Individuals will take the shortest available route to get from source  
to destination. Where the ‘shortest’ is defined by shortest time \(which  
may not be the shortest distance\), an individual may walk around a Crowd and event modelling 121  
high-density crowd to reach their objective in less time than it takes to  
walk through the high-density crowd.  
2\. Individuals try to move at their normal speed \(if they can\), but will  
reduce their walking speed if the conditions dictate \(e.g. stairs, slopes,  
rough ground, congestion, navigating obstacles, etc.\). We illustrated  
this principle in Chapter 2 with the first string experiment.  
Focal routes\: A focal route is the shortest path \(i.e. that requiring the  
least effort\) an individual can take to reach their destination.  
Multiple path interference\: This occurs when two or more focal routes  
cross, where the crowds would converge, cross flow or counter flow. The  
multiple path interference principle developed into a methodology that  
we use to define areas that may have problems in high-density crowds. It  
became a core teaching principle for risk analysis in the complex and built  
spaces of major events.  
List of modelling techniques  
As outlined in previous chapters, we have a range of modelling techniques  
for crowd risk analysis and safety engineering. We generally use all of these  
techniques for both capturing and communicating event risks. Typically,  
we would work through a major event using the tools in this order\:  
1\. RAMP analysis  
2\. DIM-ICE meta-model  
3\. Matrix analysis techniques  
We will now provide a brief description of each of these techniques;  
then we will outline the detail and rationale behind this approach, and  
in Chapter 6, we will demonstrate how the techniques can be—and have  
been—applied to major events. For all of the aforementioned techniques,  
which are qualitative \(using description and diagrams\) rather than quanti-  
tative \(numerical\), we use the following colour coding\:  
•  
•  
•  
Red to indicate any areas where the crowd density may be five people  
per square metre or more. Red is the high-risk indicator.  
Orange to indicate any areas where the crowd is three to four people  
per square metre. Orange indicates medium risks to the crowds.  
Green to indicate any areas where the crowd density is two people per  
square metre or less. Green indicates low risks to the crowds.  
By using these three colours on a map, plan or diagram of the site, show-  
ing crowd movement and crowd risk, we keep the information simple.  
Maps can be developed for different times during the event, thus showing  
the dynamics of risk; for example, during ingress the crowd risks may be 122 Introduction to crowd science  
high at the entry points, but once the crowd is inside the event these risks  
no longer apply. Showing different risk maps for different times enables us  
to project the time, location and \(using colour\) severity of risks. This shows  
risk as a dynamic. It is a low-cost approach to event modelling and has  
proven to be both useful and easy to understand.  
RAMP ANALYSIS  
RAMP is an acronym for the Routes, Areas, Movement and Profile of the  
crowd at an event. Routes are the directions the crowds take to get to the  
site, move around the site and leave the site \(under both normal and emer-  
gency situations\), and are drawn on maps, plans or site diagrams to assist  
the visualisation of the crowds’ movements.  
Areas are the locations of crowd density \(low, medium and high\) and  
risks on the site relating to those locations. It also refers to the capacity of  
each section of the event \(such as the ingress and queuing areas\). Areas need  
to have both a numerical value \(square metres\) and a capacity \(number of  
people\), and we recommend a zone designation \(A, B, C, etc.\) for identifica-  
tion purposes during an event or an incident.  
Movement is the rate of passage through the various parts of the system,  
such as the rate at which crowds are processed through a ticket or search  
area. We represent movement using both line diagrams and a numerical  
value. We normally express movement according to how many people are  
expected and over what period. An example of this would be an arrival  
profile for a concert, fireworks display, football match and the like.  
Finally, profile is the known behaviour of the crowd—their age/demo-  
graphic, mix \(male/female ratio\), family group and others, and any intel-  
ligence that may assist in the management of the crowd.  
ROUTES  
This is the process in which the routes towards, around and leaving a  
site are defined as a series of overlays on a site map/plan or diagram. We  
use simple drawing techniques to facilitate understanding, and to docu-  
ment and plan normal and emergency resources. The technique is called  
‘BATNEEC’ \(best available technology, not entailing excessive cost\), and  
is the same as the first process we used when building computer models.  
It is essential for any computer system to input correct and relevant infor-  
mation. The principle of ‘garbage in  
=  
garbage out’ expresses the problem  
succinctly; if the quality of the information you put into a computer system  
is poor, the output will be poor. For risk and safety-critical systems this is,  
of course, an unacceptable situation. Crowd and event modelling 123  
To ensure we could input accurate and useful information, we had  
to develop a systematic approach to the input of that information. We  
developed a process of mapping the paths and areas of an event and  
converting them into computer code so that the simulation could func-  
tion. This meant we had to define the routes, areas, zones and options  
for the crowd, convert that into an algorithm \(a numerical recipe\) so the  
computer programme could use that information and run a simulation.  
We also had to develop systematic methods for converting information  
about all phases of the event \(ICE\). We did this by mapping site areas  
and routes, and building these maps up in layers, which helped us to  
understand the crowd issues, the pinch points, the movement over time  
and the dimensions of risk.  
We realised that the process had more value as an analytical tool than  
the computer programme, since the programme was only emulating the  
information we had input \(the routing maps, the areas and options\). We  
found, with a systematic approach to creating the necessary input for the  
computer programme, that we did not need to run the simulation in order  
to understand the risks. We could see the risks in our analysis and mapping  
process—in the layers of information, we developed for input. In addition,  
we could see any potential site problems in the process of turning that into  
an algorithm. We also realised we could teach this process, at low cost, to  
crowd managers; it has proven to be a very useful analytical process for  
assessing risk in large and complex events. We will describe the key ele-  
ments of this analytical approach to capturing key crowd and event infor-  
mation, and we will illustrate it with some examples. The process starts  
with a site plan, which must be large enough to include the points from  
which people may travel to reach the event.  
Ingress, circulation, egress  
First, we map the event ingress routes. An event will involve a series of  
routes \(directions of approach\), which may be from car park, transport  
terminal, local pubs/clubs, town centres or other population centres and  
others. We draw the routes on a sheet of baking paper laid over a site plan,  
using different colours to represent the concentration of crowds on the dif-  
ferent approach routes. An example is shown in Figure 5.3.  
Creating layers of information  
Then we take another sheet of baking paper\* and draw out the crowd  
movements, during ingress. Typically, we use red to indicate high volume  
\*  
We use the clear kind of baking paper—it costs less than the draftsman’s tracing sheet,  
comes in 10-m rolls and is available at the local high street supermarket. 124 Introduction to crowd science  
Figure 5.3  Illustration of the distribution of crowd flow to an event, in this example,  
there is an 80–20% distribution. This helps the user plan resources and check  
during the event if the crowds are arriving as planned.  
of crowd flow \(80% or more of the population\), orange for medium crowd  
flow \(50%–80% of the crowd flow\) and green to indicate low crowd flow  
\(less than 50% of the population\). This helps define where the crowd man-  
agement resources may need to be assigned. Then we use another sheet of  
baking paper to draw out the routes for the circulation phase and then the  
egress phase of the event. Do not make the mistake of trying to put all the  
information on a single layer; you will end up in a mess.  
The materials are low cost for a reason\: if you make a mistake, you can  
throw a layer away and start again. Each layer should feature no more than  
the basic diagram, a title, your name and the date \(which helps in filing for  
later review\). In the example shown in Figure 5.3, the 80% line would be  
indicated in red and the 20% line indicated as green. We use the same colour  
convention, red, orange and green, for risk, flow and capacity. If a route or  
area is at less than 50% of its capacity, we indicate this in green; between  
50% and 80% is indicated in orange; and greater than 80% we indicate in  
red. This provides a visual indicator of how busy that route or area may be  
at that particular instant or location. Crowd and event modelling 125  
Adding layers  
The next layer\* maps out the crowd movement in an emergency during  
ingress. Again these are drawn as routes on the baking sheet placed over  
the site map \(it helps to mark the site plan corners so you can line your dia-  
grams up with the site map later\). The ‘normal’ ingress map and the ‘during  
emergency’ ingress map help us to assess the risks and formulate a strategy  
for crowd evacuation during the ingress process.  
For example, an emergency along the 80% route could give us some dif-  
ficulty, if we stop the crowds along that route, it could quickly build up into  
a high-density crowd. The people approaching may not know of the situa-  
tion ahead; as it is moving along a road with no means of diverting, there  
is potential for the crowd to reach crush-­ density. Hence, there is a potential  
risk in simply stopping this crowd. In the aforementioned example, one  
route has 80% of the approaching crowd, the other 20% of the approach-  
ing crowd. Each route has the same width so, with this diagram, we can see  
the potential problems if the crowd were to stop on either route. Clearly,  
the route with 80% of the crowd has less resilience and less space to handle  
an incident \(such as a medical emergency\). This can indicate that we need  
to provide different levels of management, monitoring and first respond-  
ers proportionately. The mapping process is a visual indicator \(the map,  
the road, the side roads, the 80% distribution, the length of the route, the  
direction, etc.\) of the anticipated situation. In one diagram, we represent  
many different types of information. This is an important aspect of crowd  
risk analysis as visualising risk helps identify duration, severity and loca-  
tion of those risks.  
As we previously stated, the process of drawing a line on a sheet of bak-  
ing paper \(a layer\) placed over a map can provide several valuable insights  
into the problems and solutions for a specific phase of an event. It is essen-  
tial to keep it simple\: ingress \(normal\) and then ingress \(emergency\), each  
on a separate layer. Putting too much on any one layer will obscure the  
information and may conceal some vital details on the map. By using bak-  
ing sheets, you can lift the layers and review the underlying map; if you  
were to draw on the map, you might cover over some of its detail.  
Inside the event  
For the circulation phase of the event, simply repeat the process with a new  
sheet of baking paper—this mapping the crowd movements inside the event  
space. For example, how will the crowd move from the entry points to other  
points around the site? These line diagrams, coupled with the colour-coded  
\*  
Use a new piece of baking sheet for each layer. 126 Introduction to crowd science  
indications of crowd concentration \(or distribution\), again provide valuable  
insights into the nature of the event and space utilisation.  
This type of exercise can be conducted as a group discussion or by indi-  
viduals; on many occasions, we have noted that delegates have seen risks  
when mapping crowd movements around the site—specifically the facilities  
planning, routes between stage and exits, toilets and bars—that they were  
previously unaware of.  
In general, if a map has many routes crossing, converging or going in  
opposite directions \(counter flows\) we can see that, at high density, those  
areas would have a higher risk. We can then take another sheet of baking  
paper and draw the emergency evacuation routes—again highlighting in  
red/orange/green those areas that may become higher density during an  
emergency egress, mid-event.  
Mapping egress  
Next, the egress maps need to be drawn \(these are the normal routes the  
crowd would take to leave the site\) and then we can consider an emergency  
during egress. For example, the crowd starts to leave and there is a problem  
in an exit corridor—say, a person falls over and we need to get to them  
quickly. By mapping the crowd flow, we can see that the crowd may hinder  
a quick response.  
By systematically mapping ingress, circulation and egress under both  
normal and emergency situations, and using low-cost materials and a little  
bit of time to think through the site, you will focus your attention on the  
time, location, severity and duration of crowd-related risks.  
This takes risk analysis from a single number \(as typically indicated in  
the conventional risk assessment\) into a visual map, and highlights the  
dynamic nature of crowd risks. The six maps \(ingress—normal and emer-  
gency; circulation—normal and emergency; and egress—normal and emer-  
gency\) should be included in the event plan so that the authorities that  
license/approve or grant permits for the event can understand the crowds’  
movements.  
We have seen hundreds—if not thousands—of event plans, but very few  
have contained these kinds of diagrams. It seems such an obvious and useful  
addition; we really do not understand why it is not a required element for  
an event—especially a major event. Figure 5.4 shows one of the circulation  
maps, we produced many of these, for each critical time period and each  
day of the olympics, featured in the work we did for crowd management  
and contingency planning workshops for the Sydney Olympics \(2000\).  
Once the delegates started using this process they found it useful and  
insightful, and continued to apply the technique for their event planning.  
We hope that once you have read this, you will apply the same methodology  
to your event plans and gain similar benefits. Crowd and event modelling 127  
Figure 5.4  Author’s original model of the common domain of the Sydney Olympic Park  
on tracing sheet.  
AREAS  
The route mapping process establishes the potential directions of crowd  
movement. These routes take the crowds into certain areas; we now need to  
map those areas, again using the baking sheet overlay process.  
To do this we need to calculate the area of the site, and there is a wide  
range of tools available for this process, including several good \(free\) map-  
ping and measuring tools on computers.  
Using the internet  
As a first-pass approximation,\* you can use an Internet satellite image or  
mapping tool for area calculations, such as Google Earth, MapInfo or  
Ordnance Survey† and the like. We typically use the computer to generate  
a map to an appropriate scale. There are also wide ranges of map measur-  
ing tools on the Internet, many are free to use and run on tablets,‡ PCs  
and Macs. We use those tools to estimate the site areas and for informa-  
tion gathering \(such as location of transportation hubs\). We have produced  
our own software to obtain all the critical dimensions from a variety of  
maps, plans and diagrams and perform the basic site calculations, such as  
\*  
A first-pass approximation helps define the order of magnitude of a solution. This is some-  
times called a ‘rough cut’ analysis.  
† You will have to check each source for appropriate copyright usage and/or licensing terms  
and conditions.  
‡ A tablet can be a very useful device to view a site from the sky and ground during a site  
inspection. 128 Introduction to crowd science  
area, perimeter, entry route width, flow rates, fill times, site capacity  
and so forth. But these software tools are not simulations, merely map-  
ping and visualing tools, which provides the user with useful crowd flow  
information.  
We would then print the relevant site/event map and use the baking sheet  
to draw the routes and areas, using red/orange/green colours to highlight  
capacity and risk on different layers.  
Be aware that the scale of some mapping tools and site maps may not be  
accurate.\* This process is qualitative in nature and the objective is to assess  
risk as a first-pass approximation. Do not make the mistake of trying to do  
this on a computer. It is a pen and paper exercise to facilitate learning about  
the nature and dynamics of event risks.  
Dividing the area into zones  
We now need to subdivide the event area into sections that are static \(where  
the crowd is usually not moving\) and dynamic \(where the crowd is usually  
moving\). For example, static areas would be front-of-stage at a concert,  
queuing spaces, bars and so forth. Dynamic areas would be circulating cor-  
ridors, entry points and the like. This produces a map/overlay of the event  
during ingress, circulation \(mid-event\) and egress for normal and emer-  
gency situations, with the circulation areas shown as two people per square  
metre and the static areas as four or five people per square metre. This can  
help inform the management, the CCTV operators and the stewards of  
what to expect; it also acts as an early warning when we see higher than  
expected density in unanticipated locations.  
By mapping different routes and areas \(green, orange and red\), we iden-  
tify risk \(low, medium and high\) for the crowd dynamic. For example, sec-  
tions where a red route \(high flow rate\) meets a red area \(high density\)  
can be identified as potentially high risk. Again, the area mapping process  
should include both normal and emergency conditions during ingress, cir-  
culation and egress.  
The use of baking sheet allows us to put an ingress routing map over an  
ingress area/risk map and assess how the crowd may flow into that area, at  
that time, under normal or emergency situations. We can overlay normal  
and emergency maps; area maps and risk maps to provide a comprehensive  
visual analysis of crowd movements, over time, into key areas. This is a  
qualitative risk analysis that is simple to implement, and it provides valu-  
able insights. The process of drawing the maps also provides a much clearer  
understanding of the event dynamics. Do this for each relevant time period.  
\*  
A photocopied map can be rescaled, and driving type road maps will show routes to be  
much wider than they are in reality. Crowd and event modelling 129  
Say each hour, and you will build up a much clearer idea about how risks  
during ingress, circulation and egress can develop.  
You can also apply the mapping/layering process at any scale \(small sec-  
tions or whole sites\); you will learn more about your event by mapping the  
crowd movements by assigning capacity to each area, and by mapping risk  
using baking sheets, than you will by trying to describe a risk in words.  
Not all the space is used  
At this stage in the process, you will notice that the entire site is seldom  
fully utilised. There may be pockets of ‘unused’ space. This is an important  
point in site design; you should consider only the static and dynamic spaces  
for site capacity. Again, the mapping process, if done by hand, provides  
a valuable insight about use and utilisation of an event space. You will  
see where crowds move, where they do not move, where congestion and  
queuing can occur, and where the moving \(dynamic\) areas may overlap the  
queuing \(static\) areas and lead to congestion.  
We do not have time for this!  
A criticism of this approach to risk analysis was made during a webinar\:  
‘We are all too busy to do this kind of analysis’. We found that the map-  
ping process is actually quicker to perform than a conventional risk assess-  
ment; it is qualitative—it tells us about the quality of the space and provides  
insights into the dynamic nature of risk. We always stress that the conven-  
tional risk assessment is an essential element of event planning that should  
not be neglected, but the diagrams/mapping provide more information in a  
much more accessible format.  
When we hear the comment ‘we don’t have time for this’, we remind the  
delegate of the consequences, if an accident or incident occurs, of using that  
statement as a defence in court. ‘We didn’t have time to do this’ would be  
viewed as both negligent and incompetent.  
‘Isn’t this easier on a computer?’  
More than one delegate have suggested that the mapping and layering pro-  
cess would be much easier on a computer. In answer to this, we point out  
that by drawing, you have a ‘feel’ for time, direction, duration, severity and  
areas. You have the ability to reorder the layers; by placing an emergency  
layer over an ingress routing map, you can see things that might not be pos-  
sible to see on a computer screen. You also have a much larger area to work  
with on paper than on a computer screen, and you are using many more of  
your senses in the process. 130 Introduction to crowd science  
Distraction-free  
To use a computer, you have to learn which buttons to press; you have to  
learn operating systems, function keys, mouse-clicks, program inputs …  
you can be distracted by email, Twitter and Facebook feeds, anti-virus  
checks and spam, spam, spam! These are all distractions that you simply  
do not experience when you are drawing a line on a piece of paper. So we  
recommend that you switch the computer off, get out a sheet of baking  
paper and some coloured pencils, and draw a route overlay on a site plan.  
Use the diagrams to understand the dynamics of crowd risks; use them  
to illustrate problems and event issues for coworkers and clients. It is a  
very simple, quick and effective means of communicating the complexity of  
crowd risks in and around a major event—one that helps focus your mind  
on the dynamics of risk. This is best achieved using pen and paper.  
Use a computer system for tasks such as map investigation, where you  
can zoom into an area and print the relevant section for the mapping pro-  
cess. Area calculations on the computer screen are useful, with the right  
software. Area mapping software can be a more accurate analysis tool—  
provided they do not distract too much from the fundamental aim of crowd  
risk analysis. We tend to do a hand calculation on a map first and then check  
the results on a computer, or vice versa. This double checks our results and  
the process and can prevent errors from being overlooked.  
Tidy up  
Once you have completed a set of routes map, areas and rough sketches,  
they can, of course, be enhanced using computer graphics. We suggest this  
should only be performed after the analysis and not as part of the analysis,  
for the aforementioned reasons.  
MOVEMENT  
Once you have drawn the routing maps and area maps, you can assess the  
crowd flow rates. Flow rates will tell you how much time it might take an  
area to fill, and how long it might take the crowds to flow into, around and  
away from a site. For this you need to assess the narrowest point along a  
route line \(using the route maps\); that point will be the limiting factor for  
crowd flow. The width at that point, multiplied by 82 people per metre  
per minute, will be the best case for crowd flow \(the optimal\). So, draw  
a route line, find the narrowest point along the route, measure the width  
\(say 10 m\), multiply by 82 \(people per metre per minute\) and you have a  
value \(in this case 820 people per minute\). That is the value for the 100%  
capacity flow rate. Eighty-two people per metre per minute is the best case, Crowd and event modelling 131  
the optimal flow rate, under ideal conditions, on level ground and without  
backpacks, luggage, children, little old ladies, picnics, umbrellas, etc. We  
always recommend that you use this value with suitable consideration to  
the nature, demographic, condition and circumstances of your site. If you  
are unsure of your site flow rates, it is relatively simple process to measure  
this for your event.  
To evaluate risk, we need to assess whether the crowd can flow towards  
the narrowest point on that route more quickly than it can flow through  
that point. An example of potential risk would be an entry gate with ticket  
and/or search processes in place, or a series of routes leading to a single  
route. For those areas, the crowd may arrive in greater numbers than can  
pass through that part of the system, and congestion may occur.  
We need the routing diagrams and a few site measurements to assess the  
potential for excessive crowd build-up over time. The routing/mapping pro-  
cess could highlight some potential problems \(converging routes, crossing  
routes, counter flow on routes\), so you will need to either walk the site or  
check the plans again. If the routing/mapping process shows a risk, action  
may need to be taken. That is the purpose of this process\: to provide an  
insight into the dynamics of risk to crowds as they move in the built and  
complex environment.  
Movement graph  
Figure 5.5 shows the number of people passing through a system for entry,  
the arrival profile.  
This was obtained from a mechanical counting system at the stadium  
entry gates. Crowd loading to the event can also be estimated using the  
transport data \(how many people per train/bus/tram per minute/hour\) and  
presented in a graph of number of people arriving at the event over time.  
When you couple this type of information with route/area mapping, you  
can build a numerical \(quantitative\) analysis of the crowd flow and produce  
a risk analysis for the potential of overcrowding.

Route/area maps should indicate the potential risks, for example, the  
risk of congestion and crushing/injury. The numerical data quantifies the  
potential scale of the risk. If you identify a risk, you then need to implement  
some changes or control mechanism; the numerical analysis is only useful  
after the initial site review and risk analysis. We will demonstrate how this  
process is applied in Chapter 6 in a range of case study applications.  
PROFILE  
A crowd profile is a description of a crowd’s historical behaviour—the intelli-  
gence we may have on past incidents, and/or any information relating to their  
arrival profile. For example, some performers’ fans are known to arrive very  
early for events and camp out on the streets. The Royal Wedding \(the UK,  
2011\) saw people arrive and take position around Queen Victoria memorial  
5 days before the event. Football supporters may display behaviour specific to  
fixtures or venues, and may require segregation strategies. We may also need  
to consider the male/female ratio in order to provide adequate toilet facili-  
ties. Examples of useful sources of information are the fan-based websites  
and discussion forums, media reports and reviews of previous events. The  
performers \(or agents\) may also provide information that could be relevant to  
management style; asking staff at other venues whether there are any specific  
issues to be aware of for fans of a specific performer may also be enlighten-  
ing. This information all helps define the management style for the event.  
Space, time, direction, flow  
As outlined earlier in this chapter, RAMP analysis is a qualitative pro-  
cess that we typically perform while assessing and designing sites for major  
events. Once the RAMP analysis is complete, the numerical data for spaces,  
times, directions and flow rates quantify the potential crowd risks. One  
common mistake we have observed is for delegates to jump straight into  
the numerical/quantitative analysis and start ‘bashing numbers’ into spread  
sheets before they have a clear understanding of the site dynamics. We  
have also seen a number of major projects pass a purely numerical analysis,  
performed in spreadsheets and using computer simulations, and fail during  
operations. To outline why this is a problem in both the rationale and the  
application of modelling/simulation, we need to review the uses and appli-  
cations of numerical/quantitative analysis.  
A downward spiral  
These concepts were originally outlined in the PhD thesis as the founda-  
tion for our study of crowd dynamics and the development of sophisticated Crowd and event modelling 133  
crowd simulations. As we have previously noted, there has been prolifera-  
tion of simulations on the market over the last decade; the cost of these  
systems will inevitably be driven by market demand, and consequentially  
users will become less and less skilled. Simulations are very attractive for  
a number of reasons; for example, their graphics/image quality has been  
increasing year by year, and their cost has been decreasing. As systems  
become cheaper, they become more widespread. The more widespread  
these systems are, the more people can use them with little or nor training.  
Be aware  
Safety-critical simulations need to be treated with caution and used by  
experienced, qualified practitioners—especially in the domain of crowd  
safety. Crowd simulations must be fully calibrated and independently vali-  
dated before being deployed in the field. We need to expand on this point  
further to highlight specific hidden risks.  
Simulations and risk analysis  
Crowd simulations, although useful, do not address many of the key risks  
associated with planning and managing events \(both large and small\). One  
of the motivations for moving away from simulations was that we could  
not validate the extremes of the crowd dynamic—the points of failure—in  
the same rigorous manner that other engineering disciplines require. To test  
steel, for example, you subject it to a series of destructive tests in order to  
establish how it performs; you cannot test a crowd to destruction to vali-  
date a computer system.  
We could simulate a crowd crush and crowd collapse to gain insight into  
the underlying mechanism of these phenomena, but it would not be a cali-  
brated or validated model. It would be an approximation at best, an edu-  
cated guess and nothing more. It may be an accurate guess, but it would be  
impossible to prove correct until we had the data—and we cannot get the  
data without subjecting the crowd to significant risk. We have measured  
lower crowd pressures at concerts and on the front-of-stage barriers, but to  
push a crowd to destruction is simply not possible!  
We could never fully validate the simulations. We could use them to  
assess risk, but this elicited no more out of the simulation than the input  
data. We had efficient, low costs techniques to help us understand certain  
types of crowd risks, sufficient to begin the process of defining a risk man-  
agement strategy. The computer simulation was not adding value to the  
overall risk analysis process.  
The lack of validation, coupled with the number of variables in  
crowd behaviour models, highlights the benefits of the meta-modelling  
approach and why this is needed in event planning, licensing/approval 134 Introduction to crowd science  
and in control rooms. The modelling approach, we would argue, is essen-  
tial to understanding and improving crowd safety. It does not attempt to  
prove a system safe; it only assists in the process of identifying the risks  
to the crowd.  
DIM-ICE\: Setting the standard  
The DIM-ICE meta-model is a template—a tool for collecting information  
with specific focus on crowd risks. There appear to be similarities in sys-  
tematic failures in other domains. Consider the sinking of the Titanic\: the  
design \(the ship, the number of lifeboats\), the information \(radar, observa-  
tions\) and the management process all contributed to the loss of life. These  
three categories \(design, information and management—DIM\) are not  
unique to crowd disasters; they are fundamental areas of failure common  
across a wide range of disciplines \(aviation, shipping, driving, crowds\). So  
focussing on these as a planning template in order to provide that valuable  
insight into how, when, where and why things can go wrong, made perfect  
sense in the development of a crowd and event model, particularly as we  
were focussing on accident and incident prevention, on risk analysis and  
safety engineering.  
Modelling events and crowds  
Unlike crowd simulations, the purpose of this type of modelling is not to  
predict how a crowd may move towards, occupy or depart a site. The meth-  
odology has one specific objective\: risk analysis. To this aim, the modelling  
tools test key elements of an event so that we can then define a strategy that  
fits that specific event/site/crowd. DIM-ICE is a blank template; the infor-  
mation that goes into the template defines the event.  
The use of a template allows the user to compare two similar events  
\(same performer, same type of people/crowd, different venue\) and two  
different events \(different performer, same venue, different crowds\) in a  
systematic and structured manner. That was the purpose of the model—  
its design was simply to capture information in a manner that retained  
those key elements, and to facilitate new users’ understanding of event  
risks.  
The DIM-ICE meta-model also has specific focus on operational, tactical  
and strategic development of site event plans, with specific focus on crowd  
management planning. It has a multitude of applications, and acts as a  
checklist for safety officers, police, licensing and site operators.  
When using the template the users capture knowledge and information  
that allows them to consider the phasing and implications of emergencies.  
For example, an emergency during ingress would provoke very different  
crowd management concerns than an emergency during egress. In most Crowd and event modelling 135  
plans, these two situations are simply not considered. For example, at Love  
Parade in 2010, where 21 lives were lost and over 541 people injured, there  
was an emergency during ingress. The event organiser failed to consider  
such an emergency; there were no contingency plans, no considerations  
for evacuation during ingress and no analysis of risk of combined crowd  
flows or crowd capacity/density at critical entry points or in areas with  
counter flow. The disaster would have been avoidable if the event planners  
had taken some time to build a simple event model. We will illustrate that  
process in Chapter 6—case studies.  
Objective of model building  
The aim of the model builder should be to construct a model that is easy  
to understand, that is easily able to evaluate information and detect errors,  
and which communicates the elements that may give rise to safety issues.  
To do this, one has to spend more time on the analysis of event and crowd  
safety requirements than on the process of building the model. All too  
often we have seen simulations being built in isolation, and key factors  
omitted in the simulation design process. These simulations are not fit for  
the purpose.  
Models are, by definition, representations of the system under study,  
and—especially when it comes to modelling crowds—there is a wide range  
of fundamentals that need to be defined and understood. It is not necessary  
to build a complex model of an environment in all of its possible configu-  
rations, with all of the individuals in all of their possible states. Indeed,  
such a model is analytically intractable and, more importantly, impossible  
to understand. There are simply too many unknowns; the model builder  
needs to break the process into logical, easy to build, easy to test, easy to  
understand sections. Often it is analysis of the problem that leads to a sim-  
pler model with clearer results. Fruin, in his thesis ‘Pedestrian planning and  
design’ \(p. 115\), stated\:  
The pedestrian planning process follows the classic sequence of prob-  
lem definition, identification of restraints, determination of program  
objectives, establishment of study scope and procedures, collection and  
analysis of data, development of alternative solutions. A planning pro-  
gram for pedestrians may involve one building, a small group of build-  
ings, a downtown core network, or even larger systems of interlocking  
networks. Project scope may range from a basic low budget improve-  
ment program, gradually implemented over a long time period, to a  
large capital project with accelerated priorities.  
The primary goals and objectives of an improvement program for  
pedestrians are\: safety, security, convenience, continuity, comfort, system  
coherence and attractiveness. All goals are interrelated and overlapping. 136 Introduction to crowd science  
In general, a good model would follow the aforementioned principles.  
It should be simple to build, modify and understand, and it should easily  
communicate its outputs to others.  
Why model crowds?  
With a model, you have a solid, worked-through, defendable position.  
Accidents can happen, but they should not be due to negligence or incomplete/  
incompetent risk assessment. Through the process of modelling, the user will  
develop a thorough and rigorous approach to the planning of crowded spaces.  
Modelling a crowd will save you from guessing and praying that every-  
thing will work out on the night of the event. It removes the uncertainty,  
saves time and money, streamlines event planning, produces a standardised  
approach to crowd management and ultimately provides you with an audit  
trail that, if done correctly, may provide a defence in court. Why did it go  
wrong? Were you adequately prepared? Can you prove it?  
During the model-building process for our consulting projects over the  
last two decades, we established the key elements of crowd risk analysis and  
safety engineering. We then questioned whether we needed to put this into  
a computer simulation at all? The DIM-ICE model we developed and used  
before building a simulation—the tools we used to structure and capture  
information of sites, events and operational processes/procedures—was  
perfectly suited to the purpose of crowd risk analysis.  
RAMP ANALYSIS\: SUMMARY  
The RAMP analysis provides the user with the geometric relationships for  
ICE under normal and emergency situations. It defines the routes, areas,  
flow rates, fill times, arrival profile and anticipated crowd behaviour.  
DIM-ICE\: SUMMARY  
The DIM-ICE meta-modelling approach is a systematic analysis of the  
behavioural influences on a crowd \(design, information and management\)  
with an emphasis on risk assessment during the different phases of an event.  
Modelling, monitoring and managing crowds is an integrated approach  
to crowd risk analysis and crowd safety. All three elements are required  
to a greater or lesser extent, and the meta-modelling approach, coupled  
with RAMP analysis, provides a framework for each of these elements. We  
define the meta-model for an event, which then indicates how to monitor  
the crowds, and from that the development of a robust crowd management  
strategy is developed. The result is a series of practical, operational and Meta model for potential crowd congestion
