DECISION SUPPORT ANALYSIS  
The DIM-ICE meta-model is a matrix approach to capturing information  
and assisting in the event definitions; it helps the user make the right deci-  
sions on resource deployment. We use the same approach for other types of  
risk-related analysis. For example, Figure 5.6 shows crowd congestion for  
different weather conditions and site attractions. It evaluates different types  
of attraction at a specific event, under different weather conditions, for the  
risk of congestion. Using a matrix approach we can map one set of variables  
\(congestion, weather, attraction, etc.\) against another \(merchandise stands,  
bars, signing marquees, etc.\) and colour the table to indicate the potential  
congestion. In this example, we have used green \(shown as light grey in  
Figure 5.6\) to indicate less than 50% of that specific area’s capacity, orange  
\(shown as grey in Figure 5.6\) to indicate between 50% and 80% of that  
specific areas capacity and red \(shown as dark grey in Figure 5.6\) to indi-  
cate more than 80% of that specific area’s capacity. This identifies function  
against congestion and can be extended to other types of analysis.  
There are many uses and applications for matrix analysis and we have  
outlines of this application on www.GKStill.com with a variety of images,  
definitions and applications of this technique. You can populate the table  
with cost, risk, information and the like to assist you in assessing the best  
possible options. It is a low-cost option for decision support and we have  
found that delegates, with a little practise, will readily adopt the technique  
for event planning, site options and risk analysis.  
In the example \(Figure 5.6\), we can see that cold/bad weather will have a  
significant impact on congestion/risk at three key locations, as the crowds  
will be concentrated into sheltered areas.  
This modelling technique \(you can apply a similar approach to any event\)  
identifies the information element \(change in the weather will impact the  
crowd\) in relation to specific site functions and locations. In Chapter 6, we  
will focus on examples of the application of RAMP analysis, DIM-ICE  
meta-modelling and matrix analysis. We have many similar examples of  
how this matrix approach assists in the overall strategic planning of events  
on the educational support site and eLearning platform \(http\://www.  
GKStill.com/Education/\). Chapter 6  
# Case studies and examples  
My concern with risk management is that many \(not all\) risk profession-  
als are trying to redefine risk management to make it something broader  
than it actually is.  
Michael Rasmussen  
Safety is not an intellectual exercise to keep us in work. It is a matter of  
life and death. It is the sum of our contributions to safety management  
that determines whether the people we work with live or die.  
Sir Brian Appleton after the Piper Alpha disaster\*  
INTRODUCTION  
We build crowd and event models to gain insights about any potential risks  
that may manifest during an event. During an event, the early indication of  
a developing risk, the warning signs that something is going wrong, is criti-  
cal to successful crowd management. Specifically, we build the DIM-ICE  
Meta model to evaluate any potential risk inherent in the design, informa-  
tion and management elements at different times during an event \(ingress,  
circulation and egress\). We use RAMP and matrix analysis tools to help  
us qualify and quantify the crowd risks at events. This is not an intellec-  
tual exercise; we need to assess risks in order to protect the public from  
harm. Unless you can identify and communicate the nature and potential  
of crowd-related event risks, they remain problems ‘lying in wait’.  
We developed these specific, visual risk analysis methodologies because  
the existing, brief description, single-number approach to risk analysis is  
flawed. Consider the process; the user has to first visualise a risk, then will  
try to describe the risk in a few words and then needs to assign a single  
number for likelihood and another number for consequence, both of which  
are highly subjective. In effect, the user is turning complex information into  
An explosion and the resulting oil and gas fires destroyed it on 6 July, 1988, killing 167 men.  
139

a code; a few words and a number, the sum of the likelihood and conse-  
quence. Does this ­ process adequately describe all risks? Specifically, risks  
that may be dynamic, may change in both severity and duration.  
Let us evaluate the risk assessment process with specific reference to  
crowd safety at events. A risk assessment is compiled and documented.  
This is, in effect, turning a potentially complex description into a short-  
hand version of the risk. Consider the moment when someone else, reading  
the risk assessment, tries to translate this shorthand back into information,  
in order to manage the risk. The reader needs to reimagine, or visualise,  
the actual risk using the description in the risk assessment. This process is  
necessary to understand the risk, and then the reader needs to consider how  
to manage, mitigate or eliminate that specific risk. To put this into context,  
below is an example from a typical risk assessment.  
The risk is given a numerical value, defined as the ‘likelihood’ of the risk  
occurring times the ‘severity’ or consequences of the risk occurring. The  
descriptions are listed in Table 6.1.  
The value, the sum of the likelihood times the severity, is then assigned  
an action plan. Typically, this would read as shown below.  
Value Action  
1–5  
6–12  
15–25  
=  
Low risk, risks controlled  
=  
Medium risk, further precautions required  
= High risk stops activities, immediate action required  
ASSESSING THE RISK ASSESSMENT  
The risk in Table 6.2 relates to trips, slips or falls; this does not describe if  
the risk applies to a specific location, or if this risk has a duration. There  
will be areas on the event site where the severity \(or consequences\) of a  
trip, a slip or a fall will have a much higher significance, and a greater  
impact on the crowd, where the time, the severity/consequences will be  
dependent on the nature of the crowd flow. For example, an entry system  
may have a much higher risk related to trips, slips and falls, for a short Case studies and examples 141

will have a higher severity \(several people could be injured\) under spe-  
cific conditions. To know where, when, and how long a risk will last is  
essential to good crowd management; yet, the process, the recommended  
methodology, does not record this information.  
In the example from Table 6.1, there are no specific areas mentioned,  
no duration and no indication that the risk may be higher under specific  
conditions. A value of 4 \(may occur from time to time\) and 2 \(minor  
injury requiring first aid\) are assigned. The action point is ‘pre-event  
check to remove loose material and level uneven ground. All cables bur-  
ied or at high level. Information about uneven ground and correct foot-  
wear’, appears to be the task of Bill Smith. Is he the person wearing the  
correct footwear? Perhaps, he is the designated person responsible for  
checking the public’s footwear. It may be his job to eject anyone not  
wearing the correct footwear! One would assume there is some definition  
of ‘correct footwear’ elsewhere and that the public know that ‘correct  
footwear’ rules apply at this event. The risk assessment does not define  
‘correct’ or to whom the ‘correct footwear’ rules apply. We also note  
that there are references to ‘Information about uneven ground’ specified  
in the risk assessment. Is that a sign or a diagram on a map? Perhaps, a  
designated person \(Bill Smith, wearing ‘correct footwear’\) will be telling  
people about uneven ground. We can see how inefficient this table is for  
both describing the risk and assignment of a risk factor.  
We note that if all these measures are enforced, then it will result in  
a reduced risk modified to 2 \(may occur but rarely\) and 2 \(minor injury  
requiring first aid\). Well, that is OK then, the risk is reduced to a value  
of 4 \(2  
×  
2\) which clearly puts the risk into the ‘low-risk, risks-controlled’  
category. This assumes that Bill will continue to check the footwear and  
maintain the site as outlined in the risk assessment, for the duration of the  
event. This is just one entry on the risk assessment, but you can understand  
the problems of decoding this information into a relevant and appropriate 142 Introduction to crowd science  
action plan. Without appropriate, understandable documentation in place,  
and an action plan, implemented correctly, you may be liable if the risks/  
hazards result in an incident.  
The current risk assessment process is cumbersome and onerous. It  
requires reams of paperwork, often, with little time to evaluate the nature  
and extent of many crowd risks. This leads to another fundamental issue  
of the conventional risk analysis process as many risk assessments are  
‘cut and paste’ from the previous events with little or no consideration  
for the evaluation of specific site- or event-based risks. Instead, crowd  
managers generally rely on experience, and this highlights a significant  
and widespread flaw in the process. Some experience cannot be gained  
until something goes wrong and, as we highlighted in the previous chap-  
ters, the near misses are seldom documented so that others do not gain  
the benefit of this experience.  
To illustrate this point, we were involved in a personal injury case \(as  
an expert witness\) where there were conditions that represented a specific  
risk to a specific demographic were not being noted. In this case, escala-  
tors were used as stairs at a main station in London. There were instances  
of old women tripping on this structure, and several instances over sev-  
eral months. This indicated that the system presented a specific risk to a  
specific demographic and during the busiest periods. This should have  
triggered an increase in the risk factor \(likelihood is high for a specific  
demographic, the little old ladies\) and introduction of specific safety con-  
ditions. The accidents resulted in significant injuries, and when the data  
were reviewed, it was clear that the process of updating the risk analysis  
was simply not being implemented. Part of the problem was this type of  
risk needs a level of understanding for the operators. Part of the problem  
is the operational team needs to be aware of the conditions under which  
specific risks can be much higher than normal. Risk can be dynamic—yet  
the risk analysis process is one description and one number. This fails to  
capture many of the risks to crowds in the built and complex environment.  
When we review the risk assessment process using a branch of mathematics  
called ‘information theory’, we can outline a fundamental failure of the sys-  
tem. We stress that the aim is not to make the process broader than it already  
is, but rather to highlight how simpler, illustrative analysis can enhance the  
risk analysis process, can make it simpler to describe, understand and use.  
Information theory  
In 1948, Claude Shannon published a paper called ‘A Mathematical Theory  
of Communication’ which defined the concept of information theory. A key  
measure of information theory relates to the number of bits of information  
necessary to successfully transmit information so that it does not suffer  
undue loss of information. Case studies and examples 143  
From Wikipedia\:  
The main concepts of information theory can be grasped by considering  
the most widespread means of human communication\: language. Two  
important aspects of a concise language are as follows\: First, the most  
common words \(e.g. ‘a’, ‘the’, ‘I’\) should be shorter than less common  
words \(e.g. ‘roundabout’, ‘generation’, ‘mediocre’,\) so that sentences  
will not be too long. Such a trade off in word length is analogous to  
data compression and is the essential aspect of source coding. Second,  
if part of a sentence is missing the recipient should still be able to glean  
the meaning of the underlying message.  
Information theory and risk assessment  
When we construct a risk assessment, we are converting complex infor-  
mation into a condensed form and recording this in a document. At some  
point in the future, this information needs to expand back into a set of  
operating instructions. To be effective, there should be no loss in data, and  
the condensed form should contain all the necessary information to allow a  
clear and comprehensive reconstruction of the original information. When  
information is converted into a smaller, more condensed form, some infor-  
mation is lost. Furthermore, when the condensed form is expanded back  
into some form of understanding \(the original form\), any lost data can  
result in misinterpretation, errors and incomplete understanding. Again,  
the process depends on the experience of the individual in both creating the  
risk assessment and in processing the data from an existing risk assessment  
that another individual may have compiled.  
Throughout all of the above, there are a variety of event, company and  
other guidelines for individuals who participate in the risk analysis process,  
but the events industry is a low-cost, low-margin business and the required  
skills set is not always present in the operators. As we have highlighted  
in the previous chapters \(and case study examples in this chapter\), many  
risks are not identified correctly and remain problems ‘lying in wait’. There  
needs to be a more comprehensive approach to developing a crowd risk  
assessment, defined qualifications and standards for checking a crowd risk  
assessment and there needs to be more awareness in the control room to  
these types of problems.  
HSE guidance for event organisers  
Although this book was written in the context of the UK guidance and leg-  
islation, the international reader is advised to review the local legislation and  
guidance for their own events. In our experience, the guidance and legisla-  
tion are highly variable from country to country, state to state and yet, the 144 Introduction to crowd science  
risks to the public are the same worldwide. Sadly, the information about  
crowd risks and causality has been poorly documented and rarely distributed  
to a wider audience, in a form that can be easily understood or applied. As  
the opening quotation stated, ‘many \(not all\) risk professionals are trying to  
redefine risk management to make it something broader than it actually is’.  
The UK HSE has useful information and we cite this specific section  
from http\://www.hse.gov.uk/event-safety/event-organisers.htm  
Event organisers  
You will have prime responsibility for the health and safety of your\:  
•  
•  
•  
Workers  
Members of the public \(your audience/the crowd\)  
Contractors working for you at the event  
Ask yourself\:  
•  
•  
Do I have enough knowledge about health and safety to organise  
work appropriately?  
Have I thought through and implemented good health and safety  
procedures and checks?  
It is an honest series of statements and questions but defines no real stan-  
dards for the process beyond the coded risk assessment \(visualise the risk,  
code this into a short sentence, assign a number, hope that someone reads  
and understands the code and then acts in a way as to prevent the risk becom-  
ing an incident\). All this assumes that the information is both complete and  
transmitted without distortion. It also assumes that there are levels of compe-  
tency throughout the process, namely, a competent person is coding the risk  
and an equally competent person is reading and acting upon the risk assess-  
ment. On the question of competency,\* the HSE \(UK\) defines the following\:  
Get competent advice  
As an employer, you must appoint someone competent to help you meet  
your health and safety duties. A competent person is someone with the  
necessary skills, knowledge and experience to manage health and safety.  
You could appoint \(one or a combination of\)\:  
•  
•  
•  
Yourself  
One or more of your workers  
Someone from outside your business  
\*  
http\://www.hse.gov.uk/business/competent-advice.htm Case studies and examples 145  
You probably manage most aspects of your business yourself, or with  
help from your staff. But if you are not confident of your ability to  
manage all health and safety in-house, you may need some external  
help or advice. There are a number of different sources of advice.  
These include\:  
•  
•  
•  
•  
•  
•  
•  
Trade associations  
Safety groups  
Trade unions  
Consultants registered on the Occupational Safety and Health  
Consultants Register \(OSHCR\)  
Local councils  
Health and safety training providers  
Health and safety equipment suppliers  
Deciding what help you need is very important. Unless you are clear  
about what you want, you probably won’t get the help you need.  
Things to consider when using external help  
•  
•  
•  
•  
•  
Make sure you clearly explain what you need and check that they  
understand you. Ask them to explain what they understand the  
work to be and what they will do, when they will do it and what  
they will charge you.  
Check for evidence of relevant training/knowledge, such as for-  
mal qualifications or practical experience of providing advice  
in your industry/area of work. Can they explain why they are  
competent to advise you on your particular problem? Are they a  
member of OSHCR?  
Shop around to find the right help at the right price. If you were  
buying equipment or another service for your business, you  
wouldn’t always accept the first offer; so, do the same with health  
and safety advice.  
Check that the person you choose is adequately insured.  
Consider whether you have received the help you needed. Do you  
have a practical, sensible solution to your problem? Or have you  
ended up with something completely ‘over the top’ or a moun-  
tain of useless paperwork? If you are not happy with the solu-  
tion, ask for an explanation and whether there may be a simpler  
alternative.  
The inspiration behind the quote above ‘… there may be a simpler alter-  
native’ resonates with Rasmussen’s quote ‘… redefine risk … to make it 146 Introduction to crowd science  
something broader than it actually is’. The statement that suggests, ‘shop-  
ping around’ needs careful consideration\:  
•  
Shop around to find the right help at the right price. If you were  
buying equipment or another service for your business, you  
wouldn’t always accept the first offer; so, do the same with health  
and safety advice.  
The service, and hence the standards, will obviously have some price  
variations and will inevitably drive the buyer to their perception of ‘best  
value for money’ in other words the ‘cheapest’ service. We highlight three  
problems with the existing process\:  
1\. The existing recommendations on risk assessment fail to identify  
the location, duration and severity of the risks, specifically as many  
crowd-related risks are time dependent.  
2\. The recommended process would fail the test criteria for information  
capture and transmission \(information theory\) and hence, it is a fun-  
damentally flawed process and needs review.  
3\. There are no standards throughout the industry relating to the  
description and classification of risk, specifically, the location, dura-  
tion and severity of risks.  
Until the process is improved, we would argue that failing to recognised,  
record and subsequently failing to manage the risk is failing an essential duty  
of care to the public, to protect them from harm for places of public assembly.  
Accident causality  
In Chapter 3, we outlined the principle of proximate and distal causality and  
how unsuitable event and crowd management plans, safety concepts and  
site design are one of the fundamental failings, that can then lead to acci-  
dent and incidents. To clarify this statement, negligent planning and lack of  
appropriate checks during the approval process can lead to over confidence  
in a plan, and event or a site, that fundamentally flawed, has a high risk of  
failing, has a problem \(or problems\) lying in wait.  
Failure to identify risks, either during the planning or during the approval  
process can lead to prosecution. If a poorly constructed event plan does pro-  
ceed to the operational phase, the ability to assess risk in the environment,  
whether it is realising that the entry system is not operating as expected, or  
the site is becoming too congested in specific areas, falls to the operations  
team, the crowd manager and the control room functions.  
It is essential that event planners and specifically anyone involved in  
approving an event realise their duty of care to protect the public from harm Case studies and examples 147  
and the primary mechanism for this is the crowd risk assessment process.  
The history of major incidents indicates that there is room for significant  
improvement in both understanding and managing the crowd dynamic in  
places of public assembly. Furthermore, there are legal, ethical and moral  
responsibilities to be adequately trained and competent to perform the risk  
analysis for the event planning and the event approval process.  
Accidents can be a result of poor planning, failure to identify a poor plan  
in the approval process or failure to implement the event to plan. During  
an accident, investigation of the proximate and distal causes can be con-  
fused, specifically if the investigators and legal teams are not familiar with  
these concepts in the context of human behaviour, events and crowd risks  
in places of public assembly. We are not aiming to replace any legal defini-  
tions of causality, only to highlight that a competent person should be able  
to assess risk during the planning and approval stages of the event process.  
It is equally important that the recommended process is fit for its intended  
purpose, in that it is neither onerous \(nor open to short cuts like ‘cut and  
paste’\) and adequately records the information relevant to the risks involved,  
in particular, the dynamics of risk.  
Adequate training  
One key question asked of witnesses, following incidents, is ‘were you  
adequately trained to perform your duties’? Obviously, this includes the  
process of risk assessment. Unfortunately, this is a poorly regulated pro-  
cess and there are no rigorous standards for the quality of a risk assess-  
ment, either in format or in content. Risk analysis is a subjective process  
and requires a wide range of experience that can be difficult to build up  
without formal training. It is not enough for an organisation to assign  
someone the duties of a safety officer, as the organisation itself can be  
accountable if it assigns an untrained safety officer. Since it is up to the  
individuals concerned to identify, document and manage risk, we would  
argue that there needs to be an introduction for standards of competence  
across the industry.  
Competency  
For crowd safety, a competent person must have a set of skills that include  
the ability to identify, understand and articulate crowd risks. Specifically,  
location, duration and severity of risks that are transient in nature. To  
assist in this classification of risks, we will outline examples of event plan-  
ning and risk analysis from case studies around the world. We hope that  
these examples will illustrate some of the key points associated with using  
the modelling approach and the systematic analysis of crowd risks. Our  
aim is to show, by an example, a cross section of risk with the objective of 148 Introduction to crowd science  
highlighting how to identify similar types of risks at different events. We  
do this using the models described in the previous chapters and illustrate  
these with case studies.  
CASE STUDY EXAMPLES  
In this chapter, we will apply the three modelling techniques, RAMP anal-  
ysis and DIM-ICE meta-modelling, to a range of events from the previ-  
ous workshops or consultancy projects from around the world to illustrate  
how these techniques are applied, how risks can be identified and resolved.  
These include analysis of the following events\:

The model solution  
We designed the modelling process to capture information using the sim-  
plest of skills and in the minimum time. Over the years, we noted that  
accident and incident reports are often written in days \(if not weeks\) after  
an incident and are both a time-consuming and onerous task. To put this  
into perspective, try this exercise\:  
1\. Describe your movements yesterday in words.  
2\. Now, describe your movements this time last week in words.  
One of the two things may happen now; you may skip over this task  
thinking it is too much of an effort, or you may not be able to articulate  
your actions in words. In either case, the information will be lost. Now,  
take a map of the area and visualise the places you went yesterday or last  
week. Print off a map and draw a line indicating the places you visited.  
Where you may have struggled to describe your movement yesterday, or  
last week in words, you will find it was quick and simple to draw your  
movement on a map. Furthermore, anyone looking at that map will be  
able to understand where you were. The picture takes a few seconds, the  
words may take several hours, both of them capture the information for  
later review, but the diagram is much easier to produce and understand.  
This is the essence of capturing the crowd movements and identifying  
the potential for risk using a mapping process. It takes a few seconds and  
captures the important information for others to review later.  
The RAMP analysis is an extension of this data-capturing process.  
Obviously, you need a few basic skills to complete an RAMP model, not  
the least of which is to know your way around a site plan, map or diagram.  
You also need some basic arithmetic skills. Some people may be good at  
report writing, some may be good at numbers and some may be good at  
reading maps, plans or diagrams. We are all different and skills vary. We  
suggest that report writing may be more challenging than sketching lines  
on a diagram. To be frank, if this book is too difficult, if these examples are  
too difficult, then you should consider another career path.  
Crowd risk analysis and crowd safety are fundamentals for event plan-  
ning and event approval \(licensing or permitting\). Over the last decade,  
we have found that the basic skills are missing, yet they are not difficult to  
learn and apply. From stewards to senior police officers, we have passed on  
these techniques, they are used, practised, enhanced and passed on through  
the industry. We have seen improvements to many major events over the  
last decade. Key competency and the core skills required to achieve a more  
informative crowd risk analysis are the subject material for the workshop  
we run around the world. This book, and the associated eLearning site  
will, hopefully, spread the word further. 150 Introduction to crowd science  
MODELLING AN EVENT  
Modelling an event is fundamental to understanding risks and keeping  
crowds safe. You should develop models based on your experience and  
things you remember about a site or similar events. During the process, you  
capture that knowledge, and process the information that may be other-  
wise difficult or onerous to document in a report. Crowd and event models  
also assist other people to both visualise the situation and to communicate  
any potential for harm that may exist. The process converts your knowl-  
edge into a form that helps communicate risk to other people, for example,  
police, emergency services, licensing officers, crowd managers and stewards.  
Drawing a site diagram, and adding layers of information, such as crowd  
flow routes and risk mapping, makes you think about how the situation  
can change through time. You can turn something complex into something  
simple and understandable. In addition to this, the process of drawing pro-  
vides a valuable tactile and visual dimension to the information, one that  
you simply do not experience when using \(say\) a computer graphics package.  
A PICTURE SPEAKS A THOUSAND WORDS  
There is no need for a site plan or risk assessment to contain thousands of  
words when a sketch can say more. A simple drawing works like a snap-  
shot through time and there are many benefits to having this snapshot. As  
you are drawing the site, and refining the model, you are capturing your  
ideas and your concerns. You are also gathering and collating information  
during the process of modelling. You are systematically working through  
plans, spotting risks and understanding the context of your event, turning  
your experience into a form that will benefit others.  
Putting diagrams into your event plans and risk assessments makes  
them more understandable. You, and others who view these diagrams, can  
instantly recognise areas that need attention. A single diagram can convey  
the crowd risks during an event; it can place it in time, pinpoint the inten-  
sity and the movement of the crowd. Creating a model of your crowd and  
space will help you present your case to those who finance the event. This  
has proven, in many cases, to assist in justifying the necessary budget for  
safety expenditure. It will help you keep your event safe. There are many  
benefits to this low-cost, simple-to-implement, process.  
HOW TO BEGIN  
The best place to start is an event site map, diagram or plan. Buy some  
baking sheet \(the white/clear kind, do not use the brown thicker kind of Case studies and examples 151  
baking sheet\) and place a layer of baking sheet over a site map. You can  
buy this in 10 m rolls from a supermarket. You can buy the same material  
from an art store or office stationary supplier and it will cost you 10 times  
the price. Baking sheet and a box of coloured pencils are the basic materi-  
als you need and these are available from supermarkets for a few pounds  
\(or dollars\).  
We typically recommend that you sketch out a site diagram using three  
colours. Red for areas of risk, orange for areas that need to be monitored  
and green for areas that were at risk and are now safer. Obviously, you can  
omit green for the bulk of the mapping and only indicate orange and red.  
We typically begin with the ingress process and use different layers to rep-  
resent different times. A 10 a.m. layer, a 11 a.m. layer, 12 p.m. layer and so  
on. Sometimes, this can be days, hours or minutes, and the timescale is only  
important depending on the rate of change in the risk. So, if there is a change  
in a risk for an area, that should become a new layer.  
To illustrate this, imagine we have an event that the crowds will gather  
outside several hours before the event \(a pop concert perhaps\). We would  
suggest that the time the first person arrives is the beginning of the map-  
ping process, perhaps showing the area as blank \(no risk, you could colour  
the whole map green, but this adds no further information\). Then if crowds  
begin to accumulate, we would suggest that the area the crowd occupies is  
coloured orange indicating that this is an area that may need to be moni-  
tored. As time progresses and the event is about to open, the crowd may  
begin to push forward and we may want to indicate this as a red section.  
Obviously, if the barrier design, information, time \(start and end time\),  
management structure, and the like are all in place, then there will be  
little or no risk to the crowd during the process of entering the event. By  
drawing the maps, we have made an estimate of the area the crowd will  
occupy; this provides us with information about the number of barriers,  
stewards and potentially the style of management required. For example,  
if you anticipate a large crowd accumulating around the entry points, the  
diagram will help you define the potential number of stewards that may be  
required to manage the situation.  
We suggest that you use big thick lines to represent large crowd move-  
ments towards sections of the map. The red area represents potential areas  
of risk, and orange sections represent areas where we need to monitor risks.  
We also suggest that you create a new map for every hour of the event  
because each hour may tell a different story. As you continue to process this  
information and draw out the maps, you are visualising the event unfold-  
ing. As you then deliver an event, you can check and enhance the models  
for the next event in this area. This may sound time consuming, but it  
takes less time than documenting reports and providing written situation  
analysis. 152 Introduction to crowd science  
VISUALISING RISK  
At the start of this chapter, we outlined the problems associated with the  
conventional risk assessment process. The final product of this is a set of  
coded instructions that are difficult to understand.  
Of what use is a risk assessment if people fail to act on the nature of the  
risk? The process of thinking through a risk analysis and then converting that  
process into a few words \(there is seldom more than a half-formed sentence in  
many of the risk assessments we have viewed over the years\) is open to misin-  
terpretation at several stages. Initially, converting a risk assessment document  
into a few words, and then assigning a single value for the risk then stating an  
action plan \(often with fewer words\). The process is both archaic and is open to  
interpretation at several levels. It is highly subjective and requires the reader to  
understand far more than the words state. It fails to capture any risk dynamic,  
where the risks may be dependent on several influences \(such as overcrowding  
at an ingress or egress point due to a sudden change in the weather\).  
RAMP ANALYSIS  
You can work through event plans in a methodical, systematic and rigor-  
ous manner, mapping routes, areas and risk. If you apply this methodol-  
ogy \(RAMP and DIM-ICE\), you can capture specific event information,  
compare and contrast different events and develop a clearer understand-  
ing of the dynamics of risk prior to, during and postevent. If you use the  
same approach for all of your events, you will understand the differences  
and similarities of a wide range of events.\*  
You are able to look at a whole variety of elements that affect ingress  
such as the arrival profile, modes of transport, the media, the information,  
and the weather forecast. You are able to capture that information during  
the event-planning process. We stress that this does not replace risk assess-  
ment, it does not replace the event plan, but it enhances both the process  
and the identification of the crowd risks. The process provides the user  
with a meta-model for the crowd element within the context of the event,  
the building and the space used by the crowds. That is why we define this  
as a standard template. There are some events where some columns might  
have very little in them, but the overall model will still fit into the DIM-ICE  
template.. To put this into perspective, the next few sections are examples  
from our projects. These will illustrate how the modelling approach helps  
us define the key risks and resolve these during the planning phase of the  
event process.  
\*  
Running workshops around the world, we see many similarities and underlying risks across  
many different types of international events. Case studies and examples 153  
INGRESS—CAPACITY—BEIJING OLYMPICS  
TORCH RELAY  
The project was to review a crowd management plan for the Gerrard  
Street leg of the Beijing Olympic Torch Relay and we produced a 68-page,  
14,400-word, illustrated report using the DIM-ICE and RAMP analy-  
sis techniques. Although there were a number of potential issues, the  
primary concern was the ingress and approach routes. To highlight the  
power of using visuals, here is the description of the key area of concern  
from the report. These were the clients words, from the original brief.  
… The arrival profile and direction of approach is of concern. The  
area on Gerrard Street is limited and has the potential to become over  
crowded. The most appropriate approach will be along Shaftsbury  
Avenue, not the direct approach, from the tube stations. This will allow  
the viewing area to fill progressively and alleviate crowd pressures and  
potential frustrations.  
The reader has to visualise the site to understand the context of the text  
above. This can, of course, lead to a potential misunderstanding. Using a  
line diagram, we can explain the process much more clearly. Figure 6.1  
Figure 6.1  Route of the Beijing Torch Relay along Shaftsbury Avenue and cutting into  
Gerrard Street \(China Town\). The access to Gerrard Street is restricted and  
the main media bus could not make the turn. The runner would take the  
Gerrard Street leg and re-join the media bus back on Shaftsbury Avenue. 154 Introduction to crowd science  
shows the route that the Torch Relay will take; the Gerrard Street section is  
the China Town area in London.  
Shortest routes  
The crowd can move towards Gerrard Street from the two London  
Underground stations; Piccadilly Circus \(lower left\) and Leicester Square  
\(mid right\) \(Figure 6.2\).  
We can measure the area on Gerrard Street \(both sides\)—it is 1200 square  
metres. At two people per square metre, this will hold 2400 people. This is  
the routes and areas part of the RAMP analysis; the routes leading from the  
stations to the site and the area of the site. This site has a limited capacity  
and the crowd will be going to the event to view the Torch Relay \(obviously\).  
Comparing the two diagrams \(Torch route and route to the event space\),  
you can see that the approaching crowd may take the shortest path and only  
the first 2400 can be accommodated on the route. If more than 2400 people  
try to reach Gerrard street, they will find themselves in a position where  
1\. They cannot see the Torch Relay.  
2\. They may be in a hazardous situation, as we do not know how many  
people may come to the event.  
As this is a free event, and the London Underground capacity for trans-  
porting people to the site is vast, we could find 2000 or 20,000 people  
Figure 6.2  Shortest routes from the London Underground stations to the Gerrard  
Street \(ChinaTown\) area. Case studies and examples 155  
trying to access Gerrard Street. We do know that only 2400 will be able to  
enter the space. In this specific example, the arrival profile is unknown; so,  
we must plan for the worst case.  
Crowd objectives  
If the crowd comes to see the Torch Relay, we need to direct them along the  
torch relay route, filling Gerrard Street, and then if more people approach,  
they are filling along the Torch route. Figure 6.3 shows the safer approach  
route. The total viewing area \(along the Torch route from both London  
underground tube stations\) is 7760 square metres; so, we can accommodate  
15,520 people along this route, two people per square metre, from the bar-  
rier line, standing shoulder to shoulder which would be a comfortable view-  
ing configuration for a Torch Relay. We now need to manage this process.  
Counting \(clickers\) at a key location would provide an accurate method of  
assessing numbers, but a visual inspection of the area and stewards instructed  
to assist an even fill across the area, by simply talking and guiding people  
along the route, a more practical solution for a safe and even crowd density.  
The analysis \(routes, areas and movement\) gave us a reasonable analysis of  
how the crowd may move to the site. The problem \(shortest path\) was identi-  
fied and an alternative was established. The profile of the crowd now needs to  
be assessed. There was a significant public outcry on the China/Tibet human  
rights issues with newspapers reporting on the situation of violent protests.  
Figure 6.3  By diverting the crowd along Shaftsbury Avenue, when Gerrard Street is full,  
the crowd will backfill along Shaftsbury Avenue along the torch relay route. 156 Introduction to crowd science  
Crowd behaviour  
The crowd coming to this event may have protestors; this will dictate the  
style of management. The Chinese Ambassador was to carry the Torch  
along part of the route \(originally planned near the British Museum\). This  
area was in the media spotlight, it was both a wide, open area and may  
attract a much larger crowd. The Gerrard Street element \(as you can see  
from Figure 6.3\) was difficult to access, the media would not be present in  
this area. The media were going to be on busses following the Torch, the  
busses could not turn into Gerrard Street, but the Chinese Ambassador  
could take the Torch through China Town \(Gerrard Street\), which seemed  
an obvious solution to a number of problems.  
This should not be publicised in advance; this type of information can  
­ influence crowd behaviour, and may cause protesting crowds to go to  
Gerrard Street to display their disapproval. Gerrard Street is a small space  
and could prove difficult to manage if there were protestors in this section.  
That was the crowd management plan, using the RAMP analysis and this  
is how it played out in the media.  
From the BBC on 6 April 2008.  
Clashes along Olympic torch route  
Thirty-seven arrests have been made after clashes between pro-Tibet  
protesters and police as the Olympic torch made its way through  
London. Protests over China’s human rights record began soon after  
the relay began at Wembley, and prompted an increasing police ­ presence  
through the city. After an unpublicised change to the route, the Chinese  
ambassador carried the torch through Chinatown.  
Summary  
In this short example of a case study, we have demonstrated the use of  
the RAMP analysis. Design, information and management will influence  
the crowds’ behaviour; so, we need to understand the design limits \(the  
space and approach routes\), we need to understand how to inform the  
­ public of the route to Gerrard Street and how this information is released  
to the media \(or not in this case\). We also need to know how to direct the  
crowds to the site from the stations \(management\), what barrier configura-  
tion we need in order to divert the crowds away from the shortest route and  
along the Torch route. This also dictates the management style we need to  
employ on the day, specifically, as there may be a protest element within  
the planned event. If we examine the site, Figure 6.1\: This is the route the  
torch relay will take. Figure 6.2\: This is how the crowd will approach the  
site \(the shortest distance to the site\), but the site has limited capacity and  
may fill too quickly \(more people could try and go to this area than the area Case studies and examples 157  
can accommodate\). Those individuals that cannot see the torch relay will  
then be backing up from the site along the shortest path. This is potentially  
dangerous as the crowd will be moving forwards to an already full space  
and people will react to not being able to see the torch. We need to route the  
crowd as shown in Figure 6.3, so that the crowds approach along the torch  
route and everybody can see the torch relay.  
The RAMP analysis provides a strategic framework for the above crowd  
management decisions. It enhances the risk analysis and risk management.  
MANCHESTER UNITED VICTORY PARADE  
A victory parade will be held on Monday 30 May to celebrate  
Manchester United’s record-breaking 19th league title and, potentially,  
their fourth Champions League trophy win.  
Local press  
We were asked to evaluate route options and potential risks related to the  
Manchester United Victory Parade scheduled for 30 May, 2011. In 1999,  
hundreds of thousands of fans attended a similar event and there were sig-  
nificant safety concerns, not the least of which was the police taking on a  
public safety role during the event, due to crowd pressure and crowd crush-  
ing. All events of a similar nature, since 1999, had been cancelled. The  
quote\* below was typical for previous years of cancellation.  
Assistant chief constable XXX said last night\: GMP’s primary con-  
cern is the safety of the people of Greater Manchester and the fans  
of the club.  
It is right and fitting that Manchester United’s fans should have the  
chance to celebrate the club’s success this year in the event of a win on  
Wednesday, but they should do this in safety.  
The last major homecoming event was in 1999 where there were seri-  
ous safety issues raised regarding the risks of crowd crush. Manchester  
United is the biggest football club in the world and we believe any event  
would attract more people than in 1999.  
We reviewed the CCTV \(helicopter\) footage from the 1999 video and  
could understand the concerns. The crowd images showed shockwaves; the  
crowd density must have been six/seven people per square metre. The bus  
was moving through the crowd with a phalanx of police officers trying to  
move the crowd aside to allow the bus to progress. This was the cause for  
\*  
http\://www.manchestereveningnews.co.uk/news/greater-manchester-news/united-parade-  
doomed-954325 158 Introduction to crowd science  
concern from the Greater Manchester Police due to the potential for crowd  
injury.  
The proposed route is shown in Figure 6.4.  
The first elements we checked for this project were the transportation  
hubs; in this case, the train stations and trams, the road and parking spaces.  
They evaluated the routes from these locations to the parade route/view-  
ing areas. As we can see in Figure 6.5, the stations are within a few blocks  
of the top of the parade route. These are main line stations, Manchester  
Victoria Station, Manchester Oxford Road and Manchester Piccadilly.  
A large section of the route is not accessible for road \(due to road closures  
which would be in place hours before the parade starts\) and is not close to  
a railway station.  
The station at Old Trafford would not be used for this event.  
We can see from Figure 6.6, that the East side of the parade route is  
accessible to three main railway stations. This immediately highlights a  
potential problem, as the route would be barriered, separating the vehicles  
and the public. The question of efficient filling of both sides of the route  
would need to be considered.  
There is also a large proportion \(\~50%\) with no close-by railway stations.  
It is reasonably foreseeable that the section to the top of Figure 6.6, on  
the Eastern approach, would have the highest density. The section with no  
immediate rail access may be sparsely populated, and the area around Old  
Trafford may experience the highest density \(there are direct motorway-to-  
Figure 6.4  Victory parade route for Manchester United Football Club. Case studies and examples 159  
Figure 6.5  Train stations that access the centre of Manchester and route from the train  
stations to the victory parade route.  
Figure 6.6  Large section of the route has no direct rail access; due to road closures,  
there will be few people along this section. 160 Introduction to crowd science  
stadium routes with no road closures in place along these routes and ample  
car parking at the stadium\).  
We can see from Figures 6.4, 6.5 and 6.6, the key elements of a crowd  
management plan are beginning to form, specifically, the areas where stew-  
arding may need to be deployed. The diagram shows the potential for high-  
density areas, specifically, the sections from the rail stations to the parade  
route, that may require specific attention. It takes a few minutes to draw  
these kinds of diagrams and you should see the immediate benefit. It helps  
visualise the crowd movement, highlight concentrated crowd flows \(and  
hence density and potential risk\) and helps to assess the potential conges-  
tion and pinch points.  
We then walked the route with the Police and highlighted several pinch  
points relating to street furniture, raised areas where crowds will gather,  
sections where the crowds should be marshalled, advised on pedestrian  
management and how best to distribute the crowds along the route, and we  
also advised on specific management strategies for distributing the crowds  
along the route. This part of the analysis is qualitative, descriptive and  
visual as the Figures 6.4 through 6.6 indicate. We then had to assess the  
numerical \(quantitative\) element of the site, specifically, the capacity \(num-  
ber of people\) along key sections of the route.  
Parade route  
The parade was going to pass through the centre of Manchester and finish  
at Old Trafford. The first part of the numerical analysis was to evaluate the  
space and estimate the crowd capacity. To do this, we used our mapping  
software, and there are several free tools available on the Internet that will  
allow you to view a site, to measure spaces and to evaluate capacity. Our  
system couples site measurements with three-dimensional \(3D\) crowds,  
graphics of people, where we can populate a site and fly around it in a  
virtual reality environment. This helps us produce control room visuals for  
density estimation.  
Each section of the route was estimated for area into account, taking all  
the available spaces, for example, the roads and between buildings. For a  
parade route analysis, we could consider the whole area of the route, as we  
knew the barriers would be placed on the road separating the parade from  
the crowds. This would reduce the total site capacity by one-third. We  
measured the available areas of the road and pavements, the spaces from  
buildings on one side of the road, across to the other side, estimating the  
total available viewing space. We evaluate the capacity of this space at two  
people per square metre, then again, at five people per square metre. This  
provides us with a lower and upper number for the total available space  
and hence the number of people who can attend the event. If we position  
barriers, we may split the road into three sections \(West, middle and East\) Case studies and examples 161

and therefore use 1/3rd of the available space for the vehicles and 2/3rds of  
the available space for the crowd. Using any of the free mapping tools, you  
can measure the site, and length of the route. You do not need specialised  
modelling tools to perform this task.  
The numerical results are shown in Figure 6.7.  
This now provides us with a good estimation of the total number of  
people these sections of the route can sustain. From these values, we need to  
review the train capacity \(how many people per train can arrive per hour\)  
and develop a fill strategy to ensure no section has excessive crowding. By  
following a similar process of evaluating the available areas, the routes  
and the times, you can evaluate your own site capacity and hence develop  
your own crowd management plan. How much space do you have? What  
is your site capacity? Will this be of low/high density? Are there any pinch  
points? From which direction will crowds approach? What are the rates of  
crowd arrival from the transport system? How quickly will your spaces fill?  
Space, time, direction, flow—the RAMP helps shape your crowd manage-  
ment plan.  
HILLSBOROUGH \(1989\)  
Figure 6.8 shows a scaled sketch of the site plan at the time of the  
Hillsborough disaster.  
Leppings Lane is approximately\* 14 m wide. At 82 people per metre per  
minute, this could reach a theoretical maximum of 1148 people per minute.  
\*  
Dimension taken from the scaled diagram in the Taylor report into the Hillsborough  
disaster. 162 Introduction to crowd science  
Figure 6.8  Diagram of the Leppings Lane end of the Sheffield Wednesday Stadium  
\(Hillsborough\) indicating the approach along a wide route into a narrow sec-  
tion. This should have been considered as a potential risk as the area can fill  
faster than the crowd and can make progress through the system.  
Each turnstile can accommodate 750 people per hour\* \(or 12.5 people per  
minute\). If we consider this from a purely geometric perspective; Leppings  
Lane could accommodate 1148 people per minute, to process this num-  
ber of people would require 1148/12.5  
=  
91.84 \(92 turnstiles\) to ensure  
no queueing in an unmanaged situation. Obviously, there is a trade-off  
between this upper value \(92 turnstiles\) and the practical limits of the local  
geometry. Some control is required \(such as a crowd management plan to  
regulate the crowd flow, a filtering system. Such a process \(filtering\) was  
used successfully in previous years to regulate the crowd flowing towards  
the turnstiles.  
Given the approach is wider than the entry system, there has to be some  
controls in place. Once the crowd begins to accumulate, to queue, we can  
estimate the fill rates, the throughput rates and hence the risks of crushing.  
The contingency plan for safe crowd management, where control points  
\(filtering\) should be positioned and the means of monitoring crowd arrivals  
further along Leppings Lane reduces the potential for overcrowding. In any  
† This information is from the Guide to Sports Grounds publication from that time. Case studies and examples 163  
system where the number of people approaching the turnstiles/entry system  
can exceed the throughput rate of the turnstiles/entry system \(the rate at  
which people progress through the turnstiles\), there will be a queue. If the  
approaching crowd flow rate \(people per metre per minute\) is very much  
greater than the turnstile capacity, a crush \(and shockwaves\) will quickly  
develop with serious risk to life and limb.  
If there are design limits, then information \(such as advising entry times,  
locations, queue management, etc.\) helps reduce the risks and management  
may be required to control or regulate the approaching crowd flow. Sadly,  
this type of thinking was not applied at that time. The principles were  
known to engineers, but not to a wider safety audience, such as the safety  
managers, local licensing officers and the Police.  
At that time \(the 1980s\), the Police were managing this area by a process  
of trial and error; there were no scientific methods of understanding the  
potential for harm at that time from a police perspective. Police forces are  
NOT trained for this function. The police role, their function and training,  
is focussed on public order and not on public safety. An overcrowded space  
can become a problem of public order when crowds react to the dangerous  
and threatening conditions. We run a number of courses, specifically for  
Police forces on this specific issue—the differences between public safety  
and public order.  
This type of crowd crush is fundamentally a design failure. This space  
can fill very quickly, which leads to the crowd reacting to the risks of  
crushing. This type of system requires a crowd management strategy to  
keep it safe. That is the core of the problem; you need to understand the  
design limitations in order to develop the most appropriate crowd manage-  
ment plan. You need to regulate the crowd flow into the areas of similar  
geometric relationships, where ingress exceeds throughput. In the previous  
years, a filtering exercise performed that specific function, regulating the  
crowd flow, but without a specific knowledge of the crowd dynamic in  
these spaces. It was, as stated, a process of ‘trial and error’ with no scien-  
tific, mathematical or geometric understanding of the flow rates, risks and  
potential for harm.  
Once the crowd density reaches the shockwave limits \(where people are  
in close-packing, physical contact\), it becomes very difficult to manage and  
the risk to the crowd rises significantly. You need to understand rates of fill  
to assess these problems and to improve the crowd management process.  
For these kind of environments, there should be a continuous process of  
reviewing the risk assessment from event to event and to revise the crowd  
management plan accordingly.  
There are many similar examples of this type of geometry around the  
world, where spaces in front of entry systems are hazardous. These are the  
types of problems we frequently hear about in the workshops, those that are  
reported as the ‘near misses’. 164 Introduction to crowd science  
INGRESS—CIRCULATION—LINCOLN  
CHRISTMAS MARKET  
Lincoln Christmas Market is one of the largest Christmas markets in  
Europe, attracting up to 250,000 visitors over the 4-day event. The mar-  
ket spreads around the historic centre of Lincoln, including the castle and  
cathedral. The number of visitors is so great that a circular one-way system  
for pedestrians around the streets of Lincoln is put into place, taking the  
crowds through the Castle.  
Over the years, the event has grown and crowd-thinning measures have  
to be implemented during the busiest times. In 2010, the Christmas mar-  
ket had to be cancelled due to bad weather. This was, of course, for public  
safety reasons and the decision not taken lightly. Considerable consulta-  
tion and discussion about the relevant public risks were factored into the  
decision. From the local press\:  
Lincoln’s Christmas market has been cancelled for the first time in 28 years  
due to severe weather conditions. Rob Bradley from City of Lincoln  
Council said the ‘sad decision’ had been taken on advice from emergency  
service teams. Lincolnshire has experienced heavy snowfalls all week and  
more snow is forecast for the weekend. The market attracts about 150,000  
visitors and contributes millions of pounds to the city’s economy.  
In the following year \(2011\), record crowds attended the event and there  
were concerns about overcrowding at the Castle entry area. The press  
reports stated\:  
Organisers of Lincoln’s Christmas Market say they are looking to improve  
pedestrian access after receiving comments that the market was too busy.  
The City of Lincoln Council said the German-style market had been  
attended by about 200,000 people. The authority said it had received  
some comments about the market being too overcrowded to enjoy.  
We ran a crowd science workshop before the 2012 event and reviewed the  
issues relating to overcrowding from the 2011 event. The safety advisory  
group, various stakeholders and crowd management company attended the  
workshop. Together, we mapped the food and beverage locations, the route  
through the castle, entry points, crowd-thinning measures \(where a diver-  
sion strategy was implemented to reduce crowd congestion\) and we mapped  
out the areas of concern. Through this process, we noted that there was a  
concentration of food and beverage areas \(this slows the crowd down and  
causes congestion in critical locations\) and that the crowd-thinning mea-  
sure had to be implemented every year. One key finding was the official  
‘start point’ of the market became an area where the crowds converged from Case studies and examples 165  
Figure 6.9  Redesign of the Lincoln Christmas market area, a longer route, distribution  
of food and beverage, market stalls and entertainments along the route and  
multiple entry points all help to reduce congestion. Multiple entry point \(this  
map is of a smaller scale to Figure 6.10\).  
several routes and progressed through the Castle. This was a bottleneck  
in the system. To reduce this kind of congestion, we defined several ‘start’  
points, extended the route and spread the market across a wider area \(the  
market footprint and extended route are shown in Figure 6.9\).  
By extending the route around the back of the cathedral, we are increas-  
ing the area for attractions and providing more space for future expansion.  
We also define several ‘start points’ when visitors entering the Christmas  
areas from the bus drop off at the point and station. Although there were  
comments about some areas not having as much atmosphere, previously, a  
function of higher-density crowds, the event was a success and with more  
space for concessions, traders and attractions, future expansion is planned.  
The event in 2012 was run without incident, but locals and stall holders  
complained about new layout, about the potential for lost profit, and the  
smaller footprint \(Figure 6.10\) was re-introduced in 2013. We highlighted  
that profit should never come before safety.  
EVENT—SITE CAPACITY—LEICESTER CARIBBEAN  
CARNIVAL  
The community-organised carnival has been held annually since 1985 on the  
first Saturday in August, with a parade around the city of Leicester \(usually 166 Introduction to crowd science  
Figure 6.10  Lincoln Castle is the main focal point of the Lincoln Christmas market, the  
system is one way through the castle and there are multiple routes leading  
to the entry point of the castle \(Market Square\).  
taking place in the city centre and the suburb of Highfields\), culminating  
in an event on Victoria Park. The parade is usually preceded by a week  
of cultural events. It is the third-largest Caribbean carnival in the United  
Kingdom, after the Leeds and Notting Hill carnivals.  
The carnival is promoted as the premier multi-cultural event within the  
Midlands and, according to the BBC and local sources, attendance is esti-  
mated between 60,000 and 100,000 in the August celebrations of 2010.  
The venue was based around Victoria Park \(site shown in Figure 6.11\),  
which has in excess of 90,000 square metres of space.  
Over the last 25 years, this has been a high-profile event generating citywide  
revenue. In 2010, the prospect of moving the event into the town centre was  
proposed and supported by the city authorities, despite an ‘advise against’ by  
the emergency services. The issue was brought to us during a workshop and  
we reviewed the problems associated with the proposed relocation.  
Space mapping  
We first need to evaluate the space available \(city centre\). For this, we used  
our site-modelling and measurement tool to establish that there were some  
10,000 square metres available \(see Figure 6.12\).  
This is not the total available space as some of this space is required for  
the market stalls \(1600 square metres\) and the parade \(1400 square metres\),  
leaving only 7000 square metres for the crowd. Case studies and examples 167  
Figure 6.11  The site of the Leicester Caribbean carnival, Victoria Park, the space is in  
the order of 90,000 square metres with crowds in the order of 60,000–  
100,000 people.  
No comparison  
We now have a number of people \(60,000\) and an area \(7000 square  
metres\). Divide the area by the number of people and you reach a figure  
of just over eight people per square metre. Clearly, the space would not be  
sufficient to accommodate everyone at the same time. We can see that if the  
previous year’s capacity figures were indicative of the numbers that may  
attend in the proposed year, there was a risk of a crowd crush.  
The counterargument to this analysis was that the crowd is transient;  
they come and go throughout the event. This is typical of a lack of risk  
awareness; the authorities understood economic constraints \(it would be  
cheaper to move the event into the town centre, cheaper barriers, no site  
infrastructure, etc.\) but are not well versed in the associated crowd risks,  
namely, there are more people who may attended than the space can accom-  
modate safely. We have seen this type of risk blindness many times, where  
the fundamental principles of overcrowding do not register as a potential  
hazard. The questions that should be asked are\:  
What do we do if 60,000–100,000 people try to attend the event?  
In this situation, the above question should have resulted in discus-  
sions relating to the use of barriers, crowd counting and crowd control, 168 Introduction to crowd science  
Figure 6.12  The proposed site, city centre, showing the event footprint. This area is in  
the order of some 10,000 square metres. Market stalls reduce the area to  
8400 square metres and the parade would reduce the area to some 7000  
square metres.  
emergency planning, ‘what if’ scenarios concerning an emergency during  
the event and required access \(such as a medical emergency or fire\), not  
questions the city authorities either consider or try to address. Making  
the event happen is someone else’s job! They made the decision to move  
the event, to save money, and it was up to the operational teams to make  
it work.  
This kind of blinkered optimism and cost saving, where the mayor of a  
city insists that the ‘event will be good for the local economy’, is a problem  
we encounter several times a year and in each case, there is a lack of risk  
awareness on the part of the higher authority. So, we devised a simple, one-  
page graphic that the politicians could understand.  
This is a very useful technique to show space issues. You take two plans  
of the same scale and place them side by side. Then shade in the areas you  
want to compare. On the left \(Figure 6.13\) is the Victoria Park site, low  
crime and disorder, successful event, low crowd density and low risk to  
the crowd, we coloured this green in the presentation. On the right \(to  
the same scale\) the city centre, which is an unknown space for the event, Figure 6.13  Although the numbers clearly indicate the problem, these do not connect with the politician’s perception. The graphic, shown side  
by side, highlights the difference in the spaces.  
Case studies and examples 169 170 Introduction to crowd science  
we coloured this red in the presentation. In the previous years, the parade  
passed through the city centre and not the entire event.  
The two maps show in Figure 6.13, featured in a one page presentation  
with the legend\*  
The crowd that occupies the site on the left will not fit into the space  
on the right!  
That is one of the most powerful uses of a simple model; the visuals did  
all the work. We did not need to calculate space, flow rates of the passage or  
simulate anything to provide an ‘advise against’ but we did need to commu-  
nicate at the level of a politician who had the assumption that, somehow, it  
can be made to work.  
CIRCULATION—CAPACITY—THE JAMARAT BRIDGE  
In 2001, we had a call asking if we would assist in evaluating the design  
of the New Jamarat Bridge in Saudi Arabia. During the first visits to the  
Kingdom of Saudi Arabia, we saw a crowd simulation of the design pro-  
posal and asked to comment on its strengths and weaknesses. This specific  
simulation was a network model based on a hexagonal lattice and, as such,  
the crowd flow and density relationships were rudimentary and had several  
fundamental modelling flaws. To evaluate the relationship between crowd  
flow and geometry, a network lattice model was over simplified, fundamen-  
tally flawed and not appropriate.  
There were many different structural designs proposed, specifically, the  
Jamarah \(the pillar that represents the devil\) which would be changed  
to a larger shape. One of our tasks was to evaluate which shape would  
have the optimal throughput for the rituals \(the Rajm or lapidation pro-  
cess\). This Jamarat Bridge was an area where many mass fatalities had  
occurred and the proposal was to replace the existing two-tier structure  
with a five-tier superstructure \(ground plus four levels\). For any project  
of this scale, the first consideration is always the key question relating to  
the fundamental model of the system. In Chapter 1, we described how  
we developed modelling and meta-modelling techniques to capture vital  
information from this specific project; we also developed a fundamental  
model of the holy site.  
Before any crowd-modelling project, it is vital to understand the key  
parameters of the environment, the crowd activities and if there are any  
physical constraints. For the Jamarat Bridge Project, we established the  
basic model of the site as shown in Figure 6.14.  
\*  
The actual legend was a bit more direct and used more colourful language. Case studies and examples 171

Figure 6.14  Fundamental model of the Jamarat Bridge showing the key areas. There  
are three pillars, and the areas 2, 3 and 4 are repeated accordingly \(but not  
shown on the fundamental model as the dimensions will be the same\). Using  
Arabic convention, the numbering system reads from the right to the left  
\(as is the direction of crowd movement\).  
In this model, we have five different areas on each tier of the bridge.  
Ingress, queueing/throwing, passing, prayer and egress \(Figure 6.15\). Each  
tier has a different geometry but has the same fundamental model.  
1\. Ingress route to each tier, these five tiers had different origins \(Mecca,  
Mina, etc.\) and the concept was to separate each origin and desti-  
nation set to avoid cross-flows and congestion. The concept was to  
streamline the system.  
2\. Queueing/stoning area, this is the stoning ritual area.  
3\. The area where the crowds move past the pillars.  
4\. The area where a short prayer \(Dua’a\) is performed, then the crowd  
will move to the next pillar.  
5\. The egress route from that tier of the structure.  
On each tier, the process is 1, 2, 3, 4, 2, 3, 4, 2, 3, 5 as there are three  
pillars on each tier. A simulation will need to consider all areas for each  
tier but the fundamental model is an analysis of the design constraints. For  
that, we need to consider on the limits to the system and, as such, we only  
need to consider the system as 1, 2, 3, 4 and 5.

Figure 6.15  A, B, C, D, E, F and G are defined using algebraic notation. 172 Introduction to crowd science  
There are several sections in the model shown in Figure 6.14 that the  
crowds need to move through for the rituals. A and B are the ingress sec-  
tions, C is the area where the stoning of the devil ritual is performed, D is  
the pillar, E and F are passing/prayer spaces \(where people can move past  
the pillar\) and G is the egress route.  
System rules  
The design criteria are to create a model of the system to allow 125,000  
pilgrims per hour, per tier, to perform the ritual \(stoning the devil\). The  
speed of the individuals in this area, as measured during previous Haj, is  
75 people per metre per minute. The first question is how big does A + B  
\(ingress\) need to be to facilitate this?  
125,000 people per hour divided by 60  
=  
= 2084 \(round up to the nearest whole person\).  
people per minute  
Seventy-five people per metre per minute means we have to divide the  
above number by 75 to obtain the minimum width  
=  
28 m \(again rounding  
up to the nearest whole number\). So, our first rule is that each tier entrance  
needs to be more than 28 m to accommodate 125,000 people per hour  
\(the design has two entry ramps, each ramp is 20 m wide\). We write this as  
\(using mathematical nomenclature\)\*  
1\. A + B ≥ 28 m available width at 75 Hajjis per metre per minute\:  
Which implies that E + F \> A + B \(for Dua’a/prayer and passing  
space\). We need enough space around the Jamarah \(the bridge is  
called Jamarat, the pillars are called Jamarah\). We simulated this  
and found that we have three times the ingress width as the ston-  
ing area, but the system would maintain equilibrium \(i.e. the ingress  
flow rate would balance the stoning ritual\). Therefore, the length \(D\)  
around the Jamarah must be three times more than the combined  
ingress width.  
2\. D ≥ 3\*\(A + B\)\: The area we needed for the rituals has to be sufficient  
so that not more than four people per square metre are packed in the  
space.  
3\. Area at C ≤ 4 people per square metre\: For the passing/prayer spaces,  
we must have more than the ingress space \(since the crowds must be  
able to pass the pillar while others are performing the ritual at the  
pillar\).  
\*  
≥ means greater than or equal to, ≤ means less than or equal to. Case studies and examples 173  
4\. E + F \> A + B \(for passing and Dua’a/prayer\)\: Finally, the egress route  
must be greater or equal to the ingress route to ensure that people can  
leave the system at a higher or the same rate if they are entering the  
system.  
5\. G ≥ A + B \(egress ≥ ingress width\)  
This is a fundamental model of a complex system. It only considers  
the ratio of the ingress, circulation and egress rates, the space and ritual  
requirements, to facilitate the desired throughput of 125,000 people per  
tier per hour. Providing the design adheres to the above rules the system  
should function. Reality means that we need to impose flow control,  
additional space, contingency for an incident and many other factors.  
The above rules are the minimum widths required for the system.  
We now have a design criterion for 125,000 people per hour, which is  
1\. Sufficient arrival capacity \(more than 28 m per tier\)  
2\. Sufficient throwing area \(the perimeter of the Jamarah must be  
three times the arrival width  
=  
28 m  
×  
3  
=  
84 m\)  
3\. Sufficient space \(density ≤4\) for the rituals \(throwing and praying\)  
4\. Sufficient passing area \(either side of the Jamarah\)  
5\. Sufficient departure capacity  
With these rules, we can test various design options. For that, we first  
develop numerical models in a spreadsheet—the example shown in Figure  
6.16, is from the Jamarat Project.  
This is a first-pass model in which there are various design options, where  
we can evaluate the design options. We use this to evaluate the question—  
does this design fulfill the design criteria? If it does not pass the numerical  
tests, we reject the design proposal. Once a design passes the numerical tests,  
we then \(and only then\) run a simulation to test the whole system. Figure  
6.17 shows the screen image of a simulation of the first tier of the New  
Jamarat Bridge.  
The numerical rules establish the fundamental design criteria. These  
rules do not give us information about design shape, congestion or poten-  
tial risks in key locations; flow diagrams and simulations provide these  
details for the analysis. It is essential to first develop a model of the fun-  
damental design criteria and system rules. When used in conjunction with  
fundamental models, a simulation can provide valuable insights for proj-  
ects of this level of complexity. The simulations are, in this case, used to  
evaluate the rates of passage, and potential for crushing. Not used to prove  
the system is safe for the pilgrims. 174 Introduction to crowd science  
Figure 6.16  Numerical models for the key areas of design alternatives. A complex spreadsheet using the logic of the fundamental model to  
test if the conditions apply. Case studies and examples 175  
Figure 6.17  Screen shot of the Jamarat Bridge agent-based model, each ‘dot’ is perform-  
ing the complex rituals on the bridge and the system produces density maps  
under a wide range of input conditions. We can alter the design shapes and  
re-run the model to test a wide range of options.  
INGRESS—EGRESS \(SHARED SPACE\)—LOVE PARADE  
DISASTER  
At 4\:20 p.m. on the 24th of July 2010, 21 people died and 541 people were  
injured at the Love Parade in Duisburg. The site had a combined entry/exit  
system that resembled an inverted ‘T’ consisting of two tunnels \(East and  
West\) and a main ramp onto the site shown below. Consider the basic geom-  
etry of the site. Figure 6.18 was obtained by drawing over a site image, and  
indicates a pinch point, that there is a barrier on the ramp. Although the  
site plan indicates the entire width would be available for two-way flow, the  
reality was very different. Figure 6.18 does not highlight the risk.  
Figure 6.18  Diagram of the Love Parade entry/exit ramp showing a barrier on the ramp.  
This was a security gate and had been in place for several years. Internet-  
mapping tools clearly show this significant constraint. 176 Introduction to crowd science  
Figure 6.19  Fundamental mode of the site, showing the crowd flow, constraint and con-  
vergence of the ingress/egress crowds. Checking this is sufficient for the  
crowd flow, you need to add the ingress crowd flow to the egress crowd  
flow due to the two-way nature of the crowd movement.  
Using only a simple line diagram, we can illustrate how the crowd  
was expected to enter and exit the site to highlight the specific nature  
of the risk in this section. The width at this narrow section, the barrier,  
was 10.59 m. In Figure 6.19, we have shown the crowd flow converging  
on the narrow section. The size of the arrows should indicate the situa-  
tion has significant risk and should raise concern about the feasibility of  
the site. The difference between Figures 6.18 and 6.19 is an important  
consideration. You need to evaluate how the crowds are going to move  
in the site, and then evaluate the numbers of people moving through the  
narrowest point and draw the risk diagram to highlight the potential for  
convergence as shown in Figure 6.19.  
An important consideration is that this section will have people mov-  
ing in both directions \(ingress plus egress\) and you need to add these two  
flows together to evaluate how many people this area will accommodate  
safely.  
Calculating crowd flow  
To assess the maximum capacity of any part of a system used for crowd  
movement, we apply a simple calculation. This was outlined in Chapter 2  
the maximum is 82 people per metre per minute, in line with international  
data-supporting rates of passage for pedestrian movements in places of  
public assembly.  
Figure 6.20 tells us that the optimal crowd movement \(flow\) conditions  
are two to three people per square metre. We have illustrated the indicative  
density diagrams on the graph. The Love Parade event plan site capacity Case studies and examples 177

Figure 6.20  Diagram of the crowd flow versus density relationship. If the crowd density  
exceeds three people per square metre, the flow rate \(people per metre  
per minute\) will drop, congestion will develop and, in this case, a fatal crush
