was calculated at two people per square metre but as we highlighted in the  
previous chapters, this only serves as an overall site capacity and does not  
indicate any areas of static/standing or dynamic/moving crowds within the  
site, the entry and exit points or around various site attractions. The critical  
area is this point on the ramp, where two-way flow was expected and hence  
the area where density is most relevant, where risks will be the greatest, if  
the flow was to exceed the capacity of the narrowest point in the system.  
Ingress/egress  
The history of major accidents and incidents highlights that most incidents  
occur during ingress and egress. It is vital that crowd flow and crowd den-  
sity \(crowd risks\) are carefully considered for the process of ingress and  
egress system design and that during operations, these systems \(ingress and  
egress\) are monitored to avoid overcrowding. As we can see from Figure  
6.20 shows, when density exceeds three people per square metre, the crowd  
flow drops rapidly. This should be clearly understood by the crowd man-  
ager, namely, that to maintain safe ingress and egress at optimal flow rates,  
the crowd density should be maintained at levels lower than three people  
per square metre.  
In the British guidance documents, ‘safety at sports events’, the value of  
four people per square metre is stated as the upper limit for any queueing  
system, this refers to slower moving queues at entry/exit points. There are 178 Introduction to crowd science  
no specific details in these guides for the size of a queue, the duration of  
the wait and the inevitable buildup of crowd pressure. In other words, the  
risks are not outlined, it is just a single number for crowd flow. To highlight  
the potential risks involved at an entry system, consider this, the back of  
the queue can move forwards, and the front of the queue may be trapped  
against the entry gates. The design of an entry system must be based on  
matching the throughput of the entry system to the anticipated arrival pro-  
file \(how many people per minute per hour are expected to enter the site\).  
This arrival profile must be matched to the throughput of the entry system  
to prevent excessive crowd build up at the entry points. The same principle  
applies to any pinch points\* in the system. Failure to consider these relation-  
ships \(arrival profile and system throughput\) leads to crowd congestion,  
potential crushing and serious risk to life and limb for large crowds.  
Consider a system where people are approaching a pinch point \(or nar-  
row point\) at a fixed flow rate. If congestion occurs at the narrow point  
\(and this may be for any number of reasons\), then the rate of passage, the  
number of people per minute passing through the system at that narrow  
point will be reduced. This can quickly result in further increases in density  
and overcrowding/crushing as people arrive at that point faster than they  
can pass through that point.  
Love parade ramp pinch point  
The fundamental error is the system design, specifically the ramp con-  
straint. The ramp was 10.59 m wide at its narrowest point. This would  
allow, at best, 82 people per metre per minute  
×  
10.59 m  
=  
868.38 \(869\)  
people per minute  
=  
52,102.8 \(52,103\) people per hour. This system should  
have been rejected; is it a shared space, a two-way flow for both ingress and  
egress? There was an expected 145,000 people per hour peak flow; this is  
three times greater than the system limit.  
There are a number of YouTube video clips of the incident and in these,  
we can see the ramp and the pinch point from various angles. In these clips,  
the ramp system appears congested and the top of the ramp area appears  
to be full around 3 p.m. The entry points \(West and East\) were also full  
of people waiting to enter. The parade, scheduled to end at 1700, would  
result in an anticipated mass egress from the site. Furthermore, the crowds  
leaving the site would do so via the ramp and against the crowd flow antici-  
pated to enter at that time.  
To evaluate the throughput of the system and potential risk to the crowd  
should have been part of the site analysis, the total number of people  
expected to enter the system needs to be added to the total number of  
\*  
A pinch point is a narrow section in a system. Case studies and examples 179  
people exiting the system as parts of the ingress/egress system are two-way  
flow \(in plus out\).  
This calculation was not present in the event plan although the data  
clearly indicated the numbers expected over the entire day. If anyone in the  
event planning and event approval teams simply added the two numbers  
together \(ingress plus egress\) and performed the calculation of the maxi-  
mum flow rate at the narrowest part of the system, they would have, should  
have, questioned and then rejected this site design.  
Highlighted in Fruin ‘Causes and Prevention of Crowd Disasters \(1993\)’  
are the problems and risks associated with shared ingress/egress systems.  
Similar warnings about the risks of shared spaces appear in several interna-  
tional guidance documents for crowd safety. Risk awareness and simple site  
analysis should have been applied. This disaster should have been averted,  
and the knowledge was neither new nor novel.  
EGRESS—CONGESTION—LONDON NEW YEAR  
EVENT \(FIREWORKS\)  
In many capital cities around the world, a public fireworks display marks a  
celebration of the New Year. These events are spectacular, typically hosted  
in urban environment and will attract large crowds. The London New  
Year Event was subject to a workshop in 2006 following a near miss, dur-  
ing which several members of the public were injured at the entrance to a  
London Underground station \(Westminster\). The station is very close to  
the event and, when the fireworks had successfully concluded, the crowd  
left the site and entered the station. The area became congested and the  
station management made the decision to close the station entrance, to pre-  
vent congestion on the platforms. At that time, there were several thousand  
people outside the station trying to get in and the inevitable crowd crush  
developed.  
Crowd crushing  
In these kinds of situations, a semi-circular crowd forms around the  
entrance and pressure builds at the centre of the semi-circle. To understand  
this kind of environment, and to illustrate how to identify this type of prob-  
lem, we shall model that part of the site. This is one of the examples we use  
in the workshops to demonstrate how to present a site issue to a layperson.  
This case study follows the typical event modelling analysis in which  
we would start with an overview of the site, then a video or photograph  
of the problem. This sets the scene for the audience, showing the site, the  
problems and risks, to position the key issues in the audience’s mind. We  
would then demonstrate how to a construct a site model to highlight the 180 Introduction to crowd science  
cause of the problem. Finally, the solution is modelled. This specific proj-  
ect was created in the workshop, then presented to the London authori-  
ties and the solution was deployed, and used. For this case study, we will  
use a similar script to the one we used during the workshops, and it has  
proven to be effective in demonstrating the modelling approach to this  
kind of problem.  
1\. Set the scene  
It is important to present the scene for the audience; they may not come  
from the same background, or have the same depth of knowledge of the  
site, its issues or operational requirements. To do this, you must not start a  
presentation with a complex site diagram, map or plan. We have found the  
best approach for positioning an event problem to a layperson is begin with  
an overview. Figure 6.21 shows the site we are going to review. The centre  
of the image is the Millennium Eye on the river Thames, in London. The  
bridge to the bottom of the image is Westminster Bridge and on the lower  
left-hand side are the Houses of Parliament and Big Ben.  
This should orientate the audience with the location \(there are a variety  
of Internet-mapping tools that will assist in this process\). The fireworks  
display focussed on the Millennium Eye, a large Ferris wheel on the river  
Thames. The best viewing area for the fireworks is the embankment on the  
opposite side of the river \(the left-hand side of Figure 6.21\). At the end of  
the fireworks, we have a congestion problem.  
Figure 6.21  Image of the event site for the London New Year Event. Case studies and examples 181  
2\. Show the problem  
We are going to model the section to the lower left of Figure 6.21, the  
area where the crowd leaves the embankment area and departs the site.  
This image is from a Police helicopter, it is inverted infrared, black is the  
hotter area; so, the darker the image, the more people are present in that  
space.  
In Figure 6.22 \(we have several minutes of footage of this specific loca-  
tion\) shows very high density crowds and the station \(near the middle of  
Figure 6.22\) has to be closed due to overcrowding on the platforms. The  
outer gates are shut, people approaching the site cannot see the problem  
ahead; so, the pressure builds up and there were several serious injuries \(13  
people with arms, legs and soft-tissue injuries\).  
We will now show the area in question and construct a model of this area  
to understand the crowd movements at that time \(egress\).  
3\. Build a model  
Figure 6.23 shows the section of the site that is of most concern. The outline  
of Big Ben is seen to the bottom left-hand side of the image. The white lines  
are the model outlines showing the embankment and Westminster Bridge.  
The crowd, tens of thousands of people in this section \(along the embank-  
ment and on Westminster Bridge\) are heading away from the area, and many  
are heading towards Westminster station or across the bridge to Waterloo  
station. This is the section we observe in the video clip \(Figure 6.22\).  
Figure 6.22  Inverted and infrared helicopter CCTV image for the congestion during  
egress at the London New Year Event. \(Source\: Metropolitian Police heli-  
copter. From project files for LYNE analysis, 2006 \(Still\).\) 182 Introduction to crowd science  
Figure 6.23  Diagram of the area of concern.  
The area around Westminster station is very congested and is a cause for  
concern. Figure 6.24 shows the area of concern, outlined on the underlying  
diagram \(this is where the tracing paper helps define the basic geometry\).  
When we remove the underlying map, we are left with the diagram show in  
Figure 6.25. This is the basic model of the area of concern.  
Figure 6.24  Outline of the area of concern. The model is shown on the image as darker  
lines. Case studies and examples 183  
Figure 6.25  Model of the area is obtained by removing all the background detail. If you  
use the baking sheet approach, you have drawn this model on the baking  
sheet. In this example, we have drawn lines on a site plan in Powerpoint, and  
then removed the background image.  
4\. Remove the background  
We remove the background and are left with the basic outline of the  
site, the key area where we need to focus our efforts to find an appropri-  
ate solution to the congestion problem during egress. We do not need  
a scale, as this process is a review of the crowd movements. In Figure  
6.25, we are going to add lines/arrows to indicate all the different crowd  
movements.  
The crowd can move from the right to the left \(Waterloo station\), and  
from the bridge \(top\) to the bottom \(this direction will take the crowd  
towards Victoria Station\). The crowd can move from the embankment  
area to either Waterloo station or Victoria Station \(leaving the site\). Finally,  
from all three directions \(bottom, top and to the left\), the crowd can move  
towards the station. Figure 6.26 shows all these movements. We would  
Figure 6.26  Model of the crowd movements \(lines\) in this area \(during egress\), showing  
convergence on the Westminster underground station. 184 Introduction to crowd science  
normally explain each direction, which displays the specific line/arrow; this  
helps the audience understand the crowd movements as there are many dif-  
ferent directions involved and building the model, step by step, can be very  
useful for presentation.  
5\. How does the crowd move in this area?  
As described above, the lines in Figure 6.26 shows all the possible direc-  
tions of crowd movement. When you couple this with a video clip, the  
audience appreciates the complexity and understands the risk; more  
importantly, the audience understands the relationship of crowd move-  
ment and congestion.  
We include a graphic of the station entrance in Figure 6.26; again, this  
helps the audience understand the site and the congestion problems. This  
process is focussed towards orientation of a complex crowd movement, at  
a specific time \(egress\) to an audience who may not have first-hand knowl-  
edge of the site or area in question.  
As you can see Figure 6.26, there are many lines crossing and converging  
on the station entrance from three different directions \(180% approach\).  
The number of people who leave the event and try to access the stations  
exceeds the stations capacity; there are more people trying to enter the  
underground system than the system can move on the underground train  
systems. There is a significant risk of crushing on the station platforms.  
This mismatch of arrival rate \(the rate at which people arrive at part of a  
system\) and the departure rate \(the number of people who can leave the sys-  
tem by the underground system\) leads to a queue. To prevent congestion on  
the underground, the station gates are shut and the queue quickly develops  
into a crush outside the station.  
We need to stress that people approaching this station cannot see the  
gates are closed; even as a public address \(PA\) announcement is made, the  
180% approach angle makes this space difficult to manage and as there is  
no route to alleviate the pressure. There are solid walls, buildings, on both  
sides of the station entrance. We need to understand the crowd’s objectives  
in the space. The station is where people are heading; we can remove that  
objective by shutting the station. We need to inform the crowd of this and  
we cannot do that during or at the end of the event. We must inform the  
crowd before the event.  
We announce \(in the station, on notice boards and in the local media/TV/  
press\) that no one can access the station after 9 p.m. The station will shut  
down at 9 p.m. and it will remain closed for the rest of the event. This pre-con-  
ditions the crowd behaviour; the aim is to utilise Waterloo station and Victoria  
Station for the departing crowds. These two stations are not too far from the  
site and, as the crowds leave, they will be moving into wider spaces; so, there  
will be less congestion and hence less risk. By closing Westminster station, we Case studies and examples 185  
Figure 6.27  Model of the proposed \(and later adopted\) solution to reducing the com-  
plexity and congestion of this area.  
remove the problem of station congestion. We still have multi-directional flow  
in this area and a further measure will remove that issue.  
The idea was to block off Westminster Bridge, allowing it to fill from the  
top \(from Waterloo station\) and not allow public access to this area from  
the Houses of Parliament/Big Ben side of the bridge. This would require the  
media \(TV/local press, station announcements, what’s on in London, Twitter  
and Facebook information\) to ensure that the public knew of the site entry  
and layout. Figure 6.27 shows the proposed changes, these were adopted in  
2006 and are still in use for the London New Year Fireworks Event.  
6\. Making the area safer  
Figure 6.28 shows the crowd movement with these two measures in place,  
closing Westminster station at 9 p.m. and placing a barrier\* at the Big Ben  
side of Westminster Bridge.  
Figure 6.28  Diagram showing the simplified egress crowd flow due to the site changes.  
\*  
The barrier can be removed in an emergency. 186 Introduction to crowd science  
During the workshop, the delegate \(a police commander\) did not apply  
any mathematics, the line diagrams illustrated the problem and communi-  
cated the concern and solution, to the audience. The line diagram does all  
the work. You cannot see the cause of the congestion in the video. But you  
can see the crowd flow, the cause of the congestion problem in the crowd  
flow lines and diagrams.  
EGRESS—CROWD MANAGEMENT—WEMBLEY  
WHITE HORSE BRIDGE  
We produced a report and analysis on the proposed White Horse Bridge  
\(Wembley Stadium, London, UK\), which included the barrier design and  
operations for crowd management during egress. This was a complex envi-  
ronment, as we needed to regulate crowd flow for North- and South-bound  
trains, allow a throughput and maintain a segregated area for the occa-  
sional VVIP\* transport \(they often had special train arrangements\). The  
area had a number of items of street furniture \(chairs and trees\), which  
we had to accommodate in the design. These non-removable items would  
enhance the space for non-event days. The specification was to design a  
system that could assist in egress crowd management. It had to be a flexible  
design suitable for many different types of events, for example, concerts  
and sports events.  
Using the DIM-ICE principles, we needed to consider the design for four  
different users \(North bound, South bound, passing through and VVIPS\).  
We need to consider the information requirements for North- and South-  
bound passengers and we need to minimise the number of stewards/mar-  
shals operating the system. During the early research years, we had worked  
at Wembley stadium; so, we understood both the departing crowd profile  
\(time to empty the stadium\) and the demands on the rail station. The site  
was close to the stadium and some 10–20,000 people would often be seen  
to queue for several hours while departing a major event.  
Barrier design  
We noted that the ratio of people travelling to North and South was sel-  
dom 50\:50; in fact, the ratios were typically 70\:30– 80\:20. We extracted  
that information from ticket sales from the train-operating companies and  
it provided the first important element of the barrier design. We needed  
a big queueing space and a smaller queueing space that we could switch,  
depending on the demand at that specific event. We also need to regulate  
crowd flow onto the platform to prevent overcrowding and the potential for  
\*  
VVIP  
=  
Very, very important persons, such as royalty, performers, ministers and so on. Case studies and examples 187  
Figure 6.29  Diagram of the proposed \(and later adopted\) barrier configuration for the  
White Horse Bridge queueing system. Each lane serves a different depart-  
ing crowd from Wembley Stadium.  
incidents as trains pulled into the station. Figure 6.29 shows the design we  
proposed and adopted.  
The two entry points would be signed as North and South depending  
on the anticipated demand. This information was available from train-operating  
company ticket sales and became part of the operational proce-  
dures. We could simply switch the North/South sign on the day. A steward  
at the entry point would assist people getting into the correct queue. The  
shaded section \(middle, bottom of Figure 6.29\) is the area that holds enough  
people to fill a train.  
We are queueing both the North- and South-bound passengers on the  
entry to the bridge. This simplified the marshalling arrangements as we were  
allowing a trainload of people onto the platform at a time. Once the train  
leaves, we allow another trainload of people onto the platform. By simplify-  
ing the operations, we reduce the risk of overcrowding on the platform. We  
will return to this specific site in the next chapter, and control room applica-  
tions, to outline how we managed the crowds after events.  
SUMMARY  
We began this chapter by evaluating the existing risk assessment process,  
how information is lost in the process and the common working practices of 188 Introduction to crowd science  
‘cut and paste’ fail to register with the crowd planners, approval or licensing  
officers and crowd managers. In turn this process fails to provide sufficient  
information to reduce, mitigate or act upon many risks. The process can be  
significantly enhanced using simple line diagrams and crowd models. We  
have outlined some of the many applications of RAMP analysis, and how  
comparative analysis, using diagrams with lines and arrows to represent  
crowd flow, can communicate key crowd risks. We have found that, when  
you begin to incorporate these models into your event documents, the event  
process \(planning, approval and operations\) is clearer for all parties, it aids  
understanding and it helps focus the users on the key issues.  
We could fill several books with case study examples from past and on-  
going projects, the examples in this chapter will give you some ideas on  
how to apply the RAMP and DIM-ICE approach to events. For further  
examples and information, coupled to an eLearning system where you can  
complete the course on-line, http\://www.gkstill.com/Education/ Chapter 7  
# Control room applications  
What we all want is public safety. We don’t want rhetoric that’s framed  
through ideology.  
Kamala Harris  
There are no secrets to success. It is the result of preparation, hard  
work, and learning from failure.  
Colin Powell  
INTRODUCTION  
When you are in charge of an event, you are responsible for the safety and  
welfare of every individual that attends your event. You are responsible for  
preventing all reasonably foreseeable incidents that may affect your crowd.  
You have a moral, ethical and legal responsibility for understanding all  
aspects of crowd risks. You need to provide a safe environment for the  
crowd attending your event and if you review the history of fatal incidents  
and mass fatalities around the world, you will note that the potential for  
injury and specifically for loss of life is an ever-present threat to your crowd.  
Although we have focussed on the planning and approval phase of the  
event process, if we assume the event plan is a robust document, there are  
still wide ranges of potential issues that can develop during the operation  
phase of the event. This chapter focusses on those issues, specifically on  
crowd monitoring and incident detection from a control room perspective.  
We have, throughout this book, demonstrated that the application of  
relatively simple and inexpensive modelling techniques can make a signifi-  
cant impact on our understanding of crowd risks. In this chapter, we will  
outline how those modelling techniques can assist in the control during the  
event. The visual mapping process can help the crowd safety manager, the  
police and other persons in the control room in understanding the event as  
it develops.  
189 190 Introduction to crowd science  
The objective of these modelling tools is to improve our understanding  
of crowd risks and to make the users more aware of the potential for crowd  
harm. In the previous chapters, we highlighted that risks are often design  
related, where the flow rate of the approaching crowds exceeds the capac-  
ity of a site component or throughput element \(such as an entry system\).  
In layperson’s terms, when crowds fill spaces too quickly or where entry  
points are not operating fast enough for the crowds to flow into the system,  
there will be problems.  
The fundamentals of calculating crowd flow, assessing the arrival rate  
\(the profile\), should be a core skill of the event planner and event licensing  
authority. It should also be a core skill in the control room. Staff may take  
up their position, and rely on the event planning process to have either  
eliminated or reduced the risk, or made the control team aware of the risks.  
In the previous chapter, we outlined how the risk assessment process may  
not transmit the relevant information in a way that can be quickly, and  
clearly, understood. Obviously, this can be an important factor for early  
detection of a risk; a process that is called ‘red flagging’, namely how are  
the early indicators of a crowd risk communicated from the field to the con-  
trol room, assessed and acted upon. One could argue that if an event begins  
to fail, whether this is due to a poor plan, or a significant deviation from the  
plan, then these early indicators are the last line of defence for crowd safety.  
It follows that risk awareness, understanding how the site should operate,  
is a safety-critical function.\*  
Site check  
It is always recommended that a site check is performed on the day of the  
event, to ensure that there is no significant deviation from the plan. This  
check should include a review of the risk analysis. A final walk around is  
a check to assess if the site is safe and the value of this check should not be  
underestimated. We have observed many events where last-minute changes  
have created significant operational challenges. We have seen generators  
appear in the last few minutes before an event starts, blocking emergency  
access and egress routes. Obviously, this kind of last-minute change can sig-  
nificantly alter the event safety and inevitable liabilities, if an incident occurs.  
As we stated at the start of this section, when you are in charge of an event,  
you are responsible for the safety and welfare of every individual that attends  
your event.  
There will be many readers who will think ‘That is obvious’; however,  
having worked in many events, reviewed many event plans and acted as  
an expert witness on several cases, where this has been a singular point of  
\*  
The phrase ‘never assume—it makes an ass out of U and me’ is most certainly a truism. Control room applications 191  
failure. Teams can be aware of the increase in risks, due to site changes, but  
will proceed with the event regardless.  
Crowd safety  
Crowd safety is a legal responsibility and failure to provide a safe environ-  
ment can result in prosecution. Crowd safety is not an ideology, and it is  
not a belief system; safety is a fundamental human right, the right to life\:  
In 1776, the United States Declaration of Independence declared that  
all men are endowed with certain inalienable rights, and that ‘among  
these are life, liberty, and the pursuit of happiness’.  
In 1948, the Universal Declaration of Human Rights, adopted by the  
United Nations General Assembly, declared in article three\: ‘Everyone  
has the right to life, liberty and security of person’.  
In 1950, the European Convention on Human Rights was adopted  
by the Council of Europe, declaring a protected human right to life in  
Article 2.  
In 1966, the International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights  
was adopted by the United Nations General Assembly. ‘Every human  
being has the inherent right to life. This right shall be protected by  
law. No one shall be arbitrarily deprived of his life’. \(Article 6.1 of the  
International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights\)  
Failure to provide a safe environment, not just within your event but also  
for the crowds entering and leaving your event, is a fundamental breach of  
your duty of care to the public. Although different countries have different  
guidance and legislation, to operate an event that has significant risk and to  
fail to understand or act upon that risk may be gross negligence. The consci-  
entious application of crowd risk analysis and safety engineering principles  
applies to the planning, approval and operational phases of the event process.  
Yet, we are always hearing of near misses in the teaching/training workshops,  
usually with a ‘this could have been another Hillsborough or Love Parade  
disaster’ comment. So, how do we make positive changes to the industry to  
improve the situation? Specifically, how do we improve control room aware-  
ness? One frequent question we are asked is\: ‘What are the early warning  
signs of a crowd problem?’  
INCIDENT DETECTION  
It is worth looking at a generic model of an event from a different perspec-  
tive to understand the concept of crisis management and where modelling  
fits into the process. It is essential to understand the cost/benefit analysis 192 Introduction to crowd science  
of an event that goes wrong and where early incident detection sits in the  
spectrum of costs.  
Four pillars of crisis management  
The four pillars of crisis management outline the pre- and post-incident  
cost analysis. The four pillars are defined as ‘anticipate’, ‘prepare/prevent’,  
‘respond’ and ‘recover’. Each pillar has a cost to implement and the overall  
event cost rises considerably when an incident occurs.  
Obviously, the objective is to prevent accidents/incidents from occurring.  
To achieve this goal, there must be some investment in the first two pil-  
lars \(anticipate, prepare/prevent\) of the planning process. There must also  
be investment in monitoring and early detection of incidents to facilitate  
successful intervention. Not surprisingly, the early detection of problems  
is closely linked to understanding crowd risks and how quickly they can  
develop into major incidents. The sole purpose of risk assessment and crowd  
management, of planning is to anticipate, prepare and prevent accidents or  
incidents from occurring. Deploying the appropriate resources to facilitate  
a rapid response and emergency/incident management will minimise this  
potential cost/exposure—but this has an associated cost. Obviously, pre-  
vention is the most cost-effective solution.  
Figure 7.1 indicates the relatively low cost of planning compared to the  
rapidly escalating cost of an incident, which will include irreparable brand  
damage, lawsuits and the historical association of a disaster to your brand.  
Resource expenditure and brand exposure

Figure 7.1  The four pillars of crisis management showing the cost escalation due to an  
incident. Brand exposure will be irreparably damaged if there are serious  
injuries or fatalities at an event, especially if these are avoidable. Control room applications 193  
For example, the Love Parade disaster is associated with the city of Duisburg.  
As a brand and a city, it is now associated with the mass fatalities at the event.  
The graph in Figure 7.1 shows that the cost increases considerably when an  
incident occurs. The scale on the vertical axis \(cost\) includes damage to the  
brand and reputation damage, resource costs plus litigation costs. If you have  
ever expressed concern about the cost of public liability insurance, Figure 7.1  
will give you some perspective on the outlay in the event of an incident.  
Obviously, the objective is to prevent crowd accidents or incidents from  
developing and there is a need to invest time and resources in the ‘antici-  
pate’ and ‘prepare/prevent’ elements, which is why we have emphasised  
the need for a more conscientious approach to risk analysis for crowds and  
events. Modelling helps in the first two pillars \(‘anticipate’ and ‘prepare/  
prevent’\) simply because it is cost effective.  
Global problem  
Around the world, the events industry operates on the same principle,  
minimum-wage staff, cost control, profit-driven focus and stories of near  
misses. Major events will often tender for a security or stewarding com-  
pany to deliver the manpower for events, driving costs \(and hence margins\)  
as low as possible during contract negotiations. These financial limitations  
create further problems. The event documentation is easily copied from  
past events; risk assessments can be ‘cut and paste’. If the last event plan  
was approved, copying it and submitting it again for a different event is a  
lower-cost option to creating a new event plan for each event. This element  
of cost reduction is endemic in the industry and it is compounded due to the  
onerous nature of compiling a new risk assessment for each new event. We  
stress that if the event and crowd management plan are ‘cut and paste’ from  
an older plan, it opens the organisation to claims of negligence if something  
goes wrong during the event.  
Subcontracting  
It can often be the case that the event planner will subcontract a stewarding or  
security company for the function of crowd management at a major event. In  
many cases, this means the subcontracting company is tasked with producing  
the crowd management plan and risk assessment. Obviously, this should be an  
integrated approach to the overall event plan, working with the subcontracts  
to understand the overall site risks and crowd management strategies.  
One problem that we have noted is that if control rooms are manned  
by staff who have had no part in the planning or approval processes, and  
who are given the event plan, expected to read and understand it, and  
then expected to manage the event safely, the reliance on the event and  
crowd management plan is paramount. In those cases, where the crowd 194 Introduction to crowd science  
management team is not part of the planning or approval process, the clar-  
ity of the event plan, specifically the crowd management plan, is a vital  
communication element for the safe and successful operation of an event.  
Specifically, the risk analysis and how that information relating to risk  
management is communicated to the control room team.  
It is not enough to hire an experienced team and hope for the best. There  
will be unique characteristics to every event and the crowd management  
plan and the risk analysis needs to both capture and communicate those  
features of the site that need specific attention. Furthermore, the crowd  
management experience in one environment may not be relevant to a dif-  
ferent environment. For example, the experience of managing a football  
crowd may not be relevant to a concert crowd at the same venue, or the  
experience of the crowd management process in one stadium may not apply  
to different stadiums’ geometry or crowd behaviour. It is a common mis-  
take we see across the industry, in every country.  
It is vital that the event planning \(and approval\) process considers the  
control room experience and ability to read and understand the crowd  
management plan. Engaging with the subcontracting company, early in the  
planning process, and validating that they have read the crowd manage-  
ment plan, have both understood and can deliver to this plan, is essential.  
Contractors who ‘turn up on the day’, who do not attend briefings and who  
take the attitude of ‘we know this stuff’ may expose the crowd to opera-  
tional risks.  
CONTROL ROOM  
The larger the event \(or crowd\), the more the consideration that needs to be  
given to the control room element of the event operations. Control rooms  
or event liaison teams \(ELTs\) require space to function. The location, con-  
struction, management and information systems with respect to crowd safety  
are all factors that may need to be considered. Control room staff roles  
and responsibilities, command and control structure, site knowledge, staff  
training, resource deployment, CCTV and radio communications are part  
of a long list of items that are essential for safe and efficient control room  
operations.  
Recognise the risk  
Hindsight is a wonderful thing; everyone can see the problems and solu-  
tions with hindsight. When we review past incidents, we note that one  
of the significant weaknesses in both the Hillsborough and Love Parade  
disasters was the inability of the crowd management team to recognise  
the crowd risks during the event and act in a timely manner to avert the Control room applications 195  
disaster. As we outlined in the previous chapters, the near misses are not  
recorded, experience is lost when key individuals change position, and  
information is neither captured nor disseminated across the industry. One  
of our objectives is to change that process and outline a set of best practices  
with the aim of improving crowd safety. Without a process of capturing  
and disseminating crowd risk analysis and best practices for major events,  
it is inevitable that major incidents will recur.  
Location of a control room is essential; a control room must have infor-  
mation from the event to function effectively. For example, lines of sight  
from the control room, whether via windows or CCTV, must be considered  
before the event starts. What the control room can see will influence their  
decision-making process and this can be the most important input during  
an event. We have seen many event plans that state ‘a control room will be  
available at location X, Y for the duration of the event’ or something simi-  
lar; yet they fail to recognise the importance of lines of sight, CCTV inputs  
and the importance of these for effective command and control decision-  
making and communication strategies.  
How the control room functions during the event is an obvious, yet some-  
times underestimated, component of the event risk analysis. In effect, the  
control room receives a range of information, processes this information,  
makes decisions and then sends out information for others to act upon dur-  
ing the event. The quality of those decisions is directly related to the way  
the staff in the control room can prioritise the information. Having worked  
in many different types of event control rooms, from the very simple to  
the highly sophisticated, we have observed many complex computer-aided  
decision support tools, and that many computerised systems integration  
is both a complex process and often unnecessary. Understanding the risk  
assessment, specifically the time, location, duration and potential sever-  
ity of the risks, is vital to risk management, and the design criteria of the  
modelling tools described in this book are aimed at assisting that control  
room function. Site diagrams and risk maps are visual aids and are a simple  
addition to an existing system in a control room.  
Best-laid plans  
Assuming we start with an excellent plan, one that has been checked and  
is fit for its intended purpose, it may be useless if the operational team has  
neither participated in its development nor read and understood the event  
plan. A poorly trained, poorly informed crowd management team could  
induce risks irrespective of the depth of safety considerations in the plan-  
ning and approval process. In other words, you can still mess up an event if  
you do not understand the event plan.  
The best laid schemes o’ Mice an’ Men,  
Gang aft agley, 196 Introduction to crowd science  
An’ lea’e us nought but grief an’ pain,  
For promis’d joy! \(Robert Burns, To a Mouse, 1785\)\*  
To paraphrase Rabbie Burns, the best-laid plans can still go wrong. It is  
vital that the crowd management plan is considered as a conduit for trans-  
ferring information from the event planning process to the event operations  
process with the objective of minimising risks, specifically if the opera-  
tions team is subcontracted. In our experience, an event plan that contains  
more diagrams is generally easier to understand. Which is why we focus on  
developing crowd management plan, including diagrams/presentation and  
communication strategies in our workshop training programmes, using  
diagrams to aid the understanding of crowd risks.  
Other side of the table  
If you are a security or stewarding company, you should not accept an event  
contract unless the plan is fit for its intended purpose, and that the site is  
suitable for the event. You should avoid event organisers that will not engage  
with you during the planning process, specifically if they are cost conscious  
and constantly trying to reduce staffing for safety-critical functions.  
If you accept a contract, you should read and ensure that you understand  
the event plan and, specifically, the crowd management plan. It is rela-  
tively simple to construct the appropriate diagrams from a written plan and  
we always recommend that this process should be part of contract accep-  
tance. You can expose your organisation, the event and the crowd to risks  
if you focus only on profit and not on understanding the potential event  
risks. Your primary duty of care, during event operations, is ensuring that  
the event runs to plan; so, it is vital that the plan is read, understood and  
actioned accordingly. This all sounds like common sense, but we are con-  
stantly surprised at the number of events, specifically those that go wrong,  
that can be attributed to lack of appropriate preparation.  
Subcontracting a security company, a group that may not be involved in  
the planning of an event, has obvious potential risks. So, from the event  
organiser’s perspective, the first element must be to ensure that the subcon-  
tracting company has thoroughly read and understood the event plan. Do  
not make this a ‘tick here if you have read the event plan’ exercise. Spend  
time making sure the site is understood, make sure the capacity, flow,  
usage, arrival profile, crowd nature and behaviour are agreed upon with  
the subcontractor, and the subcontractor can provide adequate resources  
for the event. Bear in mind that the industry is a minimum-wage, cost man-  
agement business and that corners may \(will\) be cut wherever possible. The  
\*  
Translation—The best-laid schemes of Mice and Men oft go awry, And leave us nothing  
but grief and pain, For promised joy! Control room applications 197  
consequences of failure, where crowds may be injured, are too great to  
ignore. As we have stated previously, during the workshops, we always ask  
‘how many here have had a close call?’ and ‘how many people here have  
read the risk assessment?’ It may come as no surprise that the vast majority  
of delegates admit to one or other of these oversights or short cuts.  
As we have taught the principles and applications for crowd and event  
modelling, the common feature is how rarely the event plan, safety concept,  
risk assessment and risk management process is considered as a principle  
method of analysis. More often than not, event plans are largely ‘cut and  
paste’, so the errors, misconceptions and risks remain problems ‘lying in  
wait’ for the next time.  
The analysis of risk starts in the plan should be checked in the approval  
process and checked again by the operational team on the day of the event.  
We have observed many times that elements appear on the day of the event  
that did not feature in the event plan. For example, mobile generators  
blocking or severely restricting entry/exit points. All routes that the crowds  
use, specifically if these routes are used frequently during the event need to  
be kept clear of obstructions; this should include the entry and exit systems,  
interface to the local transport infrastructure and crowd management  
resources. The competitive nature of the tendering process, where a com-  
pany is contracted into an event to provide crowd management resources,  
must not compromise crowd safety. Provision of adequately trained staff,  
in sufficient numbers, is one important element that simply cannot be com-  
promised for a major event. This applies to both parties in the contract, as  
the saying goes, ‘pay peanuts and you will get monkeys’. Ultimately, the  
duty of care to public safety is the event organiser’s responsibility, and that  
responsibility is not discharged to a sub-contractor.  
Plan, prepare, prevent  
If the event organiser has created an event and crowd management plan  
that is fit for its intended purpose, and the approval process has checked  
the plan and licenced \(or permitted\) the event. There are a range of ele-  
ments, specifically any sudden changes in the weather, which can give rise  
to operational problems. Obviously, the plan should have considerations  
for potential problems and how they should be managed during the event.  
We would argue that a thoroughly researched event plan should have con-  
tingency elements for all reasonably foreseeable considerations. All rea-  
sonably anticipated incidents should have a contingency plan and there  
should be nothing unexpected during the event. We often hear the phrase  
‘to expect the unexpected’ but this is, of course, an oxymoron.\* What is  
\*  
An apparent contradiction of terms, how can we expect the unexpected when it is, by defi-  
nition, not predictable? 198 Introduction to crowd science  
essential is that the combination of thorough planning and efficient execu-  
tion of the crowd management plan will manage the potential risks for  
all reasonably foreseeable risks, a fundamental issue, the definition of  
reasonably foreseeable. Only when the crowd risk assessment process is  
improved, when we have an accessible collation of near misses and stan-  
dards of competency defined, will the ‘Phew! We were lucky that time’  
moments be reduced.  
The process of training, desktop exercises and working with multi-  
agency teams can all help prepare for the event specifically if there is a focus  
on modelling how incidents can develop with an emphasis on crowd risk  
analysis. There are, of course, many other potential risks, such as ensuring  
that all temporary demountable structures are built to code, but those are  
beyond the scope of this book, there are experts in those types of specific  
risks, such as site construction, and we recommend the reader is well versed  
in all elements of event planning and safety.  
UNDERSTANDING CROWD DYNAMICS  
In the control room, if an incident does occur, one that may not be reason-  
ably foreseeable, you must be able to recognise, respond and recover from  
that incident promptly and efficiently. Specifically, you need to have the  
resources to be able to respond to an incident; to understand how the crowd  
may react to incidents as they unfold. Understanding how quickly crowd  
density can develop, understanding the risks of shockwaves in high-density  
situations and understanding crowd flow and how quickly areas can fill  
and potentially fail \(become dangerously overcrowding\) should be part  
of the crowd manager’s training. Failing to provide adequate emergency  
facilities can be perceived as negligent or reckless. In our experience, this is  
rarely the case as there is no requirement for core competency in many of  
the techniques described in this book. The low-margin, low-cost nature of  
the industry means that training is often neglected, record keeping is poor  
and, specifically, industry-wide knowledge sharing is non-existent.  
Planning, approval and operations  
The event process, as we have outlined, consists of a planning phase, an  
approval phase and an operational phase. In the previous chapters, we have  
focussed on the planning and approval applications of crowd/event risk  
analysis and crowd/event modelling. The objectives of modelling is to under-  
stand potentially complex environments and, in doing so, to develop better  
control mechanisms. We build models to help us discover aspects of the  
event that may not be clear in written documents, or that may only exist as  
personal experience. The process of modelling is a process of ­ discovery; this Control room applications 199  
helps us understand, and through that understanding, we can implement the  
appropriate controls to maintain safe and orderly crowds.  
There are many advantages of using these types of model during the  
operational phase of the event. Specifically, we use models designed to indi-  
cate the location, duration and severity of risks in the control room to assist  
the decision-making process during an event. To achieve this, we must  
ensure that these models are simple to understand and relate to situations  
as they develop in real time. Although this is no substitute for experience, it  
does help capture and communicate experience from operator to operator,  
event to event and site to site.  
Firstly, it is important to stress that even a good event plan, a plan that  
has been checked and approved and is fit for its intended purpose, can still  
go wrong due to mismanagement. For example, an entry system that is not  
fully manned as defined in the event plan will obviously not be as efficient  
during ingress. If fewer people gain entry than planned, queues will develop.  
This can then lead to excessive queueing and a potential pressure building  
up at the entry point. That would be a ‘management’ failure rather than a  
design failure. The design was OK, but it was not managed appropriately.  
Equally, a poorly designed entry system can be managed safely if the  
risks are identified during the operational phase early enough to intervene.  
For this specific example \(entry system design and operations\), it is essen-  
tial that the system capacity \(throughput\), the arrival profile, the potential  
crowd profile/behaviour are known to the control room operators. Without  
knowing how the entry system should perform, what the ingress rate should  
be, and how large the crowd/queue may develop, the control room opera-  
tors and crowd management team will not recognise escalating risks.  
As the history of accidents and incidents shows, the majority of injuries  
occur during ingress/egress. Therefore, we must be extra vigilant at those  
times during an event. Models, specifically diagrams, can assist us in iden-  
tifying if everything is going to plan. There are a number of ways of achiev-  
ing this and we will use the entry system as an example of how to assess  
risks during the ingress phase of the event operations.  
Entry system  
The miscalculating \(or missed calculation\) of ingress/egress rates were fac-  
tors in both the Hillsborough \(1989, the UK, 96 dead, 400 injured\) and  
Love Parade \(2010, Germany, 21 dead, 541 injured\) disasters. We outlined  
in Chapter 2 how crowd density effects flow rates; this is an important  
element in the design considerations for ingress/egress and any parts of an  
event where crowds are moving in multiple directions. It should be obvious  
from this that an efficient ingress system should not result in higher density  
as this will reduce flow rate and increase risk. If an ingress queue begins to  
develop high-density crowding, then something must be going wrong! 200 Introduction to crowd science  
High-density crowds should be a clear indicator of risk, specifically in  
any part of an event site that is predominately utilised for moving crowds.  
All parties involved in the event crowd management need to understand  
the relationship between crowd movement, crowd density and the potential  
for overcrowding and crushing. We have illustrated, through case studies,  
how these should be addressed in the planning and approval phases of the  
event process. If these are observed during an event, there may be very little  
time to react to the situation, specifically if there is a trip, slip or fall in high  
density. Where we have deployed flow and risk models, graphics and visual  
reminders, in the control room, it aids operational understanding.  
In the control room, several models can assist in understanding the safe  
density, the site risks and the expected rates of passage. We developed visu-  
als aids, some as simple red/orange/green indicators on large-scale maps.  
In several control room applications, a flip chart can be used. Simply copy  
the crowd flow and risk maps for each phase of the event, for example, the  
ingress, circulation and egress maps, each showing the location of expected  
crowd movement/density and/or risk. Control rooms can be small spaces  
or large rooms with multi-agency teams working on computers and CCTV/  
monitors, but there is always space to pin a diagram to a wall, or have a pad  
by your desk to aid understanding of how the event will unfold.  
Figure 7.2 shows an image from the Sydney Olympics control room. In the  
centre, there is a model \(in this case, back-projected onto a large screen\). We  
were involved in the conceptual design of this system, including modelling  
Figure 7.2  The control room at the Sydney Olympics showing the CCTV images of  
the event alongside the event model \(centre of the far wall\). The model was  
showing the expected crowd movements. The control room team could  
compare the model \(expectation\) to the CCTV \(real-time images\) and help  
make critical decisions for crowd management. \(From John Webster, Sydney  
Olympics project project files—GKStill.\) Control room applications 201  
the entire site over the entire event duration. This was then coded into an  
Excel spreadsheet, updated and displayed throughout the event. Figure 7.3  
shows one of the maps, indicating the anticipated crowd movements at a  
specific hour during the event. This used five colours \(green, blue, yellow,  
orange and red\), to indicate the expected crowd density. The visual nature  
of this diagram, updated for key times during the games, provided valuable  
insights for the control room team and the model was compared to the  
CCTV monitors, as a visual check of the current situation relating to crowd  
movements on the Olympic park.  
This model gave the control room operators an expectation \(the colours  
on the model, indicating crowd flow\) and the CCTV images \(the reality\). It  
helped them understand the overall site dynamics and they could check if  
the site was operating to plan. The system read information the cells of the  
spreadsheet, each of which indicated the loading and unloading of venues  
and how the routes between the various venues would populate during the  
periods between events \(change-over\).  
Figure 7.3  The plan of the common domain of the Sydney Olympics site. \(From John  
Webster, Sydney Olympics project files—GKStill.\) 202 Introduction to crowd science  
Simple systems  
The Sydney Olympics model was a spreadsheet model that would display  
the colours during operations. Simpler systems, for smaller events, use the  
same principle although it is more cost effective to produce these as a series  
of paper site plans rather than computerised versions with projected dis-  
plays. We have advised and installed many similar systems that display the  
extent of the crowd movements, expected congestion, \(marked as areas on  
a map or site plan\), the severity of the risk \(red–orange–green indicators of  
expected crowd density\) and the duration of the risk.  
Modelling ingress systems  
Without some form of model of the ingress system, you may find it dif-  
ficult to judge the efficiency of the queueing system during operations. We  
always advise that operations staff should have some clear indications of  
the expected arrival profile \(how many people will arrive over what period  
of time\) and the subsequent queue management strategy. For major events  
where a large number of people may be arriving over a short period, some  
queueing will be expected. Understanding, in advance, the location, dura-  
tion, extent and potential crowd congestion will assist in the operational  
management of the entry system.  
If you are creating an event plan, ingress flow rates should be calcu-  
lated and factored into the plan accompanied with appropriate diagrams  
for approval and for operations. When developing these plans indicate, in  
the diagram, the area that may be occupied by the queue. Doing this will  
provide a visual aid for event operations team, so they know if the system  
is working as planned, and more importantly, they can raise an alert if the  
queue is developing faster than expected. This is important in planning, but  
during operations, it acts as the early warning indicator before it turns into  
an incident or develops into a disaster.  
Queueing  
The ingress system should be designed to minimise queueing, or there  
should be a management consideration for the arriving crowds. A screening  
process, for example, may develop short-term queueing but there should be  
consideration for the size, extent and duration of wait in the queue. Shelter  
from the sun or rain may need to be a factor in the queueing. Duration of  
wait, rate of processing, searching/screening, weather conditions, welfare,  
VIP and mobility-impaired access are factors that should be considered in  
the crowd management plan. Specifically, how many people do you expect  
to be queueing? How long will the crowd be expected to queue \(duration\)?  
How much space will the crowd occupy? Control room applications 203  
If you cannot answer those questions during the event planning phase,  
then you need to engage the services of an expert \(or attend a training  
course\) to maintain safe and efficient crowd management during ingress. If  
you work in a licensing/approval or permitting capacity, you must be able  
to check that the appropriate calculations are present in the document and  
be confident to reject a plan that does not consider the queueing system  
during ingress. If you are working in crowd management, this informa-  
tion should be available so that you can assess if the system is working as  
planned during the event.  
Modelling an ingress system is neither onerous nor costly, but it is an  
essential process for safe crowd management. Inefficient, badly designed,  
poorly maintained or inadequately managed ingress systems, will result in  
excessive queueing. In turn, this will produce crowd frustration and poten-  
tially unpredictable crowd behaviour. Well-designed, well-managed and  
well-informed crowds are generally happier, more compliant and easier to  
manage. Disney has spent many years and invested in understanding crowd  
behaviour and queueing systems and gets it right, first time, every time for  
every new attraction they add to their theme parks.  
Queuing systems require an analysis of the arrival rate and the service  
rate. The arrival rate is the rate at which people will arrive, at the queueing  
system, over a period of time. The service rate is the rate at which people  
can be processes through the queueing system.  
REAL-TIME DECISION SUPPORT TOOL  
We have worked on several major events where a simple chart on the wall  
\(similar to Figure 7.4\) indicates the time crowds are expected. This can be  
very useful to display as a full-colour chart, where the resolution of the  
time \(hourly, half-hourly, etc.\) will depend on the nature and duration of  
the event, the means of arrival \(train, bus, coach, etc.\) and/or potential  
celebrity attendance times.

Coupling this information with a site risk map, a graph showing the  
arrival profile \(similar to the chart in Figure 7.5\) provides insights into  
the operational team about the expected crowds at the event. When the  
control room team have a clear idea about crowd arrival, they can monitor  
the situation and then be in a position to act quickly if there are signifi-  
cant changes from the plan. Essentially, first modelling the site, and then  
monitoring the situation as the event develops facilitate good crowd man-  
agement. Without a background model, the risks may pass unnoticed and  
develop into an incident or result in one of those ‘Phew! We were lucky  
that time’ moments.  
Do we really need this?  
There are many events where no site or event model is constructed and the  
maps, plans or diagrams are either not considered or a general site plan,  
overpopulated with information from every agency, is pinned to the wall in  
a control room, and never referred to again. As we previously stated, one of  
the first questions we ask during the workshops is ‘How many people in this  
room have run an event and felt they were lucky to avoid a major incident?’  
Of course, many events run without incident, but the few that do not  
are significant cause for concern. As it takes a few minutes to draw a site  
diagram and annotate it, and this is neither onerous nor highly skilled, we  
recommend that the practice should be universally applied to all events,  
large or small. We suggest the use of tracing sheet, a method we described in  
the previous chapters, and that you colour the areas of concern, highlighting  
risk in red. Practise this method and you will find that it adds to your under-  
standing of the event dynamics and the developing risks. Think about your  
control room and how to display this information so that all the operators  
can see the risks as the event unfolds.  
Consider the site plan as a primary method of communicating through-  
out the event planning and licence/permit application process and simplify Control room applications 205  
the diagrams to include crowd flow and potential congestion. Produce  
many different types of plans of the same area, one for risk, one indicating  
crowd flow, one for normal situations \(ingress, circulation and egress\) and  
one for emergencies. As you produce more diagrams, they will aid your  
own understanding, but more importantly, it will provide the control room  
with information for the operators to process during the event.  
REAL-TIME INFORMATION  
We have been asked, many times, to comment on the use of sophisticated  
technology in the control room, specifically a combination of CCTV  
images and video processing, and automated crowd counting. These are  
expensive systems and beyond the budget of the vast majority of events,  
and the argument that technology can replace key decision making in the  
control room is an interesting but misleading one. As we outlined, the use  
of simulations for crowd control, specifically for event planning, is a white  
elephant.\* The same problems exist with control room and wide-area inte-  
grated technology. Consider the costs of installation, training and support  
for the hardware and software, site/camera calibration and development.  
These are significant costs and at some point, the burden of cost falls on  
the event organiser.  
Is there a low-cost alternative?  
Several costs may need to be considered for the successful deployment of  
a real-time decision support system. By this, we mean some method of  
counting or estimating the crowds and providing useful and timely infor-  
mation to the control room staff. We may need to keep an audit trail of  
the counting information for future planning. If you consider the arrival  
profile, in some cases, there may be larger numbers of people arriving  
shortly before the event starts such as a firework display, or there may be  
events where the crowds arrive hours before the event starts, for example,  
a pop concert.  
We could, using some method, count people entering \(and leaving\) an  
event to ensure that the space does not become overcrowded. We could also  
assess density \(number of people per square metre\) in key locations. Some  
events lend themselves to regulating entry and some have multiple entry/  
exit points and no ticketing. Here are a few low cost techniques that have  
proven to be effective for monitoring crowd flow and density.  
\*  
A white elephant is an idiom for a valuable but burdensome possession of which its owner  
cannot dispose and whose cost \(particularly cost of upkeep\) is out of proportion to its use-  
fulness or worth. 206 Introduction to crowd science  
Wrist bands  
Imagine a hypothetical event that runs all day. In the morning, we may have  
children-based activities; in the afternoon, a family-based performance;  
and in the evening, we are planning a pop concert followed by a fireworks  
display. Our site has 5000 square metres of available space after we have  
set out various stages, stalls, merchandising, a fun fair and our emergency  
services access/egress. Our license condition states that the maximum num-  
ber of people on site, at any given time, should not exceed 10,000 people.  
The event is free and we do not know how many people will attend. The  
problem is to regulate the crowd numbers on the site to ensure the capac-  
ity does not exceed the licence condition \(no more than 10,000 people on  
site at any one time\). We could count people in and out, tally this on some  
computer system and inform the operators that we are approaching the  
limit. That would require two-way information from each gate \(number in  
and number out\) passed to a central control. Someone would need to enter  
this information and then instruct the entry/exit gates about the number of  
people onsite, and how many more people could enter. This is a fairly com-  
plicated situation and, at first glance, it seems to lend itself to some form  
of computerised counting system, perhaps a CCTV counting system that  
would automate the entire process. We can see how the cost of radios, the  
operators, their training and the extent of the crowd control could be offset  
against a computerised system. At first glance, it may appear that automat-  
ing the counting process would reduce errors and ensure safety and the cost  
of an automated counting system would be justified.  
There is a very low-cost solution and it has been used on a number of  
large events very successfully—rubber wrist bands. You buy a box of these,  
better still you canvass the local businesses and ask if anyone would like  
to promote their business on branded wrist bands and have a sponsor pay  
for the box of bands. We have used these ourselves at several events, a local  
business sponsored the event, buying 10,000 branded wrist bands that bore  
their logo and contact number.  
How this works, in practice, is that we start with 10,000 wrist bands  
distributed around the entry points. Everyone who enters the site is given a  
wrist band and asked to hand it in when leaving the site. As long as there  
are wrist bands in the box, the site is below the capacity. If any entry/exit  
point was running short of bands, a runner was sent around the entry/  
exit points to redistribute the remaining wrist bands. This process did not  
require computerisation or accurate counting; handfuls of bands taken  
from one entry/exit point to another were sufficient to maintain stocks.  
This automates the process regulating site capacity, the number of people  
in/out without any technology and, it did not cost the event organisers a  
single penny. The advantage was that when people left at the end of the  
event, they kept the wrist band \(and the advertising\) as a souvenir/keepsake. Control room applications 207  
This has proven to be a cost-effective method for a local business to promote  
their services or a local charity to further their cause. Events are a low mar-  
gin industry, solutions such as sponsored wrist bands, are practical, prag-  
matic and realistic solution to a key issue of maintaining safe site capacity.  
Crowd counting  
There are some free events, such as a city-based fireworks display, where the  
crowds will arrive in numbers that can exceed the display area’s capacity.  
One example of this is the London New Year event. For this event, the pro-  
cess of handing out wrist band would not work, there are too many people  
and, as you will see from the analysis below, they arrive close to the start of  
the event. For this type of crowd arrival profile, we must not introduce any  
element that may delay the crowd entering the site. Such delays could cause a  
crush at the entry points, we need to design a free-flowing system. It is essen-  
tial that, during the site design phase of event planning, you understand  
the crowd arrival profile, to ensure that the infrastructure of the event can  
cope with that arrival profile. Both the Hillsborough and Love Parade events  
experienced ingress crushing and we cannot overemphasise the importance  
of balancing the arrival profile with the ingress/entry capacity.  
MASS TRANSIT SYSTEMS  
The London New Year Event \(LYNE\) site is accessible via London  
Underground, which can deliver tens of thousands of people per hour and  
has a number of viewing areas. Many more people may want to attend  
than the space would safely accommodate. The problem we were asked to  
produce for this event was a method of regulating crowd flow by distribut-  
ing the crowds across several viewing areas. In the last chapter, we outlined  
how closing off Westminster Bridge would regulate the crowd egress; this  
would also assist in distributing the crowds during ingress. Using the prin-  
ciples of design, information and management influences on crowd behav-  
iour, the first task was to analyse the site design, the streets and the routes  
from the stations to the event spaces.  
Figure 7.6 shows part of the site analysis using a network analysis tool  
developed for assessing the ‘station to viewing area’ crowd flows. We can  
perform the same analysis using pen and paper or by using a spreadsheet  
model. This tool was custom designed for testing different arrival profiles,  
the tool allows us to check different levels of crowd flow. By taking the data  
from the London Underground ticket machines, we can derive an initial  
model for the arrival profile. Applying that profile to the routes, we can  
experiment with closing some routes \(Figure 7.7\). 208 Introduction to crowd science

If a system has multiple entry points, then the space will fill more  
quickly than it would with fewer entry points. There needs to be a con-  
sideration among the number of entry points, the approach routes and  
the transportation system capacity. As stated in the last section, we need  
to balance the arrival profile with the event ingress capacity. In this case,  
we use the direct routes to the site as a free-flowing system. The London
