Underground stations exit points and the direct roads from the stations  
to the event viewing areas, were defined as the primary ingress routes; all  
other routes were closed off \(using barriers\) and available for emergency  
access and egress.  
London new year events  
A manual clicker-counter is used on each of the primary routes to count  
one minute of crowd flow every 15 min. This only provides us with an  
approximation of crowd flow, but complete accuracy is not the objective.  
We are estimating when the space is going to be 80% full; this is the condi-  
tion that we will begin a diversion strategy to the other viewing areas. The  
objective is to inform the crowd management team of the rate of fill, and  
whether this is much faster than, slower than or the same as the expected  
flow based on previous years.  
If we know the space is filling faster than the previous years, faster than  
expected, then we need some estimate of when the space will be full. Again,  
we do not need complete accuracy for this time; from 2100, when people  
begin to arrive in mass, the initial input \(Figure 7.8\) indicates that ‘at this  
rate, the space will be full at 2315’.  
From that information, we can begin our diversion strategy at 2200. The  
first step is to inform the people who are about to start on their journey that  
the event may be full when they arrive. Information will influence crowd  
behaviour. The objective of the event is to have the best fireworks display in  
the world, to ensure that the crowds enjoy the event. As such, the event is  
Figure 7.8 Screen image of the fill predictor/flow estimator from the London New Year  
Event. As data is entered, it is both error checked and then projected to provide an  
estimate of the fill time. 210 Introduction to crowd science  
advertised as a free event with the underlying message of ‘everyone can see  
this, up close, for free’. The population that may want to attend the event  
and the capacity of the London Underground exceeds the event space, so  
understanding when the event will be full, a fill predictor is essential to the  
crowd management strategy.  
Things we know  
For all projects, we start with a list of things we know. In this project, we  
knew the event capacity, which is 186,000 people, based on the available  
space. We know the typical arrival profile for fireworks displays, based on  
the experience of similar events around the world, and we know the arrival  
profile for this event, based on the London Underground ticket machine  
data. We can use this information to determine the routes, the required  
capacity of the ingress system and the rate at which the event will fill.  
The arrival profile can be highly variable on the evening of the event due  
to weather, transport problems, alternative events in the area and so on.  
The purpose of the clicker/counting estimations is therefore a check on the  
rate of arrival and not, as such, an accurate counting system.  
The objective is to have controls in place, for each viewing area, when  
it reaches 50% of its capacity and to regulate entry flow, coupled to the  
diversion strategies, at 80% of its capacity. With the ability to anticipate  
when each area is going to be full, we can then issue a wide-area message  
throughout the London Underground network that the ‘event is now full’  
at the appropriate time.  
We issue that message at the time provided by the fill predictor. We have  
a reasonable estimate of the number of people that may be on the under-  
ground system, heading to the event. We also have a good estimate of the  
number of people already inside the event space. We know when the space  
will be full, from the clicker-counter, the rate the crowds are entering the  
event, and we can work back from this information to send our message  
of ‘event full’. This is similar to the advance warning you get for large car  
parks; they may say how many spaces are left, or that the car park is full.  
This prevents queueing and also shapes expectations. This is an important  
behavioural element if people expect to get into an event, they are turned  
away because the event is full, the crowd may react. If, on the other hand,  
you have pre-conditioned the crowd by informing them that the ‘event is  
full’, they will either turn back or if they decide to continue regardless,  
they may be pleasantly surprised to gain entry. This is a psychological  
strategy. We are shaping the crowd’s expectation. Similar strategies are  
used around the world to great effect. It is important that you understand  
the difference between a crowd that expects to get into an event and is then  
denied entry and a crowd that does not expect to get into the event and is  
then allowed entry. Obviously, there is a point during the evening when the Control room applications 211  
decision needs to be made to switch the message to ‘event full’ and that is  
the purpose of the sample counting system described above.  
When asked about integrating accurate counting technology to major  
events, we always look to the quality of the information, what we can do  
with that information and how accurate we need the data to be to achieve  
the objective. Examples such as rubber wrist band, clicker counting, and  
the like demonstrate that low-cost solutions can and do work for major  
events. The emphasis is not on the technology but on the crowd safety  
requirements, the command and control structure, and the actions required  
for timely intervention. If you are going to invest in CCTV, video processing  
and crowd counting technology, first consider the importance of the data,  
how you are going to act on that data and if the investment improves upon  
low-cost alternatives. Remember the sales representative may be motivated  
more by his bonus than the improvement his/her system offers for crowd  
safety and that technology, by itself, does not manage the crowd. It is,  
at best, information to assist the crowd manager. A badly designed event  
ingress system, one where the arrival profile exceeds the ingress capacity,  
will not suddenly begin to operate more safely by accurately counting the  
crowds. CCTV counting technology will not make such a system safe.  
ESTIMATING DENSITY  
There are events that do not lend themselves to counting methods because  
there are too many potential points of entry. For those, we still need some  
method of regulating crowd flow, or a diversion strategy. One example of  
this is the Royal Wedding \(2011\) in London. For this type of project we  
start with estimating the number of people who can safely attend the event.  
This comes from an area analysis where Internet mapping tools, survey  
maps, event plans or site surveys inform us of the available viewing areas.  
We know the length of the route, and the attendance from previous events  
\(video footage\). From this information, we can estimate the crowd sizes  
in key locations, for example, along the Mall, around the Queen Victoria  
Memorial and so on.  
We cannot barrier off the entire site and control access to those areas, but  
we can evaluate the crowd requirements and design an attractive alterna-  
tive. In this case, the site capacity was large, but there was a probability that  
the crowds would exceed the safe capacity. In this case, we considered large  
screens in Hyde Park, which had a capacity of several hundred thousand.  
Diversion strategy  
The strategy was to divert the crowd to larger areas at Hyde Park, and  
the solution was information based. The crowd would not want to go to 212 Introduction to crowd science  
the Hyde Park area; they would want to go to the Mall, the areas close to  
Buckingham Palace.  
When the key locations such as the Mall, Queen Victoria Memorial,  
and the areas close to Buckingham Palace were 80% of their capacity,  
we need to divert any further crowds to the alternative site. We cannot  
count the arriving crowds, as there are many possible underground sta-  
tions, bus routes, local population, business and car parks around the  
key locations. The first consideration was Green Park station, the station  
closest to the site, and we advised this station is closed for the event.\*  
There was not enough space to manage the crowds between Green Park  
station and the event site. We need the crowds to approach the key  
locations in such a way that we can reroute the approaching crowds to  
the larger site \(Hyde Park\) when the primary site \(Buckingham Palace  
areas\) were full. For that we could use information to influence crowd  
behaviour.  
Uniformity of signage  
The subtlety we used was to uniformly sign the event as ‘The Royal  
Wedding Event’ on VMS \(variable message signs\) from the Underground  
stations and along key routes to the site. This allowed us to switch the  
direction without changing the description. Consider the crowd reaction if  
we had switched the information from ‘Buckingham Palace’ to ‘Hyde Park’.  
The crowd would want to go to “Buckingham Palace” not “Hyde Park” but  
by using the words “Royal Wedding Event” we could reroute before key  
locations exceeded the safe crowd capacity.  
We mapped out the primary routes from all the stations to the most  
desirable viewing areas \(around Buckingham Palace\). This meant we could  
divert the crowds when required. The question in the control room was  
when should the messaging/information system reroute the crowds.  
Since we could not sample count in the way we designed for the London  
New Year event, we needed another method of estimating the crowd num-  
bers. For that we produced a series of graphic images, a set for each CCTV  
camera and helicopter, which gave the control room a visual guide for 80%  
full. At that time, we switch the signs to divert the crowd to Hyde Park.  
Figures 7.9 through 7.11 show a few sample screens produced for the con-  
trol room for this event.  
We need to stress that this process may be replicated using a wide  
range of tools, but you need to ensure that the crowd packing is not grid  
based, in other words, the way crowds fill space is not the same a chess  
board, where each character occupies their own square. Crowds have a  
\*  
We seem to make a habit of shutting down London Underground Stations for events. Control room applications 213  
Figure 7.9  Photograph of the crowd leaving Wembley Stadium along Olympic Way and  
heading towards Wembley Park Station.  
Figure 7.10  Image 1 from the modelling system.  
Figure 7.11  Image 2 from the modelling system. 214 Introduction to crowd science  
characteristic distribution \(spaces between individuals\) and visuals, to  
be realistic, need to emulate the spaces between people. We produced  
a system that has a sophisticated algorithm \(numerical recipe\) to mimic  
the packing of real crowds; it is not a simulation and it only illustrates  
the crowd density to assist the control room in understanding the density  
limits. When you couple this with a crowd management strategy, you can  
populate any given map, plan, diagram, satellite image, with a realistic  
looking crowd. This achieves the objective, estimating the crowd density  
and hence site/area capacity, at low cost.  
The reason we needed to produce this, and to show crowds from differ-  
ent angles, similar to the angles you would observe from the control room,  
is best demonstrated using the following series of images. Try to estimate  
the crowd density in each of these diagrams.  
Each of these images indicates the same crowd density shown from dif-  
ferent angles. Most people are surprised at this, thinking that the crowd  
density looks much higher in some images than in others. This is, of course,  
why we chose this set of images and why we developed the density estima-  
tor. We would produce a series of graphics, matching the CCTV angles,  
and print these off as a quick reference for control room operators. Again,  
this is low-cost technology and the control room only needs to flip the book  
of images to the one that looks closest to their image on the CCTV screen  
to be able to estimate density.  
The crowd management strategy for rerouteing the crowds to Hyde Park  
was based on graphic references. When the CCTV image looks like the  
graphic, switch the sign’s direction arrow from Buckingham Palace to Hyde  
Park. We do this at 80% of the event capacity, to allow for those individuals  
who are in transit, between the signs and the viewing areas. If we wait until  
we are at 100% of the event capacity, the site will overfill with the people  
in transit. We need some buffer, so we act early, but not too early, with the  
diversion strategy. If we have underestimated the crowd density, we can  
always allow more to enter later. It is far more difficult to turn people away  
once they are committed to a course of action.  
In effect, this is a sleight of mind technique; the subtle change of direction  
must be coupled to a suitable reward at the destination. The large screens  
provide the crowd with a great view of the event. The Hyde Park site has  
more space, attractions, picnic areas, merchandising, food and beverage, toi-  
lets and so on. The crowd enjoyed the event and all had a great time.  
Behind the scenes of this event, and all major events, there is a contin-  
gency plan for emergencies. We have already outlined that an emergency  
can occur during ingress, mid-event \(circulation\) and during egress, where  
crowd movements present different management challenges and where  
design, information and management can influence behaviour. We need to  
expand on these fundamental concepts in the context of the control crowd  
risk analysis for emergencies. Control room applications 215  
EMERGENCY MANAGEMENT  
The topic of emergency management is a subject of many other books and  
we would recommend that anyone intent on a career in event or crowd  
management should attend courses on emergency management and contin-  
gency planning. We focus most of our work on the prevention of incidents  
through good design, information and management principles and applica-  
tions; prevention is always the preferred option.  
It is worth considering the disaster recovery cycle in the context of crowd  
science as that provides a perspective to secondary problems that can arise  
during an incident, specifically crowd build-up during ingress and egress.  
When dealing with an incident, one that may be minor \(such as a problem  
with one of the entry gates\) or non-life-threatening, the crowd build-up can  
develop into a situation that is life-threatening. For example, the Love Parade  
\(Duisburg 2010\) disaster developed from a late start \(one hour later than  
planned\). While the site was being prepared, entry was delayed and the crowd  
density built up outside the entry point. When the ramp began to fail and the  
crowd congestion on the ramp reached critical levels, the crowd congestion on  
the entry system was beyond its design capacity, and was difficult to manage.  
Entry points failed, the ramp failed and the crowd flowed into the system.  
Disaster recovery cycle  
We need to outline the essential principles of a crowd disaster, such as Love  
Parade and Hillsborough \(and many others\) to highlight the control room  
function and consequential crowd risks. The diagram in Figure 7.12 shows  
a typical disaster recovery cycle. At the centre of this diagram is the legend  
‘crowd congestion builds up from the incident to recovery’. Meaning, the  
crowd may be unaware of a problem in the system and continue moving  
towards an incident location.  
To highlight this important aspect of incident management, we will out-  
line the various elements of the disaster recovery cycle—from incident to  
recovery.  
Operational  
The site is functioning during the operational phase. At some point, an inci-  
dent is detected and the control room shifts into an ‘incident’ or ‘major inci-  
dent’ phase. The event staff would typically manage minor incidents. Major  
incidents \(emergency situations\) would normally be under the control of the  
emergency team \(police or fire services\). What is important to note is that  
when a major incident is declared, the primacy \(the control\) will shift to the  
emergency team. This normally indicates that the event plan has failed. As  
we stressed in previous chapters, the primary duty of care, to provide a safe 216 Introduction to crowd science  
Figure 7.12  The disaster recovery cycle. During a major event, the crowd congestion  
can build up while the incident is in progress. It is essential to consider the  
consequences of blocking an entry/exit for a crowd in motion.  
environment, is the responsibility of the event organiser. During an inci-  
dent consequential action should not introduce further risks to the crowd.  
Crowd congestion can build up, at various points around the system, dur-  
ing the incident. This was the cycle at the Love Parade \(Duisburg 2010\)  
where crowd congestion continued to build up at the entry points during  
the initial delay, and again when the police actions began.  
Incident  
If an incident occurs at an event, it is essential, to understand the cause  
of the incident and the potential impact of emergency actions. Desktop  
exercises, coupled to modelling, site analysis \(RAMP and DIM-ICE\), and  
training will help assess this potential impact during the planning phase.  
However, emergency response teams and, specifically, incident command-  
ers need to understand the plan. A review of major incidents illustrates a  
significant disconnect between the critical functions of the control room,  
crowd management and incident management systems during incidents.  
For example, communication systems that are restricted to a single agency,  
e.g., event radio systems, police radio systems, ambulance and fire radio  
systems, may not be compatible. This can mean that operational and inci-  
dent management teams are unable to communicate.  
Anyone at the event, members of the public, marshals, staff, security,  
police and others may raise the alert that an incident has occurred. How  
this information is processed and passed onto the control room will be Control room applications 217  
critical in determining the scale of the required response to the incident.  
Early response is essential to minimise the potential impact of a problem,  
to prevent the problem from escalating.  
Detection  
Early detection of an incident coupled to an incident management plan  
can reduce the potential for escalation to a major incident. If there is a  
potential for crowd congestion to build up due to the incident the event  
may need an emergency crowd management plan. Detection may be via  
spotters, individuals who are tasked with monitoring the crowd either  
at the ground level or from a raised platform or tower. CCTV opera-  
tors should understand crowd movement and be trained to evaluate the  
crowd flow, crowd density and the overall system dynamics. Clearly,  
understanding the site, the ingress, circulation and egress phases of an  
event, the normal and emergency crowd movements, will assist in con-  
tingency planning. Modelling and training for individuals who may be  
tasked with crowd monitoring will make those individuals more effective  
in this task.  
Control  
Primacy, the overall responsibility for managing an incident, will typi-  
cally pass to the emergency services, for example, the police or fire com-  
mander. It is essential that these individuals also understand the impact  
of the overall site dynamics. If an incident occurs in one area, it may  
have an impact on other areas of the event. For example, an incident  
at an entry point may require a diversion strategy for further incoming  
crowds. Obviously, the larger the event crowd, the more this consider-  
ation becomes important.  
Over the years, we have read and commented on many hundreds of event  
plans. One common comment during an incident is—who has primacy? If  
primacy is passed to a commander who has no overall understanding of  
crowd dynamics, decisions could be made that create further problems. If  
we consider a Hillsborough scenario, a crush develops at a set of turnstiles;  
to alleviate the pressure, an exit gate is opened and crowds flood into an  
area of the event. Understanding site capacity, flow rates, crowd density  
and crowd flow are essential skills to make such a decision, to ensure that  
the consequences of one action do not create a problem elsewhere.  
Recovery  
The recovery phase relates to emergency management and the first respond-  
ers and emergency services are trained to handle a wide range of incidents. 218 Introduction to crowd science  
Managing crowds that can either assist or get in the way needs to be factored  
into the planning for major incidents. Coordination between ­ marshals/  
stewards, the event team and the emergency services should all be a consid-  
eration in the event and crowd management plan. To illustrate this specific  
point, we recently planned and managed an event, a parade though a Lake  
District town. The operational orders for marshals were specific; clear the  
crowds from any area of an incident and to provide the necessary space for  
the emergency services to perform their duties. The police will take control  
of the area and you should follow their instructions.  
PREVENTION  
The best form of incident management is to prevent an incident occurring in  
the first place. There is an apt saying, ‘plan for worst, hope for the best’, which  
epitomises the concept of crowd safety. If we categorise the types of incident  
and look to means of prevention, there are a few headlines to consider.  
Engineering  
Can we engineer the site to minimise the risk of crowd-related incidents?  
For example, configuring barrier systems to maintain a balanced ingress/  
egress flow, site design and facilities planning/management may minimise  
the risk of potential crowd problems. We use the phrase ‘safe fail’ and  
highlight the difference between ‘safe fail’ and ‘fail safe’ approaches to  
site design. Assume the system will fail, and then evaluate how the system  
may fail. Evaluate how the system may fail in relative safety. To illustrate  
this point, consider both a Hillsborough and Love Parade type of event. If  
pressure builds up on the entry system, how do we alleviate the pressure?  
Had the Hillsborough or the Love Parade planners, licensing \(or approval\)  
authorities or police commanders understood the overall site dynamics,  
those incidents may not have developed into mass fatalities. Engineering  
solutions typically have rigorous quality assurance for safety-critical sys-  
tems; we should look to those practices and apply similar engineering stan-  
dards to the events industry. Understanding how crowd density can build  
up at events, during incidents and during evacuation, is essential for engi-  
neering safer spaces.  
Crowd management  
Good crowd management will reduce the risk of crowd-related incidents  
and the principles and applications of the various models demonstrated  
in the previous chapters should provide the reader with a framework for  
improving crowd safety at their own events. If you review major crowd Control room applications 219  
incidents relating to mass fatalities, you will note that crowd management,  
during the incident is a critical function. The focus is typically on the site  
of the incident and not on the rest of the crowd. Secondary points of failure  
are a factor in both Hillsborough and Love Parade disasters.  
Information systems  
We touched on the subject of communication incompatibility in the previ-  
ous section of this chapter. It will be too late to discover that you have this  
kind of problem during an incident and it may lead to additional compli-  
cations/problems or needlessly escalate an incident. A review of past inci-  
dents has highlighted that poor interagency communication systems have  
exasperated the situation. One example of this is the phenomena called  
‘radio black spots’, areas where radio systems have difficulty reaching. A  
recent city-wide event we were working with had such a ‘black spot’ and  
we required an ambulance to access the area of this ‘black spot’ for a resi-  
dent. The incident was not connected with the event \(a parade though the  
town\), but we had to stop the parade to facilitate access for the ambulance.  
Although we had set up event communications and checked the area before  
the event, the assumption on the part of the ambulance service, was that if  
our radios would work in this area and then the ambulance radios would  
also work was flawed. We checked our equipment prior to and on the eve-  
ning of the event. The first responders and ambulance service had their  
own radio communications system and they had not checked if their system  
worked, they had assumed that if our radios were operational, their own  
radios would function. Sometimes, new buildings, local weather condi-  
tions, terrain and other radio frequencies can all impact radio communica-  
tions. Our contingency plan had considered radio failure and the situation  
was managed without further issue. The parade was interrupted but con-  
tinued successfully.  
If things are going to go wrong, they may go wrong at the worst pos-  
sible time in the worst possible location. During an incident is the worst  
time to discover communication problems, so invest the time to check the  
site, specifically if your event is in a built-up area such as a town or city  
centre.  
Integrated management  
Individuals in the control room may have different degrees of experience,  
training and operational understanding of events and specifically knowl-  
edge or experience of the current event. As a result, they may all be work-  
ing under a wide range of assumptions and/or event understanding. It is  
not uncommon for a team to meet for the first time in a control room  
at the start of an event. Comprehensive mapping of both crowd flow and 220 Introduction to crowd science  
potential congestion/risk, assists in the understanding of those risks to the  
crowd during events.  
Information theory, the principles defined by Shannon in 1948, out-  
lined that any information needs to be coded into a form such that, at  
some point in the future, it can be decoded without loss of information.  
The event plan and risk assessment process do not convey safety-critical  
information in a quick and easy-to-understand format. The issue we set  
out to address is the understanding of the overall site dynamics, with a  
specific focus on the crowd movement normal and emergency situations.  
A diagram of crowd movement, normal and emergency, conveys a lot of  
information at a glance. Our experience is this format allows a team to  
understand a site quickly and easily. We always recommend to walk the  
site on the day of the event, and to take a set of site diagrams with you, one  
that has the risks mapped and the other with the crowd movement/flow  
mapped to the relevant areas of the site. Those visuals assist the manage-  
ment during emergency situations where every second is vital for decision  
making during an incident.  
In the control room, specifically during an incident, it is vital that every-  
one has the same perceptions. There is no time to read or review an event  
plan; obviously site plans, flow diagrams and risk maps, assist understand-  
ing of the situation. During an incident, the control room team will be  
making safety-critical decisions and it is important that they are using a  
similar approach to crowd safety. We have worked in control rooms over a  
wide range of events, most develop an integrated approach to incident man-  
agement, and understand the concept of an integrated management team.  
Many events, specifically the larger events operating on lower budgets, may  
not adopt, or even understand, the concept of an integrated incident man-  
agement team. This can be a major weakness during an incident observed  
in several major incident investigations. Event or site managers that are  
unfamiliar with their site cannot be effective during an incident.  
Video analysis  
We recommend that every CCTV operator has some basic modelling expe-  
rience. This, coupled with their skills at monitoring a crowd, helps both  
the interpretation of the situation and the possible consequences of criti-  
cal decisions relating to crowd movements. There are several systems that  
attempt to automatically detect incidents that we discussed earlier in this  
chapter. We are yet to see a technology that is both cost effective and has  
practical applications for assessing crowd risks in real time. The events  
industry is low cost, low margin, so solutions need to consider the end  
users. A few hours of training can provide a significant improvement in site  
understanding, and produce control room diagrams that assist the team’s  
appreciation of the situation. There may come a time where technology Control room applications 221  
becomes cost effective, but this does not appear to be soon. There are, of  
course, some events, such as in Olympic events and other such major sport-  
ing fixtures, where crowd counting technology can be an asset. Turnstile  
counters provide useful real-time information about the venue loading and  
potential last-minute walk up.  
We have reviewed a number of ‘so-called’ automated wide-area crowd  
counters and in every case to date, these have high error rates at high den-  
sity and are of dubious reliability. Overall, these types of systems are costly  
and need to be coupled to a wide-area site model so that the data can  
be converted into useful information. The basic problem at low-cost, low-  
margin events, those that have the highest risk, is that these events simply  
do not have the budget for such equipment or the fundamental understand-  
ing of the problems of crowd management. For example, if they perceived  
a problem at the Love Parade, would they invest in monitoring equipment  
or changed the design? A design change would cost less, and be safer, than  
monitoring a dangerous situation poised for failure. Consider the invest-  
ment for Jamarat Bridge CCTV monitoring and systems automation for  
2006, where 363 lives were lost. Previous years relied on management  
training, integrated team understanding and seven different safety systems  
in place. This infrastructure was replaced with an automated system, large  
signs, CCTV and crowd counting were installed. 363 Pilgrims perished,  
the results speak for themselves. Technology can help, but only for a few  
large events, with appropriate integration to a working crowd management  
team. It does not, as such, replace crowd management, and cannot, no mat-  
ter how much investment is made, replace all the control room functions.  
There is still a need to manage the crowd irrespective of the technology  
investment, and still a need to plan and check the event plan and crowd  
risks. These functions do not lend themselves to computerised solutions,  
someone needs to check the plan and the efficacy of the crowd management  
team. Assuming the application of a computer system will replace critical  
functions shows a lack of understanding of the problem and the opera-  
tional functions of crowd management.  
We have, over many years, reviewed many different types of control room  
and event teams, assessed the problems and cost–benefit analysis, reviewed  
emergency response situations, near misses, guidance and legislative require-  
ments. Mega-events, such as the Olympics, invest in technology and wide-  
area modelling, in safety systems and training, but the vast majority of events  
do not understand the principles and applications of crowd risk analysis,  
crowd dynamics, crowd modelling and carry on ignorant of the potential  
risks. Those events, the vast majority, are the events that we are trying to  
improve through crowd and event modelling.  
During an incident, one of the critical factors is the consideration for which  
spaces may be available for the evacuation process and the requirement for  
accessibility \(routes and spaces to set-up/function\) for the first responders. 222 Introduction to crowd science  
Getting people out and away  
There are no ‘absolutes’ in safety engineering for crowds. The only place  
that is absolutely safe is an empty area, where there is no one whatsoever  
and where no one can get injured. As soon as people enter a venue, there are  
associated risks, and risk management becomes an operational function.  
This is why the brief description and a simple number in risk assessment  
can be misleading. The risk can be a function of the number of people, their  
packing configuration, the site design and the event as it unfolds. Crowd  
risks are dynamic in nature and the conventional risk assessment process  
fails to capture that type of risk. This can be critical during an emergency  
when a choice between several options, which may all carry different risks,  
need to be considered. Assessing which is the best option, specifically if the  
decision is time critical, requires a competent understanding of the situa-  
tion and potential consequences. We described several modelling systems  
that can assist in this decision-making process, but they are only useful if  
the decision maker has those fundamental modelling skills and invested  
some time in the process.  
Love Parade \(2010\), Hillsborough \(1989\) and Jamarat Bridge \(2001,  
2004, 2006\) are three sites where simple modelling would have led to a  
clearer understanding of the causality, the underlying dynamic risks in the  
environment. The incidents in these three sites were mass fatalities during  
ingress, stressing the need to define emergency plans for all three phases of  
the event \(ingress, circulation and egress\). During ingress, where the crowd  
is moving through the entry system, there will be a potential requirement  
for emergency vehicle access. Love Parade had no provision for emergency  
access in the critical area, during an incident. Trying to move an emergency  
vehicle through a crowd is dangerous, specifically if the crowd is packed at  
high density. Space, areas where we can move vehicles into an emergency  
situation and move the crowds away from the incident, needs to be planned  
into the site and event design. The process of emergency egress and site  
evacuation is not just about getting people out of the incident area but also  
about getting them away from the incident safely and efficiently.  
We define egress as moving people \(and in some situations, animals\)  
from a place of relative danger to a place of relative safety. Again, it should  
be stressed that there are no absolutes, specifically during an incident.  
Emergency egress is not just a process of pushing people out of a building;  
emergency evacuation needs to consider the routes and final destination  
of the crowd. You must think further than the exit of your event or build-  
ing and this can involve assessing the risks during the evacuation process.  
For example, during one workshop, we reviewed the plans for the evacua-  
tion of a major London football stadium. It would take 18 minutes to clear  
the stands of the stadium and immediate area around the site. However,  
this assumes that the entire route width was available. We highlighted Control room applications 223  
that, during an emergency, there may be people clustering, trying to stay  
together as groups, that first responders may require access along those  
same routes, that vehicles \(fire engines/trucks, ambulances\) may be com-  
ing in the same routes the crowds are trying to get out. When planning an  
emergency response, you have to think about this overlap of time, when the  
crowds leave and the first responders arrive, and that can have a significant  
impact on the rates of egress. We highlight, time and again, that egress  
calculations are based on assumptions of clear routes. You should consider  
the contingency plan for a percentage of each route being blocked. It may  
be dependent on the location and nature of the incident.  
To assist you in this process, consider the following questions\: What  
are your assumptions in calculating safety egress? Will the crowd respond  
immediately to the incident? How are they alerted to the nature of the inci-  
dent? Will the crowd leave by the nearest exit unassisted or do you need to  
facilitate that process? Are all routes clear? What is the contingency plan  
if there is a suspicious package on one of the exit routes? When the crowd  
leaves the area of the immediate threat, will they leave the area or stand  
and watch events unfold?  
PARADOX OF CHOICE  
We run an experiment in the workshops that highlights a problem of deci-  
sion making during egress. Specifically, how an individuals choice of route  
is based on a phenomena known as Ellsberg’s paradox.\* We select a dele-  
gate and ask if they would like to play a game for a sum of money, typically  
£10,000 \(or the equivalent sum in local currency\). We state that we will  
give the delegate the money if he/she selects a red ball, but they must give  
us the money if they pick a black ball. We then ask the delegate to imagine  
two buckets. Bucket number 1 has 500 red balls and 500 black balls. These  
are thoroughly mixed and the delegate cannot see into the bucket. We ask  
them to imagine a second bucket, bucket number 2, which has an unknown  
mix of red and black balls. Bucket number 2 has the same number of balls  
as bucket number 1 but the balls could be all red or all black. We stress that  
anything is possible, 50–50, 70–30, 90–10 and that we do not know the  
mix in the bucket. There are no additional clues to the game; they have to  
choose a bucket. This experiment demonstrates the perception of risk, the  
paradox of choice.  
Which bucket would you choose?  
50–50 or the unknown mix?  
\*  
http\://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ellsberg\_paradox. 224 Introduction to crowd science  
We have run this experiment for many years and 80% of the group will  
choose the 50–50 bucket, bucket 1. To ensure you have a balanced view,  
whatever the delegate chooses \(they are under a little bit of pressure\), ask  
the entire audience to put up their hand for bucket 1. You will see around  
80% will choose bucket 1.  
Mathematically, the probability of getting a red ball in either bucket is  
exactly the same. This experiment is not based on mathematical ability  
\(and we have run this experiment with a group of mathematicians with  
exactly the same results\). This experiment is phrased to illustrate our per-  
ceptions of risk. You base your choice on the information provided, about  
those things you think you know about the system. In this situation, we do  
not use mathematical or our analytical, higher brain functions; we go on  
gut feel and instinct. We make our decisions on what we think we know,  
we weigh risk; we do not measure it.  
Why is this important?  
Imagine you are in an unfamiliar building, or an event and the evacuation  
alarm sounds. You feel like you have to leave the area, and you have to get  
out quickly. You look around and you see the way you came in; you remem-  
ber that route. You know that route! You know how long it took to get in ...  
and there is option number 2, a door/exit-marked emergency exit. However,  
you do not know where it goes, you do not know if it is open and you do not  
know what is on the other side of the door! This is the paradox of choice,  
Ellsberg’s paradox, you will choose the route that you think has lesser risk.  
In this case, it is not a sum of money at stake, it is your life.  
The vast majority of fire-related deaths record people crowding round the  
primary entry point; in many cases, there were sufficient and viable exits,  
but during the first few moments of an incident, each individual has to make  
a decision. Which route to take? Two things happen\: first, there is the para-  
dox of choice, and second, is the evaluation of how others react. ‘If they are  
going that way, I should go that way too’—a flocking/following behaviour.  
This is the same type of decision process\: you may think the rest of the  
group may have the information that you do not have, and follow those  
people who appear to be leaving the area. This is one of the primary reasons  
why the majority of people will go out the way they came in.  
This has nothing to do with panic. It is related to the early moments of  
the incident, with how we perceive and act upon those perceptions of risk;  
we call this behavioural-based safety. An event \(and building evacuations\)  
will often be planned on the assumption of sufficient emergency egress  
capacity \(exit routes and width of exits\) to get people out in any direction,  
that all \(or most\) routes will be available and utilised, and that the people  
know what they are doing. There are many books and symposiums on  
fires and human behaviour \(a list of references can be found http\://www. Control room applications 225  
gkstill.com/Support/index.html\) and we recommend that anyone involved  
in emergency planning should familiarise themselves with the concepts of  
behavioural based safety and bystander behaviour. The topic of human  
behaviour, during emergency situations is beyond the scope of this book,  
but we will conclude this short overview with a strategic analysis of crowd  
movements for different types of threats.  
Strategic analysis  
What would happen if you have a security alert at your main exit, a sus-  
picious package, and you have a reason to believe there is a need to clear  
the event/building/site/area? The location of the package is on the primary  
ingress route, and the crowd may move towards the threat, unless instructed  
otherwise. How do you get the crowd to leave by alternative exits? How do  
you get that information to the crowd? How do you get them to respond  
quickly? They may try to exit the way they came in.  
If your staff are trained in emergency procedures and understand the  
paradox of choice, they can manage the process, open alternative exits,  
and guide people towards those exits. Information and management are  
the tools you will need to facilitate the process. Each environment may  
require different considerations and there could be a vast range of potential  
threats to consider. The problem appears to be intractable \(too complex\)  
and trying to document all possible options is a very difficult, complex  
and time-consuming process. A book of words outlining all the possible  
options would also be intractable; it may not be a useful reference during  
an emergency, since it contains too much information to be memorised, or  
referenced quickly during an emergency.  
FOUR SOLUTIONS  
When we first reviewed this specific problem, rather than tackle the problem  
of listing all the possible threats and all the possible exit route choices and  
issues with each threat and each response, we looked at the problem from  
the crowd perspective. There were four possible outcomes to any threat  
scenario that required the movement of people. If we break these down  
into actions, rather than incident-related responses, the problem becomes  
much simpler for the control room decision-making process. Each of these  
requires site-specific training, but the list of options becomes manageable.  
Total  
Typically, the fire-type scenario where we need to facilitate a rapid evacu-  
ation and all possible routes are available. Non-directional alarms can be 226 Introduction to crowd science  
used, exit points can be manned and staff, positioned at the exits, can guide  
people away from the area. This provides the crowd with vital information.  
‘This way’ instructions delivered by a member of the staff/police/steward/  
security will change the paradox of choice away from their point of entry  
towards that specific exit. It removes the uncertainty and this process and  
this procedure is used at all UK football stadia. Gates are manned and  
they are opened in an emergency, and stewards guide patrons towards that  
specific exit.  
Directed  
If a threat exists on a primary ingress route, you need to consider the direc-  
tion of egress. An alarm sounding across the entire site will not inform the  
crowd of the location of the threat, or the direction away from the threat.  
You will need to consider how to alert the crowd and how to move them in  
a specific direction away from the threat.  
Phased/partial  
There may be a requirement, such as a stadium or tall building, where  
the area of immediate threat needs to be evacuated, but other areas are  
relatively safe and there is no immediate threat to the people in those  
areas. Again, this process needs to start with the alarm signalling. How  
are you going to inform the crowd of specific directions for the evacuation  
process?  
Stay put  
In many cases, it can be a safer option to keep the crowd on-site, to decant  
the crowd away from a threat, but not to evacuate the area. An example of  
this may be a fireworks display in a large park. The site capacity may be  
vast and the crowds may be focussed only in one small area of the park.  
Moving the crowds away from a threat, to a safer location within the site,  
can be a safer option than site-wide evacuation. An external threat, such  
as a suspicious package outside the venue/event/space, may require moving  
the crowd on-site, away from the threat, but it does not necessarily require  
a total site evacuation.  
Each site should consider the above, total, directed, phased/partial or  
stay-put strategies for emergency situations. When we review major events,  
we are often reviewing these evacuation processes, specifically the alarm  
signals and initial crowd reactions. As we stated previously, there are a  
number of books, courses, symposia and conferences on fires and human  
behaviour. The final point to note on this topic is the use of simulations for  
evacuation assessment. Most have two fundamental flaws. Control room applications 227  
PROBLEMS WITH EVACUATION SIMULATIONS  
Apart from the previously mentioned packing density problems relating to  
simulating crowd movement, the assumptions on human behaviour in many  
simulations are questionable. For example, whenever we see an evacuation  
model where the agents \(the computer-generated people\) move immediately  
towards all possible exits and the system provides an evacuation time, we  
highlight the paradox of choice.  
We also stress that there will be a period of decision making, the time  
to make up the individual’s mind, to begin the process of evacuation. The  
late Dr. Jonathan Sime highlighted this problem \(There is a list of reference  
materials on the website http\://www.gkstill.com/Support/index.html\) and  
we recommend that you read, study and understand this type of problem.  
Typically, two-thirds of the total evacuation process is getting people to  
start to move.  
If the simulation does not show this time to start to move, it will be mis-  
leading. If the simulation shows all exits being used evenly and people mov-  
ing towards their nearest exit, it will be misleading. If the simulation does  
not evaluate human factors, it will be misleading. Caveat emptor \(buyer  
beware\). Chapter 8  
# The way forward  
Proper planning prevents piss poor performance.  
If you can’t explain it simply, you don’t understand it well enough.  
Military adage—the 7 Ps  
Albert Einstein  
INTRODUCTION  
One of the primary motives for developing this approach to crowd risk analy-  
sis and event safety applications is to improve both the awareness and under-  
standing of crowd related accidents and incidents. The objective is to reduce  
the potential for crowd injury in places of public assembly. We need to achieve  
this at low cost, using minimum skills, to be effective, and to have an impact  
on crowd safety around the world. We have defined tools and methods, not  
from an academic perspective, but from a practical, real-world set of experi-  
ences and applications for event planning. These techniques apply to all events.  
There is a clear imperative to this work, which comes from talking to  
thousands of delegates over two decades. The process of compiling an event  
plan, risk assessment and specifically the licensing application is onerous  
and time consuming; therefore, shortcuts, specifically ‘cut and paste’ are the  
normal practice. Having reviewed many hundreds of event plans and risk  
assessments \(some were excellent but most were substandard\), it is obvi-  
ous which plans are of the ‘dust off last year’s plan, slap a new date on the  
front, that will do’ category. Successful events grow larger, free events such  
as Christmas tree lights switch-on events often employ pop stars, attracting  
large audiences, and subsequently increase the risk of congestion and over-  
crowding. These are the events where last year’s plan may no longer work,  
specifically if the crowd grows larger every year.  
Monitoring the crowd arriving at the event is essential to keep ahead of  
the crowd problems. If your event falls into the ‘free’ or ‘charity’ category, it  
229 230 Introduction to crowd science  
does not absolve you of your fundamental duty of care, to protect the crowd  
from harm.  
INCREASED AWARENESS  
To achieve our goal of improving awareness of crowd risks, we must con-  
solidate the best practices and improve the fundamental understanding of  
crowd risk assessment and accident prevention. The DIM-ICE Meta model  
is a method of capturing both the structure and highlighting the nature of  
risks at the event, specifically if you colour the template red/orange or green  
for each of the 18 sections.  
The quality of the information needs to be continually improved through-  
out the planning, approval and operational phases of the event process to  
be effective, regardless of the event size or complexity. DIM-ICE Meta  
modelling captures both information \(for future event planning\) and allows  
the dissemination of information in a standard format, collecting and col-  
lating best practises and incidents of near misses. The objective for this is  
both information sharing and to assess risk in a more graphical format,  
highlighting the specific combination of phases and influences on crowd  
behaviour in the DIM-ICE meta model, the flow diagrams and risk map-  
ping in the crowd management plan.  
ULTIMATE RESPONSIBILITY  
The event organiser has the ultimate responsibility for crowd safety at their  
event. In Appendix B, you will see a summary of international incidents  
and accidents, this will give you some idea of the scale of the problem  
around the world. The responsibility is the same, no matter the scale of the  
event, whether it requires approval, a license or a permit. The event organ-  
iser should create an event plan and this plan needs to identify the risks  
associated with their event. This plan should be checked and externally  
approved, by a group or individual with experience in crowd risk assess-  
ment and safety engineering both from a legislative requirement \(Health  
and Safety at Work Act in the United Kingdom\) and from a fundamental  
duty of care to the people attending the event.  
Therein lies the basic problem with the system; there are no standards for  
event planning or the approval process and there are no designated quali-  
fications for either planning or licensing. We ran a series of workshops for  
event-licensing officers across the United Kingdom and can confirm very  
few \(if any\) had considered the principles outlined in this chapter before  
the workshop. Following a 2-day course, we have now made the majority  
of event-licensing officers in the United Kingdom aware of the DIM-ICE The way forward 231  
Meta modelling, the issues of crowd density and the dynamics of risk. We  
are also creating an online version of these basic courses on an eLearning  
platform, coupled to forums where we can develop these principles and  
applications further.  
Event planners can begin to incorporate the safety concepts into their  
event and crowd management plan, a licensing/permitting officer, use  
the DIM-ICE Meta model as a checklist. Some key questions that can be  
addressed are\: Does the plan have the consideration for an emergency dur-  
ing ingress, during circulation and also during egress and does the plan  
provides a reasonable basis for rejecting event plans that fail to consider  
such situations? This level of awareness needs to be global. Our aim is to  
provide on-line teaching and training materials to help improved world-  
wide awareness of the crowd risks in places of public assembly.  
Objectives

To then outline the crowd management plan using RAMP analysis and  
to illustrate the risk-analysis process using both the table \(illustrated in  
Figure 8.1 from a real event\) and illustrated with diagrams that shows  
the time, location and severity of the risk. The purpose of this is to move  
towards the concepts defined by Shannon information theory, in that the  
plan should facilitate anyone, from the layperson to an expert alike, with  
relevant information.  
We have found diagrams are far more descriptive than lengthy words and  
would suggest a further enhancement. The standard text of an event plan  
splits into two sections\: the legal/contractual elements, defining the duties  
and obligations of the contracting arrangements and the event description.  
By dividing the event plan into these two sections, you will find that the  
legal sections lend themselves to words, which will be very similar from  
event to event, while the event, and crowd management plans lend them-  
selves to diagrams, which will be unique to each event. Normal Ingress Circulation Egress

\*  
A ‘pinch point’ is an area where the crowd congestion builds up due to the geometry of the site, where more people approach a point \(the pinch point\)  
than can pass through that part of the system.  
232 Introduction to crowd science﻿ The way forward 233  
With emphasis on the description of the crowd movements in diagrams, the  
approval process becomes less onerous and clearer to the licensing/approval  
or permitting officers. This facilitates the event-approval process and any  
safety advisory groups \(SAGs\) or experts who need to review the plan before  
the event begins. As we have found, over many years of teaching these mod-  
elling techniques, they are low-cost, low-skill operations and add enormous  
value to the process of understanding the key elements of crowd dynamics.  
Modelling  
Modelling is a process of discovery; with discovery comes understanding  
and understanding leads to better control systems. Discovery, understand-  
ing and control are the fundamental objectives of the crowd-modelling pro-  
cess. Often, the practice of building the crowd and event models help to  
identify and qualify the potential risk and can provide valuable insights,  
leading towards risk management solutions. To enable this, there are a few  
fundamental skills required.  
Competent person  
It is essential to understand the key skill necessary for competent event risk  
analysis and specifically in the process of creating and approving an event  
plan. Throughout this book, we have described the process of crowd and  
event modelling, and we have outlined the principles we developed, during  
many major projects of capturing and disseminating information relevant  
to crowd safety.  
Figure 8.1 shows the outline of the process from event concept, develop-  
ing an event/crowd management plan through to utilising the information  
in the control room. We have taught these principles for over a decade, we  
have found they are easily adopted into the planning and approval pro-  
cess, specifically as they are low-cost approach to the problem of crowd  
risk analysis and safety engineering. We stress that these are not academic  
exercises; they are built on experience of events large and small from around  
the world. Specifically, the DIM-ICE Meta model has proven to be a robust,  
structured, methodical and systematic approach to defining the key compo-  
nents of an event plan.  
We use this template for large and small events, from the 3 million pil-  
grims who perform complex rituals on the Jamarat Bridge during the annual  
pilgrimage to Mecca and for 500 neighbours and friends who attend our  
local village events. Some elements of the DIM-ICE Meta model require  
a lot of information, some have very little; for example, a fireworks event  
may have very little mid-event crowd movements as there is no circulation  
phase; others may have ingress and egress throughout the event placing dif-  
ferent types of demand on the entry/exit system. 234 Introduction to crowd science  
The DIM-ICE Meta model is a blank template for an event and com-  
pleting it is a process of assessing the elements of a crowd management  
plan, making sure that you have thought through all 18 components. This  
process was designed to ensure that the user has a template to complete  
and that the approval process has some method of assessing the overall  
content and quality of an event plan. The importance of understanding  
that an emergency can occur during ingress, during the event and during  
egress, here, the crowd, which is leaving the event, and providing con-  
tingency planning for all phases of the event is essential. The majority of  
accidents and incidents occur during ingress and egress and, as such, may  
have very different crowd management requirements than a mid-event  
emergency.  
We also outlined how RAMP analysis helps us define the safety con-  
cept. These four elements, routes, areas, movement and crowd profile, help  
us define the key components of the safety concept and again, by using a  
standardised template, we can introduce both a checklist to the approval  
process and a systematic method of defining the event-planning process.  
The table below outlines the components of the RAMP analysis.  
Routes Direction and distribution, modal split and factors that may impact routes  
Areas Static and moving spaces defining the site areas  
Movement The arrival and departure profile, coupling routes and areas to time  
Profile The crowd composition, type, demographic, history and communication  
elements  
When we work with police, safety advisory groups \(SAGs\), licensing  
officers and the crowd managers, the principle is simple and the advice  
is always the same; use the DIM-ICE/RAMP models and if you have any  
doubts about the validity or clarity of the event plan, reject it. The onus  
of responsibility is on the event organiser to demonstrate their event plan  
that is fit for its intended purpose. If in any doubt, the plan should be  
rejected. You should note that by approving a plan that fails, that was  
not fit for it intended purpose, you may be faced with prosecution for  
negligence.  
Applications  
We have demonstrated how the RAMP analysis and the DIM-ICE models  
are useful during the planning and approval phases of the event process. This  
does not over burden the process of risk analysis; it merely helps to define  
crowd risks in a way that the typical event professional can record and later  
process the information. The objective is to do so in such a way that a lay-  
person can pick up the plan and understand the key concepts, specifically The way forward 235  
relating to crowd risks. As we have outlined, the current risk\* analysis pro-  
cess, where the information is coded to a few lines, a number and a task des-  
ignator, does not lend itself to complete reconstruction of the potential risks.  
This implies that the process is open to interpretation and can be viewed by  
different people and indicates different actions and outcomes. Cleary, this is  
neither desirable nor precise enough for the required outcome, risk manage-  
ment. We need to stress that the existing risk assessment structure is per-  
fectly adequate for many fixed risks, risks that do not have variable functions  
\(such as density and flow\). However, the risks that are dynamic in nature the  
summary and numerical assignments are not capturing the relevant informa-  
tion. Specifically, if the location, duration and severity are variable, you need  
to consider how to highlight this information in the event plan, specifically  
in the crowd management plan. We are not aiming to replace this format,  
only to augment the existing risk analysis with diagrams that highlight the  
dynamic nature of risk assessment with respect to crowd safety.  
Crowd risk is dynamic  
There are three components to a dynamic risk. These are  
Location—Risk may be focussed at a specific location \(front of stage\)  
and may not manifest until the critical density/flow is reached.  
Duration—Risk may exist for a short time \(entry and exit systems\).  
Severity—Risk may have different levels of severity during the event and  
a single risk value is not sufficient to describe the risk.  
All of the above can be influenced by external influences, weather, trans-  
port delays and adverse media before or during the event, etc. There are  
many potential variables and we always recommend a study of past acci-  
dents and incidents, as they will provide a good insight into the nature of  
crowd accidents and incidents. Appendix B is a summary of major acci-  
dents and incidents, it is worth reading this to realise how widespread the  
problems of crowd risk analysis are around the world.  
EVENT PROCESS  
It is important to note that the three stages \(planning, approval and opera-  
tions\) are typical for major events, but they should be considered for all  
events, large and small. For example, a person or group draws up an event  
plan and another person or group, should then check the plan with an  
emphasis on risk analysis. The approval process may be conducted by an  
external person/group, for example, a safety advisory group \(SAG\), a local  
licensing authority or internally, by a competent person. Be aware that, if  
\*  
With respect to crowd risks. 236 Introduction to crowd science  
a defective event plan is approved then fails, the approving body may be  
scrutinised and held, to some extent, liable.  
There is a requirement for competency in creating the plan and a com-  
petency requirement for approving the plan. In any engineering discipline,  
the degree of competency will be related to the potential for harm. We do  
not see this in the event industry around the world. In our experience, many  
events are close to failure and, sadly, it takes a major incident before the  
situation will be addressed. By defining a modelling approach, one that is  
low cost and can be taught in a short course, we hope that we can expand  
the awareness of crowd risks in places of public assembly. We hope this  
will lead to a more methodical approach to crowd safety engineering; a  
more systematic and visual approach to the process of creating a crowd  
management plan. We can improve the awareness of risks for the event  
professional, the approval process requires similar skill sets to understand  
the event plan and check for any risks.  
Safety should not be compromised under any circumstances. The event  
may be run for financial gain, or to break even, in either case cost will be  
a consideration. If the cost of developing event plans appears to be high,  
consider the cost of litigation if someone is hurt at your event.  
There are a number of on-line resources and, if the event is large, consider  
using an external person/group who has no financial interest in the event  
plan, someone that is both competent and that is responsible for checking the  
validity of the plan and the safety concept, one that has the necessary skills to  
perform a comprehensive analysis. This is essential to ensure crowd safety for  
major events, but it is equally important where smaller events are considered.  
There are many smaller events where local authorities, such as the police  
and emergency services, have this experience. Checking your event plan  
with a local authority, such as the police, fire or ambulance service, will  
result in a dialogue between the event organiser and the development of a  
good future working relationships with the emergency services. Consider  
the process as illustrated in Figure 8.2 which shows the process from event  
concept through to event operations. A simpler diagram is shown in Figure  
8.3 \(planning, approval and operations\).  
The approval stage should comment and potentially reject an event plan  
unless they are completely satisfied that it is relatively safe to conduct. We  
should highlight that, in several cases, we have acted as an expert witness,  
and the approval authority passing a poor event plan, can result in prosecu-  
tion. If in doubt, the licensing authority should reject a plan unless it can  
be demonstrated to be as safe as possible to proceed.  
We have found that many emergency services are very happy, willing and  
able to participate in a discussion where the safety of the public is con-  
cerned. Their input can make a significant difference to event safety and we  
would advise, wherever possible, to talk to the emergency services and listen  
to their experience. It will improve your event and your knowledge of local The way forward 237

Expert analysis  
We have acted as an expert witness to a number of high-profile cases over  
the years and the first information we review is the event risk analysis and  
crowd management plan. Typically, we ask the same question in every case.  
Were the risks identified, documented, reviewed, reduced, managed or mit-  
igated and if not, then why not? Who had the job of identifying the risk?  
Would a reasonably competent person have identified these risks? Would a  
layperson have recognised these risks?  
You can see in all these types of questions the same themes of competence  
and negligence point towards liability on the part of the event planners and  
approval authorities. Without competent application of risk analysis and  
the appropriate documentation, it may be very difficult to prove otherwise.  
Could the accident have been avoided?  
One typical question is ‘at what point in time could the incident have been  
avoided’? Could this incident have been identified, and hence avoided, during  
planning and in the approval process? All these questions look to identifying  
the underlying cause of the incident and appropriating the blame on a specific  
individual or group of individuals. Again, this points towards negligence and  
to the event documentation, the crowd management plan and the crowd risk  
assessment process.  
As we stated in the opening chapter, not all events go to an approval pro-  
cess; there may be no external check on the validity of the event plan. That is  
why we have outlined the process from both the event planning and approval  
perspectives. We build models to provide a means of discovering crowd risks,  
and of understanding the potential for harm to the crowd under normal and  
emergency situations. Models help to inform the user of the nature, loca-  
tions, duration and severity of crowd risks. With the information produced  
during the process of modelling, we can introduce controls \(changes or man-  
agement processes\) in the design. We can introduce information systems that  
help inform the crowd of the potential for harm and thereby reduce or miti-  
gate these risks. If we cannot eliminate the risks, we may need to monitor and  
manage the crowd to ensure that the risk does not materialise.  
Event planning\: Flowchart  
As we outlined in the previous chapter, there are major projects for which  
a comprehensive approach to the entire scope of the design, build, value The way forward 239  
engineering, project cost analysis and resource deployment are a significant  
undertaking. For those types of projects, crowd simulations may be essen-  
tial to both understand the complexity of the site and the potential crowd  
risks. Clearly, we do not use that approach for all projects and many run  
on minimum budgets. For clients we would normally advise a three step  
approach to crowd and event modelling. Figure 8.4 shows the three levels  
of modelling that can be applied to projects, large or small. We are aware  
that many international consulting firms would convince a major client that  
massive simulation investment is required. That is not our approach; we  
always begin with a workshop, where the delegates are introduced to the  
principles and applications of crowd science. In many cases, these are suf-  
ficient to resovle the basic crowd management issues; in others, the delegates  
will engage us for further work. If you are considering either modelling or  
simulation for your event, then review the chart below and define what you  
need from an analysis. Level 1 is trainable and should be an internal func-  
tion. A workshop, or on-line training, would provide the necessary skills  
and should be considered as an investment for all future events. Level 2 is  
also a trainable set of skills and utilises the flow diagrams and risk mapping.  
Level 3 is advanced, this type of project would require a much higher level  
of skills, such as computer science, mathematics, simulation skills etc.

Level 1\: Report and analysis  
Typically, the level 1 type of modelling exercise if based around clients who  
have some feeling that there is a potential problem, where a workshop based  
on their site or event, can be most effective in identifying the key issues. These  
are normally ‘reverse brief’ projects where the workshops format was specifi-  
cally designed to both educate the delegates on the key principles and appli-  
cations of crowd science and to allow them to outline their site problems.  
Many of these types of projects can be delivered remotely, via the Internet  
\(skype and email, reports in electronic format\), the client will provide event  
and crowd management plans, specify their issues and ask for varying degrees  
of assistance. We run a range of forums for an on-going discussion on crowd  
safety issues and a range of educational programmes around the world.

March 2014 Appendix A\: Essential crowd safety  
# mathematics  
INTRODUCTION  
There are only a few important calculations you need to consider for  
crowd safety. Start with the basic area and flow rate calculations \(a first  
pass approximation\). If the site fails a first pass approximation is should  
be rejected as ‘not fit for its intended purpose’. Below we highlight the key  
elements for a first pass approximation for crowd safety.  
SPACE  
The guidance indicates a value of two people per square metre as an event  
capacity. A first-pass approximation is to take the total area, then multiply  
by 2. This will give you the upper limit for the event space. So an area of  
12,000 square metres would have a maximum capacity of 24,000 people.  
We stress that this assumes all spaces are used within the event—so this is  
an approximation only. You then need to divide the event space into view-  
ing areas and areas where the crowds will be moving rather than standing.  
You also need to assess spaces that will not be used. We do this by putting  
a sheet of tracing paper over the site plan and highlighting these three types  
of space, Static, Dynamic and Unused. That diagram will be the template  
to decide whether there is sufficient space for the crowds.  
Depending on the crowd profile younger audiences have smaller frames  
and pack to a higher density. Older audiences can have larger frames \(body  
space\) so the packing density may be much lower. Buggies, children, back-  
packers and the like will all have an impact on crowd density limits. The  
value of 47 people in 10 square metres \(4.7 people per square metre\) has no  
reference to demographic—so it is not an absolute value \(guidance is as it is  
stated—guidance, not absolute\). You must assess the nature of the crowd,  
the packing density and define limits according to your event.  
Underutilised spaces should be excluded for a capacity analysis and  
spaces where the crowd are predominately moving \(such as entry and exit  
245 246 Appendix A  
points\) should be calculated at no more than two people per square metre  
\(these areas will experience some degree of congestion\). By assessing all of  
the above, you will have a better approximation for the utilised capacity.  
The area can be assessed using a range of free software, Google Earth,  
CAD or by dividing the event space into triangles. The area of a triangle is  
calculated by multiplying the base by the height, and then dividing by 2. There  
are numerous web resources that illustrate the different types of triangles,  
for example, http\://www.mathgoodies.com/lessons/vol1/area\_triangle.html.  
TIME  
One of the best methods of assessing crowd build up is to draw a graph of  
number of people against time. The vertical access should be number of  
people and the horizontal axis \(from left to right\) should be time. You can  
use this to monitor crowd build up, assessing density, or if you are count-  
ing, you can plot the counts. There are a few techniques that you can use  
to assess how this graph may develop. For example if you take a ruler and  
place it along the points of the graph—the future numbers of people can be  
assessed along the horizontal of the graph and edge of the ruler.  
DIRECTION  
We always recommend you to assess the transport hubs \(car and coach  
parks, train or tram stations, bus stops\) and local population areas, for  
example, a pub/bar prior to an event, could be a source of a number of  
people leaving the pub/bar and heading to the event. Directions can pro-  
vide useful clues to potential crowd issues. We ran a workshop for a major  
city/park event where one of the main approach routes passed a number of  
liquor stores \(in the UK we call these off-licences, shops that sell alcohol\)  
and supermarkets, where alcohol would be sold in bottles. The event was  
advertised with a policy of no glass. Clearly, the combination of selling  
glass bottles then having them confiscated at the entry point would lead  
to potential problems. By assessing the routes and potential problems, the  
decision to relax the policy or to work with the local businesses, to sell only  
plastic containers, or decanting facilities, could be made.  
FLOW  
Flow rates are normally worked out to be taking the width of a route and  
multiplying by 82 to get the best case \(maximum\) crowd flow \(for a nor-  
mal crowd\). So, when assessing a crowd flow, look to the smallest width Appendix A 247  
along the route. Measure that width and multiply the width by 82 \(people  
per metre per minute\) to obtain the upper flow rates. This is a first-pass  
approximation and the crowd flow is unlikely to exceed this value. For  
example, if the road is 10 m wide the flow rate would be 820 people per  
minute. Multiply by 60 to get people per hour  
=  
49,200 people per hour.  
We can see by this simple example that a 10 m width would allow approx-  
imately 50,000 people per hour \(again this is a normal type of crowd\). You  
need to scale this down if there are back packers, children, mixed young  
and elderly family groups, buggies, mobility impaired and/or picnic ham-  
pers. The upper limit is 82 people per metre per minute. We generally evalu-  
ate movement at this \(best possible case\) then scale down for the crowd  
demographic makeup. For example, if the crowd is tired, of mixed ability  
\(young/old\) then scale down the flow rates.  
If you are unsure of your crowd flow, measure it. Measure the width  
of the road. Count the people passing a line across that road in a minute.  
That will give you the people per width per minute. Divide by the width  
of the road and you will have a value of people per metre per minute. For  
example, if the road is 12 metres wide and you have counted \(e.g. use a  
clicker\) 840 people passing in a minute then the flow rate is 840 divided by  
12  
=  
75 people per metre per minute.  
Note\: Not all of the route will be used—there will be spaces left between  
the crowd and the walls. It’s normal to allow 50 cm each side of the route  
for this factor. You should also allow for street furniture and take the effec-  
tive width. So, if you have measured 12 m wide on the road—but there are  
lampposts then we would use 12 m – \(50 cm + 50 cm for the sides\)  
=  
11 m.  
Then deduct a further 50 cm for the lampposts—giving an effective width  
of only 10.5 m. Counting 840 people would therefore give you a flow rate  
of 840/10.5  
=  
80 people per metre per minute.  
We stress that these are optimal flow rates and should only be used to  
approximate rates of passage. In all of the examples, this is a first pass  
approximation, there are many variables that can influence crowd flow—  
but you must start with some fundamental values to be able to assess the  
risk to the crowd and avoid over crowding/crushing. Appendix B\: The disaster database  
INTRODUCTION  
Accidents do not just happen; there is always a cause! Whether there was  
an underlying lack of safety culture, poor understanding of the overwhelm-  
ing forces involved in crowd dynamics and design limits/capacity/through-  
put or mismanagement, there is always an underlying cause. We identified  
three underlying \(root/distal\) causes\: design based, information related or  
management \(human\) failure. Using these classifications, we categorised  
the historic accidents/incidents into three fundamental problems\: design  
problems, information problems and management problems.  
One of the key elements of crowd modelling is to understand the capacity  
of the space, how quickly it will fill and what time it will take to reach criti-  
cal density. This does not need complex computer simulations as simple,  
first-pass capacity analysis gives us clear indications whether a system will  
work \(or needs a radical design change\). If a system fails a first-pass analysis  
\(sometimes called a rough-cut capacity analysis\), then reject it as ‘not fit for  
purpose’. Even with queueing systems in place, whenever crowds can arrive  
in numbers that may exceed a systems capacity there is a risk. Identifying  
the nature, duration and scale of that risk is relatively simple maths.  
The following pages are from a wide range of press headlines and brief  
outlines of some of the disasters recorded. A more comprehensive list is  
available on the website http\://www.gkstill.com/CV/ExpertWitness/Crowd  
Disasters.html
