1964 \(24 May\) Lima, Peru. 318 people are killed and another 500  
injured in riots at National Stadium in an Olympic qualifying match.  
A handful of people rushed onto the pitch, the police fired tear gas  
directly into the stands.  
1967 \(17 September\) Turkey, Kayseri. Spectators at soccer game start  
fighting using pistols, knife and other weapons; 44 people died and  
approximately 600 were injured.  
1968 \(23 June\) Buenos Aires, Argentina. 74 people are killed and  
over 150 injured following a first-division game between River Plate  
and Boca Juniors when fans trying to leave the stadium mistakenly  
head towards a closed exit and are crushed against the doors by other  
fans unaware of the closed passageway.  
1971 \(2 January\) Glasgow, Scotland. 66 people are killed and 140  
are injured when barriers in Ibrox Stadium collapse near the end of a  
match between Celtic and Rangers and fans are crushed.  
1971 \(4 March\) Salvador, Brazil. A fight and a wild rush broke out in  
the grandstands, killing four and injuring 1500.  
1974 \(17 February\) Cairo, Egypt. Crowds attempting to enter a club  
game broke down barriers and 49 people were trampled to death.  
1975 \(12 March\) the USSR, Moscow. Sokolniki stadium\: after a soc-  
cer game between a Canadian and a USSR youth team, 20 people  
were crushed to death in a dark stairway.  
1976 \(6 December\) Port-au-Prince, Haiti. At a World Cup qualifier  
between Haiti and Cuba, the visitors scored and a Haitian fan set off a  
firecracker. Fans thought it was gunfire and panicked, knocking down a  
soldier, whose gun went off and killed a small boy and girl in the crowd.  
1979 Nigeria. 24 died and 27 injured in a stampede as fans stampede  
during a light failure.  
1981 \(8 February\) Piraeus, Greece. 24 died in a stampede as fans  
rush to leave ground, 54 injured.  
1981 Sheffield, Great Britain. 38 injured during a crowd surge at  
Hillsborough Stadium.  
1982 \(20 October\) Moscow. 340 are reportedly killed at a European  
Cup match between Soviet club Spartak Moscow and Haarlem of  
the Netherlands. Police are blamed for pushing fans down a narrow,  
icy staircase before the end of the match. When a late goal is scored,  
exiting fans try to re-enter the stadium and create a ‘human mincer’.  
Moscow officials dispute the claims made in the publication of the  
Soviet Sports Committee, saying only 61 died and police did not push  
fans. Luzhniki Stadium\: Up to 340 people are crushed to death when  
fans leaving the stadium try to re-enter the stands after a last-minute  
goal in a UEFA Cup tie between Moscow Spartak and Dutch side  
Haarlem, according to Sovietsky Sport. The government newspaper  
Izvestia puts the death toll at 66. Appendix B 251

1993 CROWD DISASTERS  
•  
1 January, Lan Kwai Fong, Hong Kong. 21 died in crowd crush inci-  
dent. The Lan Kwai Fong disaster that took place in Hong Kong in  
the early hours of 1 January 1993. It was a crush crowd incident,  
catching thousands of party-goers off-guard and resulted in 21 per-  
sons dying. The location \(narrow streets and a sloped gradient\), poor  
police planning and bad weather contributed to the disaster. From  
Mr Justice Bokary’s Interim Report into that disaster, it was stated  
that a number of persons, more or less in a row and more or less at the  
same time, lost or were deprived of their footing and fell. Because the  
press from behind was overwhelming, more and more people started  
falling. People piled upon those who had gone down before them. The  
pile grew until it reached such a height that the people immediately  
behind it were propped up by it and pinned against it by the press  
people behind and upslope of them. Thus came about what some wit-  
nesses have called a ‘human wall’. Tragically, men, women and chil-  
dren had the breath of life crushed out of them. 20 persons died very  
quickly, one more died in hospital some days later.

1999 CROWD DISASTERS  
•  
11 January, Egypt, Alexandria. Stampede after a derby between Korm  
and Al Ittihad, 11 people died. 254 Appendix B  
•  
31 May, Minsk, Belarus. 53 dead, 150 injured, 78 hospitalised when  
a crowd of 2500 rushed to get out of the rain at the railway station.  
From The Daily Telegraph, 1 June 1999. An unprecedented tragedy  
happened on 30 May in the centre of the Belarusian capital. Over 50  
people died and some 300 were wounded in a crush at the entrance to  
the underground station … The tragedy was caused by heavy rain that  
started at about 8 p.m. … A few thousand Minsk residents, mostly  
young people, had gathered …The first thunders and rain drops made  
people rush to find shelter in the underground crossing … Somebody  
fell down on the concrete floor and the first blood was shed. People  
were slipping over and trampling those lying on the floor … People  
were falling at the feet of the crowd. Over 2000 people poured into  
the 10-m wide underground crossing thus creating a dense moving  
jam … there were people literally smeared against the walls, pressed  
into the floor,…Meanwhile, screams of those who were unable to  
escape on their own kept echoing from this hellish meat grinder…  
‘We are soccer fans, so we know what to do in a crowd—cover your  
head with hands and make your way to the exit’.… ‘People kept arriv-  
ing until there was almost no space and then the whole mess started.  
There was no escape. The people surging in from behind just left the  
others lying and walked over them’, one of the survivors told Russian  
television. ‘About 300 people were lying here, one layer on top of  
another’, a policeman said, ‘We were carrying out the top layer of  
people and they were still alive. Those in the bottom layer were either  
dead or injured’. Two policemen were caught in the crush and also  
died as they tried to rescue those who had fallen … More than 150  
people were taken to 10 hospitals in Minsk as doctors battled through  
the night to save the lives of the victims in the tragedy. In his speech,  
president Lukashenko said\: ‘There is nobody to blame, there is no one  
to make a claim to, it happened because it happened, even if there was  
anybody responsible it was the rain that caused the disaster’.

July, Akashi Crowd Crush Disaster. Preliminary Analysis Points to  
Poor Planning and Management. 10 people were killed and over 120  
were injured. Eight of the ten victims were under the age of 10. Two 256 Appendix B  
•  
•  
•  
were in their 70s, according to the Kyodo News. The victims were  
part of a crowd of well over 150,000 people who were enjoying a  
fireworks display nearby. Among the major crowd safety flaws that  
led to the crowd crush and crowd collapse disaster are, according to  
CMS \(the USA\)\: \(1\) Reliance by organisers on one major egress route  
for tens of thousands of people. \(2\) Reliance on an egress route not  
designed for the crowd capacity that could reasonably be expected  
to use the exit route following the fireworks display. \(3\) An underes-  
timation of the anticipated event audience size. Therefore, an under  
assessment of the staffing, services and demands that would be placed  
upon the event site. \(4\) A lack of emergency exits on the walkway. \(5\)  
A flawed, poorly executed or non-existent emergency plan. \(6\) Failure  
of a timely response to the initial signs of impending disaster.

30 September. At least 147 people have been killed in a stampede at  
a Hindu temple in the north-western Indian state of Rajasthan, the  
state government says. Scores more were injured, many seriously, in  
the crush at the Chamunda Devi temple in Jodhpur.  
30 November. Jdimytai Damour, 34, was crushed as he and other  
employees attempted to unlock the doors of a Long Island, New York  
store at 5 a.m. Friday, police said. ‘This incident was avoidable’, said  
Bruce Both, president of the United Food and Commercial Workers  
Union.

of the rainy season. Survivors recounted scenes of mass suffocation  
and desperate screams after thousands went into a frenzy to flee the  
bridge, apparently after shouts went up that some people had been  
electrocuted. Police said some also shouted that the bridge was about  
to collapse.

10 January. The mother of a prospective undergraduate student  
was crushed to death after a stampede broke out in a 8000-strong  
crowd trying to snap up last-minute spaces at the University of  
Johannesburg. The crush developed early on Tuesday after security  
guards at the South African university opened a small gate to admit  
applicants through the university-clearing system. As students and  
their parents pushed to get through the gate, some fell to the ground  
and were trampled while others were forced against the sharp metal  
fencing. One woman died at the scene from head and chest injuries  
while three others were left in a critical condition. A further 17 people  
were treated for minor injuries.

Legal disclaimer Appendix D\: DIM-ICE meta model  
# \(summary\)  
INTRODUCTION  
This section is a short summary of the DIM-ICE Meta model included here  
as a reference guide for the practitioner.  
Event influences and crowd behaviour  
There are three main influences on crowd behaviour \(design, information  
and management\) and three main phases of crowd behaviour. During each  
phase, we need to plan for both normal and emergency considerations.  
This is a combination of 18 elements which, when mapped into a matrix,  
provides a unique overview of the event.  
Influences on crowd behaviour  
The three primary influences \(design, information and management\) are as  
follows\:  
Design \(D\)\: The crowd will manoeuvre through the built and complex  
spaces interacting with the local geometry, barriers, walls, doors, bridges  
and the like. It is important to calculate capacity and flow/fill rates and  
their influences on the crowd behaviour. How a barrier design or layout  
functions during ingress needs contingency planning for an emergency dur-  
ing ingress. For example, entry queueing systems, queueing spaces \(both on  
entry and during the event at concessions\) need to be matched to the arrival  
profiles \(the arriving crowds over time\). This is important when consider-  
ing the impact inefficient queueing systems can have on crowd behaviour.  
Egress capacity on exit, location and distribution of the crowd, specif-  
ically queueing at the transport terminals \(bus/trains\) and car park exit  
gates, are all part of the design element of the model. The event does not  
finish at the event site boundaries. Meta modelling considers the point of  
arrival \(transport systems\) to the point of departure \(the transport system\).  
269 270 Appendix D  
Information \(I\)\: Signage, p.a. announcements \(such as big screens, text  
updates, stewards, loud hailers, etc.\), advanced notifications, local and  
national media all influence the crowd behaviour. We map all these influ-  
ences to assist the overall crowd management strategy and help shape the  
crowd behaviour arriving, during and departing the site. This dimension  
takes into account the advanced notification and planning aspects of site  
design and have specific focus on how information systems affect behaviour.  
Management \(M\)\: Stewarding, policing and event timing \(key per-  
formers, overrunning allotted times\) can all affect the crowd behaviour.  
Specifically monitoring the crowd and using that information in an over-  
arching crowd management strategy is the purpose of the meta-model. For  
example, it is important to differentiate between a crowd management plan  
and a security, policing and stewarding plan. These have different func-  
tions and different resource deployments.  
Three principle phases of events  
Ingress \(I\) behaviour\: Where the crowd is arriving in anticipation of get-  
ting a good vantage point for the event is the first phase. Primarily seeking  
information/signage to direct them to their position, orienting them to the  
site and the event is important in reducing the typical frustrations in the  
crowd. It is important to assess the arrival profile for an event to ensure  
adequate and safe ingress capacity \(search process, ticket checking, etc.\)  
Circulation \(C\) behaviour\: During the event, the facilities planning and  
layout can influence crowd behaviour. Excessive queueing for toilets, facili-  
ties, food and beverage affects the crowd dynamics and the success of an  
event can often depend on how accessible these elements are during an  
event. You should quantify and monitor crowd behaviour, capacity and  
crowd density at key locations, such as the front of a stage.  
Egress \(E\) behaviour\: Typically the crowd dynamics during egress is to  
leave as quickly as possible. However, this can lead to anxiety, and aggres-  
sion \(e.g. car park management is an example of the kind of frustrations  
exhibited during egress\). Excessive loading on transportation systems \(trains,  
busses, etc.\) is an important consideration for the crowd management plan.  
There are two non-emergency, egress profiles to consider for an event.  
These are typically referred to as a hard and soft finishes. This has a signifi-  
cant impact on the crowds departing the event and the subsequent loading  
on the egress/transport system.  
At the end of the event  
There are two different types of events, those that finish at a fixed time  
\(hard finish\) and those that have no fixed ending and crowds drift away  
across a longer period \(soft finish\). Appendix D 271  
Hard finish\: Is defined where everyone leaves very shortly after the event  
concludes, for example, a firework display or a concert.  
Soft finish\: After the main performer at a concert, exhibition, parade,  
street carnival and so forth, secondary entertainments may typically be  
used to affect the crowd departure profile. This has the effect on the crowd  
leaving over an extended period of time. It is important to define the depar-  
ture period to ensure adequate and safe egress and balancing that to the  
transport loading.  
Both hard and soft finishes to events can have risks. We highlight that the  
event does NOT finish at the event boundary. Analysis of risk should also  
extend to transport interfaces. Although this sounds like a lot of potential  
combinations, when we outline the meta-model in a template \(see below\)  
the model becomes clear—it’s a template for developing a comprehensive  
crowd management plan.  
Normal Ingress Circulation Egress  
Design In this box, we define  
ingress design  
elements.  
Information In this box, we define  
ingress information  
elements.  
Management In this box, we define  
ingress management  
elements.  
In this box, we define  
circulation design  
elements.  
In this box, we define  
circulation information  
elements.  
In this box, we define  
circulation  
management elements.  
In this box, we  
define the egress  
design elements.  
In this box, we  
define the egress  
information  
elements.  
In this box, we  
define the egress  
management  
elements.  
We also complete a template for emergency situations as below.  
Emergency Ingress Circulation Egress  
Design In this box, we define  
the design elements  
for an emergency  
during ingress.  
Information In this box, we define  
the information  
elements for an  
emergency during  
ingress.  
Management In this box, we define  
the management  
elements for an  
emergency during  
ingress.  
In this box, we define  
design elements for  
an emergency during  
circulation.  
In this box, we define  
information elements  
for an emergency  
during circulation.  
In this box, we define  
management  
elements for an  
emergency during  
circulation.  
In this box, we define  
the design elements  
for an emergency  
during egress.  
In this box, we define  
the information  
elements for an  
emergency during  
egress.  
In this box, we define  
the management  
elements for an  
emergency during  
egress. 272 Appendix D  
Once you have a template for a number of events, you begin to capture  
important information, such as different types of design, how they work  
under normal and emergency situations, most importantly it becomes a  
systematic method of assessing risks to the public under your charge.  
Risk analysis  
The three key elements of risk analysis are to determine the likelihood and  
consequence of a risk, to describe that risk and to outline an action plan to  
eliminate, mitigate or manage the risk. We will outline this in the context  
of the DIM-ICE Meta model. The first part of this process requires a sys-  
tematic approach to identifying risk during ingress. Work through design,  
information and management aspects in the column for ingress, completing  
the template. Think about normal and emergency situations. Then move  
onto the next column and think about the circulation \(mid-event\) phase,  
design, information, management, normal and emergency situations and  
then repeat for the egress phase. Below is a typical outline, of course you  
need to create a document that states each element and supplement these  
with appropriate diagrams.  
Normal Ingress Circulation Egress  
Design Elements of the design that  
influence the crowd during  
ingress—this specifically  
relates to the elements of the  
design \(such as barriers, local  
geometry, widths of routes,  
paths and stairs, entrances,  
turnstiles, etc.\)  
Information  
Prior to the event, a lot of  
things can influence the  
crowd behaviour such as  
advanced notifications, media  
coverage, tickets and posters,  
local knowledge, previous  
event history, nature of band  
and weather forecasts. Assess  
how the information prior to  
the event, near the event, on  
the way to the event and at  
the venue could influence the  
crowd—specifically signage  
and information systems.  
Elements of the  
design that influence  
the crowd during  
the circulation \(this  
relates to ‘mid-  
event’—moving  
around\) such as  
route widths, stairs,  
layout and facilities  
management,  
concessions, etc.  
Mid-event there can  
be a lot of  
conflicting  
information, the  
performance, the  
concessions, signage,  
PA announcements,  
stewards,  
information points.  
Assess how this  
influences the  
crowds and how  
best to inform the  
crowd of the  
facilities.  
Elements of the  
design that  
influence the  
crowd during  
egress \(getting  
out\)—specifically  
the egress capacity,  
route complexity  
and geometry  
\(stairs, corridors,  
doors, gates, etc.\)  
Signage and PA  
announcements  
for departure  
\(non-emergencies\)  
influence not only  
the direction but  
the distribution of  
the crowd. Ensure  
that all routes are  
clearly signed—  
checking for lines  
of sight to ensure  
all exit routes are  
visible. Appendix D 273  
Normal Ingress Circulation Egress  
Management  
Stewards, security and police  
management can divert the  
crowd to the most  
appropriate areas but they  
ALSO influence the crowds  
behaviour \(such as reducing  
the element of hooliganism by  
increasing the visibility of  
police—this is ALSO  
information\). We typically  
consider the deployment and  
activities of active  
management in this section.  
Queues can be actively  
managed and evenly  
distributed if approach routes  
do not allow good sightlines.  
During the event, the  
stewards can actively  
manage queues and  
crowd movements.  
During egress,  
departing crowds  
can be actively  
managed—  
specifically car  
parks can be made  
more efficient if  
actively managed  
\(rather than  
allowing a  
free-for-all dash for  
exit\).  
One good idea to incorporate in the above is to colour each section red/  
orange/green\* to indicate if there is a high, medium or low risk for each  
phase/influence. This can then be mapped to a site plan providing further  
explanation of the risk, its location, duration and severity. You can outline  
the physical area of the risk, the time \(start/end, i.e. duration\), the intensity/  
severity of the risk in a single diagram. Situations may change through time  
and your models need to reflect these changes. To begin with, you need to  
think about how your crowd and venue change through time. You need  
draw different sketches for each of those changes, perhaps for each hour of  
your event. You only need three colours\: red, amber and green. Red rep-  
resents danger, green is safe and amber is something you need to monitor.  
Remember, you only need a simple diagram that anyone you give it to can  
understand. Simplify, simplify, simplify.  
Although this sounds like more work, it is a quick, low-cost and informa-  
tive process. The important point to note is that an approval process may  
not have seen the site or event and all the relevant information needs to be  
in the event plan.  
\*

Recruitment & Training  
A well-grounded and practical guide, **Introduction to Crowd Science** highlights  
the underlying causes of crowd disasters and mass fatalities—giving the reader a  
unique insight into crowd-related accidents. This book presents a clearer under-  
standing of crowd risks and provides the reader with a range of simple modelling  
techniques to plan and manage places of public assembly, leading to clear improve-  
ments in crowd safety.  
It is written for students and professionals in a number of areas such as event  
organisation and management and the police and emergency services. It is focused  
on principles independent of any particular software and heavily illustrated with  
examples from practice, both good and bad.  
**Keith Still** has over 25 years of experience in crowd risk analysis and crowd safety.  
He has worked as a consultant for major events, a trainer, an expert witness, and also  
lectures around the world on crowd safety. Projects include the crowd management  
planning for the UK Royal Wedding \(2011\), Olympic Park \(Sydney 2000 and  
London 2012\), the Jamarat Bridge and the Holy Mosque \(Saudi Arabia 2000-2012\),  
and the Manchester City Etihad Stadium development \(2014\).
